<seg id="1"> These are daylong sessions with a language teacher who prepares the new Indian hires to disguise their pronounced Indian accents when speaking English and replace them with American, Canadian, or British ones-depending on which part of the world they will be speaking with. </seg>
<seg id="2"> It's pretty bizarre to watch. </seg>
<seg id="3"> The class I sat in on was being trained to speak in a neutral middle-American accent. </seg>
<seg id="4"> The students were asked to read over and over a single phonetic paragraph designed to teach them how to soften their r's and to roll their r's. </seg>
<seg id="5"> Their teacher, a charming eight-months-pregnant young woman dressed in a traditional Indian sari, moved seamlessly among British, American, and Canadian accents as she demonstrated reading a paragraph designed to highlight phonetics. </seg>
<seg id="6"> She said to the class, </seg>
<seg id="7"> ˇ°Remember the first day I told you that the Americans flap the 'tuh' sound? </seg>
<seg id="8"> You know, it sounds like an almost 'duh' sound-not crisp and clear like the British. </seg>
<seg id="9"> So I would not sayˇ± </seg>
<seg id="10"> ˇŞhere she was crisp and sharpˇŞ </seg>
<seg id="11"> 'ˇ°Betty bought a bit of better butter' or 'Insert a quarter in the meter.' </seg>
<seg id="12"> But I would sayˇ± </seg>
<seg id="13"> ˇŞher voice very flatˇŞ </seg>
<seg id="14"> ˇ°'Insert a quarter in the meter' or 'Betty bought a bit of better butter.' </seg>
<seg id="15"> So I'm just going to read it out for you once, and then we'll read it together. </seg>
<seg id="16"> All right? ' </seg>
<seg id="17"> Thirty little turtles in a bottle of bottled water. </seg>
<seg id="18"> A bottle of bottled water held thirty little turtles. </seg>
<seg id="19"> It didn't matter that each turtle had to rattle a metal ladle in order to get a little bit of noodles.' </seg>
<seg id="20"> ˇ°All right, who's going to read first?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="21"> the instructor asked. </seg>
<seg id="22"> Each member of the class then took a turn trying to say this tongue twister in an American accent. </seg>
<seg id="23"> Some of them got it on the first try, </seg>
<seg id="24"> and others, well, let's just say that you wouldn't think they were in Kansas City if they answered your call to Delta's lost-luggage number. </seg>
<seg id="25"> After listening to them stumble through this phonetics lesson for half an hour, </seg>
<seg id="26"> I asked the teacher if she would like me to give them an authentic version-since I'm originally from Minnesota, </seg>
<seg id="27"> smack in the Midwest, </seg>
<seg id="28"> and still speak like someone out of the movie Fargo. </seg>
<seg id="29"> Absolutely, she said. </seg>
<seg id="30"> So I read the following paragraph: </seg>
<seg id="31"> ˇ°A bottle of bottled water held thirty little turtles. </seg>
<seg id="32"> It didn't matter that each turtle had to rattle a metal ladle in order to get a little bit of noodles, </seg>
<seg id="33"> The class responded enthusiastically. </seg>
<seg id="34"> It was the first time I ever got an ovation for speaking Minnesotan. </seg>
<seg id="35"> On the surface, there is something unappealing about the idea of inducing other people to flatten their accents in order to compete in a flatter world. </seg>
<seg id="36"> But before you disparage it, </seg>
<seg id="37"> you have to taste just how hungry these kids are to escape the lower end of the middle class and move up. </seg>
<seg id="38"> they say. </seg>
<seg id="39"> If a little accent modification is the price they have to pay to jump a rung of the ladder, </seg>
<seg id="40"> then so be it </seg>
<seg id="41"> ˇ°This is a high-stress environment,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="42"> said Nilekani, the CEO of Infosys, which also runs a big call center. </seg>
<seg id="43"> ˇ°It is twenty-four by seven. </seg>
<seg id="44"> You work in the day, and then the night, and then the next morning.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="45"> But the working environment, he insisted, ˇ°is not the tension of alienation. </seg>
<seg id="46"> It is the tension of success. </seg>
<seg id="47"> They are dealing with the challenges of success, of high-pressure living. </seg>
<seg id="48"> It is not the challenge of worrying about whether they would have a challenge.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="49"> That was certainly the sense I got from talking to a lot of the call center operators on the floor. </seg>
<seg id="50"> Like any explosion of modernity, outsourcing is challenging traditional norms and ways of life. </seg>
<seg id="51"> But educated Indians have been held back so many years by both poverty and a socialist bureaucracy that many of them seem more than ready to put up with the hours. </seg>
<seg id="52"> And needless to say, </seg>
<seg id="53"> it is much easier and more satisfying for them to work hard in Bangalore than to pack up and try to make a new start in America. </seg>
<seg id="54"> In the flat world they can stay in India, make a decent salary, and not have to be away from families, friends, food, and culture. </seg>
<seg id="55"> At the end of the day, </seg>
<seg id="56"> these new jobs actually allow them to be more Indian. </seg>
<seg id="57"> Said Anney Unnikrishnan, a personnel manager at 24/7, </seg>
<seg id="58"> ˇ°I finished my MBA and I remember writing the GMAT and getting into Purdue University. </seg>
<seg id="59"> But I couldn't go because I couldn't afford it. I didn't have the money for it. </seg>
<seg id="60"> Now I can, [but] I see a whole lot of American industry has come into Bangalore and I don't really need to go there. I can work for a multinational sitting right here. </seg>
<seg id="61"> So I still get my rice and sambar [a traditional Indian dish], which I eat. I don't need to, you know, learn to eat coleslaw and cold beef. </seg>
<seg id="62"> I still continue with my Indian food and I still work for a multinational. </seg>
<seg id="63"> Why should I go to America?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="64"> The relatively high standard of living that she can now enjoy-enough for a small apartment and car in Bangalore-is good for America as well. </seg>
<seg id="65"> When you look around at 24/7's call center, </seg>
<seg id="66"> you see that all the computers are running Microsoft Windows. </seg>
<seg id="67"> The chips are designed by Intel. </seg>
<seg id="68"> The phones are from Lucent. </seg>
<seg id="69"> The air-conditioning is by Carrier, </seg>
<seg id="70"> and even the bottled water is by Coke. </seg>
<seg id="71"> In addition, 90 percent of the shares in 24/7 are owned by U.S. investors. </seg>
<seg id="72"> This explains why, although the United States has lost some service jobs to India in recent years, total exports from American-based companies-merchandise and services-to India have grown from $2.5 billion in 1990 to $5 billion in 2003. </seg>
<seg id="73"> So even with the outsourcing of some service jobs from the United States to India, India's growing economy is creating a demand for many more American goods and services. </seg>
<seg id="74"> What goes around, comes around. </seg>
<seg id="75"> Nine years ago, when Japan was beating America's brains out in the auto industry, I wrote a column about playing the computer geography game Where in the World is Carmen Sandiego? with my nine-year-old daughter, Orly. </seg>
<seg id="76"> I was trying to help her by giving her a clue suggesting that Carmen had gone to Detroit, </seg>
<seg id="77"> so I asked her, </seg>
<seg id="78"> ˇ°Where are cars made?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="79"> And without missing a beat she answered, </seg>
<seg id="80"> ˇ°Japan.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="81"> Ouch! </seg>
<seg id="82"> Well, I was reminded of that story while visiting Global Edge, an Indian software design firm in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="83"> The company's marketing manager, Rajesh Rao, told me that he had just made a cold call to the VP for engineering of a U.S. company, trying to drum up business. </seg>
<seg id="84"> As soon as Mr. Rao introduced himself as calling from an Indian software firm, the U.S. executive said to him, </seg>
<seg id="85"> ˇ°Namaste,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="86"> a common Hindi greeting. </seg>
<seg id="87"> Said Mr. Rao, ˇ°A few years ago nobody in America wanted to talk to us. </seg>
<seg id="88"> Now they are eager.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="89"> And a few even know how to say hello in proper Hindu fashion. </seg>
<seg id="90"> So now I wonder: </seg>
<seg id="91"> If I have a granddaughter one day, </seg>
<seg id="92"> and I tell her I'm going to India, </seg>
<seg id="93"> will she say, </seg>
<seg id="94"> ˇ°Grandpa, is that where software comes from?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="95"> No, not yet, honey. </seg>
<seg id="96"> Every new product-from software to widgets-goes through a cycle that begins with basic research, then applied research, then incubation, then development, then testing, then manufacturing, then deployment, then support, then continuation engineering in order to add improvements. </seg>
<seg id="97"> Each of these phases is specialized and unique, and neither India nor China nor Russia has a critical mass of talent that can handle the whole product cycle for a big American multinational. </seg>
<seg id="98"> But these countries are steadily developing their reseach and development capabilities to handle more and more of these phases. </seg>
<seg id="99"> As that continues, we really will see the beginning of what Satyam Cherukuri, of Sarnoff, an American research and development firm, has called ˇ°the globalization of innovationˇ± and an end to the old model of a single American or European multinational handling all the elements of the development product cycle from its own resources. </seg>
<seg id="100"> More and more American and European companies are outsourcing significant research and development tasks to India, Russia, and China. </seg>
<seg id="101"> According to the information technology office of the state government in Karnataka, where Bangalore is located, Indian units of Cisco Systems, Intel, IBM, Texas Instruments, and GE have already filed 1,000 patent applications with the U.S. Patent Office. </seg>
<seg id="102"> Texas Instruments alone has had 225 U.S. patents awarded to its Indian operation. </seg>
<seg id="103"> ˇ°The Intel team in Bangalore is developing microprocessor chips for high-speed broadband wireless technology, to be launched in 2006,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="104"> the Karnataka IT office said, in a statement issued at the end of 2004, and ˇ°at GE's John F. Welch Technology Centre in Bangalore, engineers are developing new ideas for aircraft engines, transport systems and plastics.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="105"> Indeed, GE over the years has frequently transferred Indian engineers who worked for it in the United States back to India to integrate its whole global research effort. </seg>
<seg id="106"> GE now even sends non-Indians to Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="107"> Vivek Paul is the president of Wipro Technologies, another of the elite Indian technology companies, but he is based in Silicon Valley to be close to Wipro's American customers. </seg>
<seg id="108"> Before coming to Wipro, Paul managed GE's CT scanner business out of Milwaukee. </seg>
<seg id="109"> At the time he had a French colleague who managed GE's power generator business for the scanners out of France. </seg>
<seg id="110"> ˇ°I ran into him on an airplane recently,ˇ± said Paul, ˇ°and he told me he had moved to India to head up GE's high-energy research there.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="111"> I told Vivek that I love hearing an Indian who used to head up GE's CT business in Milwaukee but now runs Wipro's consulting business in Silicon Valley tell me about his former French colleague who has moved to Bangalore to work for GE. </seg>
<seg id="112"> That is a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="113"> Every time I think I have found the last, most obscure job that could be outsourced to Bangalore, I discover a new one. </seg>
<seg id="114"> My friend Vivek Kulkarni used to head the government office in Bangalore responsible for attracting high technology global investment. </seg>
<seg id="115"> After stepping down from that post in 2003, he started a company called B2K, with a division called Brickwork, which offers busy global executives their own personal assistant in India. </seg>
<seg id="116"> Say you are running a company and you have been asked to give a speech and a PowerPoint presentation in two days. </seg>
<seg id="117"> Your ˇ°remote executive assistantˇ± in India, provided by Brickwork, will do all the research for you, create the PowerPoint presentation, and e-mail the whole thing to you overnight so that it is on your desk the day you have to deliver it. </seg>
<seg id="118"> ˇ°You can give your personal remote executive assistant their assignment when you are leaving work at the end of the day in New York City, and it will be ready for you the next morning,ˇ± explained Kulkarni. </seg>
<seg id="119"> ˇ°Because of the time difference with India, they can work on it while you sleep and have it back in your morning.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="120"> Kulkarni suggested I hire a remote assistant in India to do all the research for this book. </seg>
<seg id="121"> ˇ°He or she could also help you keep pace with what you want to read. </seg>
<seg id="122"> When you wake up, you will find the completed summary in your in-box.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="123"> (I told him no one could be better than my longtime assistant, Maya Gorman, who sits ten feet away!) </seg>
<seg id="124"> Having your own personal remote executive assistant costs around $1,500 to $2,000 a month, </seg>
<seg id="125"> and given the pool of Indian college grads from which Brickwork can recruit, </seg>
<seg id="126"> the brainpower you can hire dollar-for-dollar is substantial. </seg>
<seg id="127"> As Brickwork's promotional material says, </seg>
<seg id="128"> ˇ°India's talent pool provides companies access to a broad spectrum of highly qualified people. </seg>
<seg id="129"> In addition to fresh graduates, which are around 2.5 million per year, many qualified homemakers are entering the job market.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="130"> India's business schools, it adds, produce around eighty-nine thousand MBAs per year. </seg>
<seg id="131"> ˇ°We've had a wonderful response,ˇ± said Kulkarni, </seg>
<seg id="132"> with clients coming from two main areas. </seg>
<seg id="133"> One is American health-care consultants, who often need lots of numbers crunched and PowerPoint presentations drawn up. </seg>
<seg id="134"> The other, he said, are American investment banks and financial services companies, which often need to prepare glossy pamphlets with graphs to illustrate the benefits of an IPO or a proposed merger. </seg>
<seg id="135"> In the case of a merger, Brickwork will prepare those sections of the report dealing with general market conditions and trends, where most of the research can be gleaned off the Web and summarized in a standard format. </seg>
<seg id="136"> ˇ°The judgment of how to price the deal will come from the investment bankers themselves,ˇ± said Kulkarni. </seg>
<seg id="137"> ˇ°We will do the lower-end work, and they will do the things that require critical judgment and experience, close to the market.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="138"> The more projects his team of remote executive assistants engages in, the more knowledge they build up. </seg>
<seg id="139"> They are full of ambition to do their higher problem solving as well, said Kulkarni. ˇ°The idea is to constantly learn. </seg>
<seg id="140"> You are always taking an examination. </seg>
<seg id="141"> There is no end to learning... </seg>
<seg id="142"> There is no real end to what can be done by whom.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="143"> Unlike Columbus, I didn't stop with India. </seg>
<seg id="144"> After I got home, I decided to keep exploring the East for more signs that the world was flat. </seg>
<seg id="145"> So after India, I was soon off to Tokyo, where I had a chance to interview Kenichi Ohmae, the legendary former McKinsey &amp; Company consultant in Japan. </seg>
<seg id="146"> Ohmae has left McKinsey and struck out on his own in business, Ohmae &amp; Associates. </seg>
<seg id="147"> And what do they do? </seg>
<seg id="148"> Not consulting anymore, explained Ohmae. </seg>
<seg id="149"> He is now spearheading a drive to outsource low-end Japanese jobs to Japanese-speaking call centers and service providers in China. </seg>
<seg id="150"> ˇ°Say what?ˇ± I asked. ˇ°To China? </seg>
<seg id="151"> Didn't the Japanese once colonize China, leaving a very bad taste in the mouths of the Chinese?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="152"> Well, yes, said Ohmae, but he explained that the Japanese also left behind a large number of Japanese speakers who have maintained a slice of Japanese culture, from sushi to karaoke, in northeastern China, </seg>
<seg id="153"> particularly around the northeastern port city of Dalian. </seg>
<seg id="154"> Dalian has become for Japan what Bangalore has become for America and the other English-speaking countries: outsourcing central. </seg>
<seg id="155"> The Chinese may never forgive Japan for what it did to China in the last century, but the Chinese are so focused on leading the world in the next century that they are ready to brush up on their Japanese and take all the work Japan can outsource. </seg>
<seg id="156"> ˇ°The recruiting is quite easy,ˇ± said Ohmae in early 2004. </seg>
<seg id="157"> ˇ°About one-third of the people in this region [around Dalian] have taken Japanese as a second language in high school. </seg>
<seg id="158"> So all of these Japanese companies are coming in.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="159"> Ohmae's company is doing primarily data-entry work in China, where Chinese workers take handwritten Japanese documents, which are scanned, faxed, or e-mailed over from Japan to Dalian, and then type them into a digital database in Japanese characters. </seg>
<seg id="160"> Ohmae's company has developed a software program that takes the data to be entered and breaks it down into packets. </seg>
<seg id="161"> These packets can then be sent around China or Japan for typing, depending on the specialty required, and then reassembled at the company's database in its Tokyo headquarters. ˇ°We have the ability to allocate the job to the person who knows the area best.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="162"> Ohmae's company even has contracts with more than seventy thousand housewives, some of them specialists in medical or legal terminologies, to do data-entry work at home. </seg>
<seg id="163"> The firm has recently expanded into computer-aided designs for a Japanese housing company. </seg>
<seg id="164"> ˇ°When you negotiate with the customer in Japan for building a house,ˇ± he explained, ˇ°you would sketch out a floor plan-most of these companies don't use computers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="165"> So the hand-drawn plans are sent electronically to China, where they are converted into digital designs, which then are e-mailed back to the Japanese building firm, which turns them into manufacturing blueprints. </seg>
<seg id="166"> ˇ°We took the best-performing Chinese data operators,ˇ± said Ohmae, ˇ°and now they are processing seventy houses a day.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="167"> Chinese doing computer drawings for Japanese homes, nearly seventy years after a rapacious Japanese army occupied China, razing many homes in the process. </seg>
<seg id="168"> Maybe there is hope for this flat world... </seg>
<seg id="169"> I needed to see Dalian, this Bangalore of China, firsthand, so I kept moving around the East. </seg>
<seg id="170"> Dalian is impressive not just for a Chinese city. </seg>
<seg id="171"> With its wide boulevards, beautiful green spaces, and nexus of universities, technical colleges, and massive software park, Dalian would stand out in Silicon Valley. </seg>
<seg id="172"> I had been here in 1998, but there had been so much new building since then that I did not recognize the place. </seg>
<seg id="173"> Dalian, which is located about an hour's flight northeast of Beijing, symbolizes how rapidly China's most modern cities </seg>
<seg id="174"> and there are still plenty of miserable, backward ones-are grabbing business as knowledge centers, not just as manufacturing hubs. </seg>
<seg id="175"> The signs on the buildings tell the whole story: GE, Microsoft, Dell, SAP, HP, Sony, and Accenture- to name but a few- </seg>
<seg id="176"> all are having backroom work done here to support their Asian operations, as well as new software research and development. </seg>
<seg id="177"> Because of its proximity to Japan and Korea, each only about an hour away by air, </seg>
<seg id="178"> its large number of Japanese speakers, </seg>
<seg id="179"> its abundance of Internet bandwidth, </seg>
<seg id="180"> and many parks and a world-class golf course (all of which appeal to knowledge workers), Dalian has become an attractive locus for Japanese outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="181"> Japanese firms can hire three Chinese software engineers for the price of one in Japan and still have change to pay a roomful of call center operators ($90 a month starting salary). </seg>
<seg id="182"> No wonder some twenty-eight hundred Japanese companies have set up operations here or teamed up with Chinese partners. </seg>
<seg id="183"> said Win Liu, director of U.S./EU projects for DHC, one of Dalian's biggest homegrown software firms, </seg>
<seg id="184"> ˇ°I've taken a lot of American people to Dalian, and they are amazed at how fast the China economy is growing in this high-tech area,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="185"> which has expanded from thirty to twelve hundred employees in six years. </seg>
<seg id="186"> ˇ°Americans don't realize the challenge to the extent that they should.ˇ±Dalian's dynamic mayor, Xia Deren, forty-nine, is a former college president. </seg>
<seg id="187"> (For a Communist authoritarian system, China does a pretty good job of promoting people on merit. </seg>
<seg id="188"> The Mandarin meritocratic culture here still runs very deep.) </seg>
<seg id="189"> Over a traditional ten-course Chinese dinner at a local hotel, the mayor told me how far Dalian has come and just where he intends to take it. </seg>
<seg id="190"> ˇ°We have twenty-two universities and colleges with over two hundred thousand students in Dalian,ˇ± he explained. </seg>
<seg id="191"> More than half those students graduate with engineering or science degrees, and even those who don't, those who study history or literature, are still being directed to spend a year studying Japanese or English, plus computer science, so that they will be employable. </seg>
<seg id="192"> The mayor estimated that more than half the residents of Dalian had access to the Internet at the office, home, or school. </seg>
<seg id="193"> ˇ°The Japanese enterprises originally started some data processing industries here,ˇ± the mayor added, ˇ°and with this as a base they have now moved to R &amp; D and software development... </seg>
<seg id="194"> In the past one or two years, the software companies of the U.S. are also making some attempts to move outsourcing of software from the U.S. to our city... </seg>
<seg id="195"> We are approaching and we are catching up with the Indians. </seg>
<seg id="196"> Exports of software products [from Dalian] have been increasing by 50 percent annually. </seg>
<seg id="197"> And China is now becoming the country that develops the largest number of university graduates. </seg>
<seg id="198"> Though in general our English is not as competent as that of the Indian people, we have a bigger population, [so] we can pick out the most intelligent students who can speak the best English.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="199"> Are Dalian residents bothered by working for the Japanese, whose government has still never formally apologized for what the wartime Japanese government did to China? </seg>
<seg id="200"> ˇ°We will never forget that a historical war occurred between the two nations,ˇ± he answered, ˇ°but when it comes to the field of economy, we only focus on the economic problems-especially if we talk about the software outsourcing business. </seg>
<seg id="201"> If the U.S. and Japanese companies make their products in our city, we consider that to be a good thing. </seg>
<seg id="202"> Our youngsters are trying to learn Japanese, to master this tool so they can compete with their Japanese counterparts to successfully land high-salary positions for themselves in the future.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="203"> The mayor then added for good measure, ˇ°My personal feeling is that Chinese youngsters are more ambitious than Japanese or American youngsters in recent years, but I don't think they are ambitious enough, because they are not as ambitious as my generation. </seg>
<seg id="204"> Because our generation, before they got into university and colleges, were sent to distant rural areas and factories and military teams, and went through a very hard time, so in terms of the spirit to overcome and face the hardships, [our generation had to have more ambition] than youngsters nowadays.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="205"> Mayor Xia had a charmingly direct way of describing the world, and although some of what he had to say gets lost in translation, he gets itˇŞand Americans should too: </seg>
<seg id="206"> ˇ°The rule of the market economy,ˇ± this Communist official explained to me, ˇ°is that if somewhere has the richest human resources and the cheapest labor, of course the enterprises and the businesses will naturally go there.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="207"> In manufacturing, he pointed out, ˇ°Chinese people first were the employees and working for the big foreign manufacturers, and after several years, after we have learned all the processes and steps, we can start our own firms. </seg>
<seg id="208"> Software will go down the same road... </seg>
<seg id="209"> It is like building a building. </seg>
<seg id="210"> Today, the U.S., you are the designers, the architects, and the developing countries are the bricklayers for the buildings. </seg>
<seg id="211"> But one day I hope we will be the architects.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="212"> I just kept exploring-east and west. </seg>
<seg id="213"> By the summer of 2004,1 was in Colorado on vacation. </seg>
<seg id="214"> I had heard about this new low-fare airline called JetBlue, which was launched in 1999. </seg>
<seg id="215"> I had no idea where they operated, but I needed to fly between Washington and Atlanta, and couldn't quite get the times I wanted, so I decided to call JetBlue and see where exactly they flew. </seg>
<seg id="216"> I confess I did have another motive. </seg>
<seg id="217"> I had heard that JetBlue had outsourced its entire reservation system to housewives in Utah, and I wanted to check this out. </seg>
<seg id="218"> So I dialed JetBlue reservations and had the following conversation with the agent: </seg>
<seg id="219"> ˇ°Hello, this is Dolly. </seg>
<seg id="220"> Can I help you?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="221"> answered a grandmotherly voice. </seg>
<seg id="222"> ˇ°Yes, I would like to fly from Washington to Atlanta,ˇ± I said. ˇ°Do you fly that route?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="223"> ˇ°No, I'm sorry we don't. We fly from Washington to Ft. Lauderdale,ˇ± said Dolly. </seg>
<seg id="224"> ˇ°How about Washington to New York City?ˇ± I asked. </seg>
<seg id="225"> ˇ°I'm sorry, we don't fly that route. We do fly from Washington to Oakland and Long Beach,ˇ± said Dolly. </seg>
<seg id="226"> ˇ°Say, can I ask you something? </seg>
<seg id="227"> Are you really at home? </seg>
<seg id="228"> I read that JetBlue agents just work at home.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="229"> ˇ°Yes, I am,ˇ± said Dolly in the most cheerful voice. </seg>
<seg id="230"> (I later confirmed with JetBlue that her full name is Dolly Baker.) </seg>
<seg id="231"> ˇ°I am sitting in my office upstairs in my house, looking out the window at a beautiful sunny day. </seg>
<seg id="232"> Just five minutes ago someone called and asked me that same question and I told them and they said, 'Good, I thought you were going to tell me you were in New Delhi.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="233"> ˇ°Where do you live?ˇ± I asked. </seg>
<seg id="234"> ˇ°Salt Lake City, Utah,ˇ± said Dolly. </seg>
<seg id="235"> ˇ°We have a two-story home, and I love working here, especially in the winter when the snow is swirling and I am up here in the office at home.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="236"> ˇ°How do you get such a job?ˇ± I asked. </seg>
<seg id="237"> ˇ°You know, they don't advertise,ˇ± said Dolly in the sweetest possible voice. </seg>
<seg id="238"> ˇ°It's all by word of mouth. </seg>
<seg id="239"> I worked for the state government and I retired, and [after a little while] I thought I have to do something else and I just love it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="240"> David Neeleman, the founder and CEO of JetBlue Airways Corp., has a name for all this. </seg>
<seg id="241"> He calls it ˇ°homesourcing.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="242"> JetBlue now has four hundred reservation agents, like Dolly, working at home in the Salt Lake City area, taking reservations-in between babysitting, exercising, writing novels, and cooking dinner. </seg>
<seg id="243"> A few months later I visited Neeleman at JetBlue's headquarters in New York, and he explained to me the virtues of homesourcing, which he actually started at Morris Air, his first venture in the airline business. (It was bought by Southwest.) </seg>
<seg id="244"> ˇ°We had 250 people in their homes doing reservations at Morris Air,ˇ± said Neeleman. </seg>
<seg id="245"> ˇ°They were 30 percent more productive-they take 30 percent more bookings, by just being happier. They were more loyal and there was less attrition. </seg>
<seg id="246"> So when I started JetBlue, I said, 'We are going to have 100 percent reservation at home.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="247"> Neeleman has a personal reason for wanting to do this. </seg>
<seg id="248"> He is a Mormon and believes that society will be better off if more mothers are able to stay at home with their young children but are given a chance to be wage earners at the same time. </seg>
<seg id="249"> So he based his home reservations system in Salt Lake City, where the vast majority of the women are Mormons and many are stay-at-home mothers. </seg>
<seg id="250"> Home reservationists work twenty-five hours a week and have to come into the JetBlue regional office in Salt Lake City for four hours a month to learn new skills and be brought up to date on what is going on inside the company. </seg>
<seg id="251"> ˇ°We will never outsource to Indiaˇ±said Neeleman. </seg>
<seg id="252"> The quality we can get here is far superior... </seg>
<seg id="253"> [Employers] are more willing to outsource to India than to their own homes, and I can't understand that. </seg>
<seg id="254"> Somehow they think that people need to be sitting in front of them or some boss they have designated. </seg>
<seg id="255"> The productivity we get here more than makes up for the India [wage] factor. </seg>
<seg id="256"> ˇ°A Los Angeles Times story about JetBlue (May 9, 2004) noted that ˇ°in 1997, 11.6 million employees of U.S. companies worked from home at least part of the time. </seg>
<seg id="257"> Today, that number has soared to 23.5 million-16% of the American labor force. </seg>
<seg id="258"> (Meanwhile, the ranks of the self-employed, who often work from home, have swelled during the same period-to 23.4 million from 18 million.) </seg>
<seg id="259"> In some eyes, homesourcing and outsourcing aren't so much competing strategies as they are different manifestations of the same thing: a relentless push by corporate America to lower costs and increase efficiency, wherever that may lead.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="260"> That is exactly what I was learning on my own travels: </seg>
<seg id="261"> Homesourcing to Salt Lake City and outsourcing to Bangalore were just flip sides of the same coin-sourcing. </seg>
<seg id="262"> And the new, new thing, I was also learning, is the degree to which it is now possible for companies and individuals to source work anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="263"> I just kept moving. </seg>
<seg id="264"> In the fall of 2004,1 accompanied the chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, General Richard Myers, on a tour of hot spots in Iraq. </seg>
<seg id="265"> We visited Baghdad, the U.S. military headquarters in Fallujah, and the 24th Marine Expeditionary Unit encampment outside Babil, in the heart of Iraq's so-called Sunni Triangle. </seg>
<seg id="266"> The makeshift 24th MEU base is a sort of Fort Apache, in the middle of a pretty hostile Iraqi Sunni Muslim population. </seg>
<seg id="267"> While General Myers was meeting with officers and enlisted men there, I was free to walk around the base, and eventually I wandered into the command center, where my eye was immediately caught by a large flat-screen TV. </seg>
<seg id="268"> On the screen was a live TV feed that looked to be coming from some kind of overhead camera. </seg>
<seg id="269"> It showed some people moving around behind a house. </seg>
<seg id="270"> Also on the screen, along the right side, was an active instant-messaging chat room, which seemed to be discussing the scene on the TV. </seg>
<seg id="271"> ˇ°What is that?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="272"> I asked the soldier who was carefully monitoring all the images from a laptop. </seg>
<seg id="273"> He explained that a U.S. Predator drone-a small pilotless aircraft with a high-power television camera-was flying over an Iraqi village, in the 24th MEU's area of operation, and feeding real-time intelligence images back to his laptop and this flat screen. </seg>
<seg id="274"> This drone was actually being ˇ°flownˇ± and manipulated by an expert who was sitting back at Nellis Air Force Base in Las Vegas, Nevada. </seg>
<seg id="275"> That's right, the drone over Iraq was actually being remotely directed from Las Vegas. </seg>
<seg id="276"> Meanwhile, the video images it was beaming back were being watched simultaneously by the 24th MEU, United States Central Command headquarters in Tampa, CentCom regional headquarters in Qatar, in the Pentagon, and probably also at the CIA. </seg>
<seg id="277"> The different analysts around the world were conducting an online chat about how to interpret what was going on and what to do about it. </seg>
<seg id="278"> It was their conversation that was scrolling down the right side of the screen. </seg>
<seg id="279"> Before I could even express my amazement, another officer traveling with us took me aback by saying that this technology had ˇ°flattenedˇ± the military hierarchy-by giving so much information to the low-level officer, or even enlisted man, who was operating the computer, and empowering him to make decisions about the information he was gathering. </seg>
<seg id="280"> While I'm sure that no first lieutenant is going to be allowed to start a firefight without consulting superiors, the days when only senior officers had the big picture are over. </seg>
<seg id="281"> The military playing field is being leveled. </seg>
<seg id="282"> I told this story to my friend Nick Burns, the U.S. ambassador to NATO and a loyal member of the Red Sox Nation. </seg>
<seg id="283"> Nick told me he was at CentCom headquarters in Qatar in April 2004, being briefed by General John Abizaid and his staff. </seg>
<seg id="284"> Abizaid's team was seated across the table from Nick with four flat-screen TVs behind them. </seg>
<seg id="285"> The first three had overhead images being relayed in real time from different sectors of Iraq by Predator drones. </seg>
<seg id="286"> The last one, which Nick was focused on, was showing a Yankees-Red Sox game. </seg>
<seg id="287"> Flat burgers and Fries </seg>
<seg id="288"> I kept moving-all the way back to my home in Bethesda, Maryland. </seg>
<seg id="289"> By the time I settled back into my house from this journey to the edges of the earth, my head was spinning. </seg>
<seg id="290"> But no sooner was I home than more signs of the flattening came knocking at my door. </seg>
<seg id="291"> Some came in the form of headlines that would unnerve any parent concerned about where his college-age children are going to fit in. </seg>
<seg id="292"> For instance, Forrester Research, Inc., was projecting that more than 3 million service and professional jobs would move out of the country by 2015. </seg>
<seg id="293"> But my jaw really dropped when I read a July 19, 2004, article from the International Herald Tribune headlined: ˇ°Want Fries With Outsourcing?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="294"> ˇ°Pull off U.S. Interstate Highway 55 near Cape Girardeau, Missouri, and into the drive-through lane of a McDonald's next to the highway and you'll get fast, friendly service, even though the person taking your order is not in the restaurant-or even in Missouri,ˇ± the article said. </seg>
<seg id="295"> ˇ°The order taker is in a call center in Colorado Springs, more than 900 miles, or 1,450 kilometers, away, connected to the customer and to the workers preparing the food by high-speed data lines. </seg>
<seg id="296"> Even some restaurant jobs, it seems, are not immune to outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="297"> ˇ°The man who owns the Cape Girardeau restaurant, Shannon Davis, has linked it and three other of his 12 McDonald's franchises to the Colorado call center, which is run by another McDonald's franchisee, Steven Bigari. </seg>
<seg id="298"> And he did it for the same reasons that other business owners have embraced call centers: </seg>
<seg id="299"> lower costs, greater speed and fewer mistakes. </seg>
<seg id="300"> ˇ°Cheap, quick and reliable telecommunications lines let the order takers in Colorado Springs converse with customers in Missouri, take an electronic snapshot of them, display their order on a screen to make sure it is right, then forward the order and the photo to the restaurant kitchen. </seg>
<seg id="301"> The photo is destroyed as soon as the order is completed, Bigari said. </seg>
<seg id="302"> People picking up their burgers never know that their order traverses two states and bounces back before they can even start driving to the pickup window. </seg>
<seg id="303"> ˇ°Davis said that he had dreamed of doing something like this for more than a decade. 'We could not wait to go with it,' he added. </seg>
<seg id="304"> Bigari, who created the call center for his own restaurants, was happy to oblige- for a small fee per transaction.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="305"> The article noted that McDonald's Corp. said it found the call center idea interesting enough to start a test with three stores near its headquarters in Oak Brook, Illinois, with different software from that used by Bigari. </seg>
<seg id="306"> ˇ°Jim Sappington, a McDonald's vice president for information technology, said that it was 'way, way too early' to tell if the call center idea would work across the thirteen thousand McDonald's restaurants in the United States... Still, franchisees of two other McDonald's restaurants, beyond Davis's, have outsourced their drive-through ordering to Bigari in Colorado Springs. </seg>
<seg id="307"> (The other restaurants are in Brainerd, Minnesota, and Norwood, Massachusetts.) </seg>
<seg id="308"> Central to the system's success, Bigari said, is the way it pairs customers' photos with their orders; </seg>
<seg id="309"> by increasing accuracy, the system cuts down on the number of complaints and therefore makes the service faster. </seg>
<seg id="310"> In the fast-food business, time is truly money: shaving even five seconds off the processing time of an order is significant,ˇ± the article noted. </seg>
<seg id="311"> ˇ°Bigari said he had cut order time in his dual-lane drive-throughs by slightly more than 30 seconds, to about 1 minute, 5 seconds, on average. </seg>
<seg id="312"> That's less than half the average of 2 minutes, 36 seconds, for all McDonald's, and among the fastest of any franchise in the country, according to QSRweb.com, which tracks such things. </seg>
<seg id="313"> His drive-throughs now handle 260 cars an hour, Bigari said, 30 more than they did before he started the call center... </seg>
<seg id="314"> Though his operators earn, on average, 40 cents an hour more than his line employees, he has cut his overall labor costs by a percentage point, even as drive-through sales have increased... </seg>
<seg id="315"> Tests conducted by outside companies found that Bigari's drive-throughs now make mistakes on fewer than 2 percent of all orders, down from about 4 percent before he started using the call centers, Bigari said. </seg>
<seg id="316"> ˇ±Bigari ˇ°is so enthusiastic about the call center idea,ˇ± the article noted, ˇ°that he has expanded it beyond the drive-through window at his seven restaurants that use the system. </seg>
<seg id="317"> While he still offers counter service at those restaurants, most customers now order through the call center, using phones with credit card readers on tables in the seating area.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="318"> On October 3, 2004,1 appeared on the CBS News Sunday morning show Face the Nation, hosted by veteran CBS correspondent Bob Schieffer. </seg>
<seg id="319"> CBS had been in the news a lot in previous weeks because of Dan Rather's 60 Minutes report about President George W. Bush's Air National Guard service that turned out to be based on bogus documents. </seg>
<seg id="320"> After the show that Sunday, Schieffer mentioned that the oddest thing had happened to him the week before. </seg>
<seg id="321"> When he walked out of the CBS studio, a young reporter was waiting for him on the sidewalk. </seg>
<seg id="322"> This isn't all that unusual, because as with all the Sunday-morning shows, the major networks-CBS, NBC, ABC, CNN, and Fox-always send crews to one another's studios to grab exit interviews with the guests. </seg>
<seg id="323"> But this young man, Schieffer explained, was not from a major network. </seg>
<seg id="324"> He politely introduced himself as a reporter for a Web site called InDC Journal and asked whether he could ask Schieffer a few questions. </seg>
<seg id="325"> Schieffer, being a polite fellow, said sure. </seg>
<seg id="326"> The young man interviewed him on a device Schieffer did not recognize and then asked if he could take his picture. </seg>
<seg id="327"> A picture? Schieffer noticed that the young man had no camera. </seg>
<seg id="328"> He didn't need one. </seg>
<seg id="329"> He turned his cell phone around and snapped Schieffer's picture. </seg>
<seg id="330"> ˇ°So I came in the next morning and looked up this Web site and there was my picture and the interview and there were already three hundred comments about it,ˇ± said Schieffer, who, though keenly aware of online journalism, was nevertheless taken aback at the incredibly fast, low-cost, and solo manner in which this young man had put him up in lights. </seg>
<seg id="331"> I was intrigued by this story, so I tracked down the young man from InDC Journal. </seg>
<seg id="332"> His name is Bill Ardolino, and he is a very thoughtful guy. </seg>
<seg id="333"> I conducted my own interview with him online -how else? -and began by asking about what equipment he was using as a one-man network/newspaper. </seg>
<seg id="334"> ˇ°I used a minuscule MP3 player/digital recorder (three and a half inches by two inches) to get the recording, and a separate small digital camera phone to snap his picture,ˇ± said Ardolino. </seg>
<seg id="335"> ˇ°Not quite as sexy as an all-in-one phone/camera/recorder (which does exist), but a statement on the ubiquity and miniaturization of technology nonetheless. </seg>
<seg id="336"> I carry this equipment around D.C. at all times because, hey, you never know. </seg>
<seg id="337"> Ardolino said the MP3 player cost him about $125. </seg>
<seg id="338"> It is ˇ°primarily designed to play music,ˇ± he explained, but it also ˇ°comes prepackaged as a digital recorder that creates a WAV sound file that can be uploaded back to a computer... </seg>
<seg id="339"> Basically, I'd say that the barrier to entry to do journalism that requires portable, ad hoc recording equipment, is [now] about $100-$200 to $300 if you add a camera, $400 to $500 for a pretty nice recorder and a pretty nice camera. </seg>
<seg id="340"> [But] $200 is all that you need to get the job done.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="341"> What prompted him to become his own news network? </seg>
<seg id="342"> ˇ°Being an independent journalist is a hobby that sprang from my frustration about biased, incomplete, selective, and/or incompetent information gathering by the mainstream media,ˇ± explained Ardolino, who describes himself as a ˇ°center-right libertarian.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="343"> ˇ°Independent journalism and its relative, blogging, are expressions of market forces-a need is not being met by current information sources. </seg>
<seg id="344"> I started taking pictures and doing interviews of the antiwar rallies in D.C, because the media was grossly misrepresenting the nature of the groups that were organizing the gatherings-unrepentant Marxists, explicit and implicit supporters of terror, etc. </seg>
<seg id="345"> I originally chose to use humor as a device, but I've since branched out. Do I have more power, power to get my message out, yes. </seg>
<seg id="346"> The Schieffer interview actually brought in about twenty-five thousand visits in twenty-four hours. </seg>
<seg id="347"> My peak day since I've started was fifty-five thousand when I helped break 'Rathergate'... I interviewed the first forensics expert in the Dan Rather National Guard story, and he was then specifically picked up by The Washington Post, Chicago Sun-Times, Globe, NYT, etc., within forty-eight hours. </seg>
<seg id="348"> ˇ°The pace of information gathering and correction in the CBS fake memo story was astoundingˇ±he continued. </seg>
<seg id="349"> It wasn't just that CBS News 'stonewalled' after the fact, it was arguably that they couldn't keep up with an army of dedicated fact-checkers. The speed and openness of the medium is something that runs rings around the old process... </seg>
<seg id="350"> I'm a twenty-nine-year-old marketing manager [who] always wanted to write for a living but hated the AP style book. </seg>
<seg id="351"> As iiberblogger Glenn Reynolds likes to say, blogs have given the people a chance to stop yelling at their TV and have a say in the process. </seg>
<seg id="352"> I think that they serve as sort of a 'fifth estate' that works in conjunction with the mainstream media (often by keeping an eye on them or feeding them raw info) and potentially function as a journalism and commentary farm system that provides a new means to establish success. </seg>
<seg id="353"> ˇ°Like many facets of the topic that you're talking about in your book, there are good and bad aspects of the development. </seg>
<seg id="354"> The splintering of media makes for a lot of incoherence or selective cognition (look at our country's polarization), but it also decentralizes power and provides a better guarantee that the complete truth is out there... somewhere... in pieces.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="355"> On any given day one can come across any number of stories, like the encounter between Bob Schieffer and Bill Ardolino, that tell you that old hierarchies are being flattened and the playing field is being leveled. </seg>
<seg id="356"> As Micah L. Sifry nicely put it in The Nation magazine (November 22, 2004): ˇ°The era of top-down politics-where campaigns, institutions and journalism were cloistered communities powered by hard-to-amass capitalˇŞis over. Something wilder, more engaging and infinitely more satisfying to individual participants is arising alongside the old order.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="357"> I offer the Schieffer-Ardolino encounter as just one example of how the flattening of the world has happened faster and changed rules, roles, and relationships more quickly than we could have imagined. </seg>
<seg id="358"> As I detail in the next chapter, we are entering a phase where we are going to see the digitization, virtualization, and automation of almost everything. </seg>
<seg id="359"> The gains in productivity will be staggering for those countries, companies, and individuals who can absorb the new technological tools. </seg>
<seg id="360"> And we are entering a phase where more people than ever before in the history of the world are going to have access to these tools- as innovators, as collaborators, and, alas, even as terrorists. </seg>
<seg id="361"> Well, the real information revolution is about to begin. </seg>
<seg id="362"> I call this new phase Globalization 3.0 because it followed Globalization 2.0, but I think this new era of globalization will prove to be such a difference of degree that it will be seen, in time, as a difference in kind. </seg>
<seg id="363"> That is why I introduced the idea that the world has gone from round to flat. </seg>
<seg id="364"> Everywhere you turn, hierarchies are being challenged from below or transforming themselves from top-down structures into more horizontal and collaborative ones. </seg>
<seg id="365"> ˇ°Globalization is the word we came up with to describe the changing relationships between governments and big businesses,ˇ± said David Rothkopf, a former senior Department of Commerce official in the Clinton administration and now a private strategic consultant. </seg>
<seg id="366"> ˇ°But what is going on today is a much broader, much more profound phenomenon.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="367"> It is not simply about how governments, business, and people communicate, not just about how organizations interact, but is about the emergence of completely new social, political, and business models. </seg>
<seg id="368"> ˇ°It is about things that impact some of the deepest, most ingrained aspects of society right down to the nature of the social contract,ˇ± added Rothkopf. </seg>
<seg id="369"> ˇ°What happens if the political entity in which you are located no longer corresponds to a job that takes place in cyberspace, or no longer really encompasses workers collaborating with other workers in different corners of the globe, or no longer really captures products produced in multiple places simultaneously? </seg>
<seg id="370"> Who regulates the work? </seg>
<seg id="371"> Who taxes it? </seg>
<seg id="372"> Who should benefit from those taxes?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="373"> If I am right about the flattening of the world, it will be remembered as one of those fundamental changes-like the rise of the nation-state or the Industrial Revolution-each of which, in its day, noted Rothkopf, produced changes in the role of individuals, the role and form of governments, the way we innovated, the way we conducted business, the role of women, the way we fought wars, the way we educated ourselves, the way religion responded, the way art was expressed, the way science and research were conducted, not to mention the political labels we assigned to ourselves and to our opponents. </seg>
<seg id="374"> ˇ°There are certain pivot points or watersheds in history that are greater than others because the changes they produced were so sweeping, multifaceted, and hard to predict at the time,ˇ± Rothkopf said. </seg>
<seg id="375"> If the prospect of this flattening-and all of the pressures, dislocations, and opportunities accompanying it-causes you unease about the future, you are neither alone nor wrong. </seg>
<seg id="376"> Whenever civilization has gone through one of these disruptive, dislocating technological revolutions- like Gutenberg's introduction of the printing press-the whole world has changed in profound ways. </seg>
<seg id="377"> But there is something about the flattening of the world that is going to be qualitatively different from other such profound changes: the speed and breadth with which it is taking hold. The introduction of printing happened over a period of decades and for a long time affected only a relatively small part of the planet. </seg>
<seg id="378"> Same with the Industrial Revolution. </seg>
<seg id="379"> This flattening process is happening at warp speed and directly or indirectly touching a lot more people on the planet at once. </seg>
<seg id="380"> The faster and broader this transition to a new era, the more likely is the potential for disruption, as opposed to an orderly transfer of power from the old winners to the new winners. </seg>
<seg id="381"> To put it another way, the experiences of the high-tech companies in the last few decades who failed to navigate the rapid changes brought about in their marketplace by these types of forces may be a warning to all the businesses, institutions, and nation-states that are now facing these inevitable, even predictable, changes but lack the leadership, flexibility, and imagination to adapt-not because they are not smart or aware, </seg>
<seg id="382"> but because the speed of change is simply overwhelming them. </seg>
<seg id="383"> And that is why the great challenge for our time will be to absorb these changes in ways that do not overwhelm people but also do not leave them behind. </seg>
<seg id="384"> None of this will be easy. </seg>
<seg id="385"> But this is our task. </seg>
<seg id="386"> It is inevitable and unavoidable. </seg>
<seg id="387"> It is the ambition of this book to offer a framework for how to think about it and manage it to our maximum benefit. </seg>
<seg id="388"> I have shared with you in this chapter how I personally discovered that the world is flat. </seg>
<seg id="389"> The next chapter details how it got that way. </seg>
<seg id="390"> TWO The Ten Forces That Flattened the World </seg>
<seg id="391"> he Bible tells us that God created the world in six days and on the seventh day he rested. </seg>
<seg id="392"> Flattening the world took a little longer. </seg>
<seg id="393"> The world has been flattened by the convergence often major political events, innovations, and companies. </seg>
<seg id="394"> None of us has rested since, or maybe ever will again. </seg>
<seg id="395"> This chapter is about the forces that flattened the world and the multiple new forms and tools for collaboration that this flattening has created. </seg>
<seg id="396"> Flattener #1: 11/9/89, When the Walls Came Down and the Windows Went Up </seg>
<seg id="397"> The first time I saw the Berlin Wall, it already had a hole in it. </seg>
<seg id="398"> It was December 1990, and I was traveling to Berlin with the reporters covering Secretary of State James A. Baker III. </seg>
<seg id="399"> Secretary Baker was making his first visit to see this crumbled monument to Soviet communism. </seg>
<seg id="400"> I was standing next to him with a small group of reporters. </seg>
<seg id="401"> ˇ°It was a foggy, overcast day,ˇ± Baker recalled in his memoir, The Politics of Diplomacy, ˇ°and in my raincoat, I felt like a character in a John le Carre novel. </seg>
<seg id="402"> But as I peered through a crack in the Wall [near the Reichstag] and saw the high-resolution drabness that characterizes East Berlin, I realized that the ordinary men and women of East Germany, peacefully and persistently, had taken matters into their own hands. </seg>
<seg id="403"> This was their revolution.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="404"> After Baker finished looking through the wall and moved along, we reporters took turns peering through the same jagged concrete hole. </seg>
<seg id="405"> I brought a couple of chunks of the wall home for my daughters. </seg>
<seg id="406"> I remember thinking how unnatural it looked-indeed, what a bizarre thing it was, this cement wall snaking across a modern city for the sole purpose of preventing the people on the other side from enjoying, even glimpsing, freedom. </seg>
<seg id="407"> For some, particularly among the older generations, this was an unwelcome transformation. </seg>
<seg id="408"> In fact, there was no better system in the world for that than communism. </seg>
<seg id="409"> Capitalism made people unequally rich, and for some who were used to the plodding, limited, but secure Socialist lifestyle-where a job, a house, an education, and a pension were all guaranteed, even if they were meager-the fall of the Berlin Wall was deeply unsettling. </seg>
<seg id="410"> But for many others, it was a get-out-of-jail-free card. </seg>
<seg id="411"> That is why the fall of the Berlin Wall was felt in so many more places than just Berlin, and why its fall was such a world-flattening event. </seg>
<seg id="412"> Indeed, to appreciate the far-reaching flattening effects of the fall of the Berlin Wall, it's always best to talk to non-Germans or non-Russians. </seg>
<seg id="413"> Tarun Das was heading the Confederation of Indian Industry when the wall fell in Berlin, and he saw its ripple effect felt all the way to India. </seg>
<seg id="414"> ˇ°We had this huge mass of regulation and controls and bureaucracy,ˇ± he recalled. </seg>
<seg id="415"> ˇ°Nehru had come to power [after the end of British colonial rule] and had a huge country to manage, and no experience of running a country. </seg>
<seg id="416"> The U.S. was busy with Europe and Japan and the Marshall Plan. </seg>
<seg id="417"> So Nehru looked north, across the Himalayas, and sent his team of economists to Moscow. </seg>
<seg id="418"> They came back and said that this country [the Soviet Union] was amazing. </seg>
<seg id="419"> They allocate resources, they give licenses, there is a planning commission that decides everything, and the country moves. </seg>
<seg id="420"> So we took that model and forgot that we had a private sector... </seg>
<seg id="421"> That private sector got put under this wall of regulation. </seg>
<seg id="422"> By 1991, the private sector was there, but under wraps, and there was mistrust about business. </seg>
<seg id="423"> They made profits! </seg>
<seg id="424"> The entire infrastructure from 1947 to 1991 was government-owned... </seg>
<seg id="425"> [The burden of state ownership] almost bankrupted the country. </seg>
<seg id="426"> We were not able to pay our debts. </seg>
<seg id="427"> As a people, we did not have self-confidence. Sure, we might have won a couple of wars with Pakistan, but that did not give the nation confidence.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="428"> In 1991, with India running out of hard currency, Manmohan Singh, the finance minister at that time (and now the prime minister), decided that India had to open its economy. </seg>
<seg id="429"> ˇ°Our Berlin Wall fell,ˇ± said Das, ˇ°and it was like unleashing a caged tiger. </seg>
<seg id="430"> Trade controls were abolished. </seg>
<seg id="431"> We were always at 3 percent growth, the so-called Hindu rate of growth-slow, cautious, and conservative. </seg>
<seg id="432"> To make [better returns], you had to go to America. </seg>
<seg id="433"> Well, three years later [after the 1991 reforms] we were at 7 percent rate of growth. </seg>
<seg id="434"> Now to make it you could stay in India and become one of Forbes's richest people in the world... </seg>
<seg id="435"> All the years of socialism and controls had taken us downhill to the point where we had only $1 billion in foreign currency. </seg>
<seg id="436"> Today we have $ 118 billion... </seg>
<seg id="437"> We went from quiet self-confidence to outrageous ambition in a decade.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="438"> It also allowed us to think about the world differently-to see it as more of a seamless whole. </seg>
<seg id="439"> Because the Berlin Wall was not only blocking our way; it was blocking our sight-our ability to think about the world as a single market, a single ecosystem, and a single community. </seg>
<seg id="440"> Before 1989, you could have an Eastern policy or a Western policy, but it was hard to think about having a ˇ°globalˇ± policy. </seg>
<seg id="441"> Amartya Sen, the Nobel Prize-winning Indian economist now teaching at Harvard, once remarked to me that ˇ°There is a lovely story in Sanskrit, Sen added, about a frog that is born in a well and stays in the well and lives its entire life in the well. ˇ°It has a worldview that consists of the well,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="442"> ˇ°That was what the world was like for many people on the planet before the fall of the wall. </seg>
<seg id="443"> When it fell, it was like the frog in the well was suddenly able to communicate with frogs in all the other wells... </seg>
<seg id="444"> If I celebrate the fall of the wall, it is because I am convinced of how much we can learn from each other. </seg>
<seg id="445"> ˇ°Women's freedom,ˇ± noted Sen, citing just one example, ˇ°which promotes women's literacy, tends to reduce fertility and child mortality and increase the employment opportunities for women, which then affects the political dialogue and gives women the opportunity for a greater role in local self-government.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="446"> Finally, the fall of the wall did not just open the way for more people to tap into one another's knowledge pools. </seg>
<seg id="447"> It also paved the way for the adoption of common standards- </seg>
<seg id="448"> standards on how economies should be run, on how accounting should be done, on how banking should be conducted, on how PCs should be made, and on how economics papers should be written. </seg>
<seg id="449"> I discuss this more later, but suffice it to say here that common standards create a flatter, more level playing field. </seg>
<seg id="450"> To put it another way, the fall of the wall enhanced the free movement of best practices. </seg>
<seg id="451"> When an economic or technological standard emerged and proved itself on the world stage, it was much more quickly adopted after the wall was out of the way. </seg>
<seg id="452"> In Europe alone, the fall of the wall opened the way for the formation of the European Union and its expansion from fifteen to twenty-five countries. </seg>
<seg id="453"> That, in combination with the advent of the euro as a common currency, has created a single economic zone out of a region once divided by an Iron Curtain. </seg>
<seg id="454"> While the positive effects of the wall coming down were immediately apparent, the cause of the wall's fall was not so clear. </seg>
<seg id="455"> There was no single cause. </seg>
<seg id="456"> To some degree the termites just ate away at the foundations of the Soviet Union, which were already weakened by the system's own internal contradictions and inefficiencies; </seg>
<seg id="457"> to some degree the Reagan administration's military buildup in Europe forced the Kremlin to bankrupt itself paying for warheads; </seg>
<seg id="458"> and to some degree Mikhail Gorbachev's hapless efforts to reform something that was unreformable brought communism to an end. </seg>
<seg id="459"> But if I had to point to one factor as first among equals, it was the information revolution that began in the early- to mid-1980s. </seg>
<seg id="460"> Totalitarian systems depend on a monopoly of information and force, and too much information started to slip through the Iron Curtain, thanks to the spread of fax machines, telephones, and other modern tools of communication. </seg>
<seg id="461"> The first IBM PC hit the markets in 1981. </seg>
<seg id="462"> The first version of the Windows operating system shipped in 1985, and the real breakthrough version that made PCs truly user-friendly-Windows 3.0-shipped on May 22, 1990, only six months after the wall went down. </seg>
<seg id="463"> They also greatly enhanced personal information gathering and personal empowerment. </seg>
<seg id="464"> The walls had fallen and the Windows had opened, making the world much flatter than it had ever been-but the age of seamless global communication had not dawned. </seg>
<seg id="465"> And Windows evolved out of the need to make all of this usable, and programmable, by the masses.) </seg>
<seg id="466"> In this same time period, some people other than scientists started to discover that if they bought a PC and a dial-up modem, they could connect their PCs to their telephones and send e-mails through private Internet service providers-like CompuServe and America Online. </seg>
<seg id="467"> ˇ°The diffusion of personal computers, fax machines, Windows, and dial-up modems connected to a global telephone network all came together in the late 1980s and early 1990s to create the basic platform that started the global information revolution,ˇ± argued Craig J. Mundie, the chief technology officer for Microsoft. </seg>
<seg id="468"> The key was the melding of them all together into a single interoperable system. </seg>
<seg id="469"> That happened, said Mundie, once we had in crude form a standardized computing platform-the IBM PC-along with a standardized graphical user interface for word processing and spreadsheets-Windows-along with a standardized tool for communication-dial-up modems and the global phone network. </seg>
<seg id="470"> Once we had that basic interoperable platform, then the killer applications drove its diffusion far and wide. </seg>
<seg id="471"> ˇ°They all had broad individual appeal and made individual people get up and buy a Windows-enabled PC and put it on their desk, and that forced the diffusion of this new platform into the world of corporate computing even more. </seg>
<seg id="472"> The more established Windows became as the primary operating system, added Mundie, ˇ°the more programmers went out and wrote applications for rich-world businesses to put on their computers, so they could do lots of new and different business tasks, which started to enhance productivity even more. </seg>
<seg id="473"> Tens of millions of people around the world became programmers to make the PC do whatever they wanted in their own languages. Windows was eventually translated into thirty-eight languages. </seg>
<seg id="474"> People were able to become familiar with the PC in their own languages.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="475"> This was all new and exciting, but we shouldn't forget how constricted this early PC-Windows-modem platform was. </seg>
<seg id="476"> ˇ°This platform was constrained by too many architectural limits,ˇ± said Mundie. </seg>
<seg id="477"> ˇ°There was missing infrastructure.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="478"> The Internet as we know it today-with seemingly magical transmission protocols that can connect everyone and everything-had not yet emerged. </seg>
<seg id="479"> Back then, networks had only very basic protocols for exchanging files and e-mail messages. So people who were using computers with the same type of operating systems and software could exchange documents through e-mail or file transfers, but even doing this was tricky enough that only the computing elite took the trouble. </seg>
<seg id="480"> You couldn't just sit down and zap an e-mail or a file to anyone anywhere-especially outside your own company or outside your own Internet service-the way you can today. </seg>
<seg id="481"> Yes, AOL users could communicate with CompuServe users, but it was neither simple nor reliable. </seg>
<seg id="482"> As a result, said Mundie, a huge amount of data and creativity was accumulating in all those computers, but there was no easy, interoperable way to share it and mold it. </seg>
<seg id="483"> People could write new applications that allowed selected systems to work together, </seg>
<seg id="484"> but in general this was limited to planned exchanges between PCs within the network of a single company. </seg>
<seg id="485"> even if networks were limited. </seg>
<seg id="486"> This period from 11/9 to the mid-1990s still led to a huge advance in personal empowerment, </seg>
<seg id="487"> It was the age of ˇ°Me and my machine can now talk to each other better and faster, so that I personally can do more tasksˇ± and the age of ˇ°Me and my machine can now talk to a few friends and some other people in my company better and faster, so we can become more productive.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="488"> Though we didn't notice it, there was a discordant note in this exciting new era. </seg>
<seg id="489"> It wasn't only Americans and Europeans who joined the people of the Soviet Empire in celebrating the fall of the wall-and claming credit for it. </seg>
<seg id="490"> Someone else was raising a glass-not of champagne but of thick Turkish coffee. His name was Osama bin Laden and he had a different narrative. </seg>
<seg id="491"> His view was that it was the jihadi fighters in Afghanistan, of which he was one, who had brought down the Soviet Empire by forcing the Red Army to withdraw from Afghanistan (with some help from U.S. and Pakistani forces). </seg>
<seg id="492"> And once that mission had been accomplished- the Soviets completed their pullout from Afghanistan on February 15, 1989, just nine months before the fall of the Berlin Wall- </seg>
<seg id="493"> bin Laden looked around and found that the other superpower, the United States, had a huge presence in his own native land, Saudi Arabia, the home of the two holiest cities in Islam. </seg>
<seg id="494"> And he did not like it. </seg>
<seg id="495"> So, while we were dancing on the wall and opening up our Windows and proclaiming that there was no ideological alternative left to free-market capitalism, </seg>
<seg id="496"> bin Laden was turning his gun sights on America. </seg>
<seg id="497"> Both bin Laden and Ronald Reagan saw the Soviet Union as the ˇ°evil empire,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="498"> but bin Laden came to see America as evil too. </seg>
<seg id="499"> He did have an ideological alternative to free-market capitalism-political Islam. </seg>
<seg id="500"> He did not feel defeated by the end of the Soviet Union; </seg>
<seg id="501"> he felt emboldened by it. </seg>
<seg id="502"> he felt repelled by it. </seg>
<seg id="503"> Some thought that Ronald Reagan brought down the wall by bankrupting the Soviet Union through an arms race; </seg>
<seg id="504"> others thought IBM, Steve Jobs, and Bill Gates brought down the wall by empowering individuals to download the future. </seg>
<seg id="505"> But a world away, in Muslim lands, many thought bin Laden and his comrades brought down the Soviet Empire and the wall with religious zeal, and millions of them were inspired to upload the past. </seg>
<seg id="506"> In short, while we were celebrating 11/9, the seeds of another memorable dateˇŞ9/11ˇŞwere being sown. </seg>
<seg id="507"> But more about that later in the book. For now, let the flattening continue. </seg>
<seg id="508"> Flattener #2: 8/9/95, When Netscape Went Public </seg>
<seg id="509"> By the mid-1990s, the PC-Windows network revolution had reached its limits. </seg>
<seg id="510"> The actual concept of the World Wide Web-a system for creating, organizing, and linking documents so they could be easily browsed-was created by British computer scientist Tim Berners-Lee. </seg>
<seg id="511"> He put up the first Web site in 1991, </seg>
<seg id="512"> Other scientists and academics had created a number of browsers </seg>
<seg id="513"> to surf this early Web, </seg>
<seg id="514"> but the first mainstream browser-and the whole culture of Web browsing for the general public-was created by a tiny start-up company in Mountain View, California, called Netscape. </seg>
<seg id="515"> Netscape went public on August 9, 1995, and the world has not been the same since. </seg>
<seg id="516"> As John Doerr, the legendary venture capitalist whose firm Kleiner Perkins Caulfield &amp; Byers had backed Netscape, </seg>
<seg id="517"> put it, ˇ°The Netscape IPO was a clarion call to the world to wake up to the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="518"> Until then, it had been the province of the early adopters and geeks.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="519"> This Netscape-triggered phase drove the flattening process in several key ways: </seg>
<seg id="520"> It gave us the first broadly popular commercial browser to surf the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="521"> The Netscape browser not only brought the Internet alive but also made the Internet accessible to everyone from five-year-olds to eighty-five-year-olds. </seg>
<seg id="522"> The more alive the Internet became, the more consumers wanted to do different things on the Web, so the more they demanded computers, software, and telecommunications networks that could easily digitize words, music, data, and photos and transport them on the Internet to anyone else's computer. </seg>
<seg id="523"> This demand was satisfied by another catalytic event: the rollout of Windows 95, </seg>
<seg id="524"> which shipped the week after Netscape took its stock public. </seg>
<seg id="525"> and unlike previous versions of Windows, </seg>
<seg id="526"> it was equipped with built-in Internet support, </seg>
<seg id="527"> so that not just browsers but all PC applications could ˇ°know about the Internetˇ± and interact with it. </seg>
<seg id="528"> Looking back, what enabled Netscape to take off was the existence, from the earlier phase, of millions of PCs, many already equipped with modems. </seg>
<seg id="529"> Those are the shoulders Netscape stood on. </seg>
<seg id="530"> What Netscape did was bring a new killer app-the browser-to this installed base of PCs, making the computer and its connectivity inherently more useful for millions of people. </seg>
<seg id="531"> This in turn set off an explosion in demand for all things digital and sparked the Internet boom, </seg>
<seg id="532"> because every investor looked at the Internet and concluded that if everything was going to be digitized-data, inventories, commerce, books, music, photos, and entertainment- </seg>
<seg id="533"> and transported and sold on the Internet, then the demand for Internet-based products and services would be infinite. </seg>
<seg id="534"> This led to the dot-com stock bubble and a massive overinvestment in the fiber-optic cable needed to carry all the new digital information. </seg>
<seg id="535"> This development, in turn, wired the whole world together, and, without anyone really planning it, made Bangalore a suburb of Boston.Let's look at each one of these developments. </seg>
<seg id="536"> When I sat down with Jim Barksdale, the former Netscape CEO, to interview him for this book, I explained to him that one of the early chapters was about the ten innovations, events, and trends that had flattened the world. </seg>
<seg id="537"> I told him, </seg>
<seg id="538"> The first event was 11/9, and I explained the significance of that date. </seg>
<seg id="539"> Then I said, ˇ°Let me see if you can guess the significance of the second date, 8/9.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="540"> It took Barksdale only a second to ponder that before shooting back with the right answer: </seg>
<seg id="541"> ˇ°The day Netscape went public!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="542"> Few would argue that Barksdale is one of the great American entrepreneurs. </seg>
<seg id="543"> He helped Federal Express develop its package tracking and tracing system, </seg>
<seg id="544"> then moved over to McCaw Cellular, the mobile phone company, built that up, and oversaw its merger with AT&amp;T in 1994. </seg>
<seg id="545"> Just before the sale closed, he was approached by a headhunter to become the CEO of a new company called Mosaic Communications, forged by two now-legendary innovators-Jim Clark and Marc Andreessen. </seg>
<seg id="546"> In mid-1994, Clark, the founder of Silicon Graphics, had joined forces with Andreessen to found Mosaic, which would quickly be renamed Netscape Communications. </seg>
<seg id="547"> Andreessen, a brilliant young computer scientist, had just spearheaded a small software project at the National Center for Supercomputing Applications (NC SA), based at the University of Illinois, that developed the first really effective Web browser, also called Mosaic. </seg>
<seg id="548"> Clark and Andreessen quickly understood the huge potential for Web-browsing software and decided to partner up to commercialize it. </seg>
<seg id="549"> As Netscape began to grow, they reached out to Barksdale for guidance and insight into how best to go public.Today we take this browser technology for granted, </seg>
<seg id="550"> but it was actually one of the most important inventions in modern history. </seg>
<seg id="551"> When Andreessen was back at the University of Illinois NCSA lab, he found that he had PCs, workstations, and the basic network connectivity to move files around the Internet, </seg>
<seg id="552"> but it was still not very exciting </seg>
<seg id="553"> because there was nothing to browse with, no user interface to pull up and display the contents of other people's Web sites. </seg>
<seg id="554"> So Andreessen and his team developed the Mosaic browser, </seg>
<seg id="555"> Marc Andreessen did not invent the Internet, but he did as much as any single person to bring it alive and popularize it. </seg>
<seg id="556"> making Web sites viewable for any idiot, scientist, student, or grandma. </seg>
<seg id="557"> ˇ°The Mosaic browser started out in 1993 with twelve users, and I knew all twelve,ˇ± said Andreessen. There were only about fifty Web sites at the time and they were mostly just single Web pages. </seg>
<seg id="558"> ˇ°Mosaic,ˇ± he explained, ˇ°was funded by the National Science Foundation. </seg>
<seg id="559"> The money wasn't actually allocated to build Mosaic. </seg>
<seg id="560"> Our specific group was to build software that would enable scientists to use supercomputers that were in remote locations, and to connect to them by the NSF network. </seg>
<seg id="561"> So we built [the first browsers as] software tools to enable researchers to 'browse' each other's research. </seg>
<seg id="562"> I looked at it as a positive feedback loop: </seg>
<seg id="563"> The more people had the browser, the more people would want to be interconnected, </seg>
<seg id="564"> and the more incentive there would be to create content and applications and tools. </seg>
<seg id="565"> Once that kind of thing gets started, it just takes off and virtually nothing can stop it. </seg>
<seg id="566"> When you are developing it, you are not sure anyone is going to use it, but once it started we realized that if anyone is going to use it everyone is going to use it, </seg>
<seg id="567"> and the only question then was how fast it would spread and what would be the barriers along the way.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="568"> Indeed, everyone who tried the browser, including Barksdale, had the same initial reaction: Wow! </seg>
<seg id="569"> ˇ°Every summer, Fortune magazine had an article about the twenty-five coolest companies around,ˇ± Barksdale recalled. </seg>
<seg id="570"> ˇ°That year [1994] Mosaic was one of them. </seg>
<seg id="571"> I not only had read about Clark and Andreessen but had turned to my wife and said, 'Honey, this a great idea.' </seg>
<seg id="572"> And then just a few weeks later I get this call from the headhunter. </seg>
<seg id="573"> So I went down and spoke to Doerr and Jim Clark, and I began using the beta version of the Mosaic browser. </seg>
<seg id="574"> I became more and more intrigued the more I used it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="575"> Since the late 1980s, people had been putting up databases with Internet access. </seg>
<seg id="576"> Barksdale said that after speaking to Doerr and Clark, </seg>
<seg id="577"> he went home, </seg>
<seg id="578"> gathered his three children around his computer, </seg>
<seg id="579"> and asked them each to suggest a topic he could browse the Internet for-and wowed them by coming up with something for each of them. </seg>
<seg id="580"> ˇ°That convinced me,ˇ± said Barksdale. </seg>
<seg id="581"> ˇ°So I called back the headhunter and said, Tm your man.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="582"> Netscape's first commercial browser-which could work on an IBM PC, an Apple Macintosh, or a Unix computer-was released in December 1994, </seg>
<seg id="583"> and within a year it completely dominated the market. </seg>
