<seg id="584"> You could download Netscape for free if you were in education or a nonprofit. </seg>
<seg id="585"> If you were an individual, you could evaluate the software for free to your heart's content and buy it on disk if you wanted it. </seg>
<seg id="586"> If you were a company, you could evaluate the software for ninety days. </seg>
<seg id="587"> ˇ°The underlying rationale,ˇ± said Andreessen, ˇ°was: If you can afford to pay for it, please do so. </seg>
<seg id="588"> If not, use it anyway.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="589"> Why? </seg>
<seg id="590"> Because all the free usage stimulated a massive growth in the network, which was valuable to all the paying customers. </seg>
<seg id="591"> We put up the Netscape browser, said barksdale, and people were downloading it for three-month trials. </seg>
<seg id="592"> For big businesses and government it was allowing them to connect and unlock all their information, </seg>
<seg id="593"> and the point-and-click system that Marc Andreessen invented allowed mere mortals to use it. </seg>
<seg id="594"> And that made it a true revolution. </seg>
<seg id="595"> And we said, 'This thing will just grow and grow and grow. </seg>
<seg id="596"> Nothing did stop it, </seg>
<seg id="597"> and that is why Netscape played another hugely important flattening role: </seg>
<seg id="598"> It helped make the Internet truly interoperable. </seg>
<seg id="599"> You will recall that in the Berlin Wall-PC-Windows phase, individuals who had e-mail and companies that had internal e-mail could not connect very far. </seg>
<seg id="600"> The first Cisco Internet router, in fact, was built by a husband and wife at Stanford who wanted to exchange e-mail; </seg>
<seg id="601"> one was working off a mainframe and the other on a PC, and they couldn't connect. </seg>
<seg id="602"> ˇ°The corporate networks at the time were proprietary and disconnected from each other,ˇ± said Andreessen. </seg>
<seg id="603"> ˇ°Each one had its own formats, data protocols, and different ways of doing content. </seg>
<seg id="604"> So there were all these islands of information out there that were disconnected. And as the Internet emerged as a public, commercial venture, there was a real danger that it would emerge in the same disconnected way.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="605"> Joe in the accounting department would get on his office PC and try to get the latest sales numbers for 1995, </seg>
<seg id="606"> but he couldn't do that because the sales department was on a different system from the one accounting was using. </seg>
<seg id="607"> It was as if one was speaking German and the other French. </seg>
<seg id="608"> And then Joe would say, ˇ°Get me the latest shipment information from Goodyear on what tires they have sent us,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="609"> and he would find that Goodyear was using a different system altogether, and the dealer in Topeka was running yet another system. </seg>
<seg id="610"> Then Joe would go home and find his seventh-grader on the World Wide Web researching a term paper, using open protocols, and looking at the holdings of some art museum in France. </seg>
<seg id="611"> And Joe would say, ˇ°This is crazy. </seg>
<seg id="612"> There has to be one totally interconnected network.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="613"> In the years before the Internet became commercial, explained Andreessen, scientists developed a series of ˇ°open protocolsˇ± meant to make everyone's e-mail system or university computer network connect seamlessly with everyone else's-to ensure that no one had some special advantage. </seg>
<seg id="614"> These mathematical-based protocols, which enable digital devices to talk to each other, were like magical pipes that, once you adopted them for your network, made you compatible with everyone else, no matter what kind of computer they were running. </seg>
<seg id="615"> These protocols were (and still are) known by their alphabet soup names: mainly FTP, HTTP, SSL, SMTP, POP, and TCP/IP. </seg>
<seg id="616"> Together, they form a system for transporting data around the Internet in a relatively secure manner, no matter what network your company or household has or what computer or cell phone or handheld device you are using. </seg>
<seg id="617"> Each protocol had a different function: TCP/IP was the basic plumbing of the Internet, or the basic railroad tracks, on which everything else above it was built and moved around. </seg>
<seg id="618"> FTP moved files; </seg>
<seg id="619"> SMTP and POP moved e-mail messages, which became standardized, so that they could be written and read on different e-mail systems. </seg>
<seg id="620"> HTML was a language that allowed even ordinary people to author Web pages that anyone with a Web browser could display. </seg>
<seg id="621"> But it was the introduction of HTTP to move HTML documents around that gave birth to the World Wide Web as we know it. </seg>
<seg id="622"> Finally, as people began to use these Web pages for electronic commerce, SSL was created to provide security for Web-based transactions. </seg>
<seg id="623"> As browsing and the Internet in general grew, </seg>
<seg id="624"> Netscape wanted to make sure that Microsoft, with its huge market dominance, would not be able to shift these Web protocols from open to proprietary standards that only Microsoft's servers would be able to handle. </seg>
<seg id="625"> ˇ°Netscape helped to guarantee that these open protocols would not be proprietary by commercializing them for the public,ˇ± said Andreessen. </seg>
<seg id="626"> ˇ°Netscape came along not only with the browser but with a family of software products that implemented all these open standards so that the scientists could communicate with each other no matter what system they were on-a Cray supercomputer, a Macintosh, or a PC. </seg>
<seg id="627"> Netscape was able to provide a real reason for everyone to say, </seg>
<seg id="628"> 'I want to be on open standards for everything I do and for all the systems I work on.' </seg>
<seg id="629"> So anyone who wanted to work on open standards went to Netscape, where we supported them, or they went to the open-source world and got the same standards for free but unsupported, </seg>
<seg id="630"> or they went to their private vendors and said, 'I am not going to buy your proprietary stuff anymore... </seg>
<seg id="631"> I am only going to stay with you if you interconnect to the Internet with these open protocols.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="632"> Netscape began pushing these open standards through the sale of its browsers, and the public responded enthusiastically. </seg>
<seg id="633"> Sun started to do the same with its servers, and Microsoft started to do the same with Windows 95, considering browsing so critical that it famously built its own browser directly into Windows with the addition of Internet Explorer. </seg>
<seg id="634"> Each realized that the public, which suddenly could not get enough of e-mail and browsing, wanted the Internet companies to work together and create one interoperable network. </seg>
<seg id="635"> They wanted companies to compete with each other over different applications, that is, over what consumers could do once they were on the Internet-not over how they got on the Internet in the first place. </seg>
<seg id="636"> As a result, after quite a few ˇ°format warsˇ± among the big companies, by the late 1990s the Internet computing platform became seamlessly integrated. </seg>
<seg id="637"> Soon anyone was able to connect with anyone else anywhere on any machine. </seg>
<seg id="638"> It turned out that the value of compatibility was much higher for everyone than the value of trying to maintain your own little walled network. </seg>
<seg id="639"> This integration was a huge flattener, because it enabled so many more people to get connected with so many more other people. </seg>
<seg id="640"> There was no shortage of skeptics at the time, who said that none of this would work because it was all too complicated, recalled Andreessen. 'Tou had to go out and get a PC and a dial-up modem. </seg>
<seg id="641"> The skeptics all said, 'It takes people a long time to change their habits and learn a new technology.' </seg>
<seg id="642"> [But] people did it very quickly, and ten years later there were eight hundred million people on the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="643"> ˇ° The reason? ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="644"> People will change their habits quickly when they have a strong reason to do so, </seg>
<seg id="645"> and people have an innate urge to connect with other people,ˇ° said Andreessen. ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="646"> And when you give people a new way to connect with other people, </seg>
<seg id="647"> they will punch through any technical barrier, </seg>
<seg id="648"> they will learn new languages </seg>
<seg id="649"> ˇ° As Joel Cawley, IBM's vice president of corporate strategy, put it, ˇ±Netscape created a standard around how data would be transported and rendered on the screen that was so simple and compelling that anyone and everyone could innovate on top of it. </seg>
<seg id="650"> It quickly scaled around the world and to everyone from kids to corporations. </seg>
<seg id="651"> ˇ°In the summer of 1995, Barksdale and his Netscape colleagues went on an old-fashioned road show with their investment bankers from Morgan Stanley to try to entice investors around the country to buy Netscape stock once it went public. </seg>
<seg id="652"> ˇ°When we went out on the road,ˇ± said Barksdale, ˇ°Morgan Stanley said the stock could sell for as high as $14. </seg>
<seg id="653"> But after the road show got going, they were getting such demand for the stock, they decided to double the opening price to $28. </seg>
<seg id="654"> The last afternoon before the offering, </seg>
<seg id="655"> we were all in Maryland. </seg>
<seg id="656"> It was our last stop. </seg>
<seg id="657"> We had this caravan of black limousines. </seg>
<seg id="658"> We looked like some kind of Mafia group. </seg>
<seg id="659"> we were somewhere where our cell phones didn't work. </seg>
<seg id="660"> So we pulled into these two filling stations across from each other, all these black limos, to use the phones. </seg>
<seg id="661"> We called up Morgan Stanley, and they said, 'We're thinking of bringing it out at $31.' </seg>
<seg id="662"> I said, 'No, let's keep it at $28,' </seg>
<seg id="663"> because I wanted people to remember it as a $20 stock, not a $30 stock, </seg>
<seg id="664"> just in case it didn't go so well. </seg>
<seg id="665"> So then the next morning I get on the conference call and the thing opened at $71. </seg>
<seg id="666"> It closed the day at $56, exactly twice the price I set.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="667"> Netscape eventually fell victim to overwhelming (and, the courts decided, monopolistic) competitive pressure from Microsoft. </seg>
<seg id="668"> Microsoft's decision to give away its browser, Internet Explorer, as part of its dominant Windows operating system, </seg>
<seg id="669"> combined with its ability to throw more programmers at Web browsing than Netscape, </seg>
<seg id="670"> led to the increasing slippage of Netscape's market share. </seg>
<seg id="671"> In the end, Netscape was sold for $10 billion to AOL, which never did much with it. </seg>
<seg id="672"> But though Netscape may have been only a shooting star in commercial terms, what a star it was, and what a trail it left. </seg>
<seg id="673"> ˇ°We were profitable almost from the start,ˇ± said Barksdale. </seg>
<seg id="674"> We did not participate in the dot-com bubble. </seg>
<seg id="675"> We started the dot-com bubble.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="676"> And what a bubble it was. </seg>
<seg id="677"> ˇ°Netscape going public stimulated a lot of things,ˇ± said Barksdale. </seg>
<seg id="678"> ˇ°The technologists loved the new technology things it could do, and the businesspeople and regular folks got excited about how much money they could make. </seg>
<seg id="679"> People saw all those young kids making money out of this and said, </seg>
<seg id="680"> 'If those young kids can do this and make all that money, I can too.' </seg>
<seg id="681"> Greed can be a bad thing-folks thought they could make a lot of money without a lot of work. </seg>
<seg id="682"> It certainly led to a degree of overinvestment, putting it mildly. </seg>
<seg id="683"> Every sillier and sillier idea got funded.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="684"> What was it that stimulated investors to believe that demand for Internet usage and Internet-related products would be infinite? </seg>
<seg id="685"> The short answer is digitization. </seg>
<seg id="686"> Once the PC-Windows revolution demonstrated to everyone the value of being able to digitize information and manipulate it on computers and word processors, </seg>
<seg id="687"> and once the browser brought the Internet alive and made Web pages sing and dance and display, everyone wanted everything digitized as much as possible so they could send it to someone else down the Internet pipes. </seg>
<seg id="688"> Thus began the digitization revolution. </seg>
<seg id="689"> Digitization is that magic process by which words, music, data, films, files, and pictures are turned into bits and bytes-combinations of Is and Os-that can be manipulated on a computer screen, stored on a microprocessor, or transmitted over satellites and fiber-optic lines. </seg>
<seg id="690"> It used to be the post office was where I went to send my mail, </seg>
<seg id="691"> but once the Internet came alive, I wanted my mail digitized so I could e-mail it. </seg>
<seg id="692"> Photography used to be a cumbersome process involving film coated with silver dug up from mines halfway across the world. </seg>
<seg id="693"> I used to take some pictures with my camera, then bring the film to the drugstore to be sent off to a big plant somewhere for processing. </seg>
<seg id="694"> But once the Internet made it possible to send pictures around the world, attached to or in e-mails, I didn't want to use silver film anymore. </seg>
<seg id="695"> I wanted to take pictures in the digital format, which could be uploaded, not developed. </seg>
<seg id="696"> (And by the way, I didn't want to be confined to using a camera to take them. </seg>
<seg id="697"> I wanted to be able to use my cell phone to do it.) </seg>
<seg id="698"> I used to have to go to Barnes &amp; Noble to buy and browse books, </seg>
<seg id="699"> but once the Internet came alive, I wanted to browse for books digitally on Amazon.com as well. </seg>
<seg id="700"> I used to go to the library to do research, but now I wanted to do it digitally through Google or Yahoo!, not just by roaming the stacks. </seg>
<seg id="701"> I used to buy a CD to listen to Simon and Garfunkel-CDs had already replaced albums as a form of digitized music-but once the Internet came alive, I wanted those music bits to be even more malleable and mobile. </seg>
<seg id="702"> I wanted to be able to download them into an iPod. </seg>
<seg id="703"> Well, as investors watched this mad rush to digitize everything, they said to themselves, ˇ°Holy cow. If everyone wants all this stuff digitized and turned into bits and transmitted over the Internet, the demand for Web service companies and the demand for fiber-optic cables to handle all this digitized stuff around the world is going to be limitless! </seg>
<seg id="704"> You cannot lose if you invest in this!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="705"> And thus was the bubble born. </seg>
<seg id="706"> Overinvestment is not necessarily a bad thing </seg>
<seg id="707"> provided that it is eventually corrected. </seg>
<seg id="708"> I'll always remember a news conference that Microsoft chairman Bill Gates held at the 1999 World Economic Forum in Davos, at the height of the tech bubble. </seg>
<seg id="709"> Over and over again, Gates was bombarded by reporters with versions of the question, </seg>
<seg id="710"> ˇ°Mr. Gates, these Internet stocks, they're a bubble, right? </seg>
<seg id="711"> They must be a bubble?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="712"> Finally an exasperated Gates said to the reporters something to the effect of, ˇ°Look, you bozos, of course they're a bubble, but you're all missing the point. </seg>
<seg id="713"> This bubble is attracting so much new capital to this Internet industry, it is going to drive innovation faster and faster.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="714"> Gates compared the Internet to the gold rush, the idea being that more money was made selling Levi's, picks, shovels, and hotel rooms to the gold diggers than from digging up gold from the earth. </seg>
<seg id="715"> Gates was right: </seg>
<seg id="716"> Booms and bubbles may be economically dangerous; </seg>
<seg id="717"> they may end up with many people losing money and a lot of companies going bankrupt. </seg>
<seg id="718"> But they also often do drive innovation faster and faster, </seg>
<seg id="719"> and the sheer overcapacity that they spur-whether it is in railroad lines or automobiles-can create its own unintended positive consequences.That is what happened with the Internet stock boom. </seg>
<seg id="720"> It sparked a huge overinvestment in fiber-optic cable companies, which then laid massive amounts of fiber-optic cable on land and under the oceans, which dramatically drove down the cost of making a phone call or transmitting data anywhere in the world. </seg>
<seg id="721"> The first commercial installation of a fiber-optic system was in 1977, after which fiber slowly began to replace copper telephone wires, </seg>
<seg id="722"> because it could carry data and digitized voices much farther and faster in larger quantities. </seg>
<seg id="723"> fiber optics are made up of strands of optically pure glass each ˇ°as thin as a human hair,ˇ± which are arranged in bundles, called ˇ°optical cables,ˇ± to carry digitized packets of information over long distances. </seg>
<seg id="724"> Because these optical fibers are so much thinner than copper wires, more fibers can be bundled into a given diameter of cable than can copper wires, which means that much more data or many more voices can be sent over the same cable at a lower cost. </seg>
<seg id="725"> The most important benefit of fiber, though, derives from the dramatically higher bandwidth of the signals it can transport over long distances. </seg>
<seg id="726"> Copper wires can carry very high frequencies too, but only for a few feet before the signal starts to degrade in strength due to certain parasitic effects. </seg>
<seg id="727"> Optical fibers, by contrast, can carry very high-frequency optical pulses on the same individual fiber without substantial signal degradation for many, many miles. </seg>
<seg id="728"> The way fiber-optic cables work, explains one of the manufacturers, ARC Electronics, on its Web site, is by converting data or voices into light pulses and then transmitting them down fiber lines, instead of using electronic pulses to transmit information down copper lines. </seg>
<seg id="729"> At one end of the fiber-optic system is a transmitter. </seg>
<seg id="730"> The transmitter accepts coded electronic pulse information-words or data-coming from copper wire out of your home telephone or office computer. </seg>
<seg id="731"> The transmitter then processes and translates those digitized, electronically coded words or data into equivalently coded light pulses. </seg>
<seg id="732"> A light-emitting diode (LED) or an injection-laser diode (ILD) can be used to generate the light pulses, which are then funneled down the fiber-optic cable. </seg>
<seg id="733"> The cable functions as a kind of light guide, guiding the light pulses introduced at one end of the cable through to the other end, where a light-sensitive receiver converts the pulses back into the electronic digital Is and Os of the original signal, so they can then show up on your computer screen as e-mail or in your cell phone as a voice. </seg>
<seg id="734"> Fiber-optic cable is also ideal for secure communications, </seg>
<seg id="735"> because it is very difficult to tap. </seg>
<seg id="736"> It was actually the coincidence of the dot-com boom and the Telecommunications Act of 1996 that launched the fiber-optic bubble. </seg>
<seg id="737"> The act allowed local and long-distance companies to get into each other's businesses, </seg>
<seg id="738"> and enabled all sorts of new local exchange carriers to compete head-to-head with the Baby Bells and AT&amp;T in providing both phone services and infrastructure. </seg>
<seg id="739"> As these new phone companies came online, offering their own local, long-distance, international, data, and Internet services, each sought to have its own infrastructure. </seg>
<seg id="740"> And why not? </seg>
<seg id="741"> The Internet boom led everyone to assume that the demand for bandwidth to carry all that Internet traffic would double every three months-indefinitely. </seg>
<seg id="742"> For about two years that was true. But then the law of large numbers started to kick in, and the pace of doubling slowed. </seg>
<seg id="743"> Unfortunately, the telecom companies weren't paying close attention to the developing mismatch between demand and reality. </seg>
<seg id="744"> The market was in the grip of an Internet fever, and companies just kept building more and more capacity. </seg>
<seg id="745"> And the stock market boom meant money was free! </seg>
<seg id="746"> In a period of about five or six years, these telecom companies invested about $ 1 trillion in wiring the world. </seg>
<seg id="747"> And virtually no one questioned the demand projections. </seg>
<seg id="748"> Few companies got crazier than Global Crossing, one of the companies hired by all these new telecoms to lay fiber-optic cable for them around the world. </seg>
<seg id="749"> Global Crossing was founded in 1997 by Gary Winnick and went public the next year. </seg>
<seg id="750"> Robert Annunziata, who lasted only a year as CEO, had a contract that the Corporate Library's Nell Minow once picked as the worst (from the point of view of shareholders) in the United States. </seg>
<seg id="751"> Among other things, it included Annunziata's mother's first-class airfare to visit him once a month. </seg>
<seg id="752"> It also included a signing bonus of 2 million shares of stock at $10 a share below market. </seg>
<seg id="753"> Henry Schacht, a veteran industrialist now with E. M. Warburg, Pincus &amp; Co., was brought in by Lucent, the successor of Western Electric, to help manage it through this crazy period. </seg>
<seg id="754"> He recalled the atmosphere: ˇ°The telecom deregulation of 1996 was hugely important. </seg>
<seg id="755"> It allowed competitive local exchange carriers to build their own capacities and sell in competition with each other and with the Baby Bells. </seg>
<seg id="756"> These new telecoms went to companies like Global Crossing and had them install fiber networks for them so they could compete at the transport level with AT&amp;T and MCI, particularly on overseas traffic... </seg>
<seg id="757"> Everyone thought this was a new world, and it would never stop. </seg>
<seg id="758"> [You had] competitive firms using free capital, and everyone thought the pie would expand infinitely. </seg>
<seg id="759"> So [each company said,] 'I will put my fiber down before you do, and I will get a bigger share than you.' </seg>
<seg id="760"> It was supposed to be just a vertical growth line, straight up, and we each thought we would get our share, so everybody built to the max projections and assumed that they would get their share.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="761"> It turned out that while business-to-business and e-commerce developed as projected, </seg>
<seg id="762"> and a lot of Web sites that no one anticipated exploded-like eBay, Amazon, and Google-they still devoured only a fraction of the capacity that was being made available. </seg>
<seg id="763"> Long-distance phone rates went from $2 a minute to 100. </seg>
<seg id="764"> And the transmission of data was virtually free. </seg>
<seg id="765"> Mike McCue, chief operations officer of Tellme Networks, a voice-activated Internet service, told CNET News.com in June 2001. </seg>
<seg id="766"> ˇ°They've laid so much fiber in the ground that they've basically commoditized themselves. </seg>
<seg id="767"> They are going to get into massive price wars with everyone </seg>
<seg id="768"> and it's going to be a disaster.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="769"> It was a disaster for many of the companies and their investors (Global Crossing filed for bankruptcy in January 2002, with $12.4 billion in debt), but it turned out to be a great boon for consumers. </seg>
<seg id="770"> Just as the national highway system that was built in the 1950s flattened the United States, broke down regional differences, and made it so much easier for companies to relocate in lower-wage regions, like the South, because it had become so much easier to move people and goods long distances, so the laying of global fiber highways flattened the developed world. </seg>
<seg id="771"> It helped to break down global regionalism, create a more seamless global commercial network, and made it simple and almost free to move digitized labor-service jobs and knowledge work-to lower-cost countries. </seg>
<seg id="772"> (It should be noted, though, that those fiber highways in America tended to stop at the last mile-before connecting to households. </seg>
<seg id="773"> While a huge amount of long-distance fiber cable was laid to connect India and America, virtually none of these new U.S. telecom companies laid any substantial new local loop infrastructure, due to a failure of the 1996 telecom deregulation act to permit real competition in the local loop between the cable companies and the telephone companies. </seg>
<seg id="774"> Where the local broadband did get installed was in office buildings, which were already pretty well served by the old companies. </seg>
<seg id="775"> So this pushed prices down for businesses </seg>
<seg id="776"> and for Indians who wanted to get online from Bangalore to do business with those businesses- </seg>
<seg id="777"> but it didn't create the sort of competition that could bring cheap broadband capability to the American masses in their homes. </seg>
<seg id="778"> That has started happening only more recently.) </seg>
<seg id="779"> Unlike other forms of Internet overinvestment, it was permanent: </seg>
<seg id="780"> Once the fiber cables were laid, no one was going to dig them up and thereby eliminate the overcapacity. </seg>
<seg id="781"> So when the telecom companies went bankrupt, the banks took them over and then sold their fiber cables for ten cents on the dollar to new companies, which continued to operate them, which they could do profitably, having bought them in a fire sale. </seg>
<seg id="782"> But the way fiber cable works is that each cable has multiple strands of fiber in it with a potential capacity to transmit many terabits of data per second on each strand. </seg>
<seg id="783"> When these fiber cables were originally laid, the optical switches-the transmitters and receivers-at each end of them could not take full advantage of the fiber's full capacity. </seg>
<seg id="784"> But every year since then, the optical switches at each end of that fiber cable have gotten better and better, meaning that more and more voices and data can be transmitted down each fiber. So as the switches keep improving, the capacity of all the already installed fiber cables just keeps growing, making it cheaper and easier to transmit voices and data every year to any part of the world. </seg>
<seg id="785"> It is as though we laid down a national highway system where people were first allowed to drive 50 mph, then 60 mph, then 70 mph, then 80 mph, then eventually 150 mph on the same highways without any fear of accidents. </seg>
<seg id="786"> Only this highway wasn't just national. It was international. </seg>
<seg id="787"> ˇ°Every layer of innovation gets built on the next,ˇ± said Andreessen, who went on from Netscape to start another high-tech firm, Opsware Inc. ˇ°And today the most profound thing to me is the fact that a fourteen-year-old in Romania or Bangalore or the Soviet Union or Vietnam has all the information, all the tools, all the software easily available to apply knowledge however they want. </seg>
<seg id="788"> That is why I am sure the next Napster is going to come out of left field. </seg>
<seg id="789"> As bioscience becomes more computational and less about wet labs, and as all the genomic data becomes easily available on the Internet, at some point you will be able to design vaccines on your laptop.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="790"> I think Andreessen touches on what is unique about the flat world and the era of Globalization 3.0. </seg>
<seg id="791"> It is going to be driven by groups and individuals, but of a much more diverse background than those twelve scientists who made up Andreessen's world when he created Mosaic. </seg>
<seg id="792"> Now we are going to see the real human mosaic emerge-from all over the world, from left field and right field, from West and East and North and South-to drive the next generation of innovation. </seg>
<seg id="793"> Indeed, a few days after Andreessen and I talked, the following headline appeared on the front page of The New York Times (July 15, 2004): ˇ°U.S. Permits 3 Cancer Drugs from Cuba.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="794"> The story went on to say, ˇ°The federal government is permitting a California biotechnology company to license three experimental cancer drugs from Cuba-making an exception to the policy of tightly restricting trade with that country.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="795"> Executives of the company, CancerVex, said that ˇ°it was the first time an American biotechnology company had obtained permission to license a drug from Cuba, a country that some industry executives and scientists say is surprisingly strong in biotechnology for a developing nation... </seg>
<seg id="796"> More than $1 billion was spent over the years to build and operate research institutes on the west side of Havana staffed by Cuban scientists, many of them educated in Europe.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="797"> Just to summarize again: </seg>
<seg id="798"> The PC-Windows flattening phase was about me interacting with my computer and me interacting with my own limited network inside my own company. </seg>
<seg id="799"> Then came along this Internet-e-mail-browser phase, and it flattened the earth a little bit more. </seg>
<seg id="800"> It was about me and my computer interacting with anyone anywhere on any machine, which is what e-mail is all about, and me and my computer interacting with anybody's Web site on the Internet, which is what browsing is all about. </seg>
<seg id="801"> In short, the PC-Windows phase begat the Netscape browsing-e-mail phase and the two together enabled more people to communicate and interact with more other people anywhere on the planet than ever before. </seg>
<seg id="802"> But the fun was just beginning. </seg>
<seg id="803"> This phase was just the foundation for the next step in flattening the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="804"> Flattener #3: Work Flow Software, Let's Do Lunch: Have Your Application, Talk to My Application </seg>
<seg id="805"> I met Scott Hyten, the CEO of Wild Brain, a cutting-edge animation studio in San Francisco that produces films and cartoons for Disney and other major studios, at a meeting in Silicon Valley in the winter of 2004. </seg>
<seg id="806"> I had been invited by John Doerr, the venture capitalist, to test out the ideas in this book to a few of the companies that he was backing. </seg>
<seg id="807"> Hyten and I really hit it off, maybe because after hearing my arguments he wrote me an e-mail that said, ˇ°I am sure in Magellan's time there were plenty of theologians, geographers, and pundits who wanted to make the world flat again. </seg>
<seg id="808"> I know the world is flat, and thank you for your support.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="809"> When I asked him to elaborate, Hyten sketched out for me how animated films are produced today through a global supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="810"> I understood immediately why he too had concluded that the world is flat. </seg>
<seg id="811"> ˇ°At Wild Brain,ˇ± he said, ˇ°we make something out of nothing. </seg>
<seg id="812"> We learn how to take advantage of the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="813"> We are not fighting it. </seg>
<seg id="814"> We are taking advantage of it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="815"> Hyten invited me to come and watch them produce a cartoon segment to really appreciate how flat the world is, which I did. </seg>
<seg id="816"> The series they were working on when I showed up was for the Disney Channel and called Higglytown Heroes. </seg>
<seg id="817"> It was inspired by all the ordinary people who rose to the challenge of 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="818"> Higglytown ˇ°is the typical 1950s small town,ˇ± said Hyten. </seg>
<seg id="819"> The foundation of the story is that every person, all the ordinary people living their lives, are the heroes in this small town-from the schoolteacher to the pizza delivery man.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="820"> This all-American show is being produced by an all-world supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="821"> ˇ°The recording session,ˇ± explained Hyten, ˇ°is located near the artist, usually in New York or L.A., the design and direction is done in San Francisco, the writers network in from their homes (Florida, London, New York, Chicago, LA, and San Francisco), and the animation of the characters is done in Bangalore with edits from San Francisco. </seg>
<seg id="822"> For this show we have eight teams in Bangalore working in parallel with eight different writers. </seg>
<seg id="823"> This efficiency has allowed us to contract with fifty 'stars' for the twenty-six episodes. </seg>
<seg id="824"> These interactive recording/writing/ animation sessions allow us to record an artist for an entire show in less than half a day, including unlimited takes and rewrites. </seg>
<seg id="825"> We record two actors per week. </seg>
<seg id="826"> For example, last week we recorded Anne Heche and Smokey Robinson. </seg>
<seg id="827"> Technically, we do this over the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="828"> We have a VPN [virtual private network] configured on computers in our offices and on what we call writers' 'footballs,' or special laptop computers that can connect over any cat-5 Ethernet connection or wireless broadband connection in the 'field.' </seg>
<seg id="829"> This VPN allows us to share the feed from the microphone, images from the session, the real-time script, and all the animation designs amongst all the locations with a simple log-in. </seg>
<seg id="830"> Therefore, one way for you to observe is for us to ship you a football. </seg>
<seg id="831"> You connect at home, the office, most hotel rooms, or go down to your local Starbucks [which has wireless broadband Internet access], log on, put on a pair of Bose noise-reduction headphones, and listen, watch, read, and comment. 'Sharon, can you sell that line a little more?' </seg>
<seg id="832"> Then, over the eleven-week production schedule for the show, you can log in twenty-four hours a day and check the progress of the production as it follows the sun around the world. </seg>
<seg id="833"> Technically, you need the 'football' only for the session. You can use your regular laptop to follow the 'dailies' and 'edits' over the production cycle.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="834"> I needed to see Wild Brain firsthand, </seg>
<seg id="835"> because it is a graphic example of the next layer of innovation, and the next flattener, that broadly followed on the Berlin Wall-Windows and Netscape phases. </seg>
<seg id="836"> I call this the ˇ°work flow phase.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="837"> When the walls went down, and the PC, Windows, and Netscape browser enabled people to connect with other people as never before, it did not take long before all these people who were connecting wanted to do more than just browse and send e-mail, instant messages, pictures, and music over this Internet platform. </seg>
<seg id="838"> They wanted to shape things, design things, create things, sell things, buy things, keep track of inventories, do somebody else's taxes, and read somebody else's X-rays from half a world away. </seg>
<seg id="839"> And they wanted to be able to do any of these things from anywhere to anywhere and from any computer to any computer-seamlessly. </seg>
<seg id="840"> The wall-Windows-Netscape phases paved the way for that by standardizing the ways words, music, pictures, and data would be digitized and transported on the Internet-so e-mail and browsing became a very rich experience.But for all of us to go to the next stage, to get more out of the Internet, the flattening process had to go another notch. </seg>
<seg id="841"> We needed two things. </seg>
<seg id="842"> We needed programmers to come along and write new applications- new software-that would enable us really to get the maximum from our computers as we worked with these digitized data, words, music, and pictures and shaped them into products. </seg>
<seg id="843"> We also needed more magic pipes, more transmissions protocols, that would ensure that everyone's software applications could connect with everyone else's software applications. </seg>
<seg id="844"> In short, we had to go from an Internet that just connected people to people, and people to their own applications, to an Internet that could connect any of my software programs to any of your software programs. </seg>
<seg id="845"> Only then could we really work together.Think of it this way: In the beginning, work flow consisted of your sales department taking an order on paper, walking it over to your shipping department, which shipped the product, and then someone from shipping walking over to billing with a piece of paper and instructing them to churn out an invoice to the customer. </seg>
<seg id="846"> As a result of the Berlin Wall-Windows-Netscape phases, work flow took a huge leap forward. </seg>
<seg id="847"> Now your sales department could electronically take that order, e-mail it to the shipping department within your own company, and then have the shipping department send out the product to the customer and automatically spit out a bill at the same time. </seg>
<seg id="848"> The fact that all the departments within your company were seamlessly interoperable and that work could flow between them was a great boost to productivity-but this could happen only if all your company's departments were using the same software and hardware systems. </seg>
<seg id="849"> More often than not, back in the 1980s and early 1990s, a company's sales department was running Microsoft and the inventory department was running Novell, and they could not communicate with each other. </seg>
<seg id="850"> So work did not flow as easily as it should. </seg>
<seg id="851"> We often forget that the software industry started out like a bad fire department. </seg>
<seg id="852"> Imagine a city where every neighborhood had a different interface for connecting the fire hose to the hydrant. </seg>
<seg id="853"> Everything was fine as long as your neighborhood fire department could handle your fire. </seg>
<seg id="854"> But when a fire became too big, and the fire engines from the next neighborhood had to be called in, they were useless because they could not connect their hoses to your hydrants. </seg>
<seg id="855"> For the world to get flat, all your internal departments-sales, marketing, manufacturing, billing, and inventory-had to become interoperable, no matter what machines or software each of them was running. </seg>
<seg id="856"> And for the world to get really flat, all your systems had to be interoperable with all the systems of any other company. </seg>
<seg id="857"> That is, your sales department had to be connected to your supplier's inventory department and your supplier's inventory department had to be seamlessly connected to its supplier's supplier, which was a factory in China. </seg>
<seg id="858"> That way, when you made a sale, an item was automatically shipped from your supplier's warehouse, and another item was automatically manufactured by your supplier's supplier, and a bill was generated from your billing department. </seg>
<seg id="859"> Technically, what made it possible was the development of a new data description language, called XML, and its related transport protocol, called SOAP. </seg>
<seg id="860"> IBM, Microsoft, and a host of other companies contributed to the development of both XML and SOAP, and both were subsequently ratified and popularized as the Internet standards. </seg>
<seg id="861"> Wild Brain wanted programs to make animated films with a production team spread out around the world. </seg>
<seg id="862"> Boeing wanted them so that its airplane factories in America could constantly resupply different airline customers with parts, through its computer ordering systems, no matter what country those orders came from. </seg>
<seg id="863"> Doctors wanted them so that an X-ray taken in Bangor could be read in a hospital in Bangalore, without the doctor in Maine ever having to think about what computers that Indian hospital had. </seg>
<seg id="864"> And Mom and Dad wanted them because they wanted their e-banking software, e-brokerage software, office e-mail, and spreadsheet software to all work off their home laptop and be able to interface with their office desktop. </seg>
<seg id="865"> And once everyone's applications started to connect to everyone else's applications-which took several years and lot of technology and brainpower to make happen-work could not only flow like never before, but it could be chopped up and disaggregated like never before and sent to the four corners of the world. </seg>
<seg id="866"> This meant that work could flow anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="867"> Instead of everyone trying to control the fire hydrant nozzle, they made all the nozzles and hoses the same, creating a much bigger market that stretched across every neighborhood of the world. </seg>
<seg id="868"> Then companies started to compete instead over the quality of the hose, the pump, and the fire truck. </seg>
<seg id="869"> Said Joel Cawley, the head of IBM's strategic planning unit, ˇ°Standards don't eliminate innovation, they just allow you to focus it. </seg>
<seg id="870"> They allow you to focus on where the real value lies, which is usually everything you can add above and around the standard.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="871"> Another of my favorite examples of this is PayPal, which enabled eBay's e-commerce bazaar to become what it is today. </seg>
<seg id="872"> PayPal is a money transfer system founded in 1998 to facilitate C2C (customer-to-customer) transactions, like a buyer and seller brought together by eBay. </seg>
<seg id="873"> According to the Web site ecommerce-guide.com, using PayPal, anyone with an e-mail address can send money to anyone else with an e-mail address, whether the recipient has a PayPal account or not. </seg>
<seg id="874"> PayPal doesn't even care whether a commercial transaction is taking place. </seg>
<seg id="875"> If someone in the office is organizing a party for someone else and everyone needs to chip in, they can all do it using PayPal. </seg>
<seg id="876"> In fact, the organizer can send everyone PayPal reminders by e-mail with clear instructions as to how to pay up. </seg>
<seg id="877"> PayPal can accept money from the purchaser in one of three ways, notes ecommerce-guide.com: charging the purchaser's credit card for any transactions (payments), debiting a checking account for any payments, or deducting payments from a PayPal account established with a personal check. </seg>
<seg id="878"> Setting up a PayPal account is simple. </seg>
<seg id="879"> As a payer, all you have to do is to provide your name, your e-mail address, your credit card information, and your billing address for your credit card. </seg>
<seg id="880"> All of these interoperable banking and e-commerce functions flattened the Internet marketplace so radically that even eBay was taken by surprise. </seg>
<seg id="881"> Before PayPal, explained eBay CEO Meg Whitman, ˇ°If I did business on eBay in 1999, the only way I could pay you as a buyer was with a check or money order, a paper-based system. There was no electronic way to send money, and you were too small a merchant to qualify for a credit card account. </seg>
<seg id="882"> What PayPal did was enable people, individuals, to accept credit cards. </seg>
<seg id="883"> I could pay you as an individual seller on eBay with a credit card. </seg>
<seg id="884"> This really leveled the playing field and made commerce more frictionless.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="885"> In fact, it was so good that eBay bought PayPal, but not on the recommendation of its Wall Street investment bankers- on the recommendation of its users. </seg>
<seg id="886"> ˇ°We woke up one day,ˇ± said Whitman, ˇ°and found out that 20 percent of the people on eBay were saying, 'I accept PayPal, please pay me that way.' </seg>
<seg id="887"> And we said, 'Who are these people and what are they doing?' </seg>
<seg id="888"> At first we tried to fight them and launched our own service, called Billpoint. </seg>
<seg id="889"> Finally, in July 2002, we were at [an] eBay Live [convention] and the drumbeat through the hall was deafening. </seg>
<seg id="890"> We want a standard-and by the way, we have picked the standard and it's called PayPal, and we know you guys at eBay would like it to be your [standard], but it's theirs.' </seg>
<seg id="891"> And that is when we knew we had to buy the company, because it was the standard and it was not ours... </seg>
<seg id="892"> It is the best acquisition we ever made.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="893"> In the coming phase of Web services-work flow, here is how you will make a dentist appointment: You will instruct your computer by voice to make an appointment. </seg>
<seg id="894"> Your computer will automatically translate your voice into a digital instruction. </seg>
<seg id="895"> It will automatically check your calendar against the available dates on your dentist's calendar and offer you three choices. </seg>
<seg id="896"> You will click on the preferred date and hour. </seg>
<seg id="897"> The week before your appointment, your dentist's calendar will automatically send you an e-mail reminding you of the appointment. </seg>
<seg id="898"> The night before, you will get a computer-generated voice message by phone, also reminding of your appointment. </seg>
<seg id="899"> For work flow to reach this next stage, and the productivity enhancements it will deliver, ˇ°we need more and more common standards,ˇ± said IBM's strategic planner Cawley. </seg>
<seg id="900"> ˇ°The first round of standards to emerge with the Internet were around basic data-how do you represent a number, how do you organize files, how do you display and store content, and how do you share and exchange information. </seg>
<seg id="901"> Now a whole new set of standards is emerging to enable work flow. </seg>
<seg id="902"> For example, when you apply for a mortgage, go to your closing, or buy a house, there are literally dozens of processes and data flows among many different companies. </seg>
<seg id="903"> One bank may handle securing your approval, checking your credit, establishing your interest rates, and handling the closing-after which the loan almost immediately is sold to a different bank.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="904"> We are already seeing standards emerging around payroll, e-commerce payment, and risk profiling, around how music and photos are digitally edited, and, most important, around how supply chains are connected. </seg>
<seg id="905"> ˇ°Work flow platforms are enabling us to do for the service industry what Henry Ford did for manufacturing,ˇ± said Jerry Rao, the entrepreneur doing accounting work for Americans from India. </seg>
<seg id="906"> ˇ°We are taking apart each task and sending it around to whomever can do it best, and because we are doing it in a virtual environment, people need not be physically adjacent to each other, </seg>
<seg id="907"> and then we are reassembling all the pieces back together at headquarters [or some other remote site]. </seg>
<seg id="908"> This is not a trivial revolution. </seg>
<seg id="909"> This is a major one. </seg>
<seg id="910"> It allows for a boss to be somewhere and his employees to be someplace else.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="911"> These work flow software platforms, Jerry added, ˇ°enable you to create virtual global offices-not limited by either the boundaries of your office or your country-and to access talent sitting in different parts of the world and have them complete tasks that you need completed in real time. </seg>
<seg id="912"> And so 24/7/365 we are all working. </seg>
<seg id="913"> And all this has happened in the twinkling of an eye-the span of the last two or three years.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="914"> Genesis: The Flat World Platform Emerges </seg>
<seg id="915"> We need to stop here and take stock, because at this point-the mid-1990s-the platform for the flattening of the world has started to emerge. </seg>
<seg id="916"> First, the falling walls, the opening of Windows, the digitization of content, and the spreading of the Internet browser seamlessly connected people with people as never before. </seg>
<seg id="917"> Then work flow software seamlessly connected applications to applications, so that people could manipulate all their digitized content, using computers and the Internet, as never before. </seg>
<seg id="918"> When you add this unprecedented new level of people-to-people communication to all these Web-based application-to-application work flow programs, you end up with a whole new global platform for multiple forms of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="919"> This is the Genesis moment for the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="920"> This is when it started to take shape. </seg>
<seg id="921"> It would take more time to converge and really become flat, but this is the moment when people started to feel that something was changing. </seg>
<seg id="922"> Suddenly more people from more different places found that they could collaborate with more other people on more different kinds of work and share more different kinds of knowledge than ever before. </seg>
<seg id="923"> ˇ°It is the creation of this platform, with these unique attributes, that is the truly important sustainable breakthrough that made what you call the flattening of the world possible,ˇ± said Microsoft's Craig Mundie. </seg>
<seg id="924"> Indeed, thanks to this platform that emerged from the first three flatteners, we were not just able to talk to each other more, we were able to do more things together. </seg>
<seg id="925"> This is the key point, argued Joel Cawley, the IBM strategist. </seg>
<seg id="926"> ˇ°We were not just communicating with each other more than ever, we were now able to collaborate-to build coalitions, projects, and products together-more than ever.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="927"> The next six flatteners represent the new forms of collaboration which this new platform empowered. </seg>
<seg id="928"> As J show, some people will use this platform for open-sourcing, some for outsourcing, some for offshoring, some for supply-chaining, some for insourcing, and some for in-forming. </seg>
<seg id="929"> Each of these forms of collaboration was either made possible by the new platform or greatly enhanced by it. </seg>
<seg id="930"> And as more and more of us learn how to collaborate in these different ways, we are flattening the world even more. </seg>
<seg id="931"> Flattener #4: Open-Sourcing, Self-Organizing Collaborative Communities </seg>
<seg id="932"> Alan Cohen still remembers the first time he heard the word ˇ°Apacheˇ± as an adult, and it wasn't while watching a cowboys-and-Indians movie. </seg>
<seg id="933"> It was the 1990s, the dot-com market was booming, and he was a senior manager for IBM, helping to oversee its emerging e-commerce business. </seg>
<seg id="934"> ˇ°I had a whole team with me and a budget of about $8 million,ˇ± Cohen recalled. </seg>
<seg id="935"> ˇ°We were competing head-to-head with Microsoft, Netscape, Oracle, Sun-all the big boys. </seg>
<seg id="936"> IBM had a huge sales force selling all this e-commerce software. </seg>
<seg id="937"> One day I asked the development director who worked for me, 'Say, Jeff, walk me through the development process for these e-commerce systems. </seg>
<seg id="938"> What is the underlying Web server?' </seg>
<seg id="939"> And he says to me, It's built on top of Apache.' </seg>
<seg id="940"> The first thing I think of is John Wayne. </seg>
<seg id="941"> 'What is Apache?' </seg>
<seg id="942"> I ask. </seg>
<seg id="943"> And he says it is a shareware program for Web server technology. </seg>
<seg id="944"> He said it was produced for free by a bunch of geeks just working online in some kind of open-source chat room. </seg>
<seg id="945"> I was floored. </seg>
<seg id="946"> I said, 'How do you buy it?' </seg>
<seg id="947"> And he says, 'I don't know-it just works!' </seg>
<seg id="948"> And that was my first exposure to Apache... </seg>
<seg id="949"> ˇ°Now you have to remember, back then Microsoft, IBM, Oracle, Netscape were all trying to build commercial Web servers. </seg>
<seg id="950"> These were huge companies. </seg>
<seg id="951"> And suddenly my development guy is telling me that he's getting ours off the Internet for free! </seg>
<seg id="952"> It's like you had all these big corporate executives plotting strategies, and then suddenly the guys in the mail room are in charge. </seg>
<seg id="953"> I kept asking, 'Who runs Apache? </seg>
<seg id="954"> I mean, who are these guys?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="955"> Yes, the geeks in the mail room are deciding what software they will be using and what you will be using too. </seg>
<seg id="956"> It's called the open-source movement, </seg>
<seg id="957"> and it involves thousands of people around the world coming together online to collaborate in writing everything from their own software to their own operating systems to their own dictionary to their own recipe for cola-building always from the bottom up rather than accepting formats or content imposed by corporate hierarchies from the top down. </seg>
<seg id="958"> The intellectual commons form of open-sourcing has its roots in the academic and scientific communities, where for a long time self-organized collaborative communities of scientists have come together through private networks and later the Internet to pool their brainpower or share insights around a particular science or math problem. </seg>
<seg id="959"> The Apache Web server had its roots in this form of open-sourcing. </seg>
<seg id="960"> When I asked a friend of mine, Mike Arguello, an IT systems architect, to explain to me why people share knowledge or work in this way, </seg>
<seg id="961"> he said, ˇ°IT people tend to be very bright people and they want everybody to know just how brilliant they are.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="962"> Marc Andreessen, who invented the first Web browser, agreed: ˇ°Open-source is nothing more than peer-reviewed science. </seg>
<seg id="963"> Sometimes people contribute to these things because they make science, and they discover things, and the reward is reputation. </seg>
<seg id="964"> Sometimes you can build a business out of it, sometimes they just want to increase the store of knowledge in the world. </seg>
<seg id="965"> And the peer review part is critical-and open-source is peer review. </seg>
<seg id="966"> Every bug or security hole or deviation from standards is reviewed.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="967"> I found this intellectual commons form of open-sourcing fascinating, so I went exploring to find out who were those guys and girls in the mail room. </seg>
<seg id="968"> Eventually, I found my way to one of their pioneers, Brian Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="969"> If Apache-the open-source Web server community-were an Indian tribe, Behlendorf would be the tribal elder. </seg>
<seg id="970"> I caught up with him one day in his glass-and-steel office near the San Francisco airport, where he is now founder and chief technology officer of CollabNet, a start-up focused on creating software for companies that want to use an open-source approach to innovation. </seg>
<seg id="971"> I started with two simple questions: </seg>
<seg id="972"> Where did you come from? </seg>
<seg id="973"> and: How did you manage to pull together an open-source community of online geeks that could go toe-to-toe with IBM?ˇ° </seg>
<seg id="974"> My parents met at IBM in Southern California, and I grew up in a town just north of Pasadena, La Canada,ˇ± Behlendorf recalled. </seg>
<seg id="975"> ˇ°The public school was very competitive academically, because a lot of the kids' parents worked at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory that was run by C Caltech there. </seg>
<seg id="976"> So from a very early age I was around a lot of science in a place where it was okay to be kind of geeky. </seg>
<seg id="977"> We always had computers around the house. We used to use punch cards from the original IBM mainframes for making shopping lists. </seg>
<seg id="978"> In grade school, I started doing some basic programming, and by high school I was pretty into computers... </seg>
<seg id="979"> I graduated in 1991, but in 1989, in the early days of the Internet, a friend gave me a copy of a program he had downloaded onto a floppy disk, called 'Fractint.' </seg>
<seg id="980"> It was not pirated, but was freeware, produced by a group of programmers, and was a program for drawing fractals. [Fractals are beautiful images produced at the intersection of art and math.] When the program started up, the screen would show this scrolling list of e-mail addresses for all the scientists and mathematicians who contributed to it. </seg>
<seg id="981"> I noticed that the source code was included with the program. </seg>
<seg id="982"> This was my first exposure to the concept of open-source. </seg>
<seg id="983"> Here was this program that you just downloaded for free, and they even gave you the source code with it, and it was done by a community of people. </seg>
<seg id="984"> It started to paint a different picture of programming in my mind. </seg>
<seg id="985"> I started to think that there were some interesting social dynamics to the way certain kinds of software were written or could be written-as opposed to the kind of image I had of the professional software developer in the back office tending to the mainframe, feeding info in and taking it out for the business. That seemed to me to be just one step above accounting and not very exciting.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="986"> After graduating in 1991, Behlendorf went to Berkeley to study physics, but he quickly became frustrated by the disconnect between the abstractions he was learning in the classroom and the excitement that was starting to emerge on the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="987"> ˇ°When you entered college back then, every student was given an e-mail address, and I started using it to talk to students and explore discussion boards that were starting to appear around music,ˇ± said Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="988"> ˇ°In 1992,1 started my own Internet mailing list focused on the local electronic music scene in the Bay Area. </seg>
<seg id="989"> People could just post onto the discussion board, and it started to grow, and we started to discuss different music events and DJs. </seg>
<seg id="990"> Then we said, 'Hey, why don't we invite our own DJs and throw our own events?' </seg>
<seg id="991"> It became a collective thing. </seg>
<seg id="992"> Someone would say, 'I have some records,' </seg>
<seg id="993"> and someone else would say, 'I have a sound system,' </seg>
<seg id="994"> and someone else would say, 'I know the beach and if we showed up at midnight we could have a party.' </seg>
<seg id="995"> By 1993, the Internet was still just mailing lists and e-mail and FTP sites [file transfer protocol repositories where you could store things]. </seg>
<seg id="996"> So I started collecting an archive of electronic music and was interested in how we could put this online and make it available to a larger audience. </seg>
<seg id="997"> That was when I heard about Mosaic [the Web browser developed by Marc Andreessen.] </seg>
<seg id="998"> So I got a job at the computer lab in the Berkeley business school, and I spent my spare time researching Mosaic and other Web technologies. </seg>
<seg id="999"> That led me to a discussion board with a lot of the people who were writing the first generation of Web browsers and Web servers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1000"> (A Web server is a software program that enables anyone to use his or her home or office computer to host a Web site on the World Wide Web. </seg>
<seg id="1001"> Amazon.com, for instance, has long run its Web site on Apache software. </seg>
<seg id="1002"> When your Web browser goes to www.amazon.com, the very first piece of software it talks to is Apache. </seg>
<seg id="1003"> The browser asks Apache for the Amazon Web page and Apache sends back to the browser the content of the Amazon Web page. </seg>
<seg id="1004"> Surfing the Web is really your Web browser interacting with different Web servers.) </seg>
<seg id="1005"> ˇ°I found myself sitting in on this forum watching Tim Berners-Lee and Marc Andreessen debating how all these things should work,ˇ± recalled Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1006"> ˇ°It was pretty exciting, and it seemed radically inclusive. </seg>
<seg id="1007"> I didn't need a Ph.D. or any special credentials, and I started to see some parallels between my music group and these scientists, who had a common interest in building the first Web software. </seg>
<seg id="1008"> I followed that [discussion] for a while and then I told a friend of mine about it. He was one of the first employees at Wired magazine, and he said Wired would be interested in having me set up a Web site for them. </seg>
<seg id="1009"> So I joined there at $10 an hour, setting up their e-mail and their first Web site-HotWired... </seg>
<seg id="1010"> It was one of the first ad-supported online magazines.ˇ±HotWired decided it wanted to start by having a registration system that required passwords-a controversial concept at that time. </seg>
<seg id="1011"> ˇ°In those days,ˇ± noted Andrew Leonard, who wrote a history of Apache for Salon.com in 1997, ˇ°most Webmasters depended on a Web server program developed at the University of Illinois's National Center for Super-computing Applications (also the birthplace of the groundbreaking Mosaic Web browser). </seg>
<seg id="1012"> But the NCSA Web server couldn't handle password authentication on the scale that HotWired needed. </seg>
<seg id="1013"> Luckily, the NCSA server was in the public domain, which meant that the source code was free to all comers. So Behlendorf exercised the hacker prerogative: </seg>
<seg id="1014"> He wrote some new code, a 'patch' to the NCSA Web server, that took care of the problem.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1015"> Leonard commented, ˇ°He wasn't the only clever programmer rummaging through the NCSA code that winter. </seg>
<seg id="1016"> All across the exploding Web, other Webmasters were finding it necessary to take matters into their own keyboards. </seg>
<seg id="1017"> The original code had been left to gather virtual dust when its primary programmer, University of Illinois student Rob McCool, had been scooped up (along with Marc Andreessen and Lynx author Eric Bina) by a little-known company in Silicon Valley named Netscape. </seg>
<seg id="1018"> Meanwhile, the Web refused to stop growingˇŞand kept creating new problems for Web servers to cope with.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1019"> So patches of one kind or another proliferated like Band-Aids on bandwidth, plugging one hole here and breaching another gap there. </seg>
<seg id="1020"> Meanwhile, all these patches were slowly, in an ad hoc open-source manner, building a new modern Web server. </seg>
<seg id="1021"> But everyone had his or her own version, trading patches here and there, because the NCSA lab couldn't keep up with it all. </seg>
<seg id="1022"> ˇ°I was just this near-dropout,ˇ± explained Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1023"> ˇ°I was having a lot of fun building this Web site for Wired and learning more than I was learning at Berkeley. </seg>
<seg id="1024"> We were sending in patches for the system and they weren't responding. </seg>
<seg id="1025"> So a discussion started in our little working group that the NCSA people were not answering our e-mails. </seg>
<seg id="1026"> And we said, 'If NCSA would not respond to our patches, what's going to happen in the future?' We were happy to continue improving this thing, yet we were worried when we were not getting any feedback and seeing our patches integrated. </seg>
<seg id="1027"> So I started to contact the other people I knew trading patches... </seg>
<seg id="1028"> Most of them were on the standards working groups [the Internet Engineering Task Force] that were setting the first standards for the interconnectivity between machines and applications on the Internet... </seg>
<seg id="1029"> And we said, 'Why don't we take our future into our own hands and release our own [Web server] version that incorporated all our patches?' </seg>
<seg id="1030"> ˇ°We looked up the copyright for the NCSA code, and it basically just said give us credit at Illinois for what we invented if you improve it-and don't blame us if it breaks,ˇ± recalled Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1031"> ˇ°So we started building our own version from all our patches. </seg>
<seg id="1032"> None of us had time to be a full-time Web server developer, but we thought if we could combine our time and do it in a public way, we could create something better than we could buy off the shelf-and nothing was available then, anyway. </seg>
<seg id="1033"> This was all before Netscape had shipped its first commercial Web server. </seg>
<seg id="1034"> That was the beginning of the Apache project.ˇ±By February 1999, they had completely rewritten the original NCSA program and formalized their cooperation under the name ˇ°Apache.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1035"> ˇ°I picked the name because I wanted it to have a positive connotation of being assertive,ˇ± said Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1036"> ˇ°The Apache tribe was the last tribe to surrender to the oncoming U.S. government, and at the time we worried that the big companies would come in and 'civilize' the landscape that the early Internet engineers built. </seg>
<seg id="1037"> So 'Apache' made sense to me as a good code name, and others said it also would make a good punˇ±-as in the APAtCHy server, because they were patching all these fixes together. </seg>
<seg id="1038"> So in many ways, Bellendorf and his open-source colleagues-most of whom he had never met but knew only by e-mail through their open-source chat room-had created a virtual, online, bottom-up software factory, which no one owned and no one supervised. </seg>
<seg id="1039"> ˇ°We had a software project, but the coordination and direction were an emergent behavior based on whoever showed up and wanted to write code,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="1040"> But how does it actually work? </seg>
<seg id="1041"> I asked Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1042"> You can't just have a bunch of people, unmonitored, throwing code together, can you? </seg>
<seg id="1043"> ˇ°Most software development involves a source code repository and is managed by tools such as the Concurrent Versions System,ˇ± he explained. </seg>
<seg id="1044"> ˇ°So there is a CVS server out there, and I have a CVS program on my computer. </seg>
<seg id="1045"> It allows me to connect to the server and pull down a copy of the code, so I can start working with it and making modifications. </seg>
<seg id="1046"> If I think my patch is something I want to share with others, I run a program called Patch, which allows me to create a new file, a compact collection of all the changes. </seg>
<seg id="1047"> That is called a patch file, and I can give that file to someone else, and they can apply it to their copy of the code to see what impact that patch has. </seg>
<seg id="1048"> If I have the right privileges to the server [which is restricted to a tightly controlled oversight board], I can then take my patch and commit it to the repository and it will become part of the source code. </seg>
<seg id="1049"> The CVS server keeps track of everything and who sent in what... </seg>
<seg id="1050"> So you might have 'read access' to the repository but not 'commit access' to change things. </seg>
<seg id="1051"> When someone makes a commit to the repository, that patch file gets e-mailed out to all the other developers, and so you get this peer review system after the fact, and if there is something wrong, you fix the bug.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1052"> So how does this community decide who are trusted members? </seg>
<seg id="1053"> ˇ°For Apache,ˇ± said Behlendorf, ˇ°we started with eight people who really trusted each other, </seg>
<seg id="1054"> and as new people showed up at the discussion forum and offered patch files posted to the discussion form, we would gain trust in others, and that eight grew to over one thousand. </seg>
<seg id="1055"> We were the first open-source project to get attention from the business community and get the backing from IBM.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1056"> Because of Apache's proficiency at allowing a single-server machine to host thousands of different virtual Web sites-music, data, text, pornography-it began to have ˇ°a commanding share of the Internet Service Provider market,ˇ± noted Salon's Leonard. </seg>
<seg id="1057"> IBM was trying to sell its own proprietary Web server, called GO, but it gained only a tiny sliver of the market. </seg>
<seg id="1058"> Apache proved to be both a better technology and free. </seg>
<seg id="1059"> So IBM eventually decided that if it could not beat Apache, it should join Apache. </seg>
<seg id="1060"> You have to stop here and imagine this. </seg>
<seg id="1061"> The world's biggest computer company decided that its engineers could not best the work of an ad hoc open-source collection of geeks, so they threw out their own technology and decided to go with the geeks! </seg>
<seg id="1062"> IBM ˇ°initiated contact with me, as I had a somewhat public speaker role for Apache,ˇ± said Behlendorf. </seg>
<seg id="1063"> ˇ°IBM said, 'We would like to figure out how we can use [Apache] and not get flamed by the Internet community, [how we can] make it sustainable and not just be ripping people off but contributing to the process...' </seg>
<seg id="1064"> IBM was saying that this new model for software development was trustworthy and valuable, so let's invest in it and get rid of the one that we are trying to make on our own, which isn't as good.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1065"> John Swainson was the senior IBM executive who led the team that approached Apache (he's now chairman of Computer Associates). </seg>
<seg id="1066"> He picked up the story: ˇ°There was a whole debate going on at the time about open-source, but it was all over the place. </seg>
<seg id="1067"> We decided we could deal with the Apache guys because they answered our questions. </seg>
<seg id="1068"> We could hold a meaningful conversation with these guys, and we were able to create the [nonprofit] Apache Software Foundation and work out all the issues.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1069"> At IBM's expense, its lawyers worked with the Apache group to create a legal framework around it so that there would be no copyright or liability problems for companies, like IBM, that wanted to build applications on top of Apache and charge money for them. </seg>
<seg id="1070"> IBM saw the value in having a standard vanilla Web server architecture-which allowed heterogeneous computer systems and devices to talk to each other, displaying e-mail and Web pages in a standard format-that was constantly being improved for free by an open-source community. </seg>
<seg id="1071"> The Apache collaborators did not set out to make free software. </seg>
<seg id="1072"> They set out to solve a common problem-Web serving-and found that collaborating for free in this open-source manner was the best way to assemble the best brains for the job they needed done. </seg>
<seg id="1073"> ˇ°When we started working with Apache, there was an apache.org Web site but no formal legal structure, and businesses and informal structures don't coexist well,ˇ± said Swainson. </seg>
<seg id="1074"> ˇ°You need to be able to vet the code, sign an agreement, and deal with liability issues. </seg>
<seg id="1075"> [Today] anybody can download the Apache code. </seg>
<seg id="1076"> The only obligation is that they acknowledge that it came from the site, and if they make any changes that they share them back.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1077"> There is an Apache development process that manages the traffic, and you earn your way into that process, added Swainson. </seg>
<seg id="1078"> It is something like a pure meritocracy. </seg>
<seg id="1079"> When IBM started using Apache, it became part of the community and started making contributions. </seg>
<seg id="1080"> Indeed, the one thing the Apache people demanded in return for their collaboration with IBM was that IBM assign its best engineers to join the Apache open-source group and contribute, like everyone else, for free. </seg>
<seg id="1081"> ˇ°The Apache people were not interested in payment of cash,ˇ± said Swainson. ˇ°They wanted contribution to the base. </seg>
<seg id="1082"> Our engineers came to us and said, 'These guys who do Apache are good and they are insisting that we contribute good people.' </seg>
<seg id="1083"> At first they rejected some of what we contributed. </seg>
<seg id="1084"> They said it wasn't up to their standards! </seg>
<seg id="1085"> On June 22, 1998, IBM announced plans to incorporate Apache into its own new Web server product, named WebSphere. </seg>
<seg id="1086"> The way the Apache collaborative community organized itself, whatever you took out of Apache's code and improved on, you had to give back to the whole community. </seg>
<seg id="1087"> But you were also free to go out and build a patented commercial product on top of the Apache code, as IBM did, provided that you included a copyright citation to Apache in your own patent. </seg>
<seg id="1088"> In other words, this intellectual commons approach to open-sourcing encouraged people to build commercial products on top of it. </seg>
<seg id="1089"> While it wanted the foundation to be free and open to all, it recognized that it would remain strong and fresh if both commercial and noncommercial engineers had an incentive to participate. </seg>
<seg id="1090"> Today Apache is one of the most successful open-source tools, powering about two-thirds of the Web sites in the world. </seg>
<seg id="1091"> And because Apache can be downloaded for free anywhere in the world, people from Russia to South Africa to Vietnam use it to create Web sites. </seg>
<seg id="1092"> Those individuals who need or want added capabilities for their Web servers can buy products like WebSphere, which attach right on top of Apache. </seg>
<seg id="1093"> At the time, selling a product built on top of an open-source program was a risky move on IBM's part. </seg>
<seg id="1094"> To its credit, IBM was confident in its ability to keep producing differentiated software applications on top of the Apache vanilla. </seg>
<seg id="1095"> This model has since been widely adopted, after everyone saw how it propelled IBM's Web server business to commercial leadership in that category of software, generating huge amounts of revenue. </seg>
<seg id="1096"> As I will repeat often in this book: There is no future in vanilla for most companies in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="1097"> A lot of vanilla making in software and other areas is going to shift to open-source communities. </seg>
<seg id="1098"> For most companies, the commercial future belongs to those who know how to make the richest chocolate sauce, the sweetest, lightest whipped cream, and the juiciest cherries to sit on top, or how to put them all together into a sundae. </seg>
<seg id="1099"> Jack Messman, chairman of the Novell software company, which has now become a big distributor of Linux, the open-source operating system, atop which Novell attaches gizmos to make it sing and dance just for your company, put it best: ˇ°Commercial software companies have to start operating further up the [software] stack to differentiate themselves. </seg>
<seg id="1100"> The open source community is basically focusing on infrastructureˇ± (Financial Times, June 14, 2004). </seg>
<seg id="1101"> The IBM deal was a real watershed. </seg>
<seg id="1102"> Big Blue was saying that it believed in the open-source model and that with the Apache Web server, this open-source community of engineers had created something that was not just useful and valuable but ˇ°best in its class.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1103"> That's why the open-source movement has become a powerful flattener, the effects of which we are just beginning to see. </seg>
<seg id="1104"> ˇ°It is incredibly empowering of individuals,ˇ± Brian Behlendorf said. ˇ°It doesn't matter where you come from or where you are-someone in India and South America can be just as effective using this software or contributing to it as someone in Silicon Valley.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1105"> The old model is winner take all: I wrote it, I own it-the standard software license model. </seg>
<seg id="1106"> ˇ°The only way to compete against that,ˇ± concluded Behlendorf, ˇ°is to all become winners.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1107"> Behlendorf, for his part, is betting his career that more and more people and companies will want to take advantage of the new flat-world platform to do open-source innovation. </seg>
<seg id="1108"> According to the openknowledge.org Web site, ˇ°The free/open source software movement began in the 'hacker' culture of U.S. computer science laboratories (Stanford, Berkeley, Carnegie Mellon, and MIT) in the 1960's and 1970's. </seg>
<seg id="1109"> The community of programmers was small, and close-knit. </seg>
<seg id="1110"> Code passed back and forth between the members of the community-if you made an improvement you were expected to submit your code to the community of developers. </seg>
<seg id="1111"> To withhold code was considered gauche-after all, you benefited from the work of your friends, you should return the favor.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1112"> The free software movement, however, was and remains inspired by the ethical ideal that software should be free and available to all, and it relies on open-source collaboration to help produce the best software possible to be distributed for free. </seg>
<seg id="1113"> The primary goal of the free software movement, however, is to get as many people as possible writing, improving, and distributing software for free, out of a conviction that this will empower everyone and free individuals from the grip of global corporations. </seg>
<seg id="1114"> In 1984, according to Wikipedia, an MIT researcher and one of these ex-hackers, Richard Stallman, launched the ˇ°free software movementˇ± along with an effort to build a free operating system called GNU. </seg>
<seg id="1115"> To promote free software, and to ensure that its code would always be freely modifiable and available to all, Stallman founded the Free Software Foundation and something called the GNU General Public License (GPL). </seg>
