<seg id="1116"> The GPL specified that users of the source code could copy, change, or upgrade the code, provided that they made their changes available under the same license as the original code. </seg>
<seg id="1117"> In 1991, a student at the University of Helsinki named Linus Torvalds, building off of Stallman's initiative, posted his Linux operating system to compete with the Microsoft Windows operating system and invited other engineers and geeks online to try to improve it-for free. </seg>
<seg id="1118"> Since Torvalds's initial post, programmers all over the world have manipulated, added to, expanded, patched, and improved the GNU/Linux operating system, whose license says anyone can download the source code and improve upon it but then must make the upgraded version freely available to everybody else. </seg>
<seg id="1119"> Torvalds insists that Linux must always be free. </seg>
<seg id="1120"> Companies that sell software improvements that enhance Linux or adapt it to certain functions have to be very careful not to touch its copyright in their commercial products. </seg>
<seg id="1121"> Much like Microsoft Windows, Linux offers a family of operating systems that can be adapted to run on the smallest desktop computers, laptops, PalmPilots, and even wristwatches, all the way up to the largest supercomputers and mainframes. </seg>
<seg id="1122"> So a kid in India with a cheap PC can learn the inner workings of the same operating system that is running in some of the largest data centers of corporate America. </seg>
<seg id="1123"> Linux has an army of developers across the globe working to make it better. </seg>
<seg id="1124"> As I was working on this segment of the book, I went to a picnic one afternoon at the Virginia country home of Pamela and Malcolm Baldwin, whom my wife came to know through her membership on the board of World Learning, an educational NGO. </seg>
<seg id="1125"> I mentioned in the course of lunch that I was thinking of going to Mali to see just how flat the world looked from its outermost edge-the town of Timbuktu. </seg>
<seg id="1126"> The Baldwins' son Peter happened to be working in Mali as part of something called the GeekCorps, which helps to bring technology to developing countries. </seg>
<seg id="1127"> A few days after the lunch, I received an e-mail from Pamela telling me that she had consulted with Peter about accompanying me to Timbuktu, and then she added the following, which told me everything I needed to know and saved me the whole trip: ˇ°Peter says that his project is creating wireless networks via satellite, making antennas out of plastic soda bottles and mesh from window screens! </seg>
<seg id="1128"> Apparently everyone in Mali uses Linux...ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1129"> That is no doubt a bit of an exaggeration, but it's a phrase that you'd hear only in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="1130"> The free software movement has become a serious challenge to Microsoft and some other big global software players. </seg>
<seg id="1131"> As Fortune magazine reported on February 23, 2004, ˇ°The availability of this basic, powerful software, which works on Intel's ubiquitous microprocessors, coincided with the explosive growth of the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="1132"> Linux soon began to gain a global following among programmers and business users... </seg>
<seg id="1133"> The revolution goes far beyond little Linux... </seg>
<seg id="1134"> Just about any kind of software [now] can be found in open-source form. </seg>
<seg id="1135"> The SourceForge.net website, a meeting place for programmers, lists an astounding 86,000 programs in progress. </seg>
<seg id="1136"> Most are minor projects by and for geeks, but hundreds pack real value... </seg>
<seg id="1137"> If you hate shelling out $350 for Microsoft Office or $600 for Adobe Photoshop, OpenOffice.org and the Gimp are surprisingly high-quality free alternatives.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1138"> Big companies like Google, E*Trade, and Amazon, by combining Intel-based commodity server components and the Linux operating system, have been able dramatically to cut their technology spending-and get more control over their software. </seg>
<seg id="1139"> Partly it is because they believe that open-source software can be kept more fresh and bugfree than any commercial software, because of the way it is constantly updated by an army of unpaid programmers. </seg>
<seg id="1140"> And partly it is because some big tech companies are paying engineers to work on Linux and other software, hoping it will cut into Microsoft's market share and make it a weaker competitor all around. </seg>
<seg id="1141"> However, in November 2004, the Mozilla Foundation, a nonprofit group supporting open-source software, released Firefox, a free Web browser that New York Times technology writer Randall Stross (December 19, 2004) described as very fast and filled with features that Microsoft's Internet Explorer lacks. </seg>
<seg id="1142"> Firefox 1.0, which is easily installed, was released on November 9. </seg>
<seg id="1143"> ˇ°Just over a month later,ˇ± Stross reported, ˇ°the foundation celebrated a remarkable milestone: 10 million downloads.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1144"> Donations from Firefox's appreciative fans paid for a two-page advertisement in The New York Times. </seg>
<seg id="1145"> ˇ°With Firefox,ˇ± Stross added, ˇ°open-source software moves from back-office obscurity to your home, and to your parents', too. </seg>
<seg id="1146"> (Your children in college are already using it.) </seg>
<seg id="1147"> It is polished, as easy to use as Internet Explorer </seg>
<seg id="1148"> Flattener #5: Outsourcing, Y2K </seg>
<seg id="1149"> India has had its ups and down since it achieved independence on August 15, 1947, but in some ways it might be remembered as the luckiest country in the history of the late twentieth century. </seg>
<seg id="1150"> Until recently, India was what is known in the banking world as ˇ°the second buyer.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1151"> You always want to be the second buyer in business-the person who buys the hotel or the golf course or the shopping mall after the first owner has gone bankrupt and its assets are being sold by the bank at ten cents on the dollar. </seg>
<seg id="1152"> Well, the first buyers of all the cable laid by all those fiber-optic cable companies-which thought they were going to get endlessly rich in an endlessly expanding digital universe-were their American shareholders. </seg>
<seg id="1153"> When the bubble burst, they were left holding either worthless or much diminished stock. </seg>
<seg id="1154"> The Indians, in effect, got to be the second buyers of the fiber-optics companies. </seg>
<seg id="1155"> They didn't actually purchase the shares, they just benefited from the overcapacity in fiber optics, which meant that they and their American clients got to use all that cable practically for free. </seg>
<seg id="1156"> This was a huge stroke of luck for India (and to a lesser degree for China, the former Soviet Union, and Eastern Europe), because what is the history of modern India? </seg>
<seg id="1157"> In short, India is a country with virtually no natural resources that got very good at doing one thing-mining the brains of its own people by educating a relatively large slice of its elites in the sciences, engineering, and medicine. </seg>
<seg id="1158"> In 1951, to his enduring credit, Jawaharlal Nehru, India's first prime minister, set up the first of India's seven Indian Institutes of Technology (IIT) in the eastern city of Kharagpur. </seg>
<seg id="1159"> In the fifty years since then, hundreds of thousands of Indians have competed to gain entry and then graduate from these IITs and their private-sector equivalents (as well as the six Indian Institutes of Management, which teach business administration). </seg>
<seg id="1160"> Given India's 1 billion-plus population, this competition produces a phenomenal knowledge meritocracy. </seg>
<seg id="1161"> It's like a factory, churning out and exporting some of the most gifted engineering, computer science, and software talent on the globe. </seg>
<seg id="1162"> This, alas, was one of the few things India did right. </seg>
<seg id="1163"> Because its often dysfunctional political system, coupled with Nehru's preference for pro-Soviet, Socialist economics, ensured that up until the mid-1990s India could not provide good jobs for most of those talented engineers. </seg>
<seg id="1164"> So America got to be the second buyer of India's brainpower! </seg>
<seg id="1165"> If you were a smart, educated Indian, the only way you could fulfill your potential was by leaving the country and, ideally, going to America, where some twenty-five thousand graduates of India's top engineering schools have settled since 1953, greatly enriching America's knowledge pool thanks to their education, which was subsidized by Indian taxpayers. </seg>
<seg id="1166"> ˇ°The IITs became islands of excellence by not allowing the general debasement of the Indian system to lower their exacting standards,ˇ± noted The Wall Street Journal (April 16, 2003). </seg>
<seg id="1167"> ˇ°You couldn't bribe your way to get into an IIT... </seg>
<seg id="1168"> Candidates are accepted only if they pass a grueling entrance exam. </seg>
<seg id="1169"> The government does not interfere with the curriculum, and the workload is demanding... </seg>
<seg id="1170"> Arguably, it is harder to get into an IIT than into Harvard or the Massachusetts Institute of Technology... </seg>
<seg id="1171"> IIT alumnus Vinod Khosla, who co-founded Sun Microsystems, said: 'When I finished IIT Delhi and went to Carnegie Mellon for my Masters, I thought I was cruising all the way because it was so easy relative to the education I got at IIT.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1172"> For most of their first fifty years, these IITs were one of the greatest bargains America ever had. </seg>
<seg id="1173"> It was as if someone installed a brain drain that filled up in New Delhi and emptied in Palo Alto. </seg>
<seg id="1174"> And then along came Netscape, the 1996 telecom deregulation, and Global Crossing and its fiber-optic friends. </seg>
<seg id="1175"> The world got flattened and that whole deal got turned on its head. </seg>
<seg id="1176"> ˇ°India had no resources and no infrastructure,ˇ± said Dinakar Singh, one of the most respected young hedge fund managers on Wall Street, whose parents graduated from an IIT and then immigrated to America, where he was born. </seg>
<seg id="1177"> ˇ°It produced people with quality and by quantity. </seg>
<seg id="1178"> But many of them rotted on the docks of India like vegetables. </seg>
<seg id="1179"> Only a relative few could get on ships and get out. </seg>
<seg id="1180"> Not anymore, because we built this ocean crosser, called fiberoptic cable... </seg>
<seg id="1181"> For decades you had to leave India to be a professional... </seg>
<seg id="1182"> Now you can plug into the world from India. </seg>
<seg id="1183"> You don't have to go to Yale and go to work for Goldman Sachs [as I did.]ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1184"> India could never have afforded to pay for the bandwidth to connect brainy India with high-tech America, so American shareholders paid for it. </seg>
<seg id="1185"> Sure, overinvestment can be good. </seg>
<seg id="1186"> The overinvestment in railroads turned out to be a great boon for the American economy. </seg>
<seg id="1187"> ˇ°But the railroad overinvestment was confined to your own country and so too were the benefits,ˇ± said Singh. </seg>
<seg id="1188"> In the case of the digital railroads, ˇ°it was the foreigners who benefited.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1189"> India got to ride for free.It is fun to talk to Indians who were around at precisely the moment when American companies started to discover they could draw on India's brainpower in India. </seg>
<seg id="1190"> One of them is Vivek Paul, now the president of Wipro, the Indian software giant. </seg>
<seg id="1191"> ˇ°In many ways the Indian information technology [outsourcing] revolution began with General Electric coming over. </seg>
<seg id="1192"> We're talking the late 1980s and early '90s. </seg>
<seg id="1193"> Some of their key designers [in America] were Indians, and they basically let them go back home and work from there [using the rather crude communications networks that existed then to stay in touch.] </seg>
<seg id="1194"> At that time, I was heading up the operations for GE Medical Systems in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="1195"> [GE's chairman] Jack Welch came to India in 1989 and was completely taken by India as a source of intellectual advantage for GE. </seg>
<seg id="1196"> Jack would say, 'India is a developing country with a developed intellectual capability.' </seg>
<seg id="1197"> He saw a talent pool that could be leveraged. </seg>
<seg id="1198"> So he said, 'We spend a lot of money doing software. </seg>
<seg id="1199"> Couldn't we do some work for our IT department here?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1200"> Because India had closed its market to foreign technology companies, like IBM, Indian companies had started their own factories to make PCs and servers, and Welch felt that if they could do it for themselves, they could do it for GE. </seg>
<seg id="1201"> To pursue the project, Welch sent a team headed by GE's chief information officer over to India to check out the possibilities. </seg>
<seg id="1202"> Paul was also filling in as GE's business development manager for India at the time. </seg>
<seg id="1203"> ˇ°So it was my job to escort the corporate CIO, in early 1990, on his first trip,ˇ± he recalled. </seg>
<seg id="1204"> ˇ°They had come with some pilot projects to get the ball rolling. </seg>
<seg id="1205"> I remember in the middle of the night going to pick them up at the Delhi airport with a caravan of Indian cars, Ambassadors, based on a very dated 1950s Morris Minor design. </seg>
<seg id="1206"> I was in the back car, and at one point we heard this loud bang, and I thought, What happened? </seg>
<seg id="1207"> I shot to the front, and the lead car's hood had flown off and smashed the windshield-with these GE people inside! </seg>
<seg id="1208"> So this whole caravan of GE execs pulls over to the side of the road, and I could just hear them saying to themselves, 'This is the place we're going to get software from?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1209"> Fortunately for India, the GE team was not discouraged by the poor quality of Indian cars. </seg>
<seg id="1210"> GE decided to sink roots, starting a joint development project with Wipro. </seg>
<seg id="1211"> But this was still pre-fiber-optic days. </seg>
<seg id="1212"> Simon &amp; Schuster, the book publisher, for instance, would ship its books over to India and pay Indians $50 a month (compared to $1,000 a month in the United States) to type them by hand into computers, converting the books into digitized electronic files that could be edited or amended easily in the futureˇŞparticularly dictionaries, which constantly need updating. </seg>
<seg id="1213"> In 1991, Manmohan Singh, then India's finance minister, began opening the Indian economy for foreign investment and introducing competition into the Indian telecom industry to bring down prices. </seg>
<seg id="1214"> To attract more foreign investment, Singh made it much easier for companies to set up satellite downlink stations in Bangalore, so they could skip over the Indian phone system and connect with their home bases in America, Europe, or Asia. </seg>
<seg id="1215"> Before then, only Texas Instruments had been willing to brave the Indian bureaucracy, becoming the first multinational to establish a circuit design and development center in India in 1985. </seg>
<seg id="1216"> TI's center in Bangalore had its own satellite downlink but had to suffer through having an Indian government official to oversee it-with the right to examine any piece of data going in or out. </seg>
<seg id="1217"> Singh loosened all those reins post-1991. </seg>
<seg id="1218"> A short time later, in 1994, HealthScribe India, a company originally funded in part by Indian-American doctors, was set up in Bangalore to do outsourced medical transcription for American doctors and hospitals. </seg>
<seg id="1219"> Those doctors at the time were taking handwritten notes and then dictating them into a Dictaphone for a secretary or someone else to transcribe, which would usually take days or weeks. </seg>
<seg id="1220"> HealthScribe set up a system that turned a doctor's touch-tone phone into a dictation machine. The doctor would punch in a number and simply dictate his notes to a PC with a voice card in it, which would digitize his voice. </seg>
<seg id="1221"> Thanks to the satellite, a housewife or student in Bangalore could go into a computer and download that doctor's digitized voice and transcribe it-not in two weeks but in two hours. </seg>
<seg id="1222"> Then this person would zip it right back by satellite as a text file that could be put into the hospital's computer system and become part of the billing file. </seg>
<seg id="1223"> Because of the twelve-hour time difference with India, Indians could do the transcription while the American doctors were sleeping, and the file would be ready and waiting the next morning. </seg>
<seg id="1224"> This was an important breakthrough for companies, because if you could safely, legally, and securely transcribe from Bangalore medical records, lab reports, and doctors' diagnoses-in one of the most litigious industries in the world-a lot of other industries could think about sending some of their backroom work to be done in India as well. </seg>
<seg id="1225"> And they did. </seg>
<seg id="1226"> But it remained limited by what could be handled by satellite, where there was a voice delay. </seg>
<seg id="1227"> (Ironically, said Gurujot Singh Khalsa, one of the founders of HealthScribe, they initially explored having Indians in Maine-that is, American Indians-do this work, using some of the federal money earmarked for the tribes to get started, but they could never get them interested enough to put the deal together.) </seg>
<seg id="1228"> The cost of doing the transcription in India was about one-fifth the cost per line of doing it the United States, a difference that got a lot of people's attention. </seg>
<seg id="1229"> By the late 1990s, though, Lady Luck was starting to shine on India from two directions: </seg>
<seg id="1230"> The fiber-optic bubble was starting to inflate, linking India with the United States, and the Y2K computer crisis-the so-called millennium bug-started gathering on the horizon. </seg>
<seg id="1231"> As you'll remember, the Y2K bug was a result of the fact that when computers were built, they came with internal clocks. </seg>
<seg id="1232"> In order to save memory space, these clocks rendered dates with just six digits-two for the day, two for the month, and, you guessed it, two for the year. </seg>
<seg id="1233"> That meant they could go up to only 12/31/99. </seg>
<seg id="1234"> So when the calendar hit January 1, 2000, many older computers were poised to register that not as 01/01/2000 but as 01/01/00, and they would think it was 1900 all over again. </seg>
<seg id="1235"> It meant that a huge number of existing computers (newer ones were being made with better clocks) needed to have their internal clocks and related systems adjusted; </seg>
<seg id="1236"> otherwise, it was feared, they would shut down, creating a global crisis, given how many different management systems-from water to air traffic control-were computerized.This computer remediation work was a huge, tedious job. </seg>
<seg id="1237"> Who in the world had enough software engineers to do it all? </seg>
<seg id="1238"> Answer: India, with all the techies from all those IITs and private technical colleges and computer schools. </seg>
<seg id="1239"> And so with Y2K bearing down on us, America and India started dating, and that relationship became a huge flattener, because it demonstrated to so many different businesses that the combination of the PC, the Internet, and fiber-optic cable had created the possibility of a whole new form of collaboration and horizontal value creation: outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="1240"> Any service, call center, business support operation, or knowledge work that could be digitized could be sourced globally to the cheapest, smartest, or most efficient provider. </seg>
<seg id="1241"> Using fiber-optic cable-connected workstations, Indian techies could get under the hood of your company's computers and do all the adjustments, even though they were located halfway around the world. </seg>
<seg id="1242"> ˇ°[Y2K upgrading] was tedious work that was not going to give them an enormous competitive advantage,ˇ± said Vivek Paul, the Wipro executive whose company did some outsourced Y2K drudge work. </seg>
<seg id="1243"> ˇ°So all these Western companies were incredibly challenged to find someone else who would do it and do it for as little money as possible. </seg>
<seg id="1244"> They said, 'We just want to get past the damn year 2000!' </seg>
<seg id="1245"> So they started to work with Indian [technology] companies who they might not have worked with otherwise.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1246"> Added Jerry Rao, 'Y2K means different things to different people. </seg>
<seg id="1247"> For Indian industry, it represented the biggest opportunity. </seg>
<seg id="1248"> India was considered as a place of backward people. </seg>
<seg id="1249"> Y2K suddenly required that every single computer in the world needed to be reviewed. </seg>
<seg id="1250"> The Indian IT industry got its footprint across the globe because of Y2K. </seg>
<seg id="1251"> Y2K became our engine of growth, our engine of being known around the world. </seg>
<seg id="1252"> ˇ°By early 2000, the Y2K work started to wind down, but then a whole new driver of business emerged-e-commerce. </seg>
<seg id="1253"> The dot-com bubble had not yet burst, engineering talent was scarce, and demand from dotcoms was enormous. </seg>
<seg id="1254"> Said Paul, ˇ°People wanted what they felt were mission-critical applications, key to their very existence, to be done and they could go nowhere else. </seg>
<seg id="1255"> So they turned to the Indian companies, and as they turned to the Indian companies they found that they were getting delivery of complex systems, with great quality, sometimes better than what they were getting from others. </seg>
<seg id="1256"> That created an enormous respect for Indian IT providers. </seg>
<seg id="1257"> And if [Y2K work] was the acquaintanceship process, this was the falling-in-love process.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1258"> Outsourcing from America to India, as a new form of collaboration, exploded. </seg>
<seg id="1259"> By just stringing a fiber-optic line from a workstation in Bangalore to my company's mainframe, I could have Indian IT firms like Wipro, Infosys, and Tata Consulting Services managing my e-commerce and mainframe applications. </seg>
<seg id="1260"> ˇ°Once we're in the mainframe business and once we're in e-commerceˇŞnow we're married,ˇ± said Paul. </seg>
<seg id="1261"> But again, India was lucky that it could exploit all that undersea fiber-optic cable. </seg>
<seg id="1262"> ˇ°I had an office very close to the Leela Palace hotel in Bangalore,ˇ± Paul added. ˇ°I was working with a factory located in the information technology park in Whitefield, a suburb of Bangalore, and I could not get a local telephone line between our office and the factory. </seg>
<seg id="1263"> Unless you paid a bribe, you could not get a line, and we wouldn't pay. </seg>
<seg id="1264"> So my phone call to Whitefield would go from my office in Bangalore to Kentucky, where there was a GE mainframe computer we were working with, and then from Kentucky to Whitefield. </seg>
<seg id="1265"> We used our own fiber-optic lease line that ran across the ocean-but the one across town required a bribe.ˇ±India didn't benefit only from the dot-com boom; </seg>
<seg id="1266"> it benefited even more from the dot-com bust! </seg>
<seg id="1267"> That is the real irony. </seg>
<seg id="1268"> The boom laid the cable that connected India to the world, and the bust made the cost of using it virtually free and also vastly increased the number of American companies that would want to use that fiber-optic cable to outsource knowledge work to India. </seg>
<seg id="1269"> Y2K led to this mad rush for Indian brainpower to get the programming work done. </seg>
<seg id="1270"> The Indian companies were good and cheap, but price wasn't first on customers' minds-getting the work done was, and India was the only place with the volume of workers to do it. </seg>
<seg id="1271"> Then the dot-com boom comes along right in the wake of Y2K, and India is one of the few places where you can find surplus English-speaking engineers, at any price, because all of those in America have been scooped up by e-commerce companies. </seg>
<seg id="1272"> Then the dot-com bubble bursts, the stock market tanks, and the pool of investment capital dries up. </seg>
<seg id="1273"> American IT companies that survived the boom and venture capital firms that still wanted to fund start-ups had much less cash to spend. </seg>
<seg id="1274"> Now they needed those Indian engineers not just because there were a lot of them, but precisely because they were low-cost. </seg>
<seg id="1275"> One of the great mistakes made by many analysts in the early 2000s was conflating the dot-com boom with globalization, suggesting that both were just fads and hot air. </seg>
<seg id="1276"> When the dot-com bust came along, these same wrongheaded analysts assumed that globalization was over as well. </seg>
<seg id="1277"> Exactly the opposite was true. </seg>
<seg id="1278"> The dot-com bubble was only one aspect of globalization, and when it imploded, rather than imploding globalization, it actually turbocharged it. </seg>
<seg id="1279"> Promod Haque, an Indian-American and one of the most prominent venture capitalists in Silicon Valley with his firm Norwest Venture Partners, was in the middle of this transition. </seg>
<seg id="1280"> ˇ°When the bust took place, a lot of these Indian engineers in the U.S. [on temporary work visas] got laid off, so they went back to India,ˇ± explained Haque. </seg>
<seg id="1281"> But as a result of the bust, the IT budgets of virtually every major U.S. firm got slashed. </seg>
<seg id="1282"> ˇ°Every IT manager was told to get the same amount of work or more done with less money. </seg>
<seg id="1283"> So guess what he does? </seg>
<seg id="1284"> He says, 'You remember Vijay from India who used to work here during the boom and then went back home? </seg>
<seg id="1285"> Let me call him over in Bangalore and see if he will do the work for us for less money than what we would pay an engineer here in the U.S.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1286"> And thanks to all that fiber cable laid during the boom, it was easy to find Vijay and put him to work. </seg>
<seg id="1287"> The Y2K computer readjustment work was done largely by low-skilled Indian programmers right out of tech schools, said Haque, ˇ°but the guys on visas who were coming to America were not trade school guys. </seg>
<seg id="1288"> They were guys with advanced engineering degrees. </seg>
<seg id="1289"> So a lot of our companies saw that these guys were good at Java and C++ and architectural design work for computers, and then they got laid off and went back home, and the IT manager back here who is told, I don't care how you get the job done, just get it done for less money,' calls Vijay.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1290"> Once America and India were dating, the burgeoning Indian IT companies in Bangalore started coming up with their own proposals. </seg>
<seg id="1291"> The Y2K work had allowed them to interact with some pretty large companies in the United States, and as a result they began to understand the pain points and how to do business-process implementation and improvement. </seg>
<seg id="1292"> So the Indians, who were doing a lot of very specific custom code maintenance to higher-value-add companies, started to develop their own products and transform themselves from maintenance to product companies, offering a range of software services and consulting. </seg>
<seg id="1293"> This took Indian companies much deeper inside American ones, and business-process outsourcing- letting Indians run your back room-went to a whole new level. </seg>
<seg id="1294"> ˇ°I have an accounts payable department and I could move this whole thing to India under Wipro or Infosys and cut my costs in half,ˇ± said Haque. </seg>
<seg id="1295"> All across America, CEOs were saying, ˇ°'Make it work for less,'ˇ± said Haque. </seg>
<seg id="1296"> ˇ°And the Indian companies were saying, 'I have taken a look under your hood and I will provide you with a total solution for the lowest price.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1297"> In other words, the Indian outsourcing companies said, ˇ°Do you remember how I fixed your tires and your pistons during Y2K? </seg>
<seg id="1298"> Well, I could actually give you a whole lube job if you like. </seg>
<seg id="1299"> And now that you know me and trust me, you know I can do it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1300"> To their credit, the Indians were not just cheap, they were also hungry and ready to learn anything. </seg>
<seg id="1301"> The scarcity of capital after the dot-com bust made venture capital firms see to it that the companies they were investing in were finding the most efficient, high-quality, low-price way to innovate. </seg>
<seg id="1302"> In the boom times, said Haque, it was not uncommon for a $50 million investment in a start-up to return $500 million once the company went public. </seg>
<seg id="1303"> After the bust, that same company's public offering might bring in only $100 million. </seg>
<seg id="1304"> Therefore, venture firms wanted to risk only $20 million to get that company from start-up to IPO. </seg>
<seg id="1305"> ˇ°For venture firms,ˇ± said Haque, ˇ°the big question became, How do I get my entrepreneurs and their new companies to a point where they were breaking even or profitable sooner, so they can stop being a draw on my capital and be sold so our firm can generate good liquidity and returns? </seg>
<seg id="1306"> The answer many firms came up with was: I better start outsourcing as many functions as I can from the beginning. </seg>
<seg id="1307"> I have to make money for my investors faster, so what can be outsourced must be outsourced.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1308"> Henry Schacht, who, as noted, was heading Lucent during part of this period, saw the whole process from the side of corporate management. </seg>
<seg id="1309"> The business economics, he told me, became ˇ°very uglyˇ± for everyone. </seg>
<seg id="1310"> Everyone found prices flat to declining and markets stagnant, yet they were still spending huge amounts of money running the backroom operations of their companies, which they could no longer afford. </seg>
<seg id="1311"> ˇ°Cost pressures were enormous,ˇ± he recalled, ˇ°and the flat world was available, [so] economics were forcing people to do things they never thought they would do or could do... </seg>
<seg id="1312"> Globalization got superchargedˇ±-for both knowledge work and manufacturing. Companies found that they could go to MIT and find four incredibly smart Chinese engineers who were ready to go back to China and work for them from there for the same amount that it would cost them to hire one engineer in America. </seg>
<seg id="1313"> Bell Labs had a research facility at Tsingdao that could connect to Lucent's computers in America. </seg>
<seg id="1314"> ˇ°They would use our computers overnight,ˇ± said Schacht. </seg>
<seg id="1315"> ˇ°Not only was the incremental computing cost close to zero, but so too was the transmission cost, and the computer was idle [at night].ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1316"> For all these reasons I believe that Y2K should be a national holiday in India, a second Indian Independence Day, in addition to August 15. </seg>
<seg id="1317"> As Johns Hopkins foreign policy expert Michael Mandelbaum, who spent part of his youth in India, put it, ˇ°Y2K should be called Indian Inter-depedence Day,ˇ± because it was India's ability to collaborate with Western companies, thanks to the interdependence created by fiber-optic networks, that really vaulted it forward and gave more Indians than ever some real freedom of choice in how, for whom, and where they worked. </seg>
<seg id="1318"> To put it another way, August 15 commemorates freedom at midnight. </seg>
<seg id="1319"> Y2K made possible employment at midnight-but not any employment, employment for India's best knowledge workers. </seg>
<seg id="1320"> August 15 gave independence to India. </seg>
<seg id="1321"> But Y2K gave independence to Indians </seg>
<seg id="1322"> In that sense, yes, India was lucky, but it also reaped what it had sowed through hard work and education and the wisdom of its elders who built all those IITs. </seg>
<seg id="1323"> Louis Pasteur said it a long time ago: ˇ°Fortune favors the prepared mind.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1324"> Flattener #6: Offshoring, Running with Gazelles, Eating with Lions </seg>
<seg id="1325"> On December 11, 2001, China formally joined the World Trade Organization, which meant Beijing agreed to follow the same global rules governing imports, exports, and foreign investments that most countries in the world were following. </seg>
<seg id="1326"> It meant China was agreeing, in principle, to make its own competitive playing field as level as the rest of the world. </seg>
<seg id="1327"> A few days later, the American-trained Chinese manager of a fuel pump factory in Beijing, which was owned by a friend of mine, Jack Perkowski, the chairman and CEO of ASIMCO Technologies, an American auto parts manufacturer in China, posted the following African proverb, translated into Mandarin, on his factory floor: </seg>
<seg id="1328"> Every morning in Africa, a gazelle wakes up. </seg>
<seg id="1329"> It knows it must run faster than the fastest lion or it will be killed. </seg>
<seg id="1330"> Every morning a lion wakes up.It knows it must outrun the slowest gazelle or it will starve to death. </seg>
<seg id="1331"> It doesn't matter whether you are a lion or a gazelle. </seg>
<seg id="1332"> When the sun comes up, you better start running. </seg>
<seg id="1333"> I don't know who is the lion and who is the gazelle, but I do know this: </seg>
<seg id="1334"> Ever since the Chinese joined the WTO, both they and the rest of the world have had to run faster and faster. </seg>
<seg id="1335"> This is because China's joining the WTO gave a huge boost to another form of collaboration- offshoring. </seg>
<seg id="1336"> Offshoring, which has been around for decades, is different from outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="1337"> Outsourcing means taking some specific, but limited, function that your company was doing in-house-such as research, call centers, or accounts receivable-and having another company perform that exact same function for you and then reintegrating their work back into your overall operation. </seg>
<seg id="1338"> Offshoring, by contrast, is when a company takes one of its factories that it is operating in Canton, Ohio, and moves the whole factory offshore to Canton, China. </seg>
<seg id="1339"> There, it produces the very same product in the very same way, only with cheaper labor, lower taxes, subsidized energy, and lower health-care costs. </seg>
<seg id="1340"> Just as Y2K took India and the world to a whole new level of outsourcing, China's joining the WTO took Beijing and the world to a whole new level of offshoring-with more companies shifting production offshore and then integrating it into their global supply chains. </seg>
<seg id="1341"> In 1977, Chinese leader Deng Xiaoping put China on the road to capitalism, declaring later that ˇ°to get rich is glorious.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1342"> When China first opened its tightly closed economy, companies in industrialized countries saw it as an incredible new market for exports. </seg>
<seg id="1343"> Every Western or Asian manufacturer dreamed of selling its equivalent of 1 billion pairs of underwear to a single market. Some foreign companies set up shop in China to do just that. </seg>
<seg id="1344"> But because China was not subject to world trade rules, it was able to restrict the penetration into its market by these Western companies through various trade and investment barriers. </seg>
<seg id="1345"> And when it was not doing that deliberately, the sheer bureaucratic and cultural difficulties of doing business in China had the same effect. </seg>
<seg id="1346"> Many of the pioneer investors in China lost their shirts and pants and underwear- and with China's Wild West legal system there was not much recourse. </seg>
<seg id="1347"> Beginning in the 1980s, many investors, particularly overseas Chinese who knew how to operate in China, started to say, ˇ°Well, if we can't sell that many things to the Chinese right now, why don't we use China's disciplined labor pool to make things there and sell them abroad?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1348"> This dovetailed with the interests of China's leaders. </seg>
<seg id="1349"> China wanted to attract foreign manufacturers and their technologies-not simply to manufacture 1 billion pairs of underwear for sale in China but to use low-wage Chinese labor to also sell 6 billion pairs of underwear to everyone else in the world, and at prices that were a fraction of what the underwear companies in Europe or America or even Mexico were charging. </seg>
<seg id="1350"> Once that offshoring process began in a range of industries-from textiles to consumer electronics to furniture to eyeglass frames to auto parts-the only way other companies could compete was by offshoring to China as well (taking advantage of its low-cost, high-quality platform), or by looking for alternative manufacturing centers in Eastern Europe, the Caribbean, or somewhere else in the developing world. </seg>
<seg id="1351"> By joining the World Trade Organization in 2001, China assured foreign companies that if they shifted factories offshore to China, they would be protected by international law and standard business practices. </seg>
<seg id="1352"> Under WTO rules, Beijing agreed-with some time for phase-in-to treat non-Chinese citizens or firms as if they were Chinese in terms of their economic rights and obligations under Chinese law. </seg>
<seg id="1353"> This meant that foreign companies could sell virtually anything anywhere in China. </seg>
<seg id="1354"> WTO membership status also meant that Beijing agreed to treat all WTO member nations equally, meaning that the same tariffs and the same regulations had to apply equally for everyone. </seg>
<seg id="1355"> And it agreed to submit itself to international arbitration in the event of a trade dispute with another country or a foreign company. </seg>
<seg id="1356"> At the same time, government bureaucrats became more customer-friendly, procedures for investments were streamlined, and Web sites proliferated in different ministries to help foreigners navigate China's business regulations. </seg>
<seg id="1357"> U.S. embassy officials in China told me that 2 million copies of the Chinese-language edition of the WTO rule book were sold in the weeks immediately after China signed on to the WTO. </seg>
<seg id="1358"> Deng Xiaoping made China open to absorbing many of the ten flatteners, and, in so doing, made China a challenge to the whole world. </seg>
<seg id="1359"> Before China signed on to the WTO, there was a sense that, while China had opened up to get the advantages of trade with the West, the government and the banks would protect Chinese businesses from any crushing foreign competition, said Jack Perkowski of ASIMCO. </seg>
<seg id="1360"> ˇ°China's entry into the WTO was a signal to the community outside of China that it was now on the capitalist track for good,ˇ± he added. </seg>
<seg id="1361"> With WTO, China said, 'We are on one course.'ˇ±Because China can amass so many low-wage workers at the unskilled, semiskilled, and skilled levels, because it has such a voracious appetite for factory, equipment, and knowledge jobs to keep its people employed, and because it has such a massive and burgeoning consumer market, it has become an unparalleled zone for offshoring. </seg>
<seg id="1362"> China has more than 160 cities with a population of 1 million or more. </seg>
<seg id="1363"> You can go to towns on the east coast of China today that you have never heard of and discover that this one town manufacturers most of the eyeglass frames in the world, while the town next door manufacturers most of the portable cigarette lighters in the world, and the one next to that is doing most of the computer screens for Dell, and another is specializing in mobile phones. </seg>
<seg id="1364"> Kenichi Ohmae, the Japanese business consultant, estimates in his book The United States of China that in the Zhu Jiang Delta area alone, north of Hong Kong, there are fifty thousand Chinese electronics component suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1365"> ˇ°China is a threat, China is a customer, and China is an opportunity,ˇ± Ohmae remarked to me one day in Tokyo. </seg>
<seg id="1366"> You cannot ignore it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1367"> Instead of competing with China as an enemy, argues Ohmae, you break down your business and think about which part of the business you would like to do in China, which part you would like to sell to China, and which part you want to buy from China. </seg>
<seg id="1368"> Here we get to the real flattening aspect of China's opening to the world market. </seg>
<seg id="1369"> The more attractive China makes itself as a base for off-shoring, the more attractive other developed and developing countries competing with it, like Malaysia, Thailand, Ireland, Mexico, Brazil, and Vietnam, have to make themselves. </seg>
<seg id="1370"> They all look at what is going on in China and the jobs moving there and say to themselves, ˇ°Holy catfish, we had better start offering these same incentives.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1371"> This has created a process of competitive flattening, in which countries scramble to see who can give companies the best tax breaks, education incentives, and subsidies, on top of their cheap labor, to encourage offshoring to their shores. </seg>
<seg id="1372"> Ohio State University business professor Oded Shenkar, author of the book The Chinese Century, told BusinessWeek (December 6, 2004) that he gives it to American companies straight: ˇ°If you still make anything labor intensive, get out now rather than bleed to death. </seg>
<seg id="1373"> Shaving 5% here and there won't work.ˇ± Chinese producers can make the same adjustments. </seg>
<seg id="1374"> ˇ°You need an entirely new business model to compete,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="1375"> China's flattening power is also fueled by the fact that it is developing a huge domestic market of its own. </seg>
<seg id="1376"> The same BusinessWeek article noted that this brings economies of scale, intense local rivalries that keep prices low, an army of engineers that is growing by 350,000 annually, young workers and managers willing to put in twelve-hour days, an unparalleled component base in electronics and light industry, ˇ°and an entrepreneurial zeal to do whatever it takes to please big retailers such as Wal-Mart Stores, Target, Best Buy and J.C. Penney.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1377"> Critics of China's business practices say that its size and economic power mean that it will soon be setting the global floor not only for low wages but also for lax labor laws and workplace standards. </seg>
<seg id="1378"> This is known in the business as ˇ°the China price.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1379"> But what is really scary is that China is not attracting so much global investment by simply racing everyone to the bottom. </seg>
<seg id="1380"> That is just a short-term strategy. </seg>
<seg id="1381"> The biggest mistake any business can make when it comes to China is thinking that it is only winning on wages and not improving quality and productivity. </seg>
<seg id="1382"> In the private, non-state-owned sector of Chinese industry, productivity increased 17 percent annually-I repeat, 17 percent annually-between 1995 and 2002, according to a study by the U.S. Conference Board. </seg>
<seg id="1383"> This is due to China's absorption of both new technologies and modern business practices, starting from a very low base. </seg>
<seg id="1384"> Incidentally, the Conference Board study noted, China lost 15 million manufacturing jobs during this period, compared with 2 million in the United States. </seg>
<seg id="1385"> ˇ°As its manufacturing productivity accelerates, China is losing jobs in manufacturing-many more than the United States is-and gaining them in services, a pattern that has been playing out in the developed world for many years,ˇ± the study said. </seg>
<seg id="1386"> China's real long-term strategy is to outrace America and the E.U. countries to the top, and the Chinese are off to a good start. </seg>
<seg id="1387"> China's leaders are much more focused than many of their Western counterparts on how to train their young people in the math, science, and computer skills required for success in the flat world, how to build a physical and telecom infrastructure that will allow Chinese people to plug and play faster and easier than others, and how to create incentives that will attract global investors. </seg>
<seg id="1388"> What China's leaders really want is the next generation of underwear or airplane wings to be designed in China as well. </seg>
<seg id="1389"> That is where things are heading in another decade. </seg>
<seg id="1390"> So in thirty years we will have gone from ˇ°sold in Chinaˇ± to ˇ°made in Chinaˇ± to ˇ°designed in Chinaˇ± to ˇ°dreamed up in Chinaˇ±-or from China as collaborator with the worldwide manufacturers on nothing to China as a low-cost, high-quality, hyperefficient collaborator with worldwide manufacturers on everything. </seg>
<seg id="1391"> Indeed, while researching this chapter, I came across an online Silicon Valley newsletter called the Inquirer, which follows the semiconductor industry. </seg>
<seg id="1392"> What caught my eye was its November 5, 2001, article headlined, ˇ°China to Become Center of Everything.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1393"> It quoted a China People's Daily article that claimed that four hundred out of the Forbes 500 companies have invested in more than two thousand projects in mainland China. </seg>
<seg id="1394"> And that was four years ago.Japan, being right next door to China, has taken a very aggressive approach to internalizing the China challenge. </seg>
<seg id="1395"> Osamu Watanabe, chairman of the Japan External Trade Organization (JETRO), Japan's official organ for promoting exports, told me in Tokyo, ˇ°China is developing very rapidly and making the shift from low-grade products to high-grade, high-tech ones.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1396"> As a result, added Watanabe, Japanese companies, to remain globally competitive, have had to shift some production and a lot of assembly of middle-range products to China, while shifting at home to making ˇ°even higher value-added products.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1397"> So China and Japan ˇ°are becoming part of the same supply chain.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1398"> After a prolonged recession, Japan's economy started to bounce back in 2003, due to the sale of thousands of tons of machinery, assembly robots, and other critical components in China. </seg>
<seg id="1399"> In 2003, China replaced the United States as the biggest importer of Japanese products. </seg>
<seg id="1400"> This China flattener has been wrenching for certain manufacturing workers around the world, but a godsend for all consumers. </seg>
<seg id="1401"> Fortune magazine (October 4, 2004) quoted a study by Morgan Stanley estimating that since the mid-1990s alone, cheap imports from China have saved U.S. consumers roughly $600 billion and have saved U.S. manufacturers untold billions in cheaper parts for their products. </seg>
<seg id="1402"> This savings, in turn, Fortune noted, has helped the Federal Reserve to hold down interest rates longer, giving more Americans a chance to buy homes or refinance the ones they have, and giving businesses more capital to invest in new innovations. </seg>
<seg id="1403"> In an effort to better understand how offshoring to China works, I sat down in Beijing with Jack Perkowski of ASIMCO, a pioneer in this form of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="1404"> If they ever have a category in the Olympics called ˇ°extreme capitalism,ˇ± bet on Perkowski to win the gold. </seg>
<seg id="1405"> In 1988 he stepped down as a top investment banker at Paine Webber and went to a leverage buyout firm, but two years later, at age forty-two, decided it was time for a new challenge. </seg>
<seg id="1406"> With some partners, he raised $150 million to buy companies in China and headed off for the adventure of his life. </seg>
<seg id="1407"> Since then he has lost and remade millions of dollars, learned every lesson the hard way, but survived to become a powerful example of what offshoring to China is all about and what a powerful collaborative tool it can become. </seg>
<seg id="1408"> ˇ°When I first started back in 1992-1993, everyone thought the hard part was to actually find and gain access to opportunities in China,ˇ± recalled Perkowski. </seg>
<seg id="1409"> It turned out that there were opportunities aplenty but a critical shortage of Chinese managers who understood how to run an auto parts factory along capitalist lines, with an emphasis on exports and making world-class products for the Chinese market. </seg>
<seg id="1410"> As Perkowski put it, the easy part was setting up shop in China. </seg>
<seg id="1411"> The hard part was getting the right local managers who could run the store. </seg>
<seg id="1412"> So when he initially started buying majority ownership in Chinese auto parts companies, Perkowski began by importing managers from abroad. </seg>
<seg id="1413"> It was too expensive, and operating in China was just too foreign for foreigners. </seg>
<seg id="1414"> Scratch plan A. </seg>
<seg id="1415"> ˇ°So we sent all the expats home, which gave me problems with my investor base, and went to plan B,ˇ± he said. ˇ°We then tried to convert the 'Old China' managers who typically came along with the plants we bought, but that didn't work either. </seg>
<seg id="1416"> They were simply too used to working in a planned economy where they never had to deal with the marketplace, just deliver their quotas. </seg>
<seg id="1417"> ˇ°The Chinese are very entrepreneurial,ˇ± said Perkowski, ˇ°but back then, before China joined the WTO, there was no rule of law and no bond or stock market to restrain this entrepreneurialism. </seg>
<seg id="1418"> Your only choices were managers from the state-owned sector, who were very bureaucratic, or managers from the first wave of private companies, who were practicing cowboy capitalism. </seg>
<seg id="1419"> One of his first purchases in China was an interest in a company making rubber parts. </seg>
<seg id="1420"> When he subsequently reached an agreement with his Chinese partner to purchase his shares in the company, the Chinese partner signed a noncompete clause as part of the transaction. </seg>
<seg id="1421"> As soon as the deal closed, however, the Chinese partner went out and opened a new factory. </seg>
<seg id="1422"> ˇ°Noncompeteˇ± did not quite translate into Mandarin. </seg>
<seg id="1423"> Scratch plan B. </seg>
<seg id="1424"> Meanwhile, Perkowski's partnership was hemorrhaging money- Perkowski's tuition for learning how to do business in China-and he found himself owning a string of Chinese auto parts factories. </seg>
<seg id="1425"> ˇ°Around 1997 was the low point,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="1426"> ˇ°Our company as a whole was shrinking and we were not profitable. </seg>
<seg id="1427"> While some of our companies were doing okay, we were generally in tough shape. Although we had majority ownership and could theoretically put anyone on the field that we wanted, I looked at my [managerial] bench and I had no one to put in the game.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1428"> Time for plan C. </seg>
<seg id="1429"> ˇ°We essentially concluded that, while we liked China, we wanted no part of 'Old China,' and instead wanted to place our bets on 'New China' managers,ˇ± said Perkowski. </seg>
<seg id="1430"> ˇ°We began looking for a new breed of Chinese managers who were open-minded and had gotten some form of management training. </seg>
<seg id="1431"> We were looking for individuals who were experienced at operating in China and yet were familiar with how the rest of the world operated and knew where China had to go. </seg>
<seg id="1432"> So between 1997 and 1999, we recruited a whole team of'New China' managers, typically mainland Chinese who had worked for multinationals, and as these managers came on board, we began one by one to replace the 'Old China' managers at our companies.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1433"> Once the new generation of Chinese managers, who understood global markets and customers and could be united around a shared company vision-and knew China-was in place, ASIMCO started making a profit. </seg>
<seg id="1434"> Today ASIMCO has sales of about $350 million a year in auto parts from thirteen Chinese factories in nine provinces. </seg>
<seg id="1435"> The company sells to customers in the United States, and it also has thirty-six sales offices throughout China servicing automakers in that country too. </seg>
<seg id="1436"> From this base, Perkowski made his next big move-taking the profits from offshoring back onshore in America. </seg>
<seg id="1437"> ˇ°In April of 2003, we bought the North American camshaft operations of Federal-Mogul Corporation, an old-line components company that is now in bankruptcy,ˇ± said Perkowski. ˇ°We bought the business first to get access to its customers, which were primarily the Big Three automakers, plus Caterpillar and Cummins. </seg>
<seg id="1438"> While we have had long-standing relationships with Cat and CumminsˇŞand this acquisition enhanced our position with them- the camshaft sales to the Big Three were our first. </seg>
<seg id="1439"> The second reason to make the acquisition was to obtain technology which we could bring back to China. </seg>
<seg id="1440"> Like most of the technology that goes into modern passenger cars and trucks, people take camshaft technology for granted. </seg>
<seg id="1441"> However, camshafts [the part of the engine that controls how the pistons go up and down] are highly engineered products which are critical to the performance of the engine. </seg>
<seg id="1442"> The acquisition of this business essentially gave us the know-how and technology that we could use to become the camshaft leader in China. </seg>
<seg id="1443"> As a result, we now have the best camshaft technology and a customer base both in China and the U.S.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1444"> This is a very important point, because the general impression is that offshoring is a lose-lose proposition for American workers-something that was here went over there, and that is the end of the story. </seg>
<seg id="1445"> The reality is more complicated. </seg>
<seg id="1446"> Most companies build offshore factories not simply to obtain cheaper labor for products they want to sell in America or Europe. </seg>
<seg id="1447"> Another motivation is to serve that foreign market without having to worry about trade barriers and to gain a dominant foothold there-particularly a giant market like China's. </seg>
<seg id="1448"> According to the U.S. Commerce Department, nearly 90 percent of the output from U.S.-owned offshore factories is sold to foreign consumers. </seg>
<seg id="1449"> But this actually stimulates American exports. </seg>
<seg id="1450"> There is a variety of studies indicating that every dollar a company invests overseas in an offshore factory yields additional exports for its home country, because roughly one-third of global trade today is within multinational companies. </seg>
<seg id="1451"> Even when production is moved offshore to save on wages, it is usually not all moved offshore. </seg>
<seg id="1452"> According to a January 26, 2004, study by the Heritage Foundation, Job Creation and the Taxation of Foreign-Source Income, American companies that produce at home and abroad, for both the American market and China's, generate more than 21 percent of U.S. economic output, produce 56 percent of U.S. exports, and employ three-fifths of all manufacturing employees, about 9 million workers. </seg>
<seg id="1453"> So if General Motors builds a factory offshore in Shanghai, it also ends up creating jobs in America by exporting a lot of goods and services to its own factory in China and benefiting from lower parts costs in China for its factories in America. </seg>
<seg id="1454"> While much attention is paid to American companies going offshore to China, little attention is paid to the huge amount of offshore investment coming into America every year, because foreigners want access to American markets and labor just like we want access to theirs. </seg>
<seg id="1455"> On September 25, 2003, DaimlerChrysler celebrated the tenth anniversary of its decision to build the first Mercedes-Benz passenger car factory outside Germany, in Tuscaloosa, Alabama, by announcing a $600 million plant expansion. </seg>
<seg id="1456"> ˇ°In Tuscaloosa we have impressively shown that we can produce a new production series with a new workforce in a new factory, and we have also demonstrated that it is possible to have vehicles successfully 'Made by Mercedes' outside of Germany,ˇ± Professor Jiirgen Hub-bert, the DaimlerChrysler Board of Management member responsible for the Mercedes Car Group, announced on the anniversary. </seg>
<seg id="1457"> Not surprisingly, ASIMCO will use its new camshaft operation in China to handle the raw material and rough machining operations, exporting semifinished products to its camshaft plant in America, where more skilled American workers can do the finished machining operations, which are most critical to quality. </seg>
<seg id="1458"> In this way, ASIMCO's American customers receive the benefit of a China supply chain and at the same time have the comfort of dealing with a known, American supplier. </seg>
<seg id="1459"> The average wage of a high-skilled machinist in America is $3,000 to $4,000 a month. </seg>
<seg id="1460"> The average wage for a factory worker in China is about $150 a month. </seg>
<seg id="1461"> In addition, ASIMCO is required to participate in a Chinese government-sponsored pension plan covering heath care, housing, and retirement benefits. </seg>
<seg id="1462"> Between 35 and 45 percent of a Chinese worker's monthly wage goes directly to the local labor bureau to cover these benefits. </seg>
<seg id="1463"> The fact that health insurance in China is so much cheaper-because of lower wages, much more limited health service offerings, and no malpractice suits-ˇ°certainly makes China an attractive place to expand and add employees,ˇ± explained Perkowski. </seg>
<seg id="1464"> ˇ°Anything which can be done to reduce a U.S. company's liability for medical coverage would be a plus in keeping jobs in the U.S.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1465"> By taking advantage of the flat world to collaborate this way- between onshore and offshore factories, and between high-wage, high-skilled American workers close to their market and low-wage Chinese workers close to theirs-said Perkowski, ˇ°we make our American company more competitive, so it is getting more orders and we are actually growing the business. </seg>
<seg id="1466"> And that is what many in the U.S. are missing when they talk about offshoring. </seg>
<seg id="1467"> Since the acquisition, for example, we have doubled our business with Cummins, and our business with Caterpillar has grown significantly. </seg>
<seg id="1468"> All of our customers are exposed to global competition and really need their supply base to the do the right thing as far as cost competitiveness. </seg>
<seg id="1469"> They want to work with suppliers who understand the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="1470"> When I went to visit our U.S. customers to explain our strategy for the camshaft business, they were very positive about what we were doing, because they could see that we were aligning our business in a way that was going to enable them to be more competitive.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1471"> This degree of collaboration has been possible only in the last couple of years. ˇ°We could not have done what we have done in China in 1983 or 1993,ˇ± said Perkowski. </seg>
<seg id="1472"> ˇ°Since 1993, a number of things have come together. </seg>
<seg id="1473"> For example, people always talk about how much the Internet has benefited the U.S. </seg>
<seg id="1474"> The point I always make is that China has benefited even more. </seg>
<seg id="1475"> What has held China back in the past was the inability of people outside China to get information about the country, and the inability of people inside China to get information about the rest of the world. </seg>
<seg id="1476"> Prior to the Internet, the only way to close that information gap was travel. </seg>
<seg id="1477"> Now you can stay home and do it with the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="1478"> We now just e-mail blueprints over the Internet-we don't even need FedEx.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1479"> The advantages for manufacturing in China, for certain industries, are becoming overwhelming, added Perkowski, and cannot be ignored. </seg>
<seg id="1480"> ˇ°If you are sitting in the U.S. and don't figure out how to get into China,ˇ± he said, ˇ°in ten or fifteen years from now you will not be a global leader.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1481"> Now that China is in the WTO, a lot of traditional, slow, inefficient, and protected sectors of the Chinese economy are being exposed to some withering global competition-something received as warmly in Canton, China, as in Canton, Ohio. </seg>
<seg id="1482"> Had the Chinese government put WTO membership to a popular vote, ˇ°it never would have passed,ˇ± said Pat Powers, who headed the U.S.-China Business Council office in Beijing during the WTO accession. </seg>
<seg id="1483"> And for the last two and half years they've been slugging it out.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1484"> But even as a free-trader, I am worried about the challenge this will pose to wages and benefits of certain workers in the United States, at least in the short run. </seg>
<seg id="1485"> Its economy is totally interlinked with those of the developed world, and trying to delink it would cause economic and geopolitical chaos that could devastate the global economy. </seg>
<seg id="1486"> Americans and Europeans will have to develop new business models that will enable them to get the best out of China and cushion themselves against some of the worst. </seg>
<seg id="1487"> As BusinessWeek, in its dramatic December 6, 2004, cover story on ˇ°The China Price,ˇ± put it, ˇ°Can China dominate everything? </seg>
<seg id="1488"> Of course not. </seg>
<seg id="1489"> America remains the world's biggest manufacturer, producing 75% of what it consumes, though that's down from 90% in the mid-'90s. </seg>
<seg id="1490"> Industries requiring huge R&amp;D budgets and capital investment, such as aerospace, pharmaceuticals, and cars, still have strong bases in the U.S.... </seg>
<seg id="1491"> America will surely continue to benefit from China's expansion.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1492"> That said, unless America can deal with the long-term industrial challenge posed by the China price in so many areas, ˇ°it will suffer a loss of economic power and influence.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1493"> Or, to put it another way, if Americans and Europeans want to benefit from the flattening of the world and the interconnecting of all the markets and knowledge centers, they will all have to run at least as fast as the fastest lion-and I suspect that lion will be China, and I suspect that will be pretty darn fast. </seg>
<seg id="1494"> Flattener #7: Supply-Chaining, Eating Sushi in Arkansas </seg>
<seg id="1495"> I had never seen what a supply chain looked like in action until I visited Wal-Mart headquarters in Bentonville, Arkansas. </seg>
<seg id="1496"> My WalMart hosts took me over to the 1.2-million-square-foot distribution center, where we climbed up to a viewing perch and watched the show. </seg>
<seg id="1497"> On one side of the building, scores of white WalMart trailer trucks were dropping off boxes of merchandise from thousands of different suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1498"> Boxes large and small were fed up a conveyor belt at each loading dock. </seg>
<seg id="1499"> These little conveyor belts fed into a bigger belt, like streams feeding into a powerful river. </seg>
<seg id="1500"> Twenty-four hours a day, seven days a week, the suppliers' trucks feed the twelve miles of conveyor streams, and the conveyor streams feed into a huge WalMart river of boxed products. </seg>
<seg id="1501"> But that is just half the show. </seg>
<seg id="1502"> As the WalMart river flows along, an electric eye reads the bar codes on each box on its way to the other side of the building. </seg>
<seg id="1503"> There, the river parts again into a hundred streams. </seg>
<seg id="1504"> Electric arms from each stream reach out and guide the boxes-ordered by particular Wal-Mart stores- off the main river and down its stream, where another conveyor belt sweeps them into a waiting Wal-Mart truck, which will rush these particular products onto the shelves of a particular Wal-Mart store somewhere in the country. </seg>
<seg id="1505"> There, a consumer will lift one of these products off the shelf, and the cashier will scan it in, and the moment that happens, a signal will be generated. </seg>
<seg id="1506"> That signal will go out across the Wal-Mart network to the supplier of that product-whether that supplier's factory is in coastal China or coastal Maine. </seg>
<seg id="1507"> That signal will pop up on the supplier's computer screen and prompt him to make another of that item and ship it via the Wal-Mart supply chain, and the whole cycle will start anew. </seg>
<seg id="1508"> So no sooner does your arm lift a product off the local Wal-Mart's shelf and onto the checkout counter than another mechanical arm starts making another one somewhere in the world. </seg>
<seg id="1509"> Call it ˇ°the Wal-Mart Symphonyˇ± in multiple movements-with no finale. </seg>
<seg id="1510"> It just plays over and over 24/7/365: delivery, sorting, packing, distribution, buying, manufacturing, reordering, delivery, sorting, packing... </seg>
<seg id="1511"> Just one company, Hewlett-Packard, will sell four hundred thousand computers through the four thousand Wal-Mart stores worldwide in one day during the Christmas season, which will require HP to adjust its supply chain, to make sure that all of its standards interface with Wal-Mart's, so that these computers flow smoothly into the Wal-Mart river, into the Wal-Mart streams, into the Wal-Mart stores. </seg>
<seg id="1512"> Wal-Mart's ability to bring off this symphony on a global scale-moving 2.3 billion general merchandise cartons a year down its supply chain into its stores-has made it the most important example of the next great flat-tener I want to discuss, which I call supply-chaining. </seg>
<seg id="1513"> Supply-chaining is a method of collaborating horizontally-among suppliers, retailers, and customers-to create value. </seg>
<seg id="1514"> Supply-chaining is both enabled by the flattening of the world and a hugely important flattener itself, because the more these supply chains grow and proliferate, the more they force the adoption of common standards between companies (so that every link of every supply chain can interface with the next), the more they eliminate points of friction at borders, the more the efficiencies of one company get adopted by the others, and the more they encourage global collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="1515"> As consumers, we love supply chains, because they deliver us all sorts of goods-from tennis shoes to laptop computers-at lower and lower prices. </seg>
<seg id="1516"> That is how Wal-Mart became the world's biggest retailer. </seg>
<seg id="1517"> But as workers, we are sometimes ambivalent or hostile to these supply chains, because they expose us to higher and higher pressures to compete, cut costs, and also, at times, cut wages and benefits. </seg>
<seg id="1518"> That is how Wal-Mart became one of the world's most controversial companies. </seg>
<seg id="1519"> No company has been more efficient at improving its supply chain (and thereby flattening the world) than Wal-Mart; </seg>
<seg id="1520"> and no company epitomizes the tension that supply chains evoke between the consumer in us and the worker in us than Wal-Mart. </seg>
<seg id="1521"> A September 30, 2002, article in Computer-world summed up Wal-Mart's pivotal role: ˇ°'Being a supplier to Wal-Mart is a two-edged sword,' says Joseph R. Eckroth Jr., CIO at Mattel Inc. 'They're a phenomenal channel but a tough customer. </seg>
<seg id="1522"> They demand excellence.' </seg>
<seg id="1523"> It's a lesson that the El Segundo, Calif.-based toy manufacturer and thousands of other suppliers learned as the world's largest retailer, Wal-Mart Stores Inc., built an inventory and supply chain man-agement system that changed the face of business. </seg>
<seg id="1524"> By investing early and heavily in cutting-edge technology to identify and track sales on the individual item level, the Bentonville, Ark.-based retail giant made its IT infrastructure a key competitive advantage that has been studied and copied by companies around the world. ' </seg>
<seg id="1525"> We view Wal-Mart as the best supply chain operator of all time/ says Pete Abell, retail research director at high-tech consultancy AMR Research Inc. in Boston.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1526"> In pursuit of the world's most efficient supply chain, Wal-Mart has piled up a list of business offenses over the years that has given the company several deserved black eyes and that it is belatedly starting to address in a meaningful way. </seg>
<seg id="1527"> But its role as one of the ten forces that flattened the world is undeniable, and it was to get a handle on this that I decided to make my own pilgrimage to Bentonville. </seg>
<seg id="1528"> I don't know why, but on the flight in from La Guardia, I was thinking, Boy, I would really like some sushi tonight. </seg>
<seg id="1529"> But where am I going to find sushi in northwest Arkansas? </seg>
<seg id="1530"> And even if I found it, would I want to eat it? </seg>
<seg id="1531"> Could you really trust the eel in Arkansas?When I arrived at the Hilton near Wal-Mart's headquarters, I was stunned to see, like a mirage, a huge Japanese steak house-sushi restaurant right next door. </seg>
<seg id="1532"> When I remarked to the desk clerk who was checking me in that I never expected to get my sushi fix in Bentonville, he told me, ˇ°We've got three more Japanese restaurants opening up soon.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1533"> Multiple Japanese restaurants in Bentonville? </seg>
<seg id="1534"> The demand for sushi in Arkansas is not an accident. It has to do with the fact that all around Wal-Mart's offices, vendors have set up their own operations to be close to the mother ship. </seg>
<seg id="1535"> Indeed, the area is known as ˇ°Vendorville.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1536"> The amazing thing about Wal-Mart's headquarters is that it is so, well, Wal-Mart. </seg>
<seg id="1537"> The corporate offices are crammed into a reconfigured warehouse. </seg>
<seg id="1538"> As we passed a large building made of corrugated metal, I figured it was the maintenance shed. </seg>
<seg id="1539"> ˇ°Those are our international offices,ˇ± said my host, spokesman William Wertz. </seg>
<seg id="1540"> The corporate suites are housed in offices that are one notch below those of the principal, vice principal, and head counselor at my daughter's public junior high school-before it was remodeled. </seg>
<seg id="1541"> When you pass through the lobby, you see these little cubicles where potential suppliers are pitching their products to Wal-Mart buyers. </seg>
<seg id="1542"> One has sewing machines all over the table, another has dolls, another has women's shirts. </seg>
<seg id="1543"> It feels like a cross between Sam's Club and the covered bazaar of Damascus. </seg>
<seg id="1544"> Attention Wal-Mart shareholders: The company is definitely not wasting your money on frills. </seg>
<seg id="1545"> But how did so much innovative thinking-thinking that has reshaped the world's business landscape in many ways-come out of such a Li'l Abner backwater? It is actually a classic example of a phenomenon I point to often in this book: the coefficient of flatness. </seg>
<seg id="1546"> The fewer natural resources your country or company has, the more you will dig inside yourself for innovations in order to survive. </seg>
<seg id="1547"> Wal-Mart became the biggest retailer in the world because it drove a hard bargain with everyone it came in contact with. </seg>
<seg id="1548"> But make no mistake about one thing: Wal-Mart also became number one because this little hick company from northwest Arkansas was smarter and faster about adopting new technology than any of its competitors. </seg>
<seg id="1549"> And it still is. </seg>
<seg id="1550"> David Glass, the company's CEO from 1988 to 2000, oversaw many of the innovations that made Wal-Mart the biggest and most profitable retailer on the planet. </seg>
<seg id="1551"> Fortune magazine once dubbed him ˇ°the most underrated CEO everˇ± for the quiet way he built on Sam Walton's vision. </seg>
<seg id="1552"> David Glass is to supply-chaining what Bill Gates is to word processing. </seg>
<seg id="1553"> When Wal-Mart was just getting started in northern Arkansas in the 1960s, explained Glass, it wanted to be a discounter. </seg>
<seg id="1554"> But in those days, every five-and-dime got its goods from the same wholesalers, so there was no way to get an edge on your competitors. </seg>
<seg id="1555"> But it wasn't efficient for manufacturers to ship to multiple Wal-Mart stores spread all over, so Wal-Mart set up a distribution center to which all the manufacturers could ship their merchandise, and then Wal-Mart got its own trucks to distribute these goods itself to its stores. </seg>
<seg id="1556"> The math worked like this: It cost roughly 3 percent more on average for Wal-Mart to maintain its own distribution center. </seg>
<seg id="1557"> But it turned out, said Glass, that cutting out the wholesalers and buying direct from the manufacturers saved on average 5 percent, so that allowed Wal-Mart to cut costs on average 2 percent and then make it up on volume. </seg>
<seg id="1558"> Once it established that basic method of buying directly from manufacturers to get the deepest discounts possible, Wal-Mart focused relentlessly on three things. </seg>
<seg id="1559"> The first was working with the manufacturers to get them to cut their costs as much as possible. </seg>
<seg id="1560"> The second was working on its supply chain from those manufacturers, wherever they were in the world, to Wal-Mart's distribution centers, to make it as low-cost and frictionless as possible. </seg>
<seg id="1561"> The third was constantly improving Wal-Mart's information systems, so it knew exactly what its customers were buying and could feed that information to all the manufacturers, so the shelves would always be stocked with the right items at the right time. </seg>
<seg id="1562"> Wal-Mart quickly realized that if it could save money by buying directly from the manufacturers, by constantly innovating to cut the cost of running its supply chain, and by keeping its inventories low by learning more about its customers, it could beat its competitors on price every time. </seg>
<seg id="1563"> Sitting in Bentonville, Arkansas, it didn't have much choice. </seg>
<seg id="1564"> ˇ°The reason we built all our own logistics and systems is because we are in the middle of nowhere,ˇ± said Jay Allen, Wal-Mart's senior vice president of corporate affairs. </seg>
<seg id="1565"> ˇ°It really was a small town. </seg>
<seg id="1566"> If you wanted to go to a third party for logistics, it was impossible. </seg>
<seg id="1567"> It was pure survival. </seg>
<seg id="1568"> Now with all the attention we are getting there is an assumption that our low prices derive from our size or because we're getting stuff from China or being able to dictate to suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1569"> The fact is the low prices are derived from efficiencies Wal-Mart has invested in-the system and the culture. </seg>
<seg id="1570"> It is a very low-cost culture.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1571"> Added Glass, ˇ°I wish that I could say we were brilliant and visionary, [but] it was all born out of necessity.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1572"> The more that supply chain grew, the more Walton and Glass understood that scale and efficiency were the keys to their whole business. </seg>
<seg id="1573"> Put simply, the more scale and scope their supply chain had, the more things they sold for less to more customers, the more leverage they had with suppliers to drive prices down even more, the more they sold to more customers, the more scale and scope their supply chain had, the more profit they reaped for their shareholders... </seg>
<seg id="1574"> Sam Walton was the father of that culture, but necessity was its mother, and its offspring has turned out to be a lean, mean supply-chain machine. </seg>
<seg id="1575"> In 2004, Wal-Mart purchased roughly $260 billion worth of merchandise and ran it through a supply chain consisting of 108 distribution centers around the United States, serving the some 3,000 Wal-Mart stores in America. </seg>
<seg id="1576"> In the early years, ˇ°we were small-we were 4 or 5 percent of Sears and Kmart,ˇ± said Glass. ˇ°If you are that small, you are vulnerable, so what we wanted to do more than anything else was grow market share. </seg>
<seg id="1577"> We had to undersell others. </seg>
<seg id="1578"> If I could reduce from 3 percent to 2 percent the cost of running my distribution centers, I could reduce retail prices and grow my market share and then not be vulnerable to anyone. So any efficiency we generated we passed on to the consumer.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1579"> For instance, after the manufacturers dropped off their goods at the Wal-Mart distribution center, Wal-Mart needed to deliver those goods in small bunches to each of its stores. It meant that Wal-Mart had trucks going all over America. </seg>
<seg id="1580"> Walton quickly realized if he connected his drivers by radios and satellites, after they dropped off at a certain Wal-Mart store, they could go a few miles down the road and pick up goods from a manufacturer so they wouldn't come back empty and so Wal-Mart could save the delivery charges from that manufacturer. </seg>
<seg id="1581"> A few pennies here, a few pennies there, and the result is more volume, scope, and scale. </seg>
<seg id="1582"> In improving its supply chain, Wal-Mart leaves no link untouched. </seg>
