<seg id="1583"> While I was touring the Wal-Mart distribution center in Bentonville, I noticed that some boxes were too big to go on the conveyor belts and were being moved around on pallets by Wal-Mart employees driving special minilift trucks with headphones on. </seg>
<seg id="1584"> A computer tracks how many pallets each employee is plucking every hour to put onto trucks for different stores, and a computerized voice tells each of them whether he is ahead of schedule or behind schedule. </seg>
<seg id="1585"> ˇ°You can choose whether you want your computer voice to be a man or a woman, and you can choose English or Spanish,ˇ± explained Rollin Ford, Wal-Mart's executive vice president, who oversees the supply chain and was giving me my tour. </seg>
<seg id="1586"> A few years ago, these pallet drivers would get written instructions for where to pluck a certain pallet and what truck to take it to, but Wal-Mart discovered that by giving them headphones with a soothing computer voice to instruct them, drivers could use both hands and not have to carry pieces of paper. </seg>
<seg id="1587"> And by having the voice constantly reminding them whether they were behind or ahead of expectations, ˇ°we got a boost in productivity,ˇ± said Ford. It is a million tiny operational innovations like this that differentiate Wal-Mart's supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="1588"> But the real breakthrough, said Glass, was when Wal-Mart realized that while it had to be a tough bargainer with its manufacturers on price, at the same time the two had to collaborate to create value for each other horizontally if Wal-Mart was going to keep driving down costs. </seg>
<seg id="1589"> Wal-Mart was one of the first companies to introduce computers to track store sales and inventory and was the first to develop a computerized network in order to share this information with suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1590"> Wal-Mart's theory was that the more information everyone had about what customers were pulling off the shelves, the more efficient Wal-Mart's buying would be, the quicker its suppliers could adapt to changing market demand. </seg>
<seg id="1591"> In 1983, Wal-Mart invested in point-of-sale terminals, which simultaneously rang up sales and tracked inventory deductions for rapid resupply. </seg>
<seg id="1592"> Four years later, it installed a large-scale satellite system linking all of the stores to company headquarters, giving Wal-Mart's central computer system real-time inventory data and paving the way for a supply chain greased by information and humming down to the last atom of efficiency. </seg>
<seg id="1593"> A major supplier can now tap into Wal-Mart's Retail Link private extranet system to see exactly how its products are selling and when it might need to up its production. </seg>
<seg id="1594"> ˇ°Opening its sales and inventory databases to suppliers is what made Wal-Mart the powerhouse it is today, says Rena Granofsky, a senior partner at J. C. Williams Group Ltd., a Toronto-based retail consulting firm,ˇ± in the 2002 Computerworld article on Wal-Mart. </seg>
<seg id="1595"> ˇ°While its competition guarded sales information, Wal-Mart approached its suppliers as if they were partners, not adversaries, says Granofsky. </seg>
<seg id="1596"> By implementing a collaborative planning, forecasting, and replenishment (CPFR) program, Wal-Mart began a just-in-time inventory program that reduced carrying costs for both the retailer and its suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1597"> 'There's a lot less excess inventory in the supply chain because of it/ Granofsky says.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1598"> Thanks to the efficiency of its supply chain alone, Wal-Mart's cost of goods is estimated to be 5 to 10 percent less than that of most of its competitors. </seg>
<seg id="1599"> Now Wal-Mart, in its latest supply-chain innovation, has introduced RFID-radio frequency identification microchips, attached to each pallet and merchandise box that comes into Wal-Mart, to replace bar codes, which have to be scanned individually and can get ripped or soiled. </seg>
<seg id="1600"> In June 2003, Wal-Mart informed its top one hundred suppliers that by January 1, 2005, all pallets and boxes that they ship to Wal-Mart distribution centers have to come equipped with RFID tags. </seg>
<seg id="1601"> (According to the RFID Journal, ˇ°RFID is a generic term for technologies that use radio waves to automatically identify people or objects. </seg>
<seg id="1602"> There are several methods of identification, but the most common is to store a serial number that identifies a person or object, and perhaps other information, on a microchip that is attached to an antenna-the chip and the antenna together are called an RFID transponder or an RFID tag. </seg>
<seg id="1603"> The antenna enables the chip to transmit the identification information to a reader. </seg>
<seg id="1604"> The reader converts the radio waves reflected back from the RFID tag into digital information that can then be passed on to computers that can make use of it.ˇ±) </seg>
<seg id="1605"> RFID will allow Wal-Mart to track any pallet or box at each stage in its supply chain and know exactly what product from which manufacturer is inside, with what expiration date. </seg>
<seg id="1606"> If a grocery item has to be stored at a certain temperature, the RFID tag will tell Wal-Mart when the temperature is too high or too low. </seg>
<seg id="1607"> Because each of these tags costs around 200, Wal-Mart is reserving them now for big boxes and pallets, not individual items. </seg>
<seg id="1608"> But this is clearly the wave of the future. </seg>
<seg id="1609"> ˇ°When you have RFID,ˇ± said Rollin Ford, the Wal-Mart logistics vice president, ˇ°you have more insights.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1610"> You can tell even faster which stores sell more of which shampoo on Fridays and which ones on Sundays, and whether Hispanics prefer to shop more on Saturday nights rather than Mondays in the stores in their neighborhoods. </seg>
<seg id="1611"> ˇ°When all this information is fed into our demand models, we can become more efficient on when we produce [a product] and when we ship it and then put it on the trucks in exactly the right place inside the trucks so it can flow more efficiently,ˇ± added Ford. </seg>
<seg id="1612"> ˇ°We used to have to count each piece, and scanning it at [the receiving end] was a bottleneck. </seg>
<seg id="1613"> Now [with RFID], we just scan the whole pallet under a bubble, and it says you have all thirty items you ordered and each box tells you, </seg>
<seg id="1614"> 'This is what I am and this is how I am feeling, this is what color I am, and am I in good shape'-so it makes receiving hugely easier.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1615"> Procter &amp; Gamble spokesperson Jeannie Tharrington talked to Salon.com (September 20, 2004) about Wal-Mart's move to RFID: ˇ°We see this as beneficial to the entire supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="1616"> Right now our out-of-stock levels are higher than we'd like and certainly higher than the consumer would like, and we think this technology can help us to keep the products on the shelf more often.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1617"> RFID will also allow for quicker remixing of the supply chain in response to events. </seg>
<seg id="1618"> During hurricanes, Wal-Mart officials told me, Wal-Mart knows that people eat more things like Pop-Tarts-easy-to-store, nonperishable items-and that their stores also sell a lot of kids' games that don't require electricity and can substitute for TV. </seg>
<seg id="1619"> It also knows that when hurricanes are coming, people tend to drink more beer. </seg>
<seg id="1620"> So the minute Wal-Mart's meteorologists tell headquarters a hurricane is bearing down on Florida, its supply chain automatically adjusts to a hurricane mix in the Florida stores-more beer early, more Pop-Tarts later. </seg>
<seg id="1621"> Wal-Mart is constantly looking for new ways to collaborate with its customers. Lately, it has gone into banking. </seg>
<seg id="1622"> It found that in areas with large Hispanic populations, many people had no affiliation with a bank and were getting ripped off by check-cashing outlets. </seg>
<seg id="1623"> So Wal-Mart offered them payroll check cashing, money orders, money transfers, and even bill payment services for standard items like electricity bills-all for very small fees. </seg>
<seg id="1624"> Wal-Mart had an internal capability to do that for its own employees and simply turned it into an external business. </seg>
<seg id="1625"> Unfortunately for Wal-Mart, the same factors that drove its instinct for constant innovation-its isolation from the world, its need to dig inside itself, and its need to connect remote locations to a global supply chain- also got it in trouble. </seg>
<seg id="1626"> It is hard to exaggerate how isolated Bentonville, Arkansas, is from the currents of global debate on labor and human rights, and it is easy to see how this insular company, obsessed with lowering prices, could have gone over the edge in some of its practices. </seg>
<seg id="1627"> Sam Walton bred not only a kind of ruthless quest for efficiency in improving Wal-Mart's supply chain but also a degree of ruthlessness period. </seg>
<seg id="1628"> I am talking about everything from Wal-Mart's recently exposed practice of locking overnight workers into its stores, to its allowing Wal-Mart's maintenance contractors to use illegal immigrants as janitors, to its role as defendant in the largest civil-rights class-action lawsuit in history, to its refusal to stock certain magazines-like Playboy-on its shelves, even in small towns where Wal-Mart is the only major store. </seg>
<seg id="1629"> This is all aside from the fact that some of Wal-Mart's biggest competitors complain that they have had to cut health-care benefits and create a lower wage tier to compete with Wal-Mart, which pays less and covers less than most big companies (more on this later). </seg>
<seg id="1630"> One can only hope that all the bad publicity Wal-Mart has received in the last few years will force it to understand that there is a fine line between a hyperefficient global supply chain that is helping people save money and improve their lives and one that has pursued cost cutting and profit margins to such a degree that whatever social benefits it is offering with one hand, it is taking away with the other. </seg>
<seg id="1631"> It has so much leverage that it can grind down any supplier to the last halfpenny. </seg>
<seg id="1632"> And it is not at all hesitant about using its ability to play its foreign and domestic suppliers off against each other. </seg>
<seg id="1633"> Some suppliers have found ways to flourish under the pressure and become better at what they do. </seg>
<seg id="1634"> If all of Wal-Mart's suppliers were being squeezed dry by Wal-Mart, Wal-Mart would have no suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1635"> So obviously many of them are thriving as Wal-Mart's partners. </seg>
<seg id="1636"> But some no doubt have translated Wal-Mart's incessant price pressure into lower wages and benefits for their employees or watched as their business moved to China, whence Wal-Mart's supply chain pulled in $18 billion worth of goods in 2004 from five thousand Chinese suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1637"> ˇ°If Wal-Mart were an individual economy, it would rank as China's eighth-biggest trading partner, ahead of Russia, Australia and Canada,ˇ± Xu Jun, the spokesman for Wal-Mart China, told the China Business Weekly (November 29, 2004). </seg>
<seg id="1638"> The successor generation to Sam Walton's leadership seems to recognize that it has both an image and a reality to fix. </seg>
<seg id="1639"> How far Wal-Mart will adjust remains to be seen. </seg>
<seg id="1640"> But when I asked Wal-Mart's CEO, H. Lee Scott Jr., directly about all these issues, he did not duck. </seg>
<seg id="1641"> In fact, he wanted to talk about it. </seg>
<seg id="1642"> ˇ°What I think I have to do is institutionalize this sense of obligation to society to the same extent that we have institutionalized the commitment to the customer,ˇ± said Scott. </seg>
<seg id="1643"> ˇ°The world has changed and we have missed that. </seg>
<seg id="1644"> We believed that good intentions and good stores and good prices would cause people to forgive what we are not as good at, and we were wrong.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1645"> In certain areas, he added, ˇ°we are not as good as we should be. </seg>
<seg id="1646"> We just have to get better.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1647"> One trend that Wal-Mart insists it is not responsible for is the off-shoring of manufacturing. </seg>
<seg id="1648"> ˇ°We are much better off if we can buy merchandise made in the United States,ˇ± said Glass. </seg>
<seg id="1649"> ˇ°I spent two years going around this country trying to talk people into manufacturing here. </seg>
<seg id="1650"> We would pay more to buy it here because the manufacturing facilities in those towns [would create jobs for] all those people who shopped in our stores. </seg>
<seg id="1651"> Sanyo had a plant here [in Arkansas] making television sets for Sears, and Sears cut them off, so they decided they were closing the plant and going to move part to Mexico and part to Asia. </seg>
<seg id="1652"> Our governor asked if we would help. </seg>
<seg id="1653"> We decided we would buy television sets from Sanyo [if they would keep the plant in Arkansas], and they didn't want to do it. </seg>
<seg id="1654"> They wanted to move it, and [the governor] even talked to the [Japanese owning] family to try to persuade them to stay. </seg>
<seg id="1655"> Between his efforts and ours, we persuaded them to do it. </seg>
<seg id="1656"> They are now the world's largest producer of televisions. </seg>
<seg id="1657"> We just bought our fifty millionth set from them. </seg>
<seg id="1658"> But for the most part people in this country have just abandoned the manufacturing process. They say, 'I want to sell to you, but I don't want the responsibility for the buildings and employees [and health care]. </seg>
<seg id="1659"> So we were forced to source merchandise in other places in the world.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1660"> He added, ˇ°One of my concerns is that, with the manufacturing out of this country, one day we'll all be selling hamburgers to each other.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1661"> The best way to get a taste of Wal-Mart's power as a global flattener is to visit Japan. </seg>
<seg id="1662"> Commodore Matthew Calbraith Perry opened a largely closed Japanese society to the Western world on July 8, 1853, when he arrived in Edo (Tokyo) Bay with four big black steamships bristling with guns. </seg>
<seg id="1663"> According to the Naval Historical Center Web site, the Japanese, not knowing that steamships even existed, were shocked by the sight of them and thought they were ˇ°giant dragons puffing smoke.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1664"> Commodore Perry returned a year later, and on March 31, 1854, concluded the Treaty of Kanagawa with the Japanese authorities, gaining U.S. vessels access to the ports of Shimoda and Hakodate and opening a U.S. consulate in Shimoda. </seg>
<seg id="1665"> This treaty led to an explosion of trade between Japan and the United States, helped open Japan to the Western world generally, and is widely credited with triggering the modernization of the Japanese state, as the Japanese realized how far behind they were and rushed to catch up. </seg>
<seg id="1666"> And catch up they did. </seg>
<seg id="1667"> In so many areas, from automobiles to consumer electronics to machine tools, from the Sony Walkman to the Lexus, the Japanese learned every lesson they could from Western nations and then proceeded to beat us at our own game-except one: retailing, especially discount retailing. </seg>
<seg id="1668"> Japan could make those Sonys like nobody else, but when it came to selling them at a discount, well, that was another matter. </seg>
<seg id="1669"> So almost exactly 150 years after Commodore Perry signed that treaty, another lesser-known treaty was signed, actually a business partnership. Call itthe Seiyu-Wal-Mart Treaty of 2003. </seg>
<seg id="1670"> Unlike Commodore Perry, Wal-Mart did not have to muscle its way into Japan with warships. Its reputation preceded it, which is why it was invited in by Seiyu, a struggling Japanese retail chain desperate to adapt the Wal-Mart formula in Japan, a country notorious for resisting big-box discount stores. </seg>
<seg id="1671"> As I traveled on the bullet train from Tokyo to Numazu, Japan, site of the first Seiyu store that was using the Wal-Mart methods, the New York Times translator pointed out that this store was located about one hundred miles from Shimoda and that first U.S. consulate. </seg>
<seg id="1672"> Commodore Perry probably would have loved shopping in the new Seiyu store, where all the music piped in consists of Western tunes designed to lull shoppers into filling their carts, and where you can buy a man's suit-made in China-for $65 and a white shirt to go with it for $5. </seg>
<seg id="1673"> That's what they call around Wal-Mart EDLP-Every Day Low Prices-and it was one of the first phrases Wal-Mart folks learned to say in Japanese. </seg>
<seg id="1674"> Wal-Mart's flattening effects are fully on display in the Seiyu store in Numazu-not just the everyday low prices, but the wide aisles, the big pallets of household goods, the huge signs displaying the lowest prices in each category, and the Wal-Mart supply-chain computer system so that store managers can quickly adjust stock.I asked Seiyu's CEO, Masao Kiuchi, why he had turned to Wal-Mart. </seg>
<seg id="1675"> ˇ°The first time I knew about Wal-Mart was about fifteen years ago,ˇ± explained Kiuchi. </seg>
<seg id="1676"> ˇ°I went to Dallas to see the Wal-Mart stores, and I thought this was a very rational method. </seg>
<seg id="1677"> It was two things: One was the signage showing the prices. </seg>
<seg id="1678"> It was very easy for us to understand.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1679"> The second, he said, was that the Japanese thought a discount store meant that you sold cheap products at cheap prices. </seg>
<seg id="1680"> What he realized from shopping at Wal-Mart, and seeing everything from plasma TVs to top-brand pet products, was that Wal-Mart sold quality products at low prices. </seg>
<seg id="1681"> ˇ°At the store in Dallas, I took pictures, and I brought those pictures to my colleagues in Seiyu and said, 'Look, we have to see what Wal-Mart is doing on the other side of the planet' </seg>
<seg id="1682"> But showing pictures was not good enough, because how can you understand by just looking at pictures?ˇ± recalled Kiuchi. </seg>
<seg id="1683"> Eventually, Kiuchi approached Wal-Mart, and they signed a partnership on December 31, 2003. </seg>
<seg id="1684"> Wal-Mart bought a piece of Seiyu; </seg>
<seg id="1685"> in return, Wal-Mart agreed to teach Seiyu its unique form of collaboration: global supply-chaining to bring consumers the best goods at the lowest prices. </seg>
<seg id="1686"> There was one big thing, though, that Seiyu had to teach Wal-Mart, Kiuchi told me: how to sell raw fish. </seg>
<seg id="1687"> Japanese discounters and department stores all have grocery sections, and they all carry fish for very discriminating Japanese consumers. </seg>
<seg id="1688"> Seiyu will discount fish several times during each day, as the freshness declines. </seg>
<seg id="1689"> Give Wal-Mart time. </seg>
<seg id="1690"> I expect that in the not-too-distant future we will see Wal-Mart sushi. </seg>
<seg id="1691"> Somebody had better warn the tuna. </seg>
<seg id="1692"> Flattener #8: Insourcing, What the Guys in Funny Brown Shorts Are Really Doing </seg>
<seg id="1693"> One of the most enjoyable things about researching this book has been discovering all sorts of things happening in the world around me of which I had no clue. </seg>
<seg id="1694"> Nothing was more surprisingly interesting than pulling the curtain back on UPS, United Parcel Service. </seg>
<seg id="1695"> Yes, those folks, the ones who wear the homely brown shorts and drive those ugly brown trucks. </seg>
<seg id="1696"> Turns out that while I was sleeping, stodgy old UPS became a huge force flattening the world. </seg>
<seg id="1697"> Once again, it was one of my Indian tutors, Nandan Nilekani, the Infosys CEO, who tipped me off to this. </seg>
<seg id="1698"> ˇ°FedEx and UPS should be one of your flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="1699"> They're not just delivering packages, they are doing logistics,ˇ± he told me on the phone from Bangalore one day. </seg>
<seg id="1700"> Naturally, I filed the thought away, making a note to check it out, without having any clue what he was getting at. </seg>
<seg id="1701"> A few months later I went to China, and while there I was afflicted with jet lag one night and was watching CNN International to pass the wee hours of the morning. </seg>
<seg id="1702"> At one point, a commercial came on for UPS, and its tag line was UPS's new slogan: ˇ°Your World Synchronized.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1703"> The thought occurred to me: </seg>
<seg id="1704"> That must be what Nandan was talking about! </seg>
<seg id="1705"> UPS, I learned, was not just delivering packages anymore; </seg>
<seg id="1706"> it was synchronizing global supply chains for companies large and small. </seg>
<seg id="1707"> The next day I made an appointment to visit UPS headquarters in Atlanta. </seg>
<seg id="1708"> I later toured the UPS Worldport distribution hub adjacent to the Louisville International Airport, which at night is basically taken over by the UPS fleet of cargo jets, as packages are flown in from all over the world, sorted, and flown back out again a few hours later. </seg>
<seg id="1709"> (The UPS fleet of 270 aircraft is the eleventh largest airline in the world.) </seg>
<seg id="1710"> What I discovered on these visits was that this is not your father's UPS. </seg>
<seg id="1711"> Yes, UPS still pulls in most of its $36 billion in sales by shipping more than 13.5 million packages a day from point A to point B. </seg>
<seg id="1712"> But behind that innocuous facade, the company founded in Seattle in 1907 as a messenger service has reinvented itself as a dynamic supply-chain manager. </seg>
<seg id="1713"> Consider this: </seg>
<seg id="1714"> If you own a Toshiba laptop computer that is under warranty and it breaks and you call Toshiba to have it repaired, Toshiba will tell you to drop it off at a UPS store and have it shipped it to Toshiba, and it will get repaired and then be shipped back to you. </seg>
<seg id="1715"> But here's what they don't tell you: </seg>
<seg id="1716"> UPS doesn't just pick up and deliver your Toshiba laptop. UPS actually repairs the computer in its own UPS-run workshop dedicated to computer and printer repairs at its Louisville hub. I went to tour that hub expecting to see only packages moving around, </seg>
<seg id="1717"> and instead I found myself dressed in a blue smock, in a special clean room, watching UPS employees replacing motherboards in broken Toshiba laptops. </seg>
<seg id="1718"> Toshiba had developed an image problem several years ago, with some customers concluding that its repair process for broken machines took too long. </seg>
<seg id="1719"> So Toshiba came to UPS and asked it to design a better system. </seg>
<seg id="1720"> UPS said, ˇ°let's cut out all the middle steps. </seg>
<seg id="1721"> We, UPS, will pick it up, repair it ourselves, and send it right back to your customer.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1722"> It is now possible to send your Toshiba laptop in one day, get it repaired the next, and have it back the third day. </seg>
<seg id="1723"> The UPS repairmen and -women were all certified by Toshiba, and its customer complaints went down dramatically. </seg>
<seg id="1724"> In addition, by making the delivery of goods and services around the world superefficient and superfast-and in huge volumes-UPS is helping to level customs barriers and harmonize trade by getting more and more people to adopt the same rules and labels and tracking systems for transporting goods. </seg>
<seg id="1725"> UPS has a smart label on all its packages so that package can be tracked and traced anywhere in its network. </seg>
<seg id="1726"> Working with the U.S. Customs Service, UPS designed a software program that allows customs to say to UPS, ˇ°I want to see any package moving through your Worldport hub that was sent from Cali, Colombia, to Miami by someone named Carlos.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1727"> Or, ˇ°I want to see any package sent from Germany to the United States by someone named Osama.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1728"> When the package arrives for sorting, the UPS computers will then automatically route that package to a customs officer in the UPS hub. </seg>
<seg id="1729"> A computerized arm will literally slide it off the conveyor belt and dump it into a bin for a closer look. </seg>
<seg id="1730"> It makes the inspection process more efficient and does not interrupt the general flow of packages. </seg>
<seg id="1731"> These efficiencies of time and scale save UPS's clients money, enabling them to recycle their capital and fund more innovation. </seg>
<seg id="1732"> But the level of collaboration it requires between UPS and its clients is unusual. </seg>
<seg id="1733"> Plow &amp; Hearth is a large national catalog and Internet retailer specializing in ˇ°Products for Country Living.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1734"> P&amp;H came to UPS one day and said that too many of its furniture deliveries were coming to customers with a piece broken. </seg>
<seg id="1735"> Did UPS have any ideas? </seg>
<seg id="1736"> UPS sent its ˇ°package engineersˇ± over and conducted a packaging seminar for the P&amp;H procurement group. </seg>
<seg id="1737"> UPS also provided guidelines for them to use in the selection of their suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1738"> The objective was to help P&amp;H understand that its purchase decisions from its suppliers should be influenced not only by the quality of the products being offered but also by how those products were being packaged and delivered. </seg>
<seg id="1739"> UPS couldn't help its customer P&amp;H without looking deep inside its business and then into its suppliers' businesses-what boxes and packing materials they were using. </seg>
<seg id="1740"> That is insourcing. </seg>
<seg id="1741"> Consider the collaboration today among eBay sellers, UPS, PayPal, and eBay buyers. Say I offer to sell a golf club on eBay and you decide to buy it. </seg>
<seg id="1742"> I e-mail you a PayPal invoice, which has your name and mailing address on it. At the same time, eBay offers me an icon on its Web site to print out a UPS mailing label to you. </seg>
<seg id="1743"> When I print that mailing label on my own printer, it comes out with a UPS tracking bar code on it. </seg>
<seg id="1744"> At the same time, UPS, through its computer system, creates a tracking number that corresponds to that label, which automatically gets e-mailed to you-the person who bought my golf club-so you can track the package by yourself, online, on a regular basis and know exactly when it will reach you. </seg>
<seg id="1745"> If UPS had not gone into this business, someone would have had to invent it. With so many more people working through horizontal global supply chains far from home, somebody had to fill in the inevitable holes and tighten the weak links. </seg>
<seg id="1746"> Said Kurt Kuehn, UPS's senior vice president for sales and marketing, ˇ°The Texas machine parts guy is worried that the customer in Malaysia is a credit risk. </seg>
<seg id="1747"> We step in as a trusted broker. </seg>
<seg id="1748"> If we have control of that package, we can collect funds subject to acceptance and eliminate letters of credit. </seg>
<seg id="1749"> Trust can be created through personal relations or through systems and controls. </seg>
<seg id="1750"> If you don't have trust, you can rely on a shipper who does not turn [your package] over until he is paid. </seg>
<seg id="1751"> We have more ability than a bank to manage this, because we have the package and the ongoing relationship with the customer as collateral, so we have two points of leverage.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1752"> More than sixty companies have moved operations closer to the UPS hub in Louisville since 1997, so they can make things and ship them straight from the hub, without having to warehouse them. </seg>
<seg id="1753"> But it is not just the little guys who benefit from the better logistics and more efficient supply chains that insourcing can provide. </seg>
<seg id="1754"> In 2001, Ford Motor Co. turned over its snarled and slow distribution network to UPS, allowing UPS to come deep inside Ford to figure out what its problems were and smooth out its supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="1755"> ˇ°For years, the bane of most Ford dealers was the auto maker's Rube Goldberg-like system for getting cars from factory to showroom,ˇ± BusinessWeek reported in its July 19, 2004, issue. </seg>
<seg id="1756"> ˇ°Cars could take as long as a month to arrive-that is, when they weren't lost along the way. </seg>
<seg id="1757"> And Ford Motor Co. was not always able to tell its dealers exactly what was coming, or even what was in inventory at the nearest rail yards. 'We'd lose track of whole trainloads of cars,' recalls Jerry Reynolds, owner of Prestige Ford in Garland, Tex. </seg>
<seg id="1758"> 'It was crazy.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1759"> But after UPS got under Ford's hood, ˇ°UPS engineers... redesigned Ford's entire North American delivery network, streamlining everything from the route cars take from the factory to how they're processed at regional sorting hubsˇ±- including pasting bar codes on the windshields of the 4 million cars coming out of Ford's U.S. plants so they could be tracked just like packages. </seg>
<seg id="1760"> As a result, UPS cut the time it takes autos to arrive at dealer lots by 40 percent, to ten days on average. </seg>
<seg id="1761"> BusinessWeek reported: ˇ°That saves Ford millions in working capital each year and makes it easy for its 6,500 dealers to track down the models most in demand... ' </seg>
<seg id="1762"> It was the most amazing transformation I had ever seen,' marvels Reynolds. </seg>
<seg id="1763"> 'My last comment to UPS was: 'Can you get us spare parts like this?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1764"> UPS maintains a think tank, the Operations Research Division, in Timonium, Maryland, which works on supply-chain algorithms. </seg>
<seg id="1765"> This ˇ°schoolˇ± of mathematics is called ˇ°package flow technology,ˇ± and it is designed to constantly match the deployment of UPS trucks, ships, airplanes, and sorting capabilities with that day's flow of packages around the world. </seg>
<seg id="1766"> ˇ°Now we can make changes in our network in hours to adjust to changes in volume,ˇ± says UPS CEO Eskew. </seg>
<seg id="1767"> ˇ°How I optimize the total supply chain is the key to the math.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1768"> The sixty-person UPS team in Timonium is made up largely of people with engineering and math degrees, including several Ph.D.'s. </seg>
<seg id="1769"> UPS also employs its own meteorologists and strategic threat analysts to track which atmospheric or geopolitical thunderstorms it will have to work around on any given day. </seg>
<seg id="1770"> To further grease its supply chains, UPS is the largest private user of wireless technology in the world, as its drivers alone make over 1 million phone calls a day in the process of picking up and delivering packages through its eighty-eight thousand package cars, vans, tractors, and motorcycles. </seg>
<seg id="1771"> On any given day, according to UPS, 2 percent of the world's GDP can be found in UPS delivery trucks or package cars. </seg>
<seg id="1772"> Oh, and did I mention that UPS also has a financing arm-UPS Capital-that will put up the money for the transformation of your supply chain, particularly if you are a small business and don't have the capital. </seg>
<seg id="1773"> For example, notes Eskew, UPS was doing business with a small biotech company in Canada that sold blood adhesives, a highly perishable alternative to stitches. </seg>
<seg id="1774"> The company had a growing market among the major hospital chains, but it had a problem keeping up with demand and could not get financing. </seg>
<seg id="1775"> It had distribution centers on the East and West coasts. </seg>
<seg id="1776"> UPS redesigned the company's system based around a refrigerator hub in Dallas and extended it financing through UPS Capital. </seg>
<seg id="1777"> The result, said Eskew, was less inventory, better cash flow, better customer service-and an embedded customer for UPS. </seg>
<seg id="1778"> A maker of bridal headpieces and veils in Montreal wanted to improve its flow of business with the U.S. Eskew recalled, ˇ°We designed a system for consolidated [customs] clearances, so their veils and headpieces would not have to come over [the border] one by one. And then we put [the merchandise] in a warehouse in [upstate] New York. </seg>
<seg id="1779"> We took the orders by Internet, we put the labels on, we delivered the packages and collected the money, and we put that money through UPS Capital into their banks electronically so they had the cash back. </seg>
<seg id="1780"> That allows them to enter new markets and minimize their inventory.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1781"> Eskew explained, ˇ°When our grandfathers owned shops, inventory was what was in the back room. </seg>
<seg id="1782"> Now it is a box two hours away on a package car, or it might be hundreds more crossing the country by rail or jet, and you have thousands more crossing the ocean. </seg>
<seg id="1783"> And because we all have visibility into that supply chain, we can coordinate all those modes of transportation.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1784"> Indeed, as consumers have become more empowered to pull their own products via the Internet and customize them for themselves, UPS has found itself in the interesting position of being not only the company actually taking the orders but also, as the delivery service, the one handing the goods to the buyer at the front door. </seg>
<seg id="1785"> As a result, companies said, ˇ°Let's try to push as many differentiating things to the end of the supply chain, rather than the beginning.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1786"> And because UPS was the last link in the supply chain before these goods were loaded onto planes, trains, and trucks, it took over many of these functions, creating a whole new business called End of Runway Services. </seg>
<seg id="1787"> The day I visited Louisville, two young UPS women were putting together Nikon cameras, with special memory cards and leather cases, which some store had offered as a weekend special. </seg>
<seg id="1788"> They were even putting them in special boxes just for that store. </seg>
<seg id="1789"> By taking over this function, UPS gives companies more options to customize products at the last minute. </seg>
<seg id="1790"> UPS has also taken full advantage of the Netscape and work flow flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="1791"> Before 1995, all tracking and tracing of UPS packages for customers was done through a call center. You called a UPS 800 number and asked an operator where your package was. </seg>
<seg id="1792"> During the week before Christmas, UPS operators were fielding six hundred thousand calls on the peak days. Each one of those calls cost UPS $2.10 to handle. </seg>
<seg id="1793"> Then, through the 1990s, as more and more UPS customers became empowered and comfortable with the Internet, and as its own tracking and tracing system improved with advances in wireless technology, UPS invited its customers to track packages themselves over the Internet, at a cost to UPS of between 5(2 and 100 a query. </seg>
<seg id="1794"> ˇ°So we dramatically reduced our service costs and increased service,ˇ± said UPS vice president Ken Sternad, especially since UPS now pulls in 7 million tracking requests on an average day and a staggering 12 million on peak days. </seg>
<seg id="1795"> At the same time, its drivers also became more empowered with their DIADs -driver delivery information acquisition devices. </seg>
<seg id="1796"> These are the brown electronic clipboards that you always see the UPS drivers carrying around. </seg>
<seg id="1797"> The latest generation of them tells each driver where in his truck to load each package-exactly what position on the shelf. </seg>
<seg id="1798"> It also tells him where his next stop is, and if he goes to the wrong address, the GPS system built into the DIAD won't allow him to deliver the package. </seg>
<seg id="1799"> It also allows Mom to go online and find out when the driver will be in her neighborhood dropping off her package. </seg>
<seg id="1800"> Insourcing is distinct from supply-chaining because it goes well beyond supply-chain management. </seg>
<seg id="1801"> Because it is third-party-managed logistics, it requires a much more intimate and extensive kind of collaboration among UPS and its clients and its clients' clients. </seg>
<seg id="1802"> The UPS people are not just synchronizing your packages- they are synchronizing your whole company and its interaction with both customers and suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="1803"> ˇ°This is no longer a vendor-customer relationship,ˇ± said Eskew. </seg>
<seg id="1804"> ˇ°We answer your phones, we talk to your customers, we house your inventory, and we tell you what sells and doesn't sell. </seg>
<seg id="1805"> We have access to your information and you have to trust us. </seg>
<seg id="1806"> We manage competitors, and the only way for this to work, as our founders told Gimbel's and Macy's, is 'trust us.' </seg>
<seg id="1807"> I won't violate that. </seg>
<seg id="1808"> Because we are asking people to let go of part of their business, and that really requires trust.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1809"> UPS is creating enabling platforms for anyone to take his or her business global or to vastly improve the efficiency of his or her global supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="1810"> It is a totally new business, but UPS is convinced it has an almost limitless upside. </seg>
<seg id="1811"> Time will tell. </seg>
<seg id="1812"> Though margins are still thin in this kind of work, in 2003 alone insourcing pulled in $2.4 billion in revenues for UPS. </seg>
<seg id="1813"> My gut tells me the folks in the funny brown shorts and funny brown trucks are on to something big-something made possible only by the flattening of the world and something that is going to flatten it a lot more. </seg>
<seg id="1814"> Flattener #9: In-forming, Google, Yahoo!, MSN Web Search </seg>
<seg id="1815"> My friend and I met a guy at a restaurant. </seg>
<seg id="1816"> My friend was very taken with him, but I was suspiciously curious about this guy. </seg>
<seg id="1817"> After a few minutes of Googling, I found out that he was arrested for felony assault. </seg>
<seg id="1818"> Although I was once again disappointed with the quality of the dating pool, I was at least able to warn my friend about this guy's violent past. </seg>
<seg id="1819"> ˇŞTestimonial from Google user </seg>
<seg id="1820"> I am completely delighted with the translation service. </seg>
<seg id="1821"> My partner arranged for two laborers to come and help with some demolition. </seg>
<seg id="1822"> There was a miscommunication: she asked for the workers to come at 11 am, and the labor service sent them at 8:30. </seg>
<seg id="1823"> They speak only Spanish, and I speak English and some French. </seg>
<seg id="1824"> Our Hispanic neighbors were out. </seg>
<seg id="1825"> With the help of the translation service, I was able to communicate with the workers, to apologize for the miscommunication, establish the expectation, and ask them to come back at 11. </seg>
<seg id="1826"> Thank you for providing this connection... </seg>
<seg id="1827"> Thank you Google. </seg>
<seg id="1828"> I just want to thank Google for teaching me how to find love. </seg>
<seg id="1829"> While looking for my estranged brother, I stumbled across a Mexican Web site for male strippers-and I was shocked. </seg>
<seg id="1830"> My brother was working as a male prostitute! </seg>
<seg id="1831"> The first chance I got, I flew to the city he was working in to liberate him from this degrading profession. </seg>
<seg id="1832"> But more than that, I met one of his co-workers... </seg>
<seg id="1833"> We got married last weekend [in Mexico], and I am positive without Google's services, I never would have found my brother, my husband, or the surprisingly lucrative nature of the male stripping industry in Mexico!! </seg>
<seg id="1834"> Thank you, Google! </seg>
<seg id="1835"> Google headquarters in Mountain View, California, has a certain Epcot Center feel to it-so many fun space age toys to play with, so little time. </seg>
<seg id="1836"> In one corner is a spinning globe that emits light beams based on the volume of people searching on Google. </seg>
<seg id="1837"> As you would expect, most of the shafts of light are shooting up from North America, Europe, Korea, Japan, and coastal China. </seg>
<seg id="1838"> The Middle East and Africa remain pretty dark. </seg>
<seg id="1839"> In another corner is a screen that shows a sample of what things people are searching for at that moment, all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="1840"> When I was there in 2001, I asked my hosts what had been the most frequent searches lately. </seg>
<seg id="1841"> One, of course, was ˇ°sex,ˇ± a perennial favorite of Googlers. </seg>
<seg id="1842"> Another was ˇ°God.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1843"> A third was ˇ°jobsˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1844"> And the fourth most searched item around the time of my visit? I didn't know whether to laugh or cry: ˇ°professional wrestling.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1845"> The weirdest one, though, is the Google recipe book, where people just open their refrigerators, see what ingredients are inside, type three of them into Google, and see what recipes come up! </seg>
<seg id="1846"> Fortunately, no single word or subject accounts for more than 1 or 2 percent of all Google searches at any given time, so no one should get too worried about the fate of humanity on the basis of Google's top search items on any particular day. </seg>
<seg id="1847"> Indeed, it is the remarkable diversity of searches going on via Google, in so many different tongues, that makes the Google search engine (and search engines in general) such huge flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="1848"> Never before in the history of the planet have so many people-on their own-had the ability to find so much information about so many things and about so many other people. </seg>
<seg id="1849"> Said Google cofounder Russian-born Sergey Brin, ˇ°If someone has broadband, dial-up, or access to an Internet cafe, whether a kid in Cambodia, the university professor, or me who runs this search engine, all have the same basic access to overall research information that anyone has. </seg>
<seg id="1850"> It is a total equalizer. </seg>
<seg id="1851"> This is very different than how I grew up. </seg>
<seg id="1852"> My best access was some library, and it did not have all that much stuff, and you either had to hope for a miracle or search for something very simple or something very recent.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1853"> When Google came along, he added, suddenly that kid had ˇ°universal accessˇ± to the information in libraries all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="1854"> That is certainly Google's goal-to make easily available all the world's knowledge in every language. </seg>
<seg id="1855"> And Google hopes that in time, with a PalmPilot or a cell phone, everyone everywhere will be able to carry around access to all the world's knowledge in their pockets. </seg>
<seg id="1856"> ˇ°Everythingˇ± and ˇ°everyoneˇ± are key words that you hear around Google all the time. </seg>
<seg id="1857"> Indeed, the official Google history carried on its home page notes that the name ˇ°Googleˇ± is a play on the word ˇ°'googol,' which is the number represented by the numeral I followed by 100 zeros. </seg>
<seg id="1858"> Google's use of the term reflects the company's mission to organize the immense, seemingly infinite amount of information available on the Web,ˇ± just for you. </seg>
<seg id="1859"> What Google's success reflects is how much people are interested in having just that-all the world's knowledge at their fingertips. </seg>
<seg id="1860"> There is no bigger flattener than the idea of making all the world's knowledge, or even just a big chunk of it, available to anyone and everyone, anytime, anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="1861"> ˇ°We do discriminate only to the degree that if you can't use a computer or don't have access to one, you can't use Google, but other than that, if you can type, you can use Google,ˇ± said Google CEO Eric Schmidt. </seg>
<seg id="1862"> And surely if the flattening of the world means anything, he added, it means that ˇ°there is no discrimination in accessing knowledge. </seg>
<seg id="1863"> Google is now searchable in one hundred languages, and every time we find another we increase it. </seg>
<seg id="1864"> Let's imagine a group with a Google iPod one day and you can tell it to search by voice-that would take care of people who can't use a computer-and then [Google access] just becomes about the rate at which we can get cheap devices into people's hands.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1865"> How does searching fit into the concept of collaboration? </seg>
<seg id="1866"> I call it ˇ°in-forming.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1867"> In-forming is the individual's personal analog to open-sourcing, outsourcing, insourcing, supply-chaining, and offshoring. </seg>
<seg id="1868"> In-forming is the ability to build and deploy your own personal supply chain-a supply chain of information, knowledge, and entertainment. </seg>
<seg id="1869"> In-forming is about self-collaboration-becoming your own self-directed and self-empowered researcher, editor, and selector of entertainment, without having to go to the library or the movie theater or through network television. </seg>
<seg id="1870"> Informing is searching for knowledge. </seg>
<seg id="1871"> It is about seeking like-minded people and communities. </seg>
<seg id="1872"> Google's phenomenal global popularity, which has spurred Yahoo! and Microsoft (through its new MSN Search) also to make power searching and in-forming prominent features of their Web sites, shows how hungry people are for this form of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="1873"> The easier and more accurate searching becomes, added Larry Page, Google's other cofounder, the more global Google's user base becomes, and the more powerful a flattener it becomes. </seg>
<seg id="1874"> Every day more and more people are able to in-form themselves in their own language. </seg>
<seg id="1875"> Today, said Page, ˇ°only a third of our searches are U.S.-based, and less than half are in English.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1876"> Moreover, he added, ˇ°as people are searching for more obscure things, people are publishing more obscure things,ˇ± which drives the flattening effect of in-forming even more. </seg>
<seg id="1877"> All the major search engines have also recently added the capability for users to search not only the Web for information but also their own computer's hard drive for words or data or e-mail they know is in there somewhere but have forgotten where. </seg>
<seg id="1878"> When you can search your own memory more efficiently, that is really in-forming. </seg>
<seg id="1879"> In late 2004, Google announced plans to scan the entire contents of both the University of Michigan and Stanford University Libraries, making tens of thousands of books available and searchable online. </seg>
<seg id="1880"> In the earliest days of search engines, people were amazed and delighted to stumble across the information they sought; eureka moments were unexpected surprises, said Yahool's cofounder Jerry Yang. </seg>
<seg id="1881"> ˇ°Today their attitudes are much more presumptive. </seg>
<seg id="1882"> They presume that the information they're looking for is certainly available and that it's just a matter of technologists making it easier to get to, and in fewer keystrokes,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="1883"> ˇ°The democratization of information is having a profound impact on society. </seg>
<seg id="1884"> Today's consumers are much more efficient-they can find information, products, services, faster [through search engines] than through traditional means. </seg>
<seg id="1885"> They are better informed about issues related to work, health, leisure, etc. </seg>
<seg id="1886"> Small towns are no longer disadvantaged relative to those with better access to information. </seg>
<seg id="1887"> And people have the ability to be better connected to things that interest them, to quickly and easily become experts in given subjects and to connect with others who share their interests.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1888"> Google's founders understood that by the late 1990s hundreds of thousands of Web pages were being added to the Internet each day, and that existing search engines, which tended to search for keywords, could not keep pace. </seg>
<seg id="1889"> Brin and Page, who met as Stanford University graduate students in computer science in 1995, developed a mathematical formula that ranked a Web page by how many other Web pages were linked to it, on the assumption that the more people linked to a certain page, the more important the page. </seg>
<seg id="1890"> The key breakthrough that enabled Google to become first among search engines was its ability to combine its PageRank technology with an analysis of page content, which determines which pages are most relevant to the specific search being conducted. </seg>
<seg id="1891"> Even though Google entered the market after other major search players, its answers were seen by people as more accurate and relevant to what they were looking for. </seg>
<seg id="1892"> (Google now employs scores of mathematicians working on its search algorithms, in an effort to always keep them one step more relevant than the competition.) </seg>
<seg id="1893"> For some reason, said Brin, ˇ°people underestimated the importance of finding information, as opposed to other things you would do online. </seg>
<seg id="1894"> If you are searching for something like a health issue, you really want to know; in some cases it is a life-and-death matter. </seg>
<seg id="1895"> We have people who search Google for heart-attack symptoms and then call nine-one-one.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1896"> But sometimes you really want to in-form yourself about something much simpler. </seg>
<seg id="1897"> When I was in Beijing in June 2004, I was riding the elevator down one morning with my wife, Ann, and sixteen-year-old daughter, Natalie, who was carrying a fistful of postcards written to her friends. </seg>
<seg id="1898"> Ann said to her, ˇ°Did you bring their addresses along?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1899"> Natalie looked at her as if she were positively nineteenth-century. </seg>
<seg id="1900"> ˇ°No,ˇ± she said, with that you-are-so-out-of-it-Mom tone of voice. </seg>
<seg id="1901"> ˇ°I just Googled their phone numbers, and their home addresses came up.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1902"> Address book? You dummy, Mom.All that Natalie was doing was in-forming, using Google in a way that I had no idea was even possible. </seg>
<seg id="1903"> Meanwhile, though, she also had her iPod with her, which empowered her to in-form herself in another way- with entertainment instead of knowledge. </seg>
<seg id="1904"> She had become her own music editor and downloaded all her favorite songs into her iPod and was carrying them all over China. </seg>
<seg id="1905"> Think about it: For decades the broadcast industry was built around the idea that you shoot out ads on network television or radio and hope that someone is watching or listening. </seg>
<seg id="1906"> But thanks to the flattening technologies in entertainment, that world is quickly fading away. </seg>
<seg id="1907"> Now with TiVo you can become your own TV editor. TiVo allows viewers to digitally record their favorite programs and skip the ads, except those they want to see. </seg>
<seg id="1908"> You watch what you want when you want. </seg>
<seg id="1909"> You don't have to make an appointment with a TV channel at the time and place someone else sets and watch the commercials foisted on you. </seg>
<seg id="1910"> With TiVo you can watch only your own shows and the commercials you want for only those products in which you might be interested. </seg>
<seg id="1911"> But just as Google can track what you are searching for, so too can TiVo, which knows which shows and which ads you are freezing, storing, and rewinding on your own TV. </seg>
<seg id="1912"> So here's a news quiz: Guess what was the most rewound moment in TV history? </seg>
<seg id="1913"> Answer: Janet's Jackson breast exposure, or, as it was euphemistically called, her ˇ°wardrobe malfunction,ˇ± at the 2004 Super Bowl. </seg>
<seg id="1914"> In a press release it issued on February 2,2004, TiVo said, ˇ°Justin Timberlake and Janet Jackson stole the show during Sunday's Super Bowl, attracting almost twice as many viewers as the most thrilling moments on the field, according to an annual measurement of second-by-second viewership in TiVo households. </seg>
<seg id="1915"> The Jackson-Timberlake moment drew the biggest spike in audience reaction TiVo has ever measured. </seg>
<seg id="1916"> TiVo said viewership spiked up to 180 percent as hundreds of thousands of households used TiVo's unique capabilities to pause and replay live television to view the incident again and again.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1917"> So if everyone can increasingly watch what he wants however many times he wants when he wants, the whole notion of broadcast TV-which is that we throw shows out there one time, along with their commercials, and then try to survey who is watching-will increasingly make less and less sense. </seg>
<seg id="1918"> The companies you want to bet on are those that, like Google or Yahoo! or TiVo, learn to collaborate with their users and offer them shows and advertisements tailored just for them. </seg>
<seg id="1919"> I can imagine a day soon when advertisers won't pay for anything other than that. </seg>
<seg id="1920"> Companies like Google, Yahoo!, Amazon.com, and TiVo have learned to thrive not by pushing products and services on their customers as much as by building collaborative systems that enable customers to pull on their own, and then responding with lightning quickness to what they pull. </seg>
<seg id="1921"> It is so much more efficient. </seg>
<seg id="1922"> ˇ°Search is so highly personal that searching is empowering for humans like nothing else,ˇ± said Google CEO Eric Schmidt. </seg>
<seg id="1923"> ˇ°It is the antithesis of being told or taught. </seg>
<seg id="1924"> It is about self-empowerment; it is empowering individuals to do what they think best with the information they want. </seg>
<seg id="1925"> It is very different from anything else that preceded it. </seg>
<seg id="1926"> Radio was one-to-many. </seg>
<seg id="1927"> TV was one-to-many. </seg>
<seg id="1928"> The telephone was one-to-one. </seg>
<seg id="1929"> Search is the ultimate expression of the power of the individual, using a computer, looking at the world, and finding exactly what they want- and everyone is different when it comes to that.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1930"> Of course what made Google not just a search engine but a hugely profitable business was its founders' realization that they could build a targeted advertising model that would show you ads that are relevant to you when you searched for a specific topic and then could charge advertisers for the number of times Google users clicked on their ads. </seg>
<seg id="1931"> Whereas CBS broadcasts a movie and has a less exact idea who is watching it or the advertisements, Google knows exactly what you are interested in- after all, you are searching for it-and can link you up with advertisers directly or indirectly connected to your searches. </seg>
<seg id="1932"> In late 2004, Google began a service whereby if you are walking around Bethesda, Maryland, and are in the mood for sushi, you just send Google an SMS message on your cell phone that says ˇ°Sushi 20817ˇ±-the Bethesda zip code-and it will send you back a text message of choices. </seg>
<seg id="1933"> Lord only knows where this will go.In-forming, though, also involves searching for friends, allies, and collaborators. </seg>
<seg id="1934"> It is empowering the formation of global communities, across all international and cultural boundaries, which is another critically important flattening function. </seg>
<seg id="1935"> People can now search out fellow collaborators on any subject, project, or theme-particularly through portals like Yahoo! Groups. </seg>
<seg id="1936"> Yahoo! has about 300 million users and 4 million active groups. </seg>
<seg id="1937"> Those groups have 13 million unique individuals accessing them each month from all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="1938"> ˇ°The Internet is growing in the self-services area, and Yahoo! Groups exemplifies this trend,ˇ± said Jerry Yang. </seg>
<seg id="1939"> ˇ°It provides a forum, a platform, a set of tools for people to have private, semiprivate, or public gatherings on the Internet regardless of geography or time. </seg>
<seg id="1940"> It enables consumers to gather around topics that are meaningful to them in ways that are either impractical or impossible offline. </seg>
<seg id="1941"> Groups can serve as support groups for complete strangers who are galvanized by a common issue or who seek others who share similar interests . </seg>
<seg id="1942"> Existing communities can migrate online and flourish in an interactive environment (local kids' soccer league, church youth group, alumni organizations), providing a virtual home for groups interested in sharing, organizing, and communicating information valuable to cultivating vibrant communities. </seg>
<seg id="1943"> Some groups exist only online and could never be as successful offline, while others mirror strong real-world communities. </seg>
<seg id="1944"> Groups can be created instantaneously and dissolved; </seg>
<seg id="1945"> topics can change or stay constant. </seg>
<seg id="1946"> There is another side to in-forming that people are going to have to get used to, and that is other people's ability to in-form themselves about you from a very early age. </seg>
<seg id="1947"> Search engines flatten the world by eliminating all the valleys and peaks, all the walls and rocks, that people used to hide inside of, atop, behind, or under in order to mask their reputations or parts of their past. </seg>
<seg id="1948"> In a flat world, you can't run, you can't hide, and smaller and smaller rocks are turned over. </seg>
<seg id="1949"> Live your life honestly, because whatever you do, whatever mistakes you make, will be searchable one day. </seg>
<seg id="1950"> The flatter the world becomes, the more ordinary people become transparent-and available. </seg>
<seg id="1951"> Before my daughter Orly went off to college in the fall of 2003, she was telling me about some of her roommates. </seg>
<seg id="1952"> When I asked her how she knew some of the things she knew- </seg>
<seg id="1953"> had she spoken to them or received an e-mail from them? </seg>
<seg id="1954"> -she told me she had done neither. </seg>
<seg id="1955"> She just Googled them. </seg>
<seg id="1956"> She came up with stuff from high school newspapers, local papers, etc., and fortunately no police records. </seg>
<seg id="1957"> These are high school kids! </seg>
<seg id="1958"> ˇ°In this world you better do it right-you don't get to pick up and move to the next town so easily,ˇ± said Dov Seidman, who runs a legal compliance and business ethics consulting firm, LRN. </seg>
<seg id="1959"> ˇ°In the world of Google, your reputation will follow you and precede you on your next stop. </seg>
<seg id="1960"> It gets there before you do... </seg>
<seg id="1961"> Your reputation is getting set much earlier in life. </seg>
<seg id="1962"> 'Always tell the truth,' said Mark Twain, 'that way you won't have to remember what you said.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1963"> In the age of the superpower search, everyone is a celebrity. </seg>
<seg id="1964"> Google levels information-it has no class boundaries or education boundaries. </seg>
<seg id="1965"> ˇ°If I can operate Google, I can find anything,ˇ± said Alan Cohen, vice president of Airespace, which sells wireless technology. </seg>
<seg id="1966"> ˇ°Google is like God. </seg>
<seg id="1967"> God is wireless, God is everywhere, and God sees everything. </seg>
<seg id="1968"> Any questions in the world, you ask Google.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1969"> Some months after Cohen made that observation to me, I came across the following brief business story on CNET News.com: ˇ°Search giant Google said on Wednesday that it has acquired Keyhole, a company specializing in Web-based software that allows people to view satellite images from around the globe... </seg>
<seg id="1970"> The software gives users the ability to zoom in from space level; </seg>
<seg id="1971"> in some cases, it can zoom in all the way to a street-level view. </seg>
<seg id="1972"> The company does not have high-resolution imagery for the entire globe, but its Website offers a list of cities that are available for more detailed viewing. </seg>
<seg id="1973"> The company has focused most on covering large metropolitan areas in the United States and is working to expand its coverage.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1974"> Flattener #10: The Steroids, Digital, Mobile, Personal, and Virtual </seg>
<seg id="1975"> But this iPaq's real distinction is its wirelessness. </seg>
<seg id="1976"> It's the first palmtop that can connect to the Internet and other gadgets in four wireless ways. </seg>
<seg id="1977"> For distances up to 30 inches, the iPaq can beam information, like your electronic business card, to another palmtop using an infrared transmitter. </seg>
<seg id="1978"> For distances up to 30 feet, it has built-in Bluetooth circuitry... </seg>
<seg id="1979"> For distances up to 150 feet, it has a Wi-Fi antenna. </seg>
<seg id="1980"> And for transmissions around the entire planet, the iPaq has one other trick up its sleeve: it's also a cell phone. </seg>
<seg id="1981"> If your office can't reach you on this, then you must be on the International Space Station. </seg>
<seg id="1982"> ˇŞFrom a New York Times article about HP's new PocketPC, July 29, 2004 </seg>
<seg id="1983"> I am on the bullet train speeding southwest from Tokyo to Mishima. </seg>
<seg id="1984"> The view is spectacular: fishing villages on my left and a snow-dusted Mt. Fuji on my right. </seg>
<seg id="1985"> My colleague Jim Brooke, the Tokyo bureau chief for The New York Times, is sitting across the aisle and paying no attention to the view. </seg>
<seg id="1986"> He is engrossed in his computer. </seg>
<seg id="1987"> So am I, actually, but he's online through a wireless connection, and I'm just typing away on a column on my unconnected laptop. </seg>
<seg id="1988"> Ever since we took a cab together the other day in downtown Tokyo and Jim whipped out his wireless-enabled laptop in the backseat and e-mailed me something through Yahoo!, I have been exclaiming at the amazing degree of wireless penetration and connectivity in Japan. </seg>
<seg id="1989"> Save for a few remote islands and mountain villages, if you have a wireless card in your computer, or any Japanese cell phone, you can get online anywhere-from deep inside the subway stations to the bullet trains speeding through the countryside. </seg>
<seg id="1990"> Jim knows I am slightly obsessed with the fact that Japan, not to mention most of the rest of the world, has so much better wireless connectivity than America. </seg>
<seg id="1991"> Anyway, Jim likes to rub it in. </seg>
<seg id="1992"> ˇ°See, Tom, I am online right now,ˇ± he says, as the Japanese countryside whizzes by. </seg>
<seg id="1993"> ˇ°A friend of mine who's the Times's stringer in Alma Ata just had a baby and I am congratulating him. </seg>
<seg id="1994"> He had a baby girl last night.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1995"> ˇ°Now I'm reading the frontings!ˇ±ˇŞa summary of the day's New York Times headlines. </seg>
<seg id="1996"> Finally, I ask Jim, who is fluent in Japanese, to ask the train conductor to come over. </seg>
<seg id="1997"> I ask Jim to ask the conductor how fast we are going. </seg>
<seg id="1998"> They rattle back and forth in Japanese for a few seconds before Jim translates: ˇ°240 kilometers per hour.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="1999"> I shake my head. </seg>
<seg id="2000"> We are on a bullet train going 240 km per hour-that's 150 mph-and my colleague is answering e-mail from Kazakhstan, and I can't drive from my home in suburban Washington to downtown DC without my cell phone service being interrupted at least twice. </seg>
<seg id="2001"> The day before, I was in Tokyo waiting for an appointment with Jim's colleague Todd Zaun, and he was preoccupied with his Japanese cell phone, which easily connects to the Internet from anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2002"> ˇ°I am a surfer,ˇ± Todd explained, as he used his thumb to manipulate the keypad. ˇ°For $3 a month I subscribe to this [Japanese] site that tells me each morning how high the waves are at the beaches near my house. </seg>
<seg id="2003"> I check it out, and I decide where the best place to surf is that day.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2004"> (The more I thought about this, the more I wanted to run for president on a one-issue ticket: ˇ°I promise, if elected, that within four years America will have as good a cell phone coverage as Ghana, and in eight years as good as Japan-provided that the Japanese sign a standstill agreement and won't innovate for eight years so we can catch up.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2005"> My campaign bumper sticker will be very simple: ˇ°Can You Hear Me Now?ˇ±) </seg>
<seg id="2006"> I know that America will catch up sooner or later with the rest of the world in wireless technology. </seg>
<seg id="2007"> But this section about the tenth flattener is not just about wireless. </seg>
<seg id="2008"> It is about what I call ˇ°the steroids.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2009"> I call certain new technologies the steroids because they are amplifying and turbocharging all the other flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="2010"> They are taking all the forms of collaboration highlighted in this section- outsourcing, offshoring, open-sourcing, supply-chaining, insourcing, and in-forming-and making it possible to do each and every one of them in a way that is ˇ°digital, mobile, virtual, and personal,ˇ± as former HP CEO Carly Fiorina put it in her speeches, thereby enhancing each one and making the world flatter by the day. </seg>
<seg id="2011"> By ˇ°digital,ˇ± Fiorina means that thanks to the PC-Windows-Netscape-work flow revolutions, all analog content and processes- everything from photography to entertainment to communication to word processing to architectural design to the management of my home lawn sprinkler system-are being digitized and therefore can be shaped, manipulated, and transmitted over computers, the Internet, satellites, or fiber-optic cable. </seg>
<seg id="2012"> By ˇ°virtual,ˇ± she means that the process of shaping, manipulating, and transmitting this digitized content can be done at very high speeds, with total ease, so that you never have to think about it-thanks to all the underlying digital pipes, protocols, and standards that have now been installed. </seg>
<seg id="2013"> By ˇ°mobile,ˇ± she means that thanks to wireless technology, all this can be done from anywhere, with anyone, through any device, and can be taken anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2014"> And by ˇ°personal,ˇ± she means that it can be done by you, just for you, on your own device. </seg>
<seg id="2015"> What does the flat world look like when you take all these new forms of collaboration and turbocharge them in this way? </seg>
<seg id="2016"> Let me give just one example. </seg>
<seg id="2017"> Bill Brody, the president of Johns Hopkins, told me this story in the summer of 2004: </seg>
<seg id="2018"> ˇ°I am sitting in a medical meeting in Vail and the [doctor] giving a lecture quotes a study from Johns Hopkins University. </seg>
<seg id="2019"> And the guy speaking is touting a new approach to treating prostate cancer that went against the grain of the current surgical method. </seg>
<seg id="2020"> This guy who is speaking proposes an alternate method-which was controversial-but he quotes from Walsh's Hopkins study in a way that supported his approach. </seg>
<seg id="2021"> When he said that, I said to myself, That doesn't sound like Dr. Walsh's study.' </seg>
<seg id="2022"> So I had a PDA [personal digital assistant], and I immediately went online [wirelessly] and got into the Johns Hopkins portal and into Medline and did a search right while I was sitting there. </seg>
<seg id="2023"> Up come all the Walsh abstracts. </seg>
<seg id="2024"> I toggled on one and read it, and it was not at all what the guy was saying it was. </seg>
<seg id="2025"> So I raised my hand during the Q and A and read two lines from the abstract, and the guy just turned beet red.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2026"> The digitization and storage of all the Johns Hopkins faculty research in recent years made it possible for Brody to search it instantly and virtually without giving it a second thought. </seg>
<seg id="2027"> The advances in wireless technology made it possible for him to do that search from anywhere with any device. </seg>
<seg id="2028"> And his handheld personal computer enabled him to do that search personally-by himself, just for himself. </seg>
<seg id="2029"> What are the steroids that made all this possible? </seg>
<seg id="2030"> One simple way to think about computing, at any scale, is that it is comprised of three things: computational capability, storage capability, and input/output capability-the speed by which information is drawn in and out of the computer/storage complexes. </seg>
<seg id="2031"> And all of these have been steadily increasing since the days of the first bulky mainframes. </seg>
<seg id="2032"> This mutually reinforcing progress constitutes a significant steroid. </seg>
<seg id="2033"> As a result of it, year after year we have been able to digitize, shape, crunch, and transmit more words, music, data, and entertainment than ever before. </seg>
<seg id="2034"> For instance, MIPS stands for ˇ°millions of instructions per second,ˇ± and it is one measure of the computational capability of a computer's microchips. </seg>
<seg id="2035"> In 1971, the Intel 4004 microprocessor produced.06 MIPS, or 60,000 instructions per second. </seg>
<seg id="2036"> Today's Intel Pentium 4 Extreme Edition has a theoretical maximum of 10.8 billion instructions per second. </seg>
<seg id="2037"> In 1971, the Intel 4004 microprocessor contained 2,300 transistors. </seg>
<seg id="2038"> Meanwhile, inputting and outputting data have leaped ahead at a staggering rate. </seg>
<seg id="2039"> At the speeds that disk drives operated back in the early days of 286 and 386 chips, it would have taken about a minute to download a single photo from my latest digital camera. </seg>
<seg id="2040"> Today I can do that in less than a second on a USB 2.0 disk drive and a Pentium processor. </seg>
<seg id="2041"> The amount of stuff you can now store to input and output ˇ°is off the charts, thanks to the steady advances in storage devices,ˇ± said Craig Mundie, Microsoft's chief technology officer. </seg>
<seg id="2042"> ˇ°Storage is growing exponentially, and this is really as much a factor in the revolution as anything else.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2043"> It's what is allowing all forms of content to become digital and to some extent portable. </seg>
<seg id="2044"> It is also becoming cheap enough that you can put massive amounts on even the personal devices people carry around with them. </seg>
<seg id="2045"> Five years ago, no one would have believed that you would be able to sell iPods with 40 gigabytes of storage, capable of holding thousands of songs, for prices that teenagers could afford. </seg>
<seg id="2046"> And when it comes to moving all these bits around, the computing world has been turbocharged. </seg>
<seg id="2047"> Advances in fiber optics will soon allow a single fiber to carry 1 terabit per second. </seg>
<seg id="2048"> With 48 fibers in a cable, that's 48 terabits per second. </seg>
<seg id="2049"> Henry Schacht, the former CEO of Lucent, which specialized in this technology, pointed out that with that much capacity, you could ˇ°transmit all the printed material in the world in minutes in a single cable. </seg>
<seg id="2050"> This means unlimited transmitting capacity at zero incremental cost.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2051"> Even though the speeds that Schacht was talking about apply only to the backbone of the fiber network, and not that last mile into your house and into your computer, we are still talking about a quantum leap forward. </seg>
<seg id="2052"> In The Lexus and the Olive Tree, I wrote about a 1999 Qwest commercial showing a businessman, tired and dusty, checking in to a roadside motel in the middle of nowhere. </seg>
<seg id="2053"> He asks the bored-looking desk clerk whether they have room service and other amenities. </seg>
<seg id="2054"> She says yes. </seg>
<seg id="2055"> Then he asks her whether entertainment is available on his room television, and the clerk answers in a what-do-you-think-you-idiot monotone, ˇ°All rooms have every movie ever made in every language, anytime, day or night.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2056"> I wrote about that back then as an example of what happens when you get connected to the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="2057"> Today it is an example of how much you can now get disconnected from the Internet, because in the next few years, as storage continues to advance and become more and more miniaturized, you will be able to buy enough storage to carry many of those movies around in your pocket. </seg>
<seg id="2058"> Then add another hardware steroid to the mix: file sharing. </seg>
<seg id="2059"> It started with Napster paving the way for two of us to share songs stored on each other's computers. </seg>
<seg id="2060"> ˇ°At its peak,ˇ± according to Howstuffworks.com, ˇ°Napster was perhaps the most popular Website ever created. </seg>
<seg id="2061"> In less than a year, it went from zero to 60 million visitors per month. </seg>
<seg id="2062"> Then it was shut down by a court order because of copyright violations, and wouldn't re-launch until 2003 as a legal music-download site. </seg>
<seg id="2063"> The original Napster became so popular so quickly because it offered a unique product-free music that you could obtain nearly effortlessly from a gigantic database.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2064"> That database was actually a file-sharing architecture by which Napster facilitated a connection between my computer and yours so that we could swap music files. </seg>
<seg id="2065"> The original Napster is dead, but file-sharing technology is still around and is getting more sophisticated every day, greatly enhancing collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="2066"> Finally, add one last hardware steroid that brings these technology breakthroughs together for consumers: Finally, add one last hardware steroid that brings these technology breakthroughs together for consumers: </seg>
<seg id="2067"> you could practically feel the breath of the other parties to the videocon-ference, when in fact half of us were in Santa Barbara and half were five hundred miles away. </seg>
<seg id="2068"> Because DreamWorks is doing film and animation work all over the world, it felt that it had to have a videoconferencing solution where its creative people could really communicate all their thoughts, facial expressions, feelings, ire, enthusiasm, and raised eyebrows. </seg>
<seg id="2069"> HP's chief strategy and technology officer, Shane Robison, told me that HP plans to have these videoconferencing suites for sale by 2005 at a cost of roughly $250,000 each. </seg>
<seg id="2070"> That is nothing compared to the airline tickets and wear and tear on executives having to travel regularly to London or Tokyo for face-to-face meetings. </seg>
<seg id="2071"> Companies could easily make one of these suites pay for itself in a year. </seg>
<seg id="2072"> This level of videoconferencing, once it proliferates, will make remote development, outsourcing, and off-shoring that much easier and more efficient. </seg>
<seg id="2073"> And now the icing on the cake, the iibersteroid that makes it all mobile: wireless. </seg>
<seg id="2074"> Wireless is what will allow you take everything that has been digitized, made virtual and personal, and do it from anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2075"> ˇ°The natural state of communications is wireless,ˇ± argued Alan Cohen, the senior vice president at Airespace. </seg>
<seg id="2076"> It started with voice, because people wanted to be able to make a phone call anytime, from anyplace, to anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2077"> That is why for many people the cell phone is the most important phone they own. </seg>
<seg id="2078"> By the early twenty-first century, people began to develop that same expectation and with it the desire for data communication-the ability to access the Internet, e-mail, or any business files anytime, anywhere, using a cell phone, PalmPilot, or some other personal device. </seg>
<seg id="2079"> (And now a third element is entering the picture, creating more demand for wireless technology and enhancing the flattening of the earth: </seg>
<seg id="2080"> machines talking to machines wirelessly, such as Wal-Mart's RFID chips, little wireless devices that automatically transmit information to suppliers' computers, allowing them to track inventory.) </seg>
<seg id="2081"> In the early days of computing (Globalization 2.0), you worked in the office. </seg>
<seg id="2082"> There was a big mainframe computer, and you literally had to walk over and get the people running the mainframe to extract or input information for you. </seg>
<seg id="2083"> It was like an oracle. </seg>
<seg id="2084"> Then, thanks to the PC and the Internet, e-mail, the laptop, the browser, and the client server, I could access from my own screen all sorts of data and information being stored on the network. </seg>
<seg id="2085"> In this era you were delinked from the office and could work at home, at the beach house, or in a hotel. </seg>
<seg id="2086"> Now we are in Globalization 3.0, where, thanks to digitization, miniaturization, virtualization, personalization, and wireless, I can be processing, collecting, or transmitting voice or data from anywhere to anywhere-as an individual or as a machine. </seg>
<seg id="2087"> ˇ°Your desk goes with you everywhere you are now,ˇ± said Cohen. </seg>
<seg id="2088"> And the more people have the ability to push and pull information from anywhere to anywhere faster, the more barriers to competition and communication disappear. </seg>
<seg id="2089"> All of a sudden, my business has phenomenal distribution. I don't care whether you are in Bangalore or Bangor, I can get to you and you can get to me. </seg>
<seg id="2090"> More and more, people now want and expect wireless mobility to be there, just like electricity. </seg>
