<seg id="2091"> We are rapidly moving into the age of the ˇ°mobile me,ˇ± said Padmasree Warrior, the chief technology officer of Motorola. </seg>
<seg id="2092"> If consumers are paying for any form of content, whether it is information, entertainment, data, games, or stock quotes, they increasingly want to be able to access it anytime, anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2093"> Right now consumers are caught in a maze of wireless technology offerings and standards that are still not totally interoperable. </seg>
<seg id="2094"> As we all know, some wireless technology works in one neighborhood, state, or country and not in another. </seg>
<seg id="2095"> The ˇ°mobile meˇ± revolution will be complete when you can move seamlessly around the town, the country, or the world with whatever device you want. </seg>
<seg id="2096"> When this is fully diffused, the ˇ°mobile meˇ± will have its full flattening effect, by freeing people to truly be able to work and communicate from anywhere to anywhere with anything. </seg>
<seg id="2097"> I got a taste of what is coming by spending a morning at the Tokyo headquarters of NTT DoCoMo, the Japanese cellular giant that is at the cutting edge of this process and far ahead of America in offering total interoperability inside Japan. </seg>
<seg id="2098"> DoCoMo is an abbreviation for Do Communications Over the Mobile Network; </seg>
<seg id="2099"> it also means ˇ°anywhereˇ± in Japanese. </seg>
<seg id="2100"> My day at DoCoMo's headquarters started with a tour conducted by a robot, which bowed in perfect Japanese fashion and then gave me a spin around DoCoMo's showroom, which now features handheld video cell phones so you can see the person you are speaking with. </seg>
<seg id="2101"> ˇ°Young people are using our mobile phones today as two-way videophones,ˇ± explained Tamon Mitsuishi, senior VP of the Ubiquitous Business Department at DoCoMo. </seg>
<seg id="2102"> ˇ°Everyone takes out their phones, they start dialing each other and have visual conversations. </seg>
<seg id="2103"> Of course there are some people who prefer not to see each other's faces.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2104"> Thanks to DoCoMo technology, if you don't want to show your face you can substitute a cartoon character for yourself and manipulate the keyboard so that it not only will speak for you but also will get angry for you and get happy for you. </seg>
<seg id="2105"> ˇ°So this is a mobile phone, and video camera, but it has also evolved to the extent that it has functions similar to a PC,ˇ± he added. </seg>
<seg id="2106"> ˇ°You need to move your buttons quickly [with your thumb]. </seg>
<seg id="2107"> We call ourselves 'the thumb people.' </seg>
<seg id="2108"> Young girls in high school can now move their thumbs faster than they can type on a PC.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2109"> By the way, I asked, what does the ˇ°Ubiquitous Departmentˇ± do? </seg>
<seg id="2110"> ˇ°Now that we have seen the spread of the Internet around the world,ˇ± answered Mitsuishi, ˇ°what we believe we have to offer is the next step. </seg>
<seg id="2111"> Internet communication until today has been mostly between individuals-e-mail and other information. </seg>
<seg id="2112"> But what we are already starting to see is communication between individuals and machines and between machines. </seg>
<seg id="2113"> We are moving into that kind of phenomenon, because people want to lead a richer lifestyle, and businesses want more efficient practices... </seg>
<seg id="2114"> So young people in their business life use PCs in the offices, but in their private time they base their lifestyles on a mobile phone. </seg>
<seg id="2115"> There is now a growing movement to allow payment by mobile phone. </seg>
<seg id="2116"> [With] a smart card you will be able to make payments in virtual shops and smart shops. </seg>
<seg id="2117"> So next to the cash register there will be a reader of the card, and you just scan your phone and it becomes your credit card too... </seg>
<seg id="2118"> oblivious of the double meaning of the English word ˇ°control.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2119"> ˇ°We believe that the mobile phone will become the essential contrailer of a person's life,ˇ± added Mitsuishi, </seg>
<seg id="2120"> ˇ°For example, in the medical field it will be your authentication system and you can examine your medical records, and to make payments you will have to hold a mobile phone. </seg>
<seg id="2121"> You will not be able to lead a life without a mobile phone, and it will control things at home too. We believe that we need to expand the range of machines that can be controlled by mobile phone.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2122"> There is plenty to worry about in this future, from kids being lured by online sexual predators through their cell phones, to employees spending too much time playing mindless phone games, to people using their phone cameras for all sorts of illicit activities. </seg>
<seg id="2123"> Some Japanese were going into bookstores, pulling down cookbooks, and taking pictures of the recipes and then walking out. </seg>
<seg id="2124"> Fortunately, camera phones are now being enabled to make a noise when they shoot a picture, so that a store owner, or the person standing next to you in the locker room, will know if he is on Candid Camera. </seg>
<seg id="2125"> Because your Internet-enabled camera phone is not just a camera; </seg>
<seg id="2126"> it is also a copy machine, with worldwide distribution potential. </seg>
<seg id="2127"> DoCoMo is now working with other Japanese companies on an arrangement by which you may be walking down the street and see a poster of a concert by Madonna in Tokyo. </seg>
<seg id="2128"> The poster will have a bar code and you can buy your tickets by just scanning the bar code. </seg>
<seg id="2129"> Another poster might be for a new Madonna CD. </seg>
<seg id="2130"> Just scan the bar code with your cell phone and it will give you a sample of the songs. If you like them, scan it again and you can buy the whole album and have it home-delivered. </seg>
<seg id="2131"> No wonder my New York Times colleague in Japan, Todd Zaun, who is married to a Japanese woman, remarked to me that there is so much information the Japanese can now access from their Internet-enabled wireless phones that ˇ°when I am with my Japanese relatives and someone has a question, the first thing they do is reach for the phone.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2132"> I'm exhausted just writing about all this. But it is hard to exaggerate how much this tenth flattener-the steroids-is going to amplify and further empower all the other forms of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="2133"> These steroids should make open-source innovation that much more open, because they will enable more individuals to collaborate with one another in more ways and from more places than ever before. </seg>
<seg id="2134"> They will enhance outsourcing, because they will make it so much easier for a single department of any company to collaborate with another company. </seg>
<seg id="2135"> They will enhance supply-chaining, because headquarters will be able to be connected in real time with every individual employee stocking the shelves, every individual package, and every Chinese factory manufacturing the stuff inside them. </seg>
<seg id="2136"> They will enhance insourcing-having a company like UPS come deep inside a retailer and manage its whole supply chain, using drivers who can interact with its warehouses, and with every customer, carrying his own PDA. </seg>
<seg id="2137"> And most obviously, they will enhance informing </seg>
<seg id="2138"> the ability to manage your own knowledge supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="2139"> Sir John Rose, the chief executive of Rolls-Royce, gave me a wonderful example of how wireless and other steroids are enhancing Rolls-Royce's ability to do work flow and other new forms of collaboration with its customers. </seg>
<seg id="2140"> Let's say you are British Airways and you are flying a Boeing 777 across the Atlantic. </seg>
<seg id="2141"> Somewhere over Greenland, one of your Rolls-Royce engines gets hit with lightning. </seg>
<seg id="2142"> The passengers and pilots might be worried, but there is no need. </seg>
<seg id="2143"> That Rolls-Royce engine is connected by transponder to a satellite and is beaming data about its condition and performance, at all times, down into a computer in Rolls-Royce's operations room. </seg>
<seg id="2144"> That is true of many Rolls-Royce airplane engines in operation. </seg>
<seg id="2145"> Thanks to the artificial intelligence in the Rolls-Royce computer, based on complex algorithms, it can track anomalies in its engines while in operation. </seg>
<seg id="2146"> The artificial intelligence in the Rolls-Royce computer knows that this engine was probably hit by lightning, and feeds out a report to a Rolls-Royce engineer. </seg>
<seg id="2147"> ˇ°With the real-time data we receive via satellites, we can identify an 'event' and our engineers can make remote diagnoses,ˇ± said Rose. </seg>
<seg id="2148"> ˇ°Under normal circumstances, after an engine gets hit by lightning you would have to land the plane, call in an engineer, do a visual inspection, and make a decision about how much damage might have been done and whether the plane has to be delayed in order to do a repair. </seg>
<seg id="2149"> ˇ°But remember, these airlines do not have much turnaround time. If this plane is delayed, you throw off the crews, you drop out of your position to fly back home. </seg>
<seg id="2150"> It gets very costly. </seg>
<seg id="2151"> We can monitor and analyze engine performance automatically in real time, with our engineers making decisions about exactly what is needed by the time the plane has landed. </seg>
<seg id="2152"> And if we can determine by all the information we have about the engine that no intervention or even inspection is needed, the airplane can return on schedule, and that saves our customers time and money.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2153"> Engines talking to computers, talking to people, talking back to the engines, followed by people talking to people-all done from anywhere to anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2154"> That is what happens when all the flatteners start to get turbocharged by all the steroids. </seg>
<seg id="2155"> Can you hear me now? </seg>
<seg id="2156"> THREE The Triple Convergence </seg>
<seg id="2157"> What is the triple convergence? </seg>
<seg id="2158"> In order to explain what I mean, let me tell a personal story and share one of my favorite television commercials. </seg>
<seg id="2159"> The story took place in March 2004. I had made plans to fly from Baltimore to Hartford on Southwest Airlines to visit my daughter Orly, who goes to school in New Haven, Connecticut. </seg>
<seg id="2160"> Being a tech-sawy guy, I didn't bother with a paper ticket but ordered an e-ticket through American Express. </seg>
<seg id="2161"> As anyone who flies regularly on Southwest knows, the cheapo airline has no reserved seats. </seg>
<seg id="2162"> When you check in, your ticket says simply A, B, or C, with the As boarding first, the Bs boarding second, and the Cs boarding last. </seg>
<seg id="2163"> As veterans of Southwest also know, you do not want to be a C. </seg>
<seg id="2164"> If you are, you will almost certainly end up in a middle seat with no space to put your carry-ons in the overhead bin. </seg>
<seg id="2165"> If you want to sit in a window or aisle seat and be able to store your stuff, you want to be an A. </seg>
<seg id="2166"> Since I was carrying some bags of clothing for my daughter, I definitely wanted to be an A. </seg>
<seg id="2167"> So I got up early to make sure I got to the Baltimore airport ninety-five minutes before my scheduled departure. </seg>
<seg id="2168"> I walked up to the Southwest Airlines e-ticket machine, stuck in my credit card, and used the touch screen to get my ticket-a thoroughly modern man, right? </seg>
<seg id="2169"> Well, out came the ticket and it said B. </seg>
<seg id="2170"> I was fuming. ˇ°How in the world could I be a B?ˇ± I said to myself, looking at my watch. </seg>
<seg id="2171"> ˇ°There is no way that many people got here before me. </seg>
<seg id="2172"> This thing is rigged! </seg>
<seg id="2173"> This is fixed! </seg>
<seg id="2174"> This is nothing more than a slot machine!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2175"> I stomped off, went through security, bought a Cinnabon, and glumly sat at the back of the B line, waiting to be herded on board so I could hunt for space in the overhead bins. </seg>
<seg id="2176"> Forty minutes later, the flight was called. </seg>
<seg id="2177"> From the B line, I enviously watched all the As file on board ahead of me, with a certain barely detectable air of superiority. </seg>
<seg id="2178"> And then I saw it. </seg>
<seg id="2179"> Many of the people in the A line didn't have normal e-tickets like mine. </seg>
<seg id="2180"> They were just carrying what looked to me like crumpled pieces of white printer paper, but they weren't blank. They had boarding passes and bar codes printed on them, as if the As had downloaded their boarding passes off the Internet at home and printed them out on their home printers. </seg>
<seg id="2181"> Which, I quickly learned, was exactly what they had done. </seg>
<seg id="2182"> I didn't know it, but Southwest had recently announced that beginning at 12:01 a.m. the night before a flight, you could download your ticket at home, print it out, and then just have the bar code scanned by the gate agent before you boarded. </seg>
<seg id="2183"> ˇ°Friedman,ˇ± I said to myself, looking at this scene, ˇ°you are so twentieth-century... </seg>
<seg id="2184"> You are so Globalization 2.0.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2185"> In Globalization 1.0 there was a ticket agent. </seg>
<seg id="2186"> In Globalization 2.0 the e-ticket machine replaced the ticket agent. </seg>
<seg id="2187"> In Globalization 3.0 you are your own ticket agent. </seg>
<seg id="2188"> The television commercial is from Konica Minolta Business Technologies for a new multipurpose device it sells called bizhub, a piece of office machinery that allows you to do black-and-white or color printing, copy a document, fax it, scan it, scan it to e-mail, or Internet-fax itˇŞall from the same machine. </seg>
<seg id="2189"> The commercial begins with a rapid cutting back and forth between two guys, one in his office and the other standing at the bizhub machine. </seg>
<seg id="2190"> They are close enough to talk by raising their voices. </seg>
<seg id="2191"> Dom is senior in authority but slow on the uptake-the kind of guy who hasn't kept up with changing technology (my kind of guy!). </seg>
<seg id="2192"> He can see Ted standing at the bizhub machine when he leans back in his chair and peers out his office doorway. </seg>
<seg id="2193"> Dom: (At his desk) Hey, I need that chart. </seg>
<seg id="2194"> Ted: (At the bizhub) I'm e-mailing it now. </seg>
<seg id="2195"> Dom: You're e-mailing from the copy machine? </seg>
<seg id="2196"> Ted: No, I'm e-mailing from bizhub. </seg>
<seg id="2197"> Dom: Bizhub? </seg>
<seg id="2198"> Wait, did you make my copies yet? </seg>
<seg id="2199"> Ted: Right after I scan this. </seg>
<seg id="2200"> Dom: You're scanning at an e-mail machine? </seg>
<seg id="2201"> Ted: E-mail machine? </seg>
<seg id="2202"> I'm at the bizhub machine. </seg>
<seg id="2203"> Dom: (Bewildered) Copying? </seg>
<seg id="2204"> Ted: (Trying to be patient) E-mailing, then scanning, then copying. </seg>
<seg id="2205"> Dom: (Long pause) Bizhub? </seg>
<seg id="2206"> VO: (Over an animated graphic of bizhub illustrating its multiple functions) Amazing versatility and affordable color. </seg>
<seg id="2207"> That's bizhub, from Konica Minolta. </seg>
<seg id="2208"> (Cut to Dom alone at the bizhub machine, trying to see if it will also dispense coffee into his mug.)Southwest was able to offer its at-home ticketing, and Konica Minolta could offer bizhub, because of what I call the triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2209"> What are the components of this triple convergence? </seg>
<seg id="2210"> The short answer is this: First, right around the year 2000, all ten of the flatteners discussed in the previous chapter started to converge and work together in ways that created a new, flatter, global playing field. </seg>
<seg id="2211"> Finally, just when all of this flattening was happening, a whole new group of people, several billion, in fact, walked out onto the playing field from China, India, and the former Soviet Empire. Thanks to the new flat world, and its new tools, some of them were quickly able to collaborate and compete directly with everyone else. </seg>
<seg id="2212"> This was the third convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2213"> Now let's look at each in detail. </seg>
<seg id="2214"> Convergence I </seg>
<seg id="2215"> All ten flatteners discussed in the previous chapter have been around, we know, since the 1990s, if not earlier. </seg>
<seg id="2216"> But they had to spread and take root and connect with one another to work their magic on the world. </seg>
<seg id="2217"> For instance, at some point around 2003, Southwest Airlines realized that there were enough PCs around, enough bandwidth, enough computer storage, enough Internet-comfortable customers, and enough software know-how for Southwest to create a work flow system that empowered its customers to download and print out their own boarding passes at home, as easily as downloading a piece of e-mail. </seg>
<seg id="2218"> Southwest could collaborate with its customers and they with Southwest in a new way. </seg>
<seg id="2219"> And somewhere around the same time, the work flow software and hardware converged in a way that enabled Konica Minolta to offer scanning, e-mailing, printing, faxing, and copying all from the same machine. </seg>
<seg id="2220"> This is the first convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2221"> As Stanford University economist Paul Romer pointed out, economists have known for a long time that ˇ°there are goods that are complementary-whereby good A is a lot more valuable if you also have good B. </seg>
<seg id="2222"> It was good to have paper and then it was good to have pencils, and soon as you got more of one you got more of the other, and as you got a better quality of one and better quality of the other, your productivity improved. </seg>
<seg id="2223"> This is known as the simultaneous improvement of complementary goods.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2224"> It is my contention that the opening of the Berlin Wall, Netscape, work flow, outsourcing, offshoring, open-sourcing, insourcing, supply-chaining, in-forming, and the steroids amplifying them all reinforced one another, like complementary goods. </seg>
<seg id="2225"> That tipping point arrived sometime around the year 2000. </seg>
<seg id="2226"> Answer: Because it always takes time for all the flanking technologies, and the business processes and habits needed to get the most out of them, to converge and create that next productivity breakthrough. </seg>
<seg id="2227"> Wal-Mart got big productivity boosts when it combined big box stores-where people could buy soap supplies for six months-with new, horizontal supply-chain management systems that allowed Wal-Mart instantly to connect what a consumer took off the shelf from a Wal-Mart in Kansas City with what a Wal-Mart supplier in coastal China would produce. </seg>
<seg id="2228"> When computers were first introduced into offices, everyone expected a big boost in productivity. </seg>
<seg id="2229"> But that did not happen right away, and it sparked both disappointment and a little confusion. </seg>
<seg id="2230"> The noted economist Robert Solow quipped that computers are everywhere- except ˇ°in the productivity statistics.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2231"> In a pathbreaking 1989 essay, ˇ°Computer and Dynamo: The Modern Productivity Paradox in a Not-Too Distant Mirror,ˇ± the economic historian Paul A. David explained such a lag by pointing to a historical precedent. </seg>
<seg id="2232"> He noted that while the lightbulb was invented in 1879, it took several decades for electrification to kick in and have a big economic and productivity impact. </seg>
<seg id="2233"> Why? </seg>
<seg id="2234"> Because it was not enough just to install electric motors and scrap the old technology-steam engines. The whole way of doing manufacturing had to be reconfigured. </seg>
<seg id="2235"> In the case of electricity, David pointed out, the key breakthrough was in how buildings, and assembly lines, were redesigned and managed. </seg>
<seg id="2236"> Factories in the steam age tended to be heavy, costly multistory buildings designed to brace the weighty belts and other big transmission devices needed to drive steam-powered systems. </seg>
<seg id="2237"> Once small, powerful electric motors were introduced, everyone hoped for a quick productivity boost. </seg>
<seg id="2238"> It took time, though. </seg>
<seg id="2239"> To get all the savings, you needed to redesign enough buildings. </seg>
<seg id="2240"> You needed to have long, low, cheaper-to-build single-story factories, with small electric motors powering machines of all sizes. </seg>
<seg id="2241"> Only when there was a critical mass of experienced factory architects and electrical engineers and managers, who understood the complementarities among the electric motor, the redesign of the factory, and the redesign of the production line, did electrification really deliver the productivity breakthrough in manufacturing, David wrote. </seg>
<seg id="2242"> The same thing is happening today with the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="2243"> Many of the ten flatteners have been around for years. </seg>
<seg id="2244"> But for the full flattening effects to be felt, we needed not only the ten flatteners to converge but also something else. </seg>
<seg id="2245"> We needed the emergence of a large cadre of managers, innovators, business consultants, business schools, designers, IT specialists, CEOs, and workers to get comfortable with, and develop, the sorts of horizontal collaboration and value-creation processes and habits that could take advantage of this new, flatter playing field. </seg>
<seg id="2246"> In short, the convergence of the ten flatteners begat the convergence of a set of business practices and skills that would get the most out of the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="2247"> And then the two began to mutually reinforce each other. </seg>
<seg id="2248"> ˇ°When people asked, 'Why didn't the IT revolution lead to more productivity right away?' it was because you needed more than just new computers,ˇ± said Romer. ˇ°You needed new business processes and new types of skills to go with them. </seg>
<seg id="2249"> The new way of doing things makes the information technologies more valuable, and the new and better information technologies make the new ways of doing things more possible.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2250"> Globalization 2.0 was really the era of mainframe computing, which was very vertical-command-and-control oriented, with companies and their individual departments tending to be organized in vertical silos. </seg>
<seg id="2251"> Globalization 3.0, which is built around the convergence of the ten flatteners, and particularly the combination of the PC, the microprocessor, the Internet, and fiber optics, flipped the playing field from largely top-down to more side to side. </seg>
<seg id="2252"> And this naturally fostered and demanded new business practices, which were less about command and control and more about connecting and collaborating horizontally. </seg>
<seg id="2253"> ˇ°We have gone from a vertical chain of command for value creation to a much more horizontal chain of command for value creation,ˇ± explained Carly Fiorina. </seg>
<seg id="2254"> Innovations in companies like HP, she said, now come more and more often from horizontal collaboration among different departments and teams spread all across the globe. </seg>
<seg id="2255"> For instance, HP, Cisco, and Nokia recently collaborated on the development of a camera/ cell phone that beams its digitized pictures to an HP printer, which quickly prints them out. </seg>
<seg id="2256"> Each company had developed a very sophisticated technological specialty, but it could add value only when its specialty was horizontally combined with the specialties of the other two companies. </seg>
<seg id="2257"> ˇ°How you collaborate horizontally and manage horizontally requires a totally different set of skillsˇ± from traditional top-down approaches, Fiorina added. </seg>
<seg id="2258"> Let me offer just a few examples. </seg>
<seg id="2259"> In the past five years, HP has gone from a company that had eighty-seven different supply chains-each managed vertically and independently, with its own hierarchy of managers and back-office support-to a company with just five supply chains that manage $50 billion in business, and where functions like accounting, billing, and human resources are handled through a companywide system. </seg>
<seg id="2260"> Southwest Airlines took advantage of the convergence of the ten flat-teners to create a system where its customers can download their boarding passes at home. </seg>
<seg id="2261"> But until I personally altered my ticket-buying habits and reengineered myself to collaborate horizontally with Southwest, this technological breakthrough didn't produce a productivity breakthrough for me or Southwest. </seg>
<seg id="2262"> What the bizhub commercial is about is the difference between the employee who understands the convergent technologies in the new bizhub machine (and how to get the most out of them) and the employee in the very same office who does not. </seg>
<seg id="2263"> Not until the latter changes his work habits will productivity in that fictional office go up, even though the office has this amazing new machine. </seg>
<seg id="2264"> Finally, consider the example of WPP-the second-largest advertising-marketing-communications consortium in the world. </seg>
<seg id="2265"> WPP, which is based in England, did not exist as we now know it twenty years ago. </seg>
<seg id="2266"> It is a product of the consolidation of some of the biggest names in the business-from Young &amp; Rubicam to Ogilvy &amp; Mather to Hill &amp; Knowlton. </seg>
<seg id="2267"> The alliance was put together to capture more and more of big clients' marketing needs, such as advertising, direct mail, media buying, and branding. </seg>
<seg id="2268"> ˇ°For years the big challenge for WPP was how to get its own companies to collaborate,ˇ± said Allen Adamson, managing director of WPP's branding firm, Landor Associates. </seg>
<seg id="2269"> ˇ°Now, though, it is often no longer enough just to get the companies in WPP to work together per se. </seg>
<seg id="2270"> Increasingly, we find ourselves pulling together individuals from within each of these companies to form a customized collaborative team just for one client. </seg>
<seg id="2271"> The solution that will create value for that client did not exist in any one company or even in the traditional integration of the companies. It had to be much more specifically tailored. </seg>
<seg id="2272"> So we had to go down inside the whole group and pluck the individual who is the right ad person, to work with the right branding person, to work with the right media person for this particular client.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2273"> When GE decided in 2003 to spin off its insurance businesses into a separate company, WPP assembled a customized team to handle everything from the naming of the new company-Genworth-all the way down to its first advertising campaign and direct-marketing program. </seg>
<seg id="2274"> ˇ°As a leader within this organization,ˇ± said Adamson, ˇ°what you have to do is figure out the value proposition that is needed for each client and then identify and assemble the individual talents within WPP's workforce that will in effect form a virtual company just for that client. </seg>
<seg id="2275"> In the case of GE, we even gave a name to the virtual collaborative team we formed: Klamath Communications.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2276"> When the world went flat, WPP adapted itself to get the most out of itself. </seg>
<seg id="2277"> It changed its office architecture and practices, just like those companies that adjusted their steam-run factories to the electric motor. </seg>
<seg id="2278"> But WPP not only got rid of all its walls, it got rid of all its floors. </seg>
<seg id="2279"> It looked at all its employees from all its companies as a vast pool of individual specialists who could be assembled horizontally into collaborative teams, depending on the unique demands of any given project. </seg>
<seg id="2280"> And that team would then become a de facto new company with its own name. </seg>
<seg id="2281"> It will take time for this new playing field and the new business practices to be fully aligned. </seg>
<seg id="2282"> It's a work in progress. </seg>
<seg id="2283"> But here's a little warning. </seg>
<seg id="2284"> It is happening much faster than you think, and it is happening globally. </seg>
<seg id="2285"> Remember, this was a triple convergence! </seg>
<seg id="2286"> Convergence III </seg>
<seg id="2287"> Just as we finished creating this new, more horizontal playing field, and companies and individuals primarily in the West started quickly adapting to it, 3 billion people who had been frozen out of the field suddenly found themselves liberated to plug and play with everybody else. </seg>
<seg id="2288"> Save for a tiny minority, these 3 billion people had never been allowed to compete and collaborate before, because they lived in largely closed economies with very vertical, hierarchical political and economic structures. </seg>
<seg id="2289"> I am talking about the people of China, India, Russia, Eastern Europe, Latin America, and Central Asia. Their economies and political systems all opened up during the course of the 1990s, so that their people were increasingly free to join the free-market game. </seg>
<seg id="2290"> And when did these 3 billion people converge with the new playing field and the new processes? Right when the field was being flattened, right when millions of them could compete and collaborate more equally, more horizontally, and with cheaper and more readily available tools than ever before. </seg>
<seg id="2291"> Indeed, thanks to the flattening of the world, many of these new entrants didn't even have to leave home to participate. </seg>
<seg id="2292"> Thanks to the ten flatteners, the playing field came to them!It is this triple convergence-of new players, on a new playing field, developing new processes and habits for horizontal collaborationˇŞthat I believe is the most important force shaping global economics and politics in the early twenty-first century. </seg>
<seg id="2293"> Giving so many people access to all these tools of collaboration, along with the ability through search engines and the Web to access billions of pages of raw information, ensures that the next generation of innovations will come from all over Planet Flat. </seg>
<seg id="2294"> The scale of the global community that is soon going to be able to participate in all sorts of discovery and innovation is something the world has simply never seen before. </seg>
<seg id="2295"> Throughout the Cold War there were just three major trading blocs-North America, Western Europe, and Japan plus East Asia-and the competition among the three was relatively controlled, since they were all Cold War allies on the same side of the great global divide. </seg>
<seg id="2296"> There were also still a lot of walls around for labor and industries to hide behind. </seg>
<seg id="2297"> The wage rates in these three trading blocs were roughly the same, the workforces roughly the same size, and the education levels roughly equivalent. </seg>
<seg id="2298"> ˇ°You had a gentlemanly competition,ˇ± noted Intel's Chairman Craig Barrett. </seg>
<seg id="2299"> Then along came the triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2300"> The Berlin Wall came down, the Berlin mall opened up, and suddenly some 3 billion people who had been behind walls walked onto the flattened global piazza. </seg>
<seg id="2301"> Here's what happened in round numbers: According to a November 2004 study by Harvard University economist Richard B. Freeman, in 1985 ˇ°the global economic worldˇ± comprised North America, Western Europe, Japan, as well as chunks of Latin America, Africa, and the countries of East Asia. </seg>
<seg id="2302"> The total population of this global economic world, taking part in international trade and commerce, said Freeman, was about 2.5 billion people. </seg>
<seg id="2303"> By 2000, as a result of the collapse of communism in the Soviet Empire, India's turn from autarky, China's shift to market capitalism, and population growth all over, the global economic world expanded to encompass 6 billion people. </seg>
<seg id="2304"> As a result of this widening, another roughly 1.5 billion new workers entered the global economic labor force, Freeman said, which is almost exactly double the number we would have had in 2000 had China, India, and the Soviet Empire not joined. </seg>
<seg id="2305"> True, maybe only 10 percent of this new 1.5 billion-strong workforce entering the global economy have the education and connectivity to collaborate and compete at a meaningful level. </seg>
<seg id="2306"> But that is still 150 million people, roughly the size of the entire U.S. workforce. </seg>
<seg id="2307"> Said Barrett, ˇ°You don't bring three billion people into the world economy overnight without huge consequences, especially from three societies [like India, China, and Russia] with rich educational heritages.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2308"> That is exactly right. </seg>
<seg id="2309"> And a lot of those new workers are not just walking onto the playing field. </seg>
<seg id="2310"> No, this is no slow-motion triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2311"> They are jogging and even sprinting there. </seg>
<seg id="2312"> Because once the world has been flattened and the new forms of collaboration made available to more and more people, the winners will be those who learn the habits, processes, and skills most quickly-and there is simply nothing that guarantees it will be Americans or Western Europeans permanently leading the way. </seg>
<seg id="2313"> And be advised, these new players are stepping onto the playing field legacy free, meaning that many of them were so far behind they can leap right into the new technologies without having to worry about all the sunken costs of old systems. </seg>
<seg id="2314"> It means that they can move very fast to adopt new, state-of-the-art technologies, which is why there are already more cell phones in use in China today than there are people in the United States. </seg>
<seg id="2315"> Many Chinese just skipped over the landline phase. </seg>
<seg id="2316"> South Koreans put Americans to shame in terms of Internet usage and broadband penetration. </seg>
<seg id="2317"> We tend to think of global trade and economics as something driven by the IMF, the G-8, the World Bank, the WTO, and the trade treaties forged by trade ministers. </seg>
<seg id="2318"> I don't want to suggest that these governmental agencies are irrelevant. They are not. </seg>
<seg id="2319"> But they are going to become less important. </seg>
<seg id="2320"> In the future globalization is going to be increasingly driven by the individuals who understand the flat world, adapt themselves quickly to its processes and technologies, and start to march forward-without any treaties or advice from the IMF. </seg>
<seg id="2321"> They will be every color of the rainbow and from every corner of the world. </seg>
<seg id="2322"> The global economy from here forward will be shaped less by the ponderous deliberations of finance ministers and more by the spontaneous explosion of energy from the zippies. </seg>
<seg id="2323"> Yes, Americans grew up with the hippes in the 1960s. </seg>
<seg id="2324"> Thanks to the high-tech revolution, many of us became yuppies in the 1980s. </seg>
<seg id="2325"> Well, now let me introduce the zippies. </seg>
<seg id="2326"> ˇ°The Zippies Are Here,ˇ± declared the Indian weekly magazine Outlook. </seg>
<seg id="2327"> Zippies are the huge cohort of Indian youth who are the first to come of age since India shifted away from socialism and dived headfirst into global trade and the information revolution by turning itself into the world's service center. </seg>
<seg id="2328"> Outlook called India's zippies ˇ°Liberalization's Childrenˇ± and defined a zippie as a ˇ°young city or suburban resident, between 15 and 25 years of age, with a zip in the stride. </seg>
<seg id="2329"> Belongs to Generation Z. </seg>
<seg id="2330"> Can be male or female, studying or working. </seg>
<seg id="2331"> Oozes attitude, ambition and aspiration. </seg>
<seg id="2332"> Cool, confident and creative. </seg>
<seg id="2333"> Seeks challenges, loves risks and shuns fear.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2334"> Indian zippies feel no guilt about making money or spending it. </seg>
<seg id="2335"> They are, says one Indian analyst quoted by Outlook, ˇ°destination driven, not destiny driven, outward looking, not inward, upwardly mobile, not stuck-in-my-station-in-life.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2336"> With 54 percent of India under the age of twenty-five-that's 555 million people-six out of ten Indian households have at least one potential zippie. </seg>
<seg id="2337"> And the zippies don't just have a pent-up demand for good jobs; they want the good life.It all happened so fast. </seg>
<seg id="2338"> P. V. Kannan, the CEO and cofounder of the Indian call-center company 24/7 Customer, told me that in the last decade, he went from sweating out whether he would ever get a chance to work in America to becoming one of the leading figures in the outsourcing of services from America to the rest of the world. </seg>
<seg id="2339"> ˇ°I will never forget when I applied for a visa to come to the United States,ˇ± Kannan recalled. </seg>
<seg id="2340"> ˇ°It was March 1991.1 had gotten a B.A. in chartered accountancy from the [Indian] Institute of Chartered Accountants. </seg>
<seg id="2341"> I was twenty-three, and my girlfriend was twenty-five. </seg>
<seg id="2342"> She was also a chartered accountant. </seg>
<seg id="2343"> I had graduated at age twenty and had been working for the Tata Consultancy group. </seg>
<seg id="2344"> So was my girlfriend. </seg>
<seg id="2345"> And we both got job offers through a body shop [a recruiting firm specializing in importing Indian talent for companies in America] to work as programmers for IBM. </seg>
<seg id="2346"> So we went to the U.S. consulate in Bombay. </seg>
<seg id="2347"> In those days, there was always a very long line to get visas to the United States, and there were people who would actually sleep in the line and hold places and you could go buy their place for 20 rupees. </seg>
<seg id="2348"> But we went by ourselves and stood in line and we finally got in to see the man who did the interview. </seg>
<seg id="2349"> His job was to ask questions and try to figure out whether we were going to do the work and then come back to India or try to stay in America. </seg>
<seg id="2350"> They judge by some secret formula. We used to call it 'the lottery'-you went and stood in line and it was a life lottery, because everything was dependent on it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2351"> There were actually books and seminars in India devoted entirely to the subject of how to prepare for a work visa interview at the U.S. embassy. </seg>
<seg id="2352"> It was the only way for skilled Indian engineers really to exploit their talent. </seg>
<seg id="2353"> ˇ°I remember one tip was to always go professionally dressed,ˇ± said Kannan, ˇ°so [my girlfriend and I] were both in our best clothes. </seg>
<seg id="2354"> After the interview is over, the man doesn't tell you anything. </seg>
<seg id="2355"> You had to wait until the evening to know the results. </seg>
<seg id="2356"> But meanwhile, the whole day was hell. </seg>
<seg id="2357"> To distract our minds, we just walked the streets of Bombay and went shopping. </seg>
<seg id="2358"> We would go back and forth, </seg>
<seg id="2359"> 'What if I get in and you don't? </seg>
<seg id="2360"> What if you get in and I don't?' </seg>
<seg id="2361"> I can't tell you how anxious we were, because so much was riding on it. </seg>
<seg id="2362"> It was torture. </seg>
<seg id="2363"> So in the evening we go back and both of us got visas, but I got a five-year multiple entry and my girlfriend got a six-month visa. </seg>
<seg id="2364"> She was crying. </seg>
<seg id="2365"> She did not understand what it meant. 'I can only stay for six months?' </seg>
<seg id="2366"> I tried to explain to her that you just need to get in and then everything can be worked out.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2367"> While many Indians still want to come to America to work and study, thanks to the triple convergence many of them can now compete at the highest levels, and be decently paid, by staying at home. </seg>
<seg id="2368"> In a flat world, you can innovate without having to emigrate. </seg>
<seg id="2369"> Said Kannan, ˇ°My daughter will never have to sweat that out.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2370"> In a flat world, he explained, ˇ°there is no one visa officer who can keep you out of the system... </seg>
<seg id="2371"> One of the most dynamic pluggers and players I met in India was Rajesh Rao, founder and CEO of Dhruva Interactive, a small Indian game company based in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="2372"> If I could offer you one person who embodies the triple convergence, it is Rajesh. </seg>
<seg id="2373"> He and his firm show us what happens when an Indian zippie plugs into the ten flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="2374"> Dhruva is located in a converted house on a quiet street in a residential neighborhood of Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="2375"> When I stopped in for a visit, I found two floors of Indian game designers and artists, trained in computer graphics, working on PCs, drawing various games and animated characters for American and European clients. </seg>
<seg id="2376"> The artists and designers were listening to music on headphones as they worked. </seg>
<seg id="2377"> Occasionally, they took a break by playing a group computer game, in which all the designers could try to chase and kill one another at once on their computer screens. </seg>
<seg id="2378"> Dhruva has already produced some very innovative games- from a computer tennis game you can play on the screen of your cell phone to a computer pool game you can play on your PC or laptop. </seg>
<seg id="2379"> In 2004, it bought the rights to use Charlie Chaplin's image for mobile computer games. </seg>
<seg id="2380"> That's right-a start-up Indian game company today owns the Chaplin image for use in mobile computer games. </seg>
<seg id="2381"> In Bangalore and in later e-mail conversations, I asked Rajesh, who is in his early thirties, to walk me through how he became a player in the global game business from Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="2382"> ˇ°The first defining moment for me dates back to the early nineties,ˇ± said Rajesh, a smallish, mustachioed figure with the ambition of a heavyweight boxer. </seg>
<seg id="2383"> ˇ°Having lived and worked in Europe, as a student, I was clear in my choice that I would not leave India. </seg>
<seg id="2384"> I wanted to do my thing from India, do something that would be globally respected and something that would make a difference in India. </seg>
<seg id="2385"> I started my company in Bangalore as a one-man operation on March 15, 1995. </seg>
<seg id="2386"> My father gave me the seed money for the bank loan that bought me a computer and a 14.4 kbp modem. </seg>
<seg id="2387"> I set out to do multimedia applications aimed at the education and industry sectors. </seg>
<seg id="2388"> By 1997, we were a five-man team. We had done some pathbreaking work in our chosen field, but we realized that this was not challenging us enough. </seg>
<seg id="2389"> End of Dhruva 1.0. </seg>
<seg id="2390"> ˇ°In March 1997, we partnered with Intel and began the process of reinventing ourselves into a gaming company. </seg>
<seg id="2391"> By mid-1998, we were showing global players what we were capable of by way of both designing games and developing the outsourced portions of games designed by others. </seg>
<seg id="2392"> On November 26, 1998, we signed our first major game development project with Infogrames Entertainment, a French gaming company. </seg>
<seg id="2393"> We did a great job on the game, but it was never published. </seg>
<seg id="2394"> It was a big blow for us, but the quality of our work spoke for itself, so we survived. </seg>
<seg id="2395"> The most important lesson we learned: We could do it, but we had to get smart. </seg>
<seg id="2396"> We had to look at positioning ourselves differently. </seg>
<seg id="2397"> End of Dhruva 2.0.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2398"> This led to the start of Dhruva's 3.0 era-positioning Dhruva as a provider of game development services. </seg>
<seg id="2399"> The computer game business is already enormous, every year grossing more revenue than Hollywood, and it already had some tradition of outsourcing game characters to countries like Canada and Australia. </seg>
<seg id="2400"> ˇ°In March 2001, we sent out our new game demo, Saloon, to the world,ˇ± said Rajesh. ˇ°The theme was the American Wild Wild West, and the setting was a saloon in a small town after business hours, with the barman cleaning up... </seg>
<seg id="2401"> None of us had ever seen a real saloon before, but we researched the look and feel [of a saloon] using the Internet and Google. </seg>
<seg id="2402"> The choice of the theme was deliberate. We wanted potential clients in the U.S.A. and Europe to be convinced that Indians can 'get it.' </seg>
<seg id="2403"> The demo was a hit, it landed us a bunch of outsourced business, and we have been a successful company ever since.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2404"> Could he have done this a decade earlier, before the world got so flat? </seg>
<seg id="2405"> ˇ°Never,ˇ± said Rajesh. </seg>
<seg id="2406"> Several things had to come together. </seg>
<seg id="2407"> The first was to have enough installed bandwidth so he could e-mail game content and instructions back and forth between his own company and his American clients. </seg>
<seg id="2408"> The second factor, said Rajesh, was the spread of PCs for use in both business and at home, with people getting very comfortable using them in a variety of tasks. </seg>
<seg id="2409"> ˇ°PCs are everywhere,ˇ± he said. ˇ°The penetration is relatively decent even in India today.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2410"> The third factor, though, was the emergence of the work flow software and Internet applications that made it possible for a Dhruva to go into business as a minimultinational from day one: Word, Outlook, NetMeeting, 3D Studio MAX. </seg>
<seg id="2411"> But Google is the key. </seg>
<seg id="2412"> ˇ°It's fantastic,ˇ± said Rajesh. </seg>
<seg id="2413"> ˇ°One of the things that's always an issue for our clients from the West is, 'Will you Indians be able to understand the subtle nuances of Western content?' Now, to a large extent, it was a very valid question. </seg>
<seg id="2414"> But the Internet has helped us to be able to aggregate different kinds of content at the touch of a button, and today if someone asks you to make something that looks like Tom and Jerry, you just say 'Google Tom &amp; Jerry' and you've got tons and tons of pictures and information and reviews and write-ups about Tom and Jerry, which you can read and simulate.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2415"> While people were focusing on the boom and bust of the dot-coms, Rajesh explained, the real revolution was taking place more quietly. It was the fact that all over the world, people, en masse, were starting to get comfortable with the new global infrastructure. </seg>
<seg id="2416"> ˇ°We are just at the beginning of being efficient in using it,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="2417"> ˇ°There is a lot more we can do with this infrastructure, as more and more people shift to becoming paperless in their offices and realize that distances really [do] not matter... </seg>
<seg id="2418"> It will supercharge all of this. It's really going to be a different world.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2419"> Moreover, in the old days, these software programs would have been priced beyond the means of a little Indian game start-up, but not anymore, thanks in part to the open-source free software movement. </seg>
<seg id="2420"> Said Rajesh, ˇ°The cost of software tools would have remained where the interested parties wanted them to be if it was not for the deluge of rather efficient freeware and shareware products that sprung up in the early 2000s. </seg>
<seg id="2421"> Microsoft Windows, Office, 3D Studio MAX, Adobe Photoshop-each of these programs would have been priced higher than they are today if not for the many freeware/shareware programs that were comparable and compelling. </seg>
<seg id="2422"> The Internet brought to the table the element of choice and instant comparison that did not exist before for a little company like ours... </seg>
<seg id="2423"> Already we have in our gaming industry artists and designers working from home, something unimaginable a few years back, given the fact that developing games is a highly interactive process. </seg>
<seg id="2424"> They connect into the company's internal system over the Internet, using a secure feature called VPN [virtual private network], making their presence no different from the guy in the next cubicle.ˇ±The Internet now makes this whole world ˇ°like one marketplace,ˇ± added Rajesh. </seg>
<seg id="2425"> ˇ°This infrastructure is not only going to facilitate sourcing of work to the best price, best quality, from the best place, it is also going to enable a great amount of sharing of practices and knowledge, and it's going to be 'I can learn from you and you can learn from me' like never before. </seg>
<seg id="2426"> It's very good for the world. </seg>
<seg id="2427"> The economy is going to drive integration and the integration is going to drive the economy.ˇ±There is no reason the United States should not benefit from this trend, Rajesh insisted. </seg>
<seg id="2428"> What Dhruva is doing is pioneering computer gaming within Indian society. When the Indian market starts to embrace gaming as a mainstream social activity, Dhruva will already be positioned to take advantage. </seg>
<seg id="2429"> But by then, he argued, the market ˇ°will be so huge that there will be a lot of opportunity for content to come from outside. </seg>
<seg id="2430"> And, hey, the Americans are way ahead in terms of the ability to know what games can work and what won't work and in terms of being at the cutting edge of design-so this is a bilateral thing... </seg>
<seg id="2431"> Every perceived dollar or opportunity that is lost today [from an American point of view because of outsourcing] is actually going to come back to you times ten, once the market here is unleashed... </seg>
<seg id="2432"> Just remember, we are a 300-million middle class-larger than the size of your country or Europe.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2433"> Yes, he noted, India right now has a great advantage in having a pool of educated, low-wage English speakers with a strong service etiquette in their DNA and an enterprising spirit. </seg>
<seg id="2434"> ˇ°So, sure, for the moment, we are leading the so-called wave of service outsourcing of various kinds of new things,ˇ± said Rajesh. ˇ°But I believe that there should be no doubt that this is just the beginning. </seg>
<seg id="2435"> If [Indians] think that they've got something going and there is something they can keep that's not going to go anywhere, that will be a big mistake, because we have got Eastern Europe, which is waking up, and we have got China, which is waiting to get on the services bandwagon to do various things. </seg>
<seg id="2436"> I mean, you can source the best product or service or capacity or competency from anywhere in the world today, because of this whole infrastructure that is being put into place. </seg>
<seg id="2437"> So as different businesses, and as different people, get more comfortable using this infrastructure, you are going to see a huge explosion. </seg>
<seg id="2438"> It is a matter of five to seven years and we will have a huge batch of excellent English-speaking Chinese graduates coming out of their universities. </seg>
<seg id="2439"> Poles and Hungarians are already very well connected, very close to Europe, and their cultures are very similar [to Western Europe's]. </seg>
<seg id="2440"> So today India is ahead, but it has to work very hard if it wants to keep this position. </seg>
<seg id="2441"> It has to never stop inventing and reinventing itself.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2442"> The raw ambition that Rajesh and so many of his generation possess is worthy of note by Americans-a point I will elaborate on later. </seg>
<seg id="2443"> ˇ°We can't relax,ˇ± said Rajesh. </seg>
<seg id="2444"> Please look at me: I am from India. </seg>
<seg id="2445"> We have been at a very different level before in terms of technology and business. </seg>
<seg id="2446"> But once we saw we had an infrastructure which made the world a small place, we promptly tried to make the best use of it. </seg>
<seg id="2447"> We saw there were so many things we could do. </seg>
<seg id="2448"> We went ahead, and today what we are seeing is a result of that... </seg>
<seg id="2449"> There is no time to rest. </seg>
<seg id="2450"> There are dozens of people who are doing the same thing you are doing, and they are trying to do it better. </seg>
<seg id="2451"> It is like water in a tray, you shake it and it will find the path of least resistance. </seg>
<seg id="2452"> That is what is going to happen to so many jobs-they will go to that corner of the world where there is the least resistance and the most opportunity. </seg>
<seg id="2453"> If there is a skilled person in Timbuktu, he will get work if he knows how to access the rest of the world, which is quite easy today. </seg>
<seg id="2454"> You can make a Web site and have an e-mail address and you are up and running. </seg>
<seg id="2455"> And if you are able to demonstrate your work, using the same infrastructure, and if people are comfortable giving work to you, and if you are diligent and clean in your transactions, then you are in business.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2456"> Instead of complaining about outsourcing, said Rajesh, Americans and Western Europeans would ˇ°be better off thinking about how you can raise your bar and raise yourselves into doing something better. </seg>
<seg id="2457"> Americans have consistently led in innovation over the last century. </seg>
<seg id="2458"> Americans whining-we have never seen that before. </seg>
<seg id="2459"> People like me have learned a lot from Americans. </seg>
<seg id="2460"> We have learned to become a little more aggressive in the way we market ourselves, which is something we would not have done given our typical British background.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2461"> So what is your overall message? </seg>
<seg id="2462"> I asked Rajesh, before leaving with my head spinning. </seg>
<seg id="2463"> ˇ°My message is that what's happening now is just the tip of the iceberg... </seg>
<seg id="2464"> What is really necessary is for everybody to wake up to the fact that there is a fundamental shift that is happening in the way people are going to do business. </seg>
<seg id="2465"> And everyone is going to have to improve themselves and be able to compete. </seg>
<seg id="2466"> Look, we just made [baseball] caps for Dhruva to give away. They came from Sri Lanka.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2467"> ˇ°Not from a factory in South Bangalore?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2468"> I asked. </seg>
<seg id="2469"> ˇ°Not from South Bangalore,ˇ± said Rajesh, ˇ°even though Bangalore is one of the export hubs for garments. </seg>
<seg id="2470"> Among the three or four caps we got quotations for, this [Sri Lankan one] was the best in terms of quality and the right price, and we thought the finish was great. </seg>
<seg id="2471"> ˇ°This is the situation you are going to see moving forward,ˇ± Rajesh concluded. ˇ°If you are seeing all this energy coming out of Indians, it's because we have been underdogs and we have that drive to kind of achieve and to get there... </seg>
<seg id="2472"> India is going to be a superpower and we are going to rule.ˇ±ˇ°Rule whom?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2473"> I asked.Rajesh laughed at his own choice of words. </seg>
<seg id="2474"> ˇ°It's not about ruling anybody. </seg>
<seg id="2475"> That's the point. </seg>
<seg id="2476"> There is nobody to rule anymore. </seg>
<seg id="2477"> It's about how you can create a great opportunity for yourself and hold on to that or keep creating new opportunities where you can thrive. </seg>
<seg id="2478"> I think today that rule is about efficiency, it's about collaboration and it is about competitiveness and it is about being a player. </seg>
<seg id="2479"> It is about staying sharp and being in the game... </seg>
<seg id="2480"> The world is a football field now and you've got to be sharp to be on the team which plays on that field. </seg>
<seg id="2481"> If you're not good enough, you're going to be sitting and watching the game. </seg>
<seg id="2482"> That's all.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2483"> How Do You Say ˇ°Zippieˇ± in Chinese? </seg>
<seg id="2484"> As in Bangalore ten years ago, the best place to meet zippies in Beijing today is in the line at the consular section of the U.S. embassy. </seg>
<seg id="2485"> In Beijing in the summer of 2004, I discovered that the quest by Chinese students for visas to study or work in America was so intense that it had spawned dedicated Internet chat rooms, where Chinese students swapped stories about which arguments worked best with which U.S. embassy consular officials. </seg>
<seg id="2486"> They even gave the U.S. diplomats names like ˇ°Amazon Goddess,ˇ± ˇ°Too Tall Baldy,ˇ± and ˇ°Handsome Guy.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2487"> Just how intensely Chinese students strategize over the Internet was revealed, U.S. embassy officials told me, when one day a rookie U.S. consular official had student after student come before him with the same line that some chat room had suggested would work for getting a visa: </seg>
<seg id="2488"> ˇ°I want to go to America to become a famous professor.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2489"> After hearing this all day, the U.S. official was suddenly surprised to get one student who came before him and pronounced, ˇ°My mother has an artificial limb and I want to go to America to learn how to build a better artificial limb for her.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2490"> The official was so relieved to hear a new line that he told the young man, ˇ°You know, this is the best story I've heard all day. </seg>
<seg id="2491"> I really salute you. </seg>
<seg id="2492"> I'm going to give you a visa.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2493"> You guessed it. </seg>
<seg id="2494"> The next day, a bunch of students showed up at the embassy saying they wanted a visa to go to America to learn how to build better artificial limbs for their mothers. </seg>
<seg id="2495"> Talking to these U.S. embassy officials in Beijing, who are the gatekeepers for these visas, it quickly became apparent to me that they had mixed feelings about the process. </seg>
<seg id="2496"> On the one hand, they were pleased that so many Chinese wanted to come study and work in America. </seg>
<seg id="2497"> On the other hand, they wanted to warn American kids: </seg>
<seg id="2498"> Do you realize what is coming your way? </seg>
<seg id="2499"> As one U.S. embassy official in Beijing said to me, ˇ°What I see happening [in China] is what has been going on for the last several decades in the rest of Asia-the tech booms, the tremendous energy of the people. </seg>
<seg id="2500"> I saw it elsewhere, but now it is happening here.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2501"> I was visiting Yale in the spring of 2004. </seg>
<seg id="2502"> As I was strolling through the central quad, near the statue of Elihu Yale, two Chinese-speaking tours came through, with Chinese tourists of all ages. </seg>
<seg id="2503"> Chinese have started to tour the world in large numbers, and as China continues to develop toward a more open society, it is quite likely that Chinese leisure tourists will alter the whole world-tourism industry. </seg>
<seg id="2504"> But Chinese are not visiting Yale just to admire the ivy. </seg>
<seg id="2505"> Consider these statistics from Yale's admissions office. </seg>
<seg id="2506"> The fall 1985 class had 71 graduate and undergraduate students from China and 1 from the Soviet Union. </seg>
<seg id="2507"> The fall 2003 class had 297 Chinese graduate and undergraduate students and 23 Russians. </seg>
<seg id="2508"> Yale's total international student contingent went from 836 in the fall of 1985 to 1,775 in the fall of 2003. </seg>
<seg id="2509"> Applications from Chinese and Russian high school students to attend Yale as undergraduates have gone from a total of 40 Chinese for the class of 2001 to 276 for the class of 2008, and 18 Russians for the class of 2001 to 30 for the class of 2008. </seg>
<seg id="2510"> In 1999, Yiting Liu, a schoolgirl from Chengdu, China, got accepted to Harvard on a full scholarship. </seg>
<seg id="2511"> Her parents then wrote a build-your-own handbook about how they managed to prepare their daughter to get accepted to Harvard. The book, in Chinese, titled Harvard Girl Yiting Liu, </seg>
<seg id="2512"> offered ˇ°scientifically proven methodsˇ± to get your Chinese kid into Harvard. </seg>
<seg id="2513"> The book became a runaway best seller in China. </seg>
<seg id="2514"> By 2003 it had sold some 3 million copies and spawned more than a dozen copycat books about how to get your kid into Columbia, Oxford, or Cambridge. </seg>
<seg id="2515"> While many Chinese aspire to go to Harvard and Yale, they aren't just waiting around to get into an American university. </seg>
<seg id="2516"> They are also trying to build their own at home. </seg>
<seg id="2517"> In 2004,1 was a speaker for the 150th anniversary of Washington University in St. Louis, a school noted for its strength in science and engineering. </seg>
<seg id="2518"> Mark Wrighton, the university's thoughtful chancellor, and I were chatting before the ceremony. </seg>
<seg id="2519"> He mentioned in passing that in the spring of 2001 he had been invited (along with many other foreign and American academic leaders) to Tsinghua University in Beijing, one of the finest in China, to participate in the celebration of its ninetieth anniversary. </seg>
<seg id="2520"> He said the invitation left him scratching his head at first: Why would any university celebrate its ninetieth anniversary-not its hundredth? </seg>
<seg id="2521"> ˇ°Perhaps a Chinese tradition?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2522"> Wrighton asked himself. </seg>
<seg id="2523"> When he arrived at Tsinghua, though, he learned the answer. </seg>
<seg id="2524"> The Chinese had brought academics from all over the world to Tsinghua-more than ten thousand people attended the ceremony-in order to make the declaration ˇ°that at the one hundredth anniversary Tsinghua University would be among the world's premier universities,ˇ± Wrighton later explained to me in an e-mail. </seg>
<seg id="2525"> ˇ°The event involved all of the leaders of the Chinese government, from the Mayor of Beijing to the head of state. </seg>
<seg id="2526"> Each expressed the conviction that an investment in the university to support its development as one of the world's great universities within ten years would be a rewarding one. </seg>
<seg id="2527"> With Tsinghua University already regarded as one of the leading universities in China, focused on science and technology, it was evident that there is a seriousness of purpose in striving for a world leadership position in [all the areas involved] in spawning technological innovation.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2528"> And as a result of China's drive to succeed, Microsoft chairman Bill Gates argued to me, the ˇ°ovarian lotteryˇ± has changed-as has the whole relationship between geography and talent. </seg>
<seg id="2529"> Thirty years ago, he said, if you had a choice between being born a genius on the outskirts of Bombay or Shanghai or being born an average person in Poughkeepsie, you would take Poughkeepsie, because your chances of thriving and living a decent life there, even with average talent, were much greater. </seg>
<seg id="2530"> But as the world has gone flat, Gates said, and so many people can now plug and play from anywhere, natural talent has started to trump geography. </seg>
<seg id="2531"> ˇ°Now,ˇ± he said, ˇ°I would rather be a genius born in China than an average guy born in Poughkeepsie.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2532"> That's what happens when the Berlin Wall turns into the Berlin mall and 3 billion people converge with all these new tools for collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="2533"> ˇ°We're going to tap into the energy and talent of five times as many people as we did before,ˇ± said Gates. </seg>
<seg id="2534"> From Russia with Love </seg>
<seg id="2535"> I didn't get a chance to visit Russia and interview Russian zippies for this book, but I did the next best thing. </seg>
<seg id="2536"> I asked my friend Thomas R. Pickering, the former U.S. ambassador to Moscow and now a top international relations executive with Boeing, to explain a new development I had heard about: that Boeing was using Russian engineers and scientists, who once worked on MiGs, to help design its next generation of passenger planes.Pickering unraveled the story for me. </seg>
<seg id="2537"> Beginning in 1991, Boeing started assigning out work to Russian scientists to take advantage of their expertise in aerodynamic problems and new aviation alloys. </seg>
<seg id="2538"> In 1998, Boeing decided to take this a step further and open an aeronautical engineering design office in Moscow. </seg>
<seg id="2539"> Boeing located the office in the twelve-story Moscow tower that McDonald's built with all the rubles it made from selling Big Macs in Moscow before the end of communism- money that McDonald's had pledged not to take out of the country. </seg>
<seg id="2540"> Seven years later, said Pickering, ˇ°we now have eight hundred Russian engineers and scientists working for us and we're going up to at least one thousand and maybe, over time, to fifteen hundred.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2541"> The way it works, he explained, is that Boeing contracts with different Russian aircraft companies-companies that were famous in the Cold War for making warplanes, companies with names like Ilyushin, Tupolev, and Sukhoi-and they provide the engineers-to-order for Boeing's different projects. </seg>
<seg id="2542"> Using French-made airplane design software, the Russian engineers collaborate with their colleagues at Boeing America -in both Seattle and Wichita, Kansas-in computer-aided airplane designs. </seg>
<seg id="2543"> Boeing has set up a twenty-four-hour workday. </seg>
<seg id="2544"> It consists of two shifts in Moscow and one shift in America. </seg>
<seg id="2545"> Using fiber-optic cables, advanced compression technologies, and aeronautical work flow software, ˇ°they just pass their designs back and forth from Moscow to America,ˇ± Pickering said. </seg>
<seg id="2546"> There are videoconferencing facilities on every floor of Boeing's Moscow office, so the engineers don't have to rely on e-mail when they have a problem to solve with their American counterparts. </seg>
<seg id="2547"> They can have a face-to-face conversation.Boeing started outsourcing airplane design work to Moscow as an experiment, a sideline; </seg>
<seg id="2548"> but today, with a shortage of aeronautical engineers in America, it is a necessity. </seg>
<seg id="2549"> Boeing's ability to blend these lower-cost Russian engineers with higher-cost, more advanced American design teams is enabling Boeing to compete head-to-head with its archrival, Airbus Industries, which is subsidized by a consortium of European governments and is using Russian talent as well. </seg>
<seg id="2550"> A U.S. aeronautical engineer costs $120 per design hour; </seg>
<seg id="2551"> a Russian costs about one-third of that.But the outsourcees are also outsourcers. </seg>
<seg id="2552"> The Russian engineers have outsourced elements of their work for Boeing to Hindustan Aeronautics in Bangalore, which specializes in digitizing airplane designs so as to make them easier to manufacture. </seg>
<seg id="2553"> In the old days, explained Pickering, Boeing would say to its Japanese subcontractors, ˇ°We will send you the plans for the wings of the 777. </seg>
<seg id="2554"> We will let you make some of them and then we will count on you buying the whole airplanes from us. </seg>
<seg id="2555"> It's a win-win.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2556"> Today Boeing says to the giant Japanese industrial company Mitsubishi, ˇ°Here are the general parameters for the wings of the new 7E7. </seg>
<seg id="2557"> You design the finished product and build it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2558"> But Japanese engineers are very expensive. </seg>
<seg id="2559"> So what happens? </seg>
<seg id="2560"> Mitsubishi outsources elements of the outsourced 7E7 wing to the same Russian engineers Boeing is using for other parts of the plane. </seg>
<seg id="2561"> Meanwhile, some of these Russian engineers and scientists are leaving the big Russian airplane companies, setting up their own firms, and Boeing is considering buying shares in some of these start-ups to have reserve engineering capacity. </seg>
<seg id="2562"> All of this global sourcing is for the purpose of designing and building planes faster and cheaper, so that Boeing can use its cash to keep innovating for the next generation and survive the withering competition from Airbus. </seg>
<seg id="2563"> Thanks to the triple convergence, it now takes Boeing eleven days to build a 737, down from twenty-eight days just a few years ago. </seg>
<seg id="2564"> Boeing will build its next generation of planes in three days, because all the parts are being computer-designed for assembly, and Boeing's global supply chain will enable it to move parts from one facility to another just in time. </seg>
<seg id="2565"> To make sure that it is getting the best deals on its parts and other supplies, Boeing now runs regular ˇ°reverse auctions,ˇ± in which companies bid down against each other rather than bid up against each other. </seg>
<seg id="2566"> They bid for contracts on everything from toilet paper for the Boeing factories to nuts and bolts-the off-the-shelf commodity parts-for Boeing's supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="2567"> Boeing will announce an auction for a stated time on a specially designed Internet site. </seg>
<seg id="2568"> It will begin the auction for each supply item at what it considers a fair price. </seg>
<seg id="2569"> Then it will just sit back and watch how far each supplier wants to undercut the others to win Boeing's business. </seg>
<seg id="2570"> Bidders are prequalified by Boeing, and everyone can see everyone else's bids as they are submitted. </seg>
<seg id="2571"> ˇ°You can really see the pressures of the marketplace and how they work,ˇ± said Pickering. </seg>
<seg id="2572"> ˇ°It's like watching a horse race.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2573"> The Other Triple Convergence </seg>
<seg id="2574"> I once heard Bill Bradley tell a story about a high-society woman from Boston who goes to San Francisco for the first time. </seg>
<seg id="2575"> When she comes home and is asked by a friend how she liked it, she says, ˇ°Not very much-it's too far from the ocean.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2576"> The perspective and predispositions that you carry around in your head are very important in shaping what you see and what you don't see. </seg>
<seg id="2577"> That helps to explain why a lot of people missed the triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2578"> Their heads were completely somewhere else-even though it was happening right before their eyes. </seg>
<seg id="2579"> Three other things-another convergence- came together to create this smoke screen. </seg>
<seg id="2580"> The first was the dot-com bust, which began in March 2001. </seg>
<seg id="2581"> As I said earlier, many people wrongly equated the dot-com boom with globalization. So when the dot-com boom went bust, and so many dot-coms (and the firms that supported them) imploded, these same people assumed that globalization was imploding as well. </seg>
<seg id="2582"> The sudden flameout of dogfood.com and ten other Web sites offering to deliver ten pounds of puppy chow to your door in thirty minutes was supposed to be proof that globalization and the IT revolution were all sizzle and no beef. </seg>
<seg id="2583"> This was pure foolishness. </seg>
<seg id="2584"> Those who thought that globalization was the same thing as the dot-com boom and that the dot-com bust marked the end of globalization could not have been more wrong. </seg>
<seg id="2585"> To say it again, the dot-com bust actually drove globalization into hypermode by forcing companies to outsource and offshore more and more functions in order to save on scarce capital. </seg>
<seg id="2586"> This was a key factor in laying the groundwork for Globalization 3.0. </seg>
<seg id="2587"> Between the dot-com bust and today, Google went from processing roughly 150 million searches per day to roughly one billion searches per day, with only a third coming from inside the United States. </seg>
<seg id="2588"> As its auction model caught on worldwide, eBay went from twelve hundred employees in early 2000 to sixty-three hundred by 2004, all in the period when globalization was supposed to be ˇ°over.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2589"> Between 2000 and 2004, total global Internet usage grew 125 percent, including 186 percent in Africa, 209 percent in Latin America, 124 percent in Europe, and 105 percent in North America, according to Nielsen/ NetRatings. </seg>
<seg id="2590"> It was not just the dot-com bust and all the hot air surrounding it that obscured all this from view. </seg>
<seg id="2591"> There were two other big clouds that moved in. </seg>
<seg id="2592"> The biggest, of course, was 9/11, which was a profound shock to the American body politic. Given 9/11, and the Afghanistan and Iraq invasions that followed, it's not surprising that the triple convergence was lost in the fog of war and the chatter of cable television. </seg>
<seg id="2593"> Finally, there was the Enron corporate governance scandal, quickly followed by blowups at Tyco and WorldCom-which all sent CEOs and the Bush administration running for cover. </seg>
<seg id="2594"> CEOs, with some justification, became guilty until proven innocent of boardroom shenanigans, and even the slavishly probusiness, pro-CEO Bush administration was wary of appearing-in public-to be overly solicitous of the concerns of big business. </seg>
<seg id="2595"> In the spring of 2004,1 met with the head of one of America's biggest technology companies, who had come to Washington to lobby for more federal funding for the National Science Foundation to help nurture a stronger industrial base for American industry. </seg>
<seg id="2596"> I asked him why the administration wasn't convening a summit of CEOs to highlight this issue, and he just shook his head and said one word: ˇ°Enron.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2597"> The result: At the precise moment when the world was being flattened, and the triple convergence was reshaping the whole global business environment-requiring some very important adjustments in our own society and that of many other Western developed nations-American politicians not only were not educating the American public, they were actively working to make it stupid. </seg>
<seg id="2598"> During the 2004 election campaign we saw the Democrats debating whether NAFTA was a good idea and the Bush White House putting duct tape over the mouth of N. Gregory Mankiw, the chairman of the White House Council of Economic Advisers, and stashing him away in Dick Cheney's basement, because Mankiw, author of a popular college economics textbook, had dared to speak approvingly of oursourcing as just the ˇ°latest manifestation of the gains from trade that economists have talked about at least since Adam Smith.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2599"> Mankiw's statement triggered a competition for who could say the most ridiculous thing in response. </seg>
<seg id="2600"> The winner was Speaker of the House Dennis Hastert, who said that Mankiw's ˇ°theory fails a basic test of real economics.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2601"> And what test was that, Dennis? </seg>
<seg id="2602"> Poor Mankiw was barely heard from again. </seg>
<seg id="2603"> For all these reasons, most people missed the triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2604"> Something really big was happening, and it was simply not part of public discourse in America or Europe. </seg>
<seg id="2605"> Until I visited India in early 2004,1 too was largely ignorant of it, although I was picking up a few hints that something was brewing. </seg>
<seg id="2606"> One of the most thoughtful business leaders I have come to know over the years is Nobuyuki Idei, the chairman of Sony. </seg>
<seg id="2607"> Whenever he speaks, I pay close attention. </seg>
<seg id="2608"> We saw each other twice during 2004, and both times he said something through his heavy Japanese accent that stuck in my ear. </seg>
<seg id="2609"> Idei said that a change was under way in the business-technology world that would be remembered, in time, like ˇ°the meteor that hit the earth and killed all the dinosaurs.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2610"> Fortunately, the cutting-edge global companies knew what was going on out there, and the best companies were quietly adapting to it so that they would not be one of those dinosaurs. </seg>
<seg id="2611"> As I started researching this book, I felt at times like I was in a Twilight Zone segment. </seg>
<seg id="2612"> I would interview CEOs and technologists from major companies, both American-based and foreign, and they would describe in their own ways what I came to call the triple convergence. </seg>
<seg id="2613"> But, for all the reasons I explained above, most of them weren't telling the public or the politicians. </seg>
<seg id="2614"> They were either too distracted, too focused on their own businesses, or too afraid. </seg>
<seg id="2615"> Yes, they all knew the secret-but nobody wanted to tell the kids. </seg>
<seg id="2616"> Well, here's the truth that no one wanted to tell you: The world has been flattened. </seg>
<seg id="2617"> You know ˇ°the IT revolutionˇ± that the business press has been touting for the last twenty years? </seg>
<seg id="2618"> Sorry, but that was only the prologue. </seg>
<seg id="2619"> The last twenty years were just about forging, sharpening, and distributing all the new tools with which to collaborate and connect. </seg>
<seg id="2620"> Now the real IT revolution is about to begin, as all the complementarities between these tools start to really work together to level the playing field. </seg>
<seg id="2621"> One of those who pulled back the curtain and called this moment by its real name was HP's Carly Fiorina, who in 2004 began to declare in her public speeches that the dot-com boom and bust were just ˇ°the end of the beginning.ˇ± </seg>
