<seg id="2622"> The last twenty-five years in technology, said Fiorina, then the CEO of HP, have been just ˇ°the warm-up act.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2623"> Now we are going into the main event, she said, ˇ°and by the main event, I mean an era in which technology will literally transform every aspect of business, every aspect of life and every aspect of society.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2624"> FOUR The Great Sorting Out </seg>
<seg id="2625"> Because when the world starts to move from a primarily vertical (command and control) value-creation model to an increasingly horizontal (connect and collaborate) creation model, it doesn't affect just how business gets done. </seg>
<seg id="2626"> It affects everything-how communities and companies define themselves, where companies and communities stop and start, how individuals balance their different identities as consumers, employees, shareholders, and citizens, and what role government has to play. </seg>
<seg id="2627"> I first began thinking about the great sorting out after a conversation with Harvard University's noted political theorist Michael J. Sandel. </seg>
<seg id="2628"> Sandel startled me slightly by remarking that the sort of flattening process that I was describing was actually first identified by Karl Marx and Friedrich Engels in the Communist Manifesto, published in 1848. </seg>
<seg id="2629"> While the shrinking and flattening of the world that we are seeing today constitute a difference of degree from what Marx saw happening in his day, said Sandel, it is nevertheless part of the same historical trend Marx highlighted in his writings on capitalism-the inexorable march of technology and capital to remove all barriers, boundaries, frictions, and restraints to global commerce. </seg>
<seg id="2630"> ˇ°Marx was one of the first to glimpse the possibility of the world as a global market, uncomplicated by national boundaries,ˇ± Sandel explained. </seg>
<seg id="2631"> In the Communist Manifesto, he described capitalism as a force that would dissolve all feudal, national, and religious identities, giving rise to a universal civilization governed by market imperatives. </seg>
<seg id="2632"> Marx considered it inevitable that capital would have its way-inevitable and also desirable. </seg>
<seg id="2633"> Because once capitalism destroyed all national and religious allegiances, Marx thought, it would lay bare the stark struggle between capital and labor. </seg>
<seg id="2634"> Forced to compete in a global race to the bottom, the workers of the world would unite in a global revolution to end oppression. Deprived of consoling distractions such as patriotism and religion, they would see their exploitation clearly and rise up to end it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2635"> Indeed, reading the Communist Manifesto today, I am in awe at how incisively Marx detailed the forces that were flattening the world during the rise of the Industrial Revolution, and how much he foreshadowed the way these same forces would keep flattening the world right up to the present. </seg>
<seg id="2636"> In what is probably the key paragraph of the Communist Manifesto, Marx and Engels wrote:All fixed, fast, frozen relations, with their train of ancient and venerable prejudices and opinions, are swept away, all new-formed ones become antiquated before they can ossify. </seg>
<seg id="2637"> All that is solid melts into air, all that is holy is profaned, and man is at last compelled to face with sober senses his real conditions of life and his relations with his kind. </seg>
<seg id="2638"> The need of a constantly expanding market for its products chases the bourgeoisie over the whole surface of the globe. </seg>
<seg id="2639"> It must nestle everywhere, settle everywhere, establish connections everywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2640"> The bourgeoisie has through its exploitation of the world market given a cosmopolitan character to production and consumption in every country. </seg>
<seg id="2641"> To the great chagrin of reactionaries, it has drawn from under the feet of industry the national ground on which it stood. </seg>
<seg id="2642"> All old-established national industries have been destroyed or are daily being destroyed. </seg>
<seg id="2643"> They are dislodged by new industries, whose introduction becomes a life and death question for all civilised nations, by industries that no longer work up indigenous raw material, but raw material drawn from the remotest zones; </seg>
<seg id="2644"> industries whose products are consumed, not only at home, but in every quarter of the globe. </seg>
<seg id="2645"> In place of the old wants, satisfied by the production of the country, we find new wants, requiring for their satisfaction the products of distant lands and climes. </seg>
<seg id="2646"> In place of the old local and national seclusion and self-sufficiency, we have intercourse in every direction, universal inter-dependence of nations. </seg>
<seg id="2647"> And as in material, so also in intellectual production. </seg>
<seg id="2648"> The intellectual creations of individual nations become common property. </seg>
<seg id="2649"> National one-sidedness and narrow-mindedness become more and more impossible, and from the numerous national and local literatures there arises a world literature. </seg>
<seg id="2650"> The bourgeoisie, by the rapid improvement of all instruments of production, by the immensely facilitated means of communication, draws all, even the most barbarian nations into civilisation. </seg>
<seg id="2651"> The cheap prices of commodities are the heavy artillery with which it barters down all Chinese walls, with which it forces the barbarians' intensely obstinate hatred of foreigners to capitulate. </seg>
<seg id="2652"> It compels all nations, on pain of extinction, to adopt the bourgeois mode of production; it compels them to introduce what it calls civilisation into their midst, i.e., to become bourgeois themselves. </seg>
<seg id="2653"> In one word, it creates a world after its own image. </seg>
<seg id="2654"> It is hard to believe that Marx published that in 1848. </seg>
<seg id="2655"> Referring to the Communist Manifesto, Sandel told me, ˇ°You are arguing something similar. </seg>
<seg id="2656"> What you are arguing is that developments in information technology are enabling companies to squeeze out all the inefficiencies and friction from their markets and business operations. </seg>
<seg id="2657"> That is what your notion of'flattening' really means. </seg>
<seg id="2658"> But a flat, frictionless world is a mixed blessing. </seg>
<seg id="2659"> It may, as you suggest, be good for global business. Or it may, as Marx believed, augur well for a proletarian revolution. </seg>
<seg id="2660"> But it may also pose a threat to the distinctive places and communities that give us our bearings, that locate us in the world. </seg>
<seg id="2661"> From the first stirrings of capitalism, people have imagined the possibility of the world as a perfect market- unimpeded by protectionist pressures, disparate legal systems, cultural and linguistic differences, or ideological disagreement. </seg>
<seg id="2662"> But this vision has always bumped up against the world as it actually is-full of sources of friction and inefficiency. </seg>
<seg id="2663"> Some obstacles to a frictionless global market are truly sources of waste and lost opportunities. But some of these inefficiencies are institutions, habits, cultures, and traditions that people cherish precisely because they reflect nonmarket values like social cohesion, religious faith, and national pride. </seg>
<seg id="2664"> If global markets and new communications technologies flatten those differences, we may lose something important. </seg>
<seg id="2665"> That is why the debate about capitalism has been, from the very beginning, about which frictions, barriers, and boundaries are mere sources of waste and inefficiency, and which are sources of identity and belonging that we should try to protect. </seg>
<seg id="2666"> From the telegraph to the Internet, every new communications technology has promised to shrink the distance between people, to increase access to information, and to bring us ever closer to the dream of a perfectly efficient, frictionless global market. </seg>
<seg id="2667"> And each time, the question for society arises with renewed urgency: To what extent should we stand aside, 'get with the program,' and do all we can to squeeze out yet more inefficiencies, and to what extent should we lean against the current for the sake of values that global markets can't supply? </seg>
<seg id="2668"> Some sources of friction are worth protecting, even in the face of a global economy that threatens to flatten them.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2669"> The biggest source of friction, of course, has always been the nation-state, with its clearly defined boundaries and laws. </seg>
<seg id="2670"> Are national boundaries a source of friction we should want to preserve, or even can preserve, in a flat world? </seg>
<seg id="2671"> What about legal barriers to the free flow of information, intellectual property, and capital-such as copyrights, worker protections, and minimum wages? </seg>
<seg id="2672"> In the wake of the triple convergence, the more the flattening forces reduce friction and barriers, the sharper the challenge they will pose to the nation-state and to the particular cultures, values, national identities, democratic traditions, and bonds of restraint that have historically provided some protection and cushioning for workers and communities. </seg>
<seg id="2673"> Which do we keep and which do we let melt away into air so we can all collaborate more easily? </seg>
<seg id="2674"> This will take some sorting out, which is why the point that Michael Sandel raises is critical and is sure to be at the forefront of political debate both within and between nation-states in the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="2675"> As Sandel argued, what I call collaboration could be seen by others as just a nice name for the ability to hire cheap labor in India. </seg>
<seg id="2676"> You cannot deny that when you look at it from an American perspective. </seg>
<seg id="2677"> From the Indian worker's perspective, that same form of collaboration, outsourcing, could be seen as another name for empowering individuals in the developing world as never before, enabling them to nurture, exploit, and profit from their God-given intellectual talents-talents that before the flattening of the world often rotted on the docks of Bombay and Calcutta. </seg>
<seg id="2678"> Looking at it from the American corner of the flat world, you might conclude that the frictions, barriers, and values that restrain outsourcing should be maintained, maybe even strengthened. </seg>
<seg id="2679"> But from the point of view of Indians, fairness, justice, and their own aspirations demand that those same barriers and sources of friction be removed. </seg>
<seg id="2680"> In the flat world, one person's economic liberation could be another's unemployment. </seg>
<seg id="2681"> India versus Indiana: Who Is Exploiting Whom? </seg>
<seg id="2682"> Consider this case of multiple identity disorder. </seg>
<seg id="2683"> In 2003, the state of Indiana put out to bid a contract to upgrade the state's computer systems that process unemployment claims. </seg>
<seg id="2684"> Guess who won? </seg>
<seg id="2685"> Tata America International, which is the U.S.-based subsidiary of India's Tata Consultancy Services Ltd. </seg>
<seg id="2686"> Tata's bid of $15.2 million came in $8.1 million lower than that of its closest rivals, the New York-based companies Deloitte Consulting and Accenture Ltd. </seg>
<seg id="2687"> No Indiana firms bid on the contract, because it was too big for them to handle. </seg>
<seg id="2688"> In other words, an Indian consulting firm won the contract to upgrade the unemployment department of the state of Indiana! </seg>
<seg id="2689"> Indiana was outsourcing the very department that would cushion the people of Indiana from the effects of outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="2690"> Tata was planning to send some sixty-five contract employees to work in the Indiana Government Center, alongside eighteen state workers. </seg>
<seg id="2691"> Tata also said it would hire local subcontractors and do some local recruiting, but most workers would come from India to do the computer overhauls, which, once completed, were ˇ°supposed to speed the processing of unemployment claims, as well as save postage and reduce hassles for businesses that pay unemployment taxes,ˇ± the Indianapolis Star reported on June 25, 2004. </seg>
<seg id="2692"> You can probably guess how the story ended. </seg>
<seg id="2693"> ˇ°Top aides to then-Gov. Frank O'Bannon had signed off on the politically sensitive, four-year contract before his death [on] September 13, [2003],ˇ± the Star reported. </seg>
<seg id="2694"> But when word of the contract was made public, Republicans made it a campaign issue. </seg>
<seg id="2695"> It became such a political hot potato that Governor Joe Kernan, a Democrat who had succeeded O'Bannon, ordered the state agency, which helps out-of-work Indiana residents, to cancel the contract-and also to put up some legal barriers and friction to prevent such a thing from happening again. </seg>
<seg id="2696"> He also ordered that the contract be broken up into smaller bites that Indiana firms could bid for-good for Indiana firms but very costly and inefficient for the state. </seg>
<seg id="2697"> The Indianapolis Star reported that a check for $993,587 was sent to pay off Tata for eight weeks of work, during which it had trained forty-five state programmers in the development and engineering of up-to-date software: ˇ°'The company was great to work with,' said Alan Degner, Indiana's commissioner of workforce development.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2698"> So now I have just one simple question: Who is the exploiter and who is the exploited in this India-Indiana story? The American arm of an Indian consulting firm proposes to save the taxpayers of Indiana $8.1 million by revamping their computersˇŞusing both its Indian employees and local hires from Indiana. </seg>
<seg id="2699"> The deal would greatly benefit the American arm of the Indian consultancy; </seg>
<seg id="2700"> it would benefit some Indiana tech workers; </seg>
<seg id="2701"> and it would save Indiana state residents precious tax dollars that could be deployed to hire more state workers somewhere else, or build new schools that would permanently shrink its roles of unemployed. </seg>
<seg id="2702"> And yet the whole contract, which was signed by pro-labor Democrats, got torn up under pressure from free-trade Republicans </seg>
<seg id="2703"> In the old world, where value was largely being created vertically, usually within a single company and from the top down, it was very easy to see who was on the top and who was on the bottom, who was exploiting and who was being exploited. </seg>
<seg id="2704"> But when the world starts to flatten out and value increasingly gets created horizontally (through multiple forms of collaboration, in which individuals and little guys have much more power), who is on the top and who is on the bottom, who is exploiter and who is exploited, gets very complicated. </seg>
<seg id="2705"> Some of our old political reflexes no longer apply. </seg>
<seg id="2706"> Were the Indian engineers not being ˇ°exploitedˇ± when their government educated them in some of the best technical institutes in the world inside India, but then that same Indian government pursued a socialist economic policy that could not provide those engineers with work in India, so that those who could not get out of India had to drive taxis to eat? </seg>
<seg id="2707"> Are those same engineers now being exploited when they join the biggest consulting company in India, are paid a very comfortable wage in Indian terms, and, thanks to the flat world, can now apply their skills globally? </seg>
<seg id="2708"> Or are those Indian engineers now exploiting the people of Indiana by offering to revamp their state unemployment system for much less money than an American consulting firm? </seg>
<seg id="2709"> Or were the people of Indiana exploiting those cheaper Indian engineers? </seg>
<seg id="2710"> Someone please tell me: Who is exploiting whom in this story? </seg>
<seg id="2711"> With whom does the traditional Left stand in this story? </seg>
<seg id="2712"> With the knowledge workers from the developing world, being paid a decent wage, who are trying to use their hard-won talents in the developed world? </seg>
<seg id="2713"> Or with the politicians of Indiana, who wanted to deprive these Indian engineers of work so that it could be done, more expensively, by their constituents? </seg>
<seg id="2714"> And with whom does the traditional Right stand in this story? </seg>
<seg id="2715"> With those who want to hold down taxes and shrink the state budget of Indiana by outsourcing some work, or with those who say, ˇ°Let's raise taxes more in order to reserve the work here and reserve it just for people from Indianaˇ±? </seg>
<seg id="2716"> If you are against globalization because you think it harms people in developing countries, whose side are you on in this story: India's or Indiana's? </seg>
<seg id="2717"> The India versus Indiana dispute highlights the difficulties in drawing lines between the interests of two communities that never before imagined they were connected, much less collaborators. </seg>
<seg id="2718"> But suddenly they each woke up and discovered that in a flat world, where work increasingly becomes a horizontal collaboration, they were not only connected and collaborating but badly in need of a social contract to govern their relations. </seg>
<seg id="2719"> The larger point here is this: Whether we are talking about management science or political science, manufacturing or research and development, many, many players and processes are going to have to come to grips with ˇ°horizontalization.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2720"> And it is going to take a lot of sorting out. </seg>
<seg id="2721"> Where Do Companies Stop and Start? </seg>
<seg id="2722"> Tust as the relationship between different groups of workers will have to I be sorted out in a flat world, so too will the relationship between companies and the communities in which they operate. </seg>
<seg id="2723"> Whose values will govern a particular company and whose interests will that company respect and promote? </seg>
<seg id="2724"> It used to be said that as General Motors goes, so goes America. </seg>
<seg id="2725"> But today it would be said, ˇ°As Dell goes, so goes Malaysia, Taiwan, China, Ireland, India...ˇ± HP today has 142,000 employees in 178 countries. </seg>
<seg id="2726"> It is not only the largest consumer technology company in the world; it is the largest IT company in Europe, the largest IT company in Russia, the largest IT company in the Middle East, and the largest IT company in South Africa. </seg>
<seg id="2727"> Is HP an American company if a majority of its employees and customers are outside of America, even though it is headquartered in Palo Alto? </seg>
<seg id="2728"> Corporations cannot survive today as entities bounded by any single nation-state, not even one as big as the United States. </seg>
<seg id="2729"> So the current keep-you-awake-at-night issue for nation-states and their citizens is how to deal with corporations that are no longer bounded by a thing called the nation-state. </seg>
<seg id="2730"> To whom are they loyal? </seg>
<seg id="2731"> ˇ°Corporate America has done very well, and there is nothing wrong with that, but it has done well by aligning itself with the flat world,ˇ± said Dinakar Singh, the hedge fund manager. ˇ°It has done that by outsourcing as many components as possible to the cheapest, most efficient suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="2732"> If Dell can build every component of its computers in coastal China and sell them in coastal America, Dell benefits, and American consumers benefit, but it is hard to make the case that American labor benefits.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2733"> So Dell wants as flat a world as possible, with as little friction and as few barriers as possible. So do most other corporations today, because this allows them to build things in the most low-cost, efficient markets and sell in the most lucrative markets. </seg>
<seg id="2734"> There is almost nothing about Globalization 3.0 that is not good for capital. </seg>
<seg id="2735"> Capitalists can sit back, buy up any innovation, and then hire the best, cheapest labor input from anywhere into the world to research it, develop it, produce it, and distribute it. </seg>
<seg id="2736"> Dell stock does well, Dell shareholders do well, Dell customers do well, and the Nasdaq does well. </seg>
<seg id="2737"> All the things related to capital do fine. </seg>
<seg id="2738"> But only some American workers will benefit, and only some communities. Others will feel the pain that the flattening of the world brings about. </seg>
<seg id="2739"> Since multinationals first started scouring the earth for labor and markets, their interests have always gone beyond those of the nation-state in which they were headquartered. </seg>
<seg id="2740"> But what is going on today, on the flat earth, is such a difference of degree that it amounts to a difference in kind. </seg>
<seg id="2741"> Companies have never had more freedom, and less friction, in the way of assigning research, low-end manufacturing, and high-end manufacturing anywhere in the world. </seg>
<seg id="2742"> What this will mean for the long-term relationship between companies and the country in which they are headquartered is simply unclear. </seg>
<seg id="2743"> Consider this vivid example: On December 7, 2004, IBM announced that it was selling its whole Personal Computing Division to the Chinese computer company Lenovo to create a new worldwide PC company- the globe's third largest-with approximately $12 billion in annual revenue. </seg>
<seg id="2744"> Simultaneously, though, IBM said that it would be taking an 18.9 percent equity stake in Lenovo, creating a strategic alliance between IBM and Lenovo in PC sales, financing, and service worldwide. </seg>
<seg id="2745"> The new combined company's worldwide headquarters, it was announced, would be in New York, but its principal manufacturing operations would be in Beijing and Raleigh, North Carolina; research centers would be in China, the United States, and Japan; and sales offices would be around the world. </seg>
<seg id="2746"> The new Lenovo will be the preferred supplier of PCs to IBM, and IBM will also be the new Lenovo's preferred supplier of services and financing. </seg>
<seg id="2747"> Are you still with me? </seg>
<seg id="2748"> About ten thousand people will move from IBM to Lenovo, which was created in 1984 and was the first company to introduce the home computer concept in China. </seg>
<seg id="2749"> Since 1997, Lenovo has been the leading PC brand in China. </seg>
<seg id="2750"> My favorite part of the press release is the following, which identifies the new company's senior executives. </seg>
<seg id="2751"> ˇ°Yang Yuanqing-Chairman of the Board. [He's currently CEO of Lenovo.] </seg>
<seg id="2752"> Steve Ward-Chief Executive Officer. [He's currently IBM's senior vice president and general manager of IBM's Personal Systems Group.] </seg>
<seg id="2753"> Fran O'Sullivan-Chief Operating Officer. [She's currently general manager of IBM's PC division.] </seg>
<seg id="2754"> Mary Ma-Chief Financial Officer. [She's currently CFO of Lenovo.]ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2755"> Talk about horizontal value creation: This new Chinese-owned computer company headquartered in New York with factories in Raleigh and Beijing will have a Chinese chairman, an American CEO, an American CPO, and a Chinese CFO, and it will be listed on the Hong Kong stock exchange. </seg>
<seg id="2756"> Would you call this an American company? </seg>
<seg id="2757"> A Chinese company? </seg>
<seg id="2758"> To which country will Lenovo feel most attached? </seg>
<seg id="2759"> Or will it just see itself sort of floating above a flat earth? </seg>
<seg id="2760"> This question was anticipated in the press release announcing the new company: ˇ°Where will Lenovo be headquartered?ˇ± it asked. </seg>
<seg id="2761"> Answer: ˇ°As a global business, the new Lenovo will be geographically dispersed, with people and physical assets located worldwide.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2762"> Sort that out. </seg>
<seg id="2763"> The cold, hard truth is that management, shareholders, and investors are largely indifferent to where their profits come from or even where the employment is created. But they do want sustainable companies. </seg>
<seg id="2764"> Politicians, though, are compelled to stimulate the creation of jobs in a certain place. And residents-whether they are Americans, Europeans, or Indians-want to know that the good jobs are going to stay close to home. </seg>
<seg id="2765"> The CEO of a major European multinational remarked to me, ˇ°We are a global research company now.ˇ± That's great news for his shareholders and investors. </seg>
<seg id="2766"> He is accessing the best brains on the planet, wherever they are, and almost certainly saving money by not doing all the research in his backyard. </seg>
<seg id="2767"> ˇ°But ultimately,ˇ± he confided to me, ˇ°this is going to have implications down the road on jobs in my own country-maybe not this year but in five or fifteen years.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2768"> As a CEO and European Union citizen, ˇ°you might have a dialogue with your government about how we can retain capabilities in [our own country]-but day by day you have to make decisions with the shareholders in mind.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2769"> Translation: If I can buy five brilliant researchers in China and/or India for the price of one in Europe or America, I will buy the five; </seg>
<seg id="2770"> and if, in the long run, that means my own society loses part of its skills base, so be it. </seg>
<seg id="2771"> The only way to converge the interests of the two-the company and its country of origin-is to have a really smart population that can not only claim its slice of the bigger global pie but invent its own new slices as well. </seg>
<seg id="2772"> ˇ°We have grown addicted to our high salaries, and now we are really going to have to earn them,ˇ± the CEO said. </seg>
<seg id="2773"> But even identifying a company's country of origin today is getting harder and harder. </seg>
<seg id="2774"> Sir John Rose, the chief executive of Rolls-Royce, told me once, ˇ°We have a big business in Germany. </seg>
<seg id="2775"> We are the biggest high-tech employer in the state of Brandenburg. </seg>
<seg id="2776"> I was recently at a dinner with Chancellor [Gerhard] Schroeder. </seg>
<seg id="2777"> And he said to me, ˇ±You are a German company, why don't you come along with me on my next visit to Russiaˇ°ˇŞto try to drum up business there for German companies.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2778"> The German chancellor, said Rose, ˇ°was recognizing that although my headquarters were in London, my business was involved in creating value in Germany, and that could be constructive in his relationship with Russia.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2779"> Here you have the quintessential British company, Rolls-Royce, which, though still headquartered in England, now operates through a horizontal global supply chain, and its CEO, a British citizen knighted by the queen, is being courted by the chancellor of Germany to help him drum up business in Russia, because one link in the Rolls-Royce supply chain happens to run through Brandenburg. </seg>
<seg id="2780"> Sort that out. </seg>
<seg id="2781"> From Command and Control to Collaborate and Connect </seg>
<seg id="2782"> Before Colin Powell stepped down as secretary of state, I went in for an interview, which was also attended by two of his press advisers, in his seventh-floor State Department suite. </seg>
<seg id="2783"> I could not resist asking him about where he was when he realized the world had gone flat. </seg>
<seg id="2784"> He answered with one word: ˇ°Google.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2785"> Powell said that when he took over as secretary of state in 2001, and he needed some bit of information-say, the text of a UN resolution -he would call an aide and have to wait for minutes or even hours for someone to dig it up for him. </seg>
<seg id="2786"> ˇ°Now I just type into Google 'UNSC Resolution 242' and up comes the text,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="2787"> Powell explained that with each passing year, he found himself doing more and more of his own research, at which point one of his press advisers remarked, ˇ°Yes, now he no longer comes asking for information. </seg>
<seg id="2788"> He already has the information. </seg>
<seg id="2789"> He comes asking for action.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2790"> Powell, a former member of the AOL board, also regularly used e-mail to contact other foreign ministers and, according to one of his aides, kept up a constant instant-messaging relationship with Britain's foreign secretary, Jack Straw, at summit meetings, as if they were a couple of college students. </seg>
<seg id="2791"> Thanks to the cell phone and wireless technology, said Powell, no foreign minister can run and hide from him. </seg>
<seg id="2792"> He said he had been looking for Russia's foreign minister the previous week. </seg>
<seg id="2793"> First he tracked him down on his cell phone in Moscow, then on his cell phone in Iceland, and then on his cell phone in Vientiane, Laos. </seg>
<seg id="2794"> ˇ°We have everyone's cell phone number,ˇ± said Powell of his fellow foreign ministers. </seg>
<seg id="2795"> The point I take away from all this is that when the world goes flat, hierarchies are not being leveled just by little people being able to act big. They are also being leveled by big people being able to act really smallˇŞin the sense that they are enabled to do many more things on their own. </seg>
<seg id="2796"> It really hit me when Powell's junior media adviser, a young woman, walked me down from his office and remarked along the way that because of e-mail, Powell could get hold of her and her boss at any hour, via their BlackBerrys-and did. </seg>
<seg id="2797"> ˇ°I can't get away from the guy,'' she said jokingly of his constant e-mail instructions. </seg>
<seg id="2798"> But in the next breath she added that on the previous weekend, she was shopping at the mall with some friends when she got an instant message from Powell asking her to do some public affairs task. </seg>
<seg id="2799"> ˇ±My friends were all impressed,ˇ° she said. </seg>
<seg id="2800"> ˇ±Little me, and I'm talking to the secretary of state!ˇ° </seg>
<seg id="2801"> This is what happens when you move from a vertical (command and control) world to a much more horizontal (connect and collaborate) flat world. </seg>
<seg id="2802"> Your boss can do his job and your job. </seg>
<seg id="2803"> He can be secretary of state and his own secretary. </seg>
<seg id="2804"> He can give you instructions day or night. </seg>
<seg id="2805"> Bosses, if they are inclined, can collaborate more directly with more of their staff than ever before-no matter who they are or where they are in the hierarchy. </seg>
<seg id="2806"> But staffers will also have to work much harder to be better informed than their bosses. </seg>
<seg id="2807"> There are a lot more conversations between bosses and staffers today that start like this: ˇ°I know that already! </seg>
<seg id="2808"> I Googled it myself. </seg>
<seg id="2809"> Multiple Identity Disorder  It is not only communities and companies that have multiple identities that will need sorting out in a flat world. So too will individuals. </seg>
<seg id="2810"> In a flat world, the tensions among our identities as consumers, employees, citizens, taxpayers, and shareholders are going to come into sharper and sharper conflict. </seg>
<seg id="2811"> ˇ°In the nineteenth century,ˇ± said business consultant Michael Hammer, ˇ°the great conflict was between labor and capital. </seg>
<seg id="2812"> Now it is between customer and worker, and the company is the guy in the middle. </seg>
<seg id="2813"> The consumer turns to the company and says, 'Give me more for less.' </seg>
<seg id="2814"> And then companies turn to employees and say, 'If we don't give them more for less, we are in trouble. </seg>
<seg id="2815"> I can't guarantee you a job and a union steward can't guarantee you a job, only a customer can.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2816"> The New York Times reported (November 1, 2004) that Wal-Mart spent about $1.3 billion of its $256 billion in revenue in 2003 on employee health care, to insure about 537,000 people, or about 45 percent of its workforce. </seg>
<seg id="2817"> Wal-Mart's biggest competitor, though, Costco Wholesale, insured 96 percent of its eligible full-time or part-time employees. Costco employees become eligible for health insurance after three months working full-time or six months working part-time. </seg>
<seg id="2818"> At Wal-Mart, most full-time employees have to wait six months to become eligible, while part-timers are not eligible for at least two years. </seg>
<seg id="2819"> According to the Times, full-time employees at Wal-Mart make about $1,200 per month, or $8 per hour. </seg>
<seg id="2820"> Wal-Mart requires employees to cover 33 percent of the cost of their benefits, and it plans to reduce that employee contribution to 30 percent. </seg>
<seg id="2821"> Wal-Mart-sponsored health plans have monthly premiums for family coverage ranging as high as $264 and out-of-pocket expenses as high as $13,000 in some cases, and such medical costs make health coverage unaffordable even for many Wal-Mart employees who are covered, the Times said. </seg>
<seg id="2822"> But the same article went on to say this: ˇ°If there is any place where Wal-Mart's labor costs find support, it is Wall Street, where Costco has taken a drubbing from analysts who say its labor costs are too high.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2823"> Wal-Mart has taken more fat and friction out than Costco, which has kept more in, because it feels a different obligation to its workers. </seg>
<seg id="2824"> Costco's pretax profit margin is only 2.7 percent of revenue, less than half Wal-Mart's margin of 5.5 percent. </seg>
<seg id="2825"> The Wal-Mart shopper in all of us wants the lowest price possible, with all the middlemen, fat, and friction removed. </seg>
<seg id="2826"> And the Wal-Mart shareholder in us wants Wal-Mart to be relentless about removing the fat and friction in its supply chain and in its employee benefits packages, in order to fatten the company's profits. </seg>
<seg id="2827"> But the Wal-Mart worker in us hates the benefits and pay packages that Wal-Mart offers its starting employees. </seg>
<seg id="2828"> And the Wal-Mart citizen in us knows that because Wal-Mart, the biggest company in America, doesn't cover all its employees with health care, some of them will just go to the emergency ward of the local hospital and the taxpayers will end up picking up the tab. </seg>
<seg id="2829"> The Times reported that a survey by Georgia officials found that ˇ°more than 10,000 children of Wal-Mart employees were in the state's health program for children at an annual cost of nearly $10 million to taxpayers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2830"> Similarly, it said, a ˇ°North Carolina hospital found that 31 percent of 1,900 patients who described themselves as Wal-Mart employees were on Medicaid, while an additional 16 percent had no insurance at all.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2831"> In her 2004 book, Selling Women Short: The Landmark Battle for Workers' Rights at Wal-Mart, journalist Liza Featherstone followed the huge women's discrimination suit against Wal-Mart. </seg>
<seg id="2832"> In an interview about the book with Salon.com (November 22, 2004), she made the following important point: ˇ°American taxpayers chip in to pay for many full-time Wal-Mart employees because they usually require incremental health insurance, public housing, food stamps -there are so many ways in which Wal-Mart employees are not able to be self-sufficient. </seg>
<seg id="2833"> This is very ironic, because Sam Walton is embraced as the American symbol of self-sufficiency. </seg>
<seg id="2834"> It is really troubling and dishonest that Wal-Mart supports Republican candidates in the way that they do: 80 percent of their corporate campaign contributions go to Republicans. </seg>
<seg id="2835"> But Republicans tend not to support the types of public assistance programs that Wal-Mart depends on. </seg>
<seg id="2836"> If anything, Wal-Mart should be crusading for national health insurance. </seg>
<seg id="2837"> They should at least be acknowledging that because they are unable to provide these things for their employees, we should have a more general welfare state.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2838"> As you sort out and weigh your multiple identities-consumer, employee, citizen, taxpayer, shareholder-you have to decide: Do you prefer the Wal-Mart approach or the Costco approach? </seg>
<seg id="2839"> This is going to be an important political issue in a flat world: Just how flat do you want corporations to be when you factor in all your different identities? </seg>
<seg id="2840"> Because when you take the middleman out of business, when you totally flatten your supply chain, you also take a certain element of humanity out of life. </seg>
<seg id="2841"> The same question applies to government. </seg>
<seg id="2842"> How flat do you want government to be? </seg>
<seg id="2843"> How much friction would you like to see government remove, through deregulation, to make it easier for companies to compete on Planet Flat? </seg>
<seg id="2844"> Said Congressman Rahm Emanuel, an Illinois Democrat who was a senior adviser to President Clinton, ˇ°When I served in the White House, we streamlined the FDA's drug approval process in response to concerns about its cumbersome nature. </seg>
<seg id="2845"> We took those steps with one objective in mind: to move drugs to the marketplace more quickly. </seg>
<seg id="2846"> The result, however, has been an increasingly cozy relationship between the FDA and the pharmaceutical industry, which has put public health at risk. </seg>
<seg id="2847"> The Vioxx debacle [over an anti-inflammatory drug that was found to lead to an increased risk for heart attacks and strokes] shows the extent to which drug safety has taken a backseat to speedy approval. </seg>
<seg id="2848"> A recent Senate hearing on Vioxx's recall revealed major deficiencies in the FDA's ability to remove dangerous drugs from the market.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2849"> As consumers we want the cheapest drugs that the global supply chains can offer, but as citizens we want and need government to oversee and regulate that supply chain, even if it means preserving or adding friction. </seg>
<seg id="2850"> Sort that out. </seg>
<seg id="2851"> Who Owns What? </seg>
<seg id="2852"> Something else is absolutely going to have to be sorted out in a flat world: Who owns what? </seg>
<seg id="2853"> How do we build legal barriers to protect an innovator's intellectual property so he or she can reap its financial benefits and plow those profits into a new invention? </seg>
<seg id="2854"> And from the other side, how do we keep walls low enough so that we encourage the sharing of intellectual property, which is required more and more to do cutting-edge innovation? </seg>
<seg id="2855"> ˇ°The world is decidedly not flat when it comes to uniform treatment of intellectual property,ˇ± said Craig Mundie, Microsoft's chief technology officer. </seg>
<seg id="2856"> It is wonderful, he noted, to have a world where a single innovator can summon so many resources by himself or herself, assemble a team of partners from around the flat world, and make a real breakthrough with some product or service. </seg>
<seg id="2857"> But what does that wonderful innovative engineer do, asked Mundie, ˇ°when someone else uses the same flat-world platform and tools to clone and distribute his wonderful new product?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2858"> This happens in the world of software, music, and pharmaceuticals every day. </seg>
<seg id="2859"> And the technology is reaching a point now where ˇ°you should assume that there isn't anything that can't be counterfeited quicklyˇ±-from Microsoft Word to airplane parts, he added. </seg>
<seg id="2860"> The flatter the world gets, the more we are going to need a system of global governance that keeps up with all the new legal and illegal forms of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="2861"> We can also see this in the case of patent law as it has evolved inside the United States. </seg>
<seg id="2862"> Companies can do one of three things with an innovation. </seg>
<seg id="2863"> They can patent the widget they invent and sell it themselves; </seg>
<seg id="2864"> they can patent it and license it to someone else to manufacture; </seg>
<seg id="2865"> and they can patent it and cross-license with several other companies so that they all have freedom of action to make a product-like a PC-that comes from melding many different patents. </seg>
<seg id="2866"> American patent law is technically neutral on this. </seg>
<seg id="2867"> But the way established case law has evolved, experts tell me, it is decidedly biased against cross-licensing and other arrangements that encourage collaboration or freedom of action for as many players as possible; </seg>
<seg id="2868"> it is more focused on protecting the rights of individual firms to manufacture their own patents. </seg>
<seg id="2869"> In a flat world, companies need a patent system that encourages both. </seg>
<seg id="2870"> The more your legal structure fosters cross-licensing and standards, the more collaborative innovation you will get. </seg>
<seg id="2871"> The PC is the product of a lot of cross-licensing between the company that had the patent on the cursor and the company that had the patent on the mouse and the screen. </seg>
<seg id="2872"> And while you are sorting that out, sort this out as well. </seg>
<seg id="2873"> On November 13, 2004, Lance Cpl. Justin M. Ellsworth, twenty, was killed by a roadside bomb during a foot patrol in Iraq. </seg>
<seg id="2874"> On December 21, 2004, the Associated Press reported that his family was demanding that Yahoo! give them the password for their deceased son's e-mail account so they could have access to all his e-mail, including notes to and from others. </seg>
<seg id="2875"> ˇ°I want to be able to remember him in his words. </seg>
<seg id="2876"> I know he thought he was doing what he needed to do. </seg>
<seg id="2877"> I want to have that for the future,ˇ± John Ellsworth, Justin's father, told the AP. </seg>
<seg id="2878"> ˇ°It's the last thing I have of my son.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2879"> We are moving into a world where more and more communication is in the form of bits traveling through cyberspace and stored on servers located all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="2880"> No government controls this cyber-realm. </seg>
<seg id="2881"> So the question is: Who owns your bits when you die? </seg>
<seg id="2882"> The AP reported that Yahoo! denied the Ellsworth family their son's password, citing the fact that Yahoo! policy calls for erasing all accounts that are inactive for ninety days and the fact that all Yahoo! users agree at sign-up that rights to a member's ID or account contents terminate upon death. </seg>
<seg id="2883"> ˇ°While we sympathize with any grieving family, Yahoo! accounts and any contents therein are nontransferableˇ± even after death, Karen Mahon, a Yahoo! spokeswoman told the AP. </seg>
<seg id="2884"> As we get rid of more and more paper and communicate through more and more digitized formats, you better sort out before you die, and include in your will, to whom, if anyone, you want to leave your bits. </seg>
<seg id="2885"> This is very real. </seg>
<seg id="2886"> I stored many chapters of this book in my AOL account, feeling it would be safest in cyberspace. </seg>
<seg id="2887"> If something had happened to me during my writing, my family and publisher would have had to sue AOL to try to get this text. </seg>
<seg id="2888"> Death of the Salesmen </seg>
<seg id="2889"> In the fall of 2004, I went out to Minneapolis to visit my mother and had three world-is-flat encounters right in a row. </seg>
<seg id="2890"> First, before I left home in Washington, I dialed 411 -directory assistance-to try to get a friend's phone number in Minneapolis. </seg>
<seg id="2891"> A computer answered and a computerized voice asked me to pronounce the name of the person whose number I was requesting. </seg>
<seg id="2892"> For whatever reason, I could not get the computer to hear me correctly, and it kept saying back to me in a computerized voice, ˇ°Did you say...?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2893"> I kept having to say the family name in a voice that masked my exasperation (otherwise the computer never would have understood me). </seg>
<seg id="2894"> Eventually, I was connected to an operator, but I did not enjoy this friction-free encounter with directory information. </seg>
<seg id="2895"> I craved the friction of another human being. </seg>
<seg id="2896"> It may be cheaper and more efficient to have a computer dispense phone numbers, but for me it brought only frustration. </seg>
<seg id="2897"> When I arrived in Minneapolis, I had dinner with family friends, one of whom has spent his life working as a wholesaler in the Midwest, selling goods to the biggest retailers in the region. </seg>
<seg id="2898"> He is a natural salesman. </seg>
<seg id="2899"> When I asked him what was new, he sighed and said that business just wasn't what it used to be. </seg>
<seg id="2900"> Everything was now being sold at 1 percent margins, he explained. </seg>
<seg id="2901"> No problem. </seg>
<seg id="2902"> He was selling mostly commodity items so that, given his volumes, he could handle the slim profit margin. </seg>
<seg id="2903"> But what bothered him, he mentioned, was the fact that he no longer had human contact with some of his biggest accounts. </seg>
<seg id="2904"> Even commodities and low-cost goods have certain differentiating elements that need to be sold and highlighted. </seg>
<seg id="2905"> ˇ°Everything is by e-mail now,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="2906"> ˇ°I am dealing with a young kid at [one of the biggest retailers in the nation], and he says, 'Just e-mail me your bid.' </seg>
<seg id="2907"> I've never met him. </seg>
<seg id="2908"> Half the time he doesn't get back to me. </seg>
<seg id="2909"> I am not sure how to deal with him... </seg>
<seg id="2910"> In the old days, I used to stop by the office, give the buyers a few Vikings tickets. </seg>
<seg id="2911"> We were friends... </seg>
<seg id="2912"> Tommy, all anyone cares about today is price.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2913"> Fortunately, my friend is a successful businessman and has a range of enterprises. </seg>
<seg id="2914"> But as I reflected later on what he was saying, I was drawn back to that scene in Death of a Salesman in which Willy Loman says that, unlike his colleague Charley, he intends to be ˇ°well liked.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2915"> He tells his sons that in business and in life, character, personality, and human connections are more important than smarts. </seg>
<seg id="2916"> Says Willy, ˇ°The man who makes an appearance in the business world, the man who creates personal interest, is the man who gets ahead. </seg>
<seg id="2917"> Be liked and you will never want.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2918"> Not when the world goes flat. </seg>
<seg id="2919"> It's hard to create a human bond with e-mail and streaming Internet. </seg>
<seg id="2920"> The next day, I had dinner with my friend Ken Greer, who runs a media company that I discuss in greater detail later. </seg>
<seg id="2921"> Ken had a similar lament: So many contracts were going these days to the advertising firms that were selling just numbers, not creative instinct. </seg>
<seg id="2922"> Then Ken said something that really hit home with me: ˇ°It is like they have cut all the fat out of the businessˇ± and turned everything into a numbers game. </seg>
<seg id="2923"> ˇ°But fat is what gives meat its taste,ˇ± Ken added. </seg>
<seg id="2924"> ˇ°The leanest cuts of meat don't taste very good. </seg>
<seg id="2925"> You want it marbled with at least a little fat.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2926"> The flattening process relentlessly trims the fat out of business and life, but, as Ken noted, fat is what gives life taste and texture. </seg>
<seg id="2927"> Fat is also what keeps us warm. </seg>
<seg id="2928"> Yes, the consumer in us wants Wal-Mart prices, with all the fat gone. </seg>
<seg id="2929"> But the employee in us wants a little fat left on the bone, the way Costco does it, so that it can offer health care to almost all its employees, rather than just less than half of them, as Wal-Mart does. </seg>
<seg id="2930"> But the shareholder in us wants Wal-Mart's profit margins, not Costco's. </seg>
<seg id="2931"> Yet the citizen in us wants Costco's benefits, rather than Wal-Mart's, because the difference ultimately may have to be paid for by society. </seg>
<seg id="2932"> The consumer in me wants lower phone bills, but the human being in me also wants to speak to an operator when I call 411. </seg>
<seg id="2933"> Yes, the reader in me loves to surf the Net and read the bloggers, but the citizen in me also wishes that some of those bloggers had an editor, a middleman, to tell them to check some of their facts one more time before they pressed the Send button and told the whole world that something was wrong or unfair. </seg>
<seg id="2934"> Given these conflicting emotions and pressures, there is potential here for American politics to get completely reshuffled-with workers and corporate interests realigning themselves into different parties. </seg>
<seg id="2935"> Think about it: Social conservatives from the right wing of the Republican party, who do not like globalization or closer integration with the world because it brings too many foreigners and foreign cultural mores into America, might align themselves with unions from the left wing of the Democratic Party, who don't like globalization for the way it facilitates the outsourcing and offshoring of jobs. </seg>
<seg id="2936"> They might be called the Wall Party and militate for more friction and fat everywhere. </seg>
<seg id="2937"> Let's face it: Republican cultural conservatives have much more in common with the steelworkers of Youngstown, Ohio, the farmers of rural China, and the mullahs of central Saudi Arabia, who would also like more walls, than they do with investment bankers on Wall Street or service workers linked to the global economy in Palo Alto, who have been enriched by the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="2938"> Meanwhile, the business wing of the Republican Party, which believes in free trade, deregulation, more integration, and lower taxes-everything that would flatten the world even more-may end up aligning itself with the social liberals of the Democratic Party, many of whom are East Coast or West Coast global service industry workers. </seg>
<seg id="2939"> They might also be joined by Hollywood and other entertainment workers. </seg>
<seg id="2940"> All of them are huge beneficiaries of the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="2941"> They might be called the Web Party, whose main platform would be to promote more global integration. </seg>
<seg id="2942"> Many residents of Manhattan and Palo Alto have more interests in common with the people of Shanghai and Bangalore than they do with the residents of Youngstown or Topeka. </seg>
<seg id="2943"> In short, in a flat world, we are likely to see many social liberals, white-collar global service industry workers, and Wall Street types driven together, and many social conservatives, white-collar local service industry workers, and labor unions driven together. </seg>
<seg id="2944"> The Passion of the Christ audience will be in the same trench with the Teamsters and the AFL-CIO, while the Hollywood and Wall Street liberals and the You've Got Mail crowd will be in the same trench with the high-tech workers of Silicon Valley and the global service providers of Manhattan and San Francisco. </seg>
<seg id="2945"> It will be Mel Gibson and Jimmy Hoffa Jr. versus Bill Gates and Meg Ryan. </seg>
<seg id="2946"> More and more, politics in the flat world will consist of asking which values, frictions, and fats are worth preserving-which should, in Marx's language, be kept solid-and which must be left to melt away into the air. </seg>
<seg id="2947"> Countries, companies, and individuals will be able to give intelligent answers to these questions only if they understand the real nature and texture of the global playing field and how different it is from the one that existed in the Cold War era and before. </seg>
<seg id="2948"> And countries, companies, and individuals will be able to make sound political choices only if they fully appreciate the flattened playing field and understand all the new tools now available to them for collaborating and competing on it. </seg>
<seg id="2949"> I hope this book will provide a nuanced framework for this hugely important political debate and the great sorting out that is just around the corner. </seg>
<seg id="2950"> To that end, the next three sections look at how the flattening of the world and the triple convergence will affect Americans, developing countries, and companies. </seg>
<seg id="2951"> Brace yourself: You are now about to enter the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="2952"> AMERICA AND THE FLAT WORLD FIVE America and Free Trade </seg>
<seg id="2953"> Is Ricardo Still Right? </seg>
<seg id="2954"> As an American who has always believed in the merits of free trade, I had an important question to answer after my India trip: Should I still believe in free trade in a fiat world? </seg>
<seg id="2955"> Here was an issue that needed sorting out immediately-not only because it was becoming a hot issue in the presidential campaign of 2004 but also because my whole view of the flat world would depend on my view of free trade. </seg>
<seg id="2956"> I know that free trade won't necessarily benefit every American, and that our society will have to help those who are harmed by it. </seg>
<seg id="2957"> But for me the key question was: Will free trade benefit America as a whole when the world becomes so flat and so many more people can collaborate, and compete, with my kids? </seg>
<seg id="2958"> It seems that so many jobs are going to be up for grabs. </seg>
<seg id="2959"> Wouldn't individual Americans be better off if our government erected some walls and banned some outsourcing and offshoring? </seg>
<seg id="2960"> I first wrestled with this issue while filming the Discovery Times documentary in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="2961"> One day we went to the Infosys campus around five p.m. -just when the Infosys call-center workers were flooding into the grounds for the overnight shift on foot, minibus, and motor scooter, while many of the more advanced engineers were leaving at the end of the day shift. </seg>
<seg id="2962"> The crew and I were standing at the gate observing this river of educated young people flowing in and out, many in animated conversation. </seg>
<seg id="2963"> They all looked as if they had scored 1,600 on their SATs, and I felt a real mind-eye split overtaking me. </seg>
<seg id="2964"> My mind just kept telling me, ˇ°Ricardo is right, Ricardo is right, Ricardo is right.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2965"> David Ricardo (1772-1823) was the English economist who developed the free-trade theory of comparative advantage, which stipulates that if each nation specializes in the production of goods in which it has a comparative cost advantage and then trades with other nations for the goods in which they specialize, there will be an overall gain in trade, and overall income levels should rise in each trading country. </seg>
<seg id="2966"> So if all these Indian techies were doing what was their comparative advantage and then turning around and using their income to buy all the products from America that are our comparative advantage-from Corning Glass to Microsoft Windows-both our countries would benefit, even if some individual Indians or Americans might have to shift jobs in the transition. </seg>
<seg id="2967"> And one can see evidence of this mutual benefit in the sharp increase in exports and imports between the United States and India in recent years. </seg>
<seg id="2968"> But my eye kept looking at all these Indian zippies and telling me something else: ˇ°Oh, my God, there are so many of them, and they all look so serious, so eager for work. </seg>
<seg id="2969"> And they just keep coming, wave after wave. </seg>
<seg id="2970"> How in the world can it possibly be good for my daughters and millions of other young Americans that these Indians can do the same jobs as they can for a fraction of the wages?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="2971"> When Ricardo was writing, goods were tradable, but for the most part knowledge work and services were not. </seg>
<seg id="2972"> There was no undersea fiberoptic cable to make knowledge jobs tradable between America and India back then. </seg>
<seg id="2973"> Just as I was getting worked up with worry, the Infosys spokeswoman accompanying me casually mentioned that last year Infosys India received ˇ°one million applicationsˇ± from young Indians for nine thousand tech jobs. </seg>
<seg id="2974"> I struggled over what to make of this scene. </seg>
<seg id="2975"> I don't want to see any American lose his or her job to foreign competition or to technological innovation. </seg>
<seg id="2976"> When you lose your job, the unemployment rate is not 5.2 percent; </seg>
<seg id="2977"> it's 100 percent. </seg>
<seg id="2978"> No book about the flat world would be honest if it did not acknowledge such concerns, or acknowledge that there is some debate among economists about whether Ricardo is still right.Having listened to the arguments on both sides, though, I come down where the great majority of economists come down-that Ricardo is still right and that more American individuals will be better off if we don't erect barriers to outsourcing, supply-chaining, and offshoring than if we do. </seg>
<seg id="2979"> The simple message of this chapter is that even as the world gets flat, America as a whole will benefit more by sticking to the basic principles of free trade, as it always has, than by trying to erect walls. </seg>
<seg id="2980"> The main argument of the anti-outsourcing school is that in a flat world, not only are goods tradable, but many services have become tradable as well. </seg>
<seg id="2981"> Because of this change, America and other developed countries could be headed for an absolute decline, not just a relative one, in their economic power and living standards unless they move to formally protect certain jobs from foreign competition. </seg>
<seg id="2982"> So many new players cannot enter the global economy-in service and knowledge fields now dominated by Americans, Europeans, and Japanese-without wages settling at a newer, lower equilibrium, this school argues. </seg>
<seg id="2983"> The main counterargument from free-trade/outsourcing advocates is that while there may be a transition phase in certain fields, during which wages are dampened, there is no reason to believe that this dip will be permanent or across the board, as long as the global pie keeps growing. </seg>
<seg id="2984"> To suggest that it will be is to invoke the so-called lump of labor theory- the notion that there is a fixed lump of labor in the world and that once that lump is gobbled up, by either Americans or Indians or Japanese, there won't be any more jobs to go around. </seg>
<seg id="2985"> If we have the biggest lump of labor now, and then Indians offer to do this same work for less, they will get a bigger piece of the lump, and we will have less, or so this argument goes. </seg>
<seg id="2986"> The main reason the lump of labor theory is wrong is that it is based on the assumption that everything that is going to be invented has been invented, and that therefore economic competition is a zero-sum game, a fight over a fixed lump. </seg>
<seg id="2987"> This assumption misses the fact that although jobs are often lost in bulk-to outsourcing or offshoring-by big individual companies, and this loss tends to make headlines, new jobs are also being created in fives, tens, and twenties by small companies that you can't see. </seg>
<seg id="2988"> It often takes a leap of faith to believe that it is happening. </seg>
<seg id="2989"> But it is happening. </seg>
<seg id="2990"> If it were not, America's unemployment rate would be much higher today than 5 percent. </seg>
<seg id="2991"> The reason it is happening is that as lower-end service and manufacturing jobs move out of Europe, America, and Japan to India, China, and the former Soviet Empire, the global pie not only grows larger-because more people have more income to spend-it also grows more complex, as more new jobs, and new specialties, are created.Let me illustrate this with a simple example. </seg>
<seg id="2992"> Imagine that there are only two countries in the world-America and China. </seg>
<seg id="2993"> And imagine that the American economy has only 100 people. </seg>
<seg id="2994"> Of those 100 people, 80 are well-educated knowledge workers and 20 are less-educated low-skilled workers. </seg>
<seg id="2995"> Now imagine that the world goes flat and America enters into a free-trade agreement with China, which has 1,000 people but is a less developed country. </seg>
<seg id="2996"> So today China too has only 80 well-educated knowledge workers out of that 1,000, and it has 920 low-skilled workers. </seg>
<seg id="2997"> Before America entered into its free-trade agreement with China, there were only 80 knowledge workers in its world. </seg>
<seg id="2998"> Now there are 160 in our two-country world. </seg>
<seg id="2999"> The American knowledge workers feel like they have more competition, and they do. </seg>
<seg id="3000"> But if you look at the prize they are going after, it is now a much expanded and more complex market. </seg>
<seg id="3001"> It went from a market of 100 people to a market of 1,100 people, with many more needs and wants. </seg>
<seg id="3002"> So it should be win-win for both the American and Chinese knowledge workers.Sure, some of the knowledge workers in America may have to move horizontally into new knowledge jobs, because of the competition from China. </seg>
<seg id="3003"> But with a market that big and complex, you can be sure that new knowledge jobs will open up at decent wages for anyone who keeps up his or her skills. </seg>
<seg id="3004"> So do not worry about our knowledge workers or the Chinese knowledge workers. They will both do fine with this bigger market. </seg>
<seg id="3005"> ˇ°What do you mean, don't worry?ˇ± you ask. </seg>
<seg id="3006"> ˇ°How do we deal with the fact that those eighty knowledge workers from China will be willing to work for so much less than the eighty knowledge workers from America? </seg>
<seg id="3007"> How will this difference get resolved?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3008"> It won't happen overnight, so some American knowledge workers may be affected in the transition, but the effects will not be permanent. </seg>
<seg id="3009"> Here, argues Stanford new economy specialist Paul Romer, is what you need to understand: The wages for the Chinese knowledge workers were so low because, although their skills were marketable globally like those of their American counterparts, they were trapped inside a stifled economy. </seg>
<seg id="3010"> But as the Chinese economy opens up to the world and reforms, the wages of Chinese knowledge workers will rise up to American/world levels. </seg>
<seg id="3011"> Ours will not go down to the level of a stifled, walled-in economy. </seg>
<seg id="3012"> You can already see this happening in Bangalore, where competition for Indian software writers is rapidly pushing up their wages toward American/European levels-after decades of languishing while the Indian economy was closed. </seg>
<seg id="3013"> It is why Americans should be doing all they can to promote more and faster economic reform in India and China. </seg>
<seg id="3014"> Do worry, though, about the 20 low-skilled Americans, who now have to compete more directly with the 920 low-skilled Chinese. </seg>
<seg id="3015"> One reason the 20 low-skilled Americans were paid a decent wage before was that, relative to the 80 skilled Americans, there were not that many of them. </seg>
<seg id="3016"> Every economy needs some low-skilled manual labor. </seg>
<seg id="3017"> But now that China and America have signed their free-trade pact, there are a total of 940 low-skilled workers and 160 knowledge workers in our two-country world. </seg>
<seg id="3018"> Those American low-skilled workers doing fungible jobs-jobs that can easily be moved to China-will have a problem. </seg>
<seg id="3019"> There is no denying this. </seg>
<seg id="3020"> Their wages are certain to be depressed. </seg>
<seg id="3021"> In order to maintain or improve their living standards, they will have to move vertically, not horizontally. </seg>
<seg id="3022"> They will have to upgrade their education and upgrade their knowledge skills so that they can occupy one of the new jobs sure to be created in the much expanded United States-China market. </seg>
<seg id="3023"> (In Chapter 8 I will talk about our society's obligation to ensure that everyone gets a chance to acquire those skills.) </seg>
<seg id="3024"> As Romer notes, we know from the history of our own country that an increase in knowledge workers does not necessarily lead to a decrease in their pay the way it does with low-skilled workers. </seg>
<seg id="3025"> From the 1960s to the 1980s, the supply of college-educated workers grew dramatically, and yet their wages grew even faster. </seg>
<seg id="3026"> Because as the pie grew in size and complexity, so too did people's wants, and this increased the demand for people able to do complex work and specialized tasks. </seg>
<seg id="3027"> Romer explains this in part by the fact that ˇ°there is a difference between idea-based goods and physical goods.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3028"> If you are a knowledge worker making and selling some kind of idea-based product-consulting or financial services or music or software or marketing or design or new drugs-the bigger the market is, the more people there are out there to whom you can sell your product. </seg>
<seg id="3029"> And the bigger the market, the more new specialties and niches it will create. </seg>
<seg id="3030"> If you come up with the next Windows or Viagra, you can potentially sell one to everyone in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3031"> So idea-based workers do well in globalization, and fortunately America as a whole has more idea-driven workers than any country in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3032"> But if you are selling manual labor-or a piece of lumber or a slab of steel-the value of what you have to sell does not necessarily increase when the market expands, and it may decrease, argues Romer. </seg>
<seg id="3033"> There are only so many factories that will buy your manual labor, and there are many more people selling it. </seg>
<seg id="3034"> What the manual laborer has to sell can be bought by only one factory or one consumer at a time, explains Romer, while what the software writer or drug inventor has to sellˇŞidea-based products-can be sold to everyone in the global market at once. </seg>
<seg id="3035"> That is why America, as a whole, will do fine in a flat world with free trade-provided it continues to churn out knowledge workers who are able to produce idea-based goods that can be sold globally and who are able to fill the knowledge jobs that will be created as we not only expand the global economy but connect all the knowledge pools in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3036"> There may be a limit to the number of good factory jobs in the world, but there is no limit to the number of idea-generated jobs in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3037"> If we go from a world in which there were fifteen drug companies and fifteen software companies in America (thirty in all) and two drug companies and two software companies in China (four in all) to a world in which there are thirty drug and software companies in America and thirty drug and software companies in China, it is going to mean more innovation, more cures, more new products, more niches to specialize in, and many more people with higher incomes to buy those products. </seg>
<seg id="3038"> ˇ°The pie keeps growing because things that look like wants today are needs tomorrow,ˇ± argued Marc Andreessen, the Netscape cofounder, who helped to ignite a whole new industry, e-commerce, that now employs millions of specialists around the world, specialists whose jobs weren't even imagined when Bill Clinton became president. </seg>
<seg id="3039"> I like going to coffee shops occasionally, but now that Starbucks is here, I need my coffee, and that new need has spawned a whole new industry. </seg>
<seg id="3040"> I always wanted to be able to search for things, but once Google was created, I must have my search engine. </seg>
<seg id="3041"> So a whole new industry has been built up around search, and Google is hiring math Ph.D.'s by the bushel-before Yahoo! or Microsoft hires them. </seg>
<seg id="3042"> People are always assuming that everything that is going to be invented must have been invented already. </seg>
<seg id="3043"> But it hasn't </seg>
<seg id="3044"> ˇ°If you believe human wants and needs are infinite,ˇ± said Andreeseen, ˇ°then there are infinite industries to be created, infinite businesses to be started, and infinite jobs to be done, and the only limiting factor is human imagination. </seg>
<seg id="3045"> The world is flattening and rising at the same time. </seg>
<seg id="3046"> And I think the evidence is overwhelmingly clear: If you look over the sweep of history, every time we had more trade, more communications, we had a big upswing in economic activity and standard of living.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3047"> America integrated a broken Europe and Japan into the global economy after World War II, with both Europe and Japan every year upgrading their manufacturing, knowledge, and service skills, often importing and sometimes stealing ideas and equipment from the United States, just as America did from Britain in the late 1770s. </seg>
<seg id="3048"> Yet in the sixty years since World War II, our standard of living has increased every decade, and our unemployment rate-even with all the outcry about outsourcing- stands at only a little above 5 percent, roughly half that of the most developed countries in Western Europe. </seg>
<seg id="3049"> ˇ°We just started a company that created 180 new jobs in the middle of a recession,ˇ± said Andreessen, whose company, Opsware, uses automation and software to replace human beings in the operation of huge server farms in remote locations. </seg>
<seg id="3050"> By automating these jobs, Opsware enables companies to save money and free up talented brainpower from relatively mundane tasks to start new businesses in other areas. </seg>
<seg id="3051"> You should be afraid of free markets, argued Andreessen, only if you believe that you will never need new medicines, new work flow software, new industries, new forms of entertainment, new coffeehouses. </seg>
<seg id="3052"> ˇ°Yes,ˇ± he concluded, ˇ°it takes a leap of faith, based on economics, to say there will be new things to do.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3053"> But there always have been new jobs to do, and there is no fundamental reason to believe the future will be different. </seg>
<seg id="3054"> Some 150 years ago, 90 percent of Americans worked in agriculture and related fields. </seg>
<seg id="3055"> What if the government had decided to protect and subsidize all those agricultural jobs and not embrace industrialization and then computerization? </seg>
<seg id="3056"> Would America as a whole really be better off today? </seg>
<seg id="3057"> Hardly. </seg>
<seg id="3058"> As noted, it is true that as Indians or Chinese move up the value chain and start producing more knowledge-intensive goods-the sorts of things Americans have been specializing in-our comparative advantage in some of these areas will diminish, explains Jagdish Bhagwati, the Columbia University expert on free trade. </seg>
<seg id="3059"> There will be a downward pressure on wages in certain fields, and some of the jobs in those fields may permanently migrate abroad. </seg>
<seg id="3060"> That is why some knowledge workers will have to move horizontally. </seg>
<seg id="3061"> But the growing pie will surely create new specialties for them to fill that are impossible to predict right now. </seg>
<seg id="3062"> For instance, there was a time when America's semiconductor industry dominated the world, but then companies from other countries came along and gobbled up the low end of the market. </seg>
<seg id="3063"> Some even moved into the higher end. American companies were then forced to find newer, deeper specialties in the expanded market. </seg>
<seg id="3064"> If that weren't happening, Intel would be out of business today. </seg>
<seg id="3065"> Instead, it is thriving. </seg>
<seg id="3066"> Paul Otellini, Intel's president, told The Economist (May 8, 2003) that as chips become good enough for certain applications, new applications pop up that demand more powerful and more complex chips, which are Intel's specialty. </seg>
<seg id="3067"> Once Google starts offering video searches, for instance, there will be demand for new machines and the chips that power them, of which no one was even dreaming five years ago. </seg>
<seg id="3068"> This process takes time to unfold. </seg>
<seg id="3069"> But it will, argued Bhagwati, because what is happening in services today is the same thing that happened in manufacturing as trade barriers were lowered. </seg>
<seg id="3070"> In manufacturing, said Bhagwati, as the global market expanded and more and more players came onto the field, you saw greater and greater ˇ°intraindustry trade, with more and more specialization,ˇ± and as we move into the knowledge economy, you are now seeing more and more intraservice trade, with more and more specialization. </seg>
<seg id="3071"> Don't be surprised if your son or daughter graduates from college and calls you one day and says he or she is going to be a ˇ°search engine optimizer.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3072"> A what? </seg>
<seg id="3073"> What these search engine optimizers (SEOs as they are called in the trade) do is constantly study the algorithms being used by the major search engines and then design marketing and Web strategies that will push you up the rankings. </seg>
<seg id="3074"> The business involves a combination of math and marketing-a whole new specialty created entirely by the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="3075"> And always remember: The Indians and Chinese are not racing us to the bottom. They are racing us to the top-and that is a good thing! </seg>
<seg id="3076"> They want higher standards of living, not sweatshops; they want brand names, not junk; they want to trade their motor scooters for cars and their pens and pencils for computers. </seg>
<seg id="3077"> And the more they do that, the higher they climb, the more room is created at the top-because the more they have, the more they spend, the more diverse product markets become, and the more niches for specialization are created as well. </seg>
<seg id="3078"> Look at what is happening already: As American companies send knowledge work to India, Indian companies are turning around and using their earnings and insights to start inventing new products that poorer Indians can use to lift themselves out of poverty into the middle class, where they will surely become consumers of American products. </seg>
<seg id="3079"> BusinessWeek cited the Tata Motors factory, near Pune, south of Mumbai, ˇ°where a group of young designers, technicians, and marketers pore over drawings and examine samples of steel and composite plastics. </seg>
<seg id="3080"> By early next year, they plan to design a prototype for Tata Group's most ambitious project yet: a compact car that will sell for $2,200. </seg>
<seg id="3081"> The company hopes the car will beat out Suzuki's $5,000 Maruti compact to become India's cheapest car-and an export model for the rest of the developing world. ' </seg>
<seg id="3082"> This is the need of the day in India-a people's car,' says Ratan Tata, chairman of the $12.5 billion Tata Group. </seg>
<seg id="3083"> Indians are increasingly demanding better products and services at an affordable cost. </seg>
<seg id="3084"> Strong economic growth this year will only enlarge that demand. </seg>
<seg id="3085"> The phrase 'Made in India' may come to represent low-cost innovation in the new global economyˇ± (October 11, 2004).Raghuram Rajan, the director of research for the International Monetary Fund, sits on the board of a company that puts Indian students to work tutoring students in Singapore. The company, called Heymath.com, is paid for by the schools in Singapore. </seg>
<seg id="3086"> Cambridge University in England is also part of this equation, providing the overall quality controls and certifying the lesson plans and teaching methods. </seg>
<seg id="3087"> ˇ°Everyone wins,ˇ± says Rajan. </seg>
<seg id="3088"> ˇ°The company is run by two Indians who worked for Citibank and CSFB in London and came back to India to start this business... </seg>
<seg id="3089"> Cambridge University is making money from a company that has created a whole new niche. </seg>
<seg id="3090"> The Indian students are making pocket money. </seg>
<seg id="3091"> And the Singapore students are learning better.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3092"> Meanwhile, the underlying software is probably being provided by Microsoft and the chips by Intel, and the enriched Indian students are probably buying cheap personal computers from Apple, Dell, or HP. </seg>
<seg id="3093"> But you can't really see any of this. </seg>
<seg id="3094"> ˇ°The pie grew, but no one saw it,ˇ± said Rajan. </seg>
<seg id="3095"> An essay in the McKinsey Quarterly, ˇ°Beyond Cheap Labor: Lessons for Developing Economiesˇ± (January 2005), offers a nice example of this: ˇ°In northern Italy's textile and apparel industry... the majority of garment production has moved to lower-cost locations, but employment remains stable because companies have put more resources into tasks such as designing clothes and coordinating global production networks.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3096"> It is so easy to demonize free markets-and the freedom to outsource and offshore-because it is so much easier to see people being laid off than being hired. </seg>
<seg id="3097"> But occasionally a newspaper tries to dig deep into the issue. </seg>
<seg id="3098"> My hometown paper, the Minneapolis Star Tribune, did just that. </seg>
<seg id="3099"> It looked at exactly how the Minnesota economy was being affected by the flattening of the world, actually daring to run an article on September 5, 2004, headlined, ˇ°Offshore Jobs Bring Gains at Home.ˇ± </seg>
