<seg id="3100"> The article, date-lined Wuxi, China, began like this: ˇ°Outside the air is dank, dusty and hot as tropical fever. </seg>
<seg id="3101"> Inside, in an environment that's dry, spotless and cool, hundreds of former farm laborers covered head to toe in suits looking like something out of NASA are performing work for Bloomington-based Donaldson Co. Inc.... </seg>
<seg id="3102"> In Donaldson's case, the company has twice as many workers in China-2,500-as the 1,100 it has in Bloomington. </seg>
<seg id="3103"> The Chinese operation not only has allowed Donaldson to keep making a product it no longer could make at a profit in the United States, it also has helped boost the company's Minnesota employment, up by 400 people since 1990. </seg>
<seg id="3104"> Donaldson's highly paid engineers, chemists and designers in Minnesota spend their days designing updated filters that the Chinese plant will make for use in computers, MP3 players and digital video recorders. </seg>
<seg id="3105"> The falling disk-drive prices made possible by Chinese production are feeding demand for the gadgets. 'If we didn't follow [the trend], we'd be out of business,' said David Timm, general manager of Donaldson's disk-drive and microelectronics unit. </seg>
<seg id="3106"> In Minnesota, Global Insight estimates that 1,854 jobs were created as a result of foreign outsourcing in 2003. </seg>
<seg id="3107"> By 2008, the firm expects nearly 6,700 new jobs in Minnesota as a consequence of the trend.ˇ±Economists often compare China's and India's entry into the global economy to the moment when the railroad lines crossing America finally connected New Mexico to California, with its much larger population. </seg>
<seg id="3108"> ˇ°When the railroad comes to town,ˇ± noted Vivek Paul, the Wipro president, ˇ°the first thing you see is extra capacity, and all the people in New Mexico say those people-Californians-will wipe out all our factories along the line. </seg>
<seg id="3109"> That will happen in some areas, and some companies along the line will go out of business. </seg>
<seg id="3110"> But then capital will get reallocated. </seg>
<seg id="3111"> In the end, everyone along the line will benefit. </seg>
<seg id="3112"> Sure, there is fear, and that fear is good because that stimulates a willingness to change and explore and find more things to do better.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3113"> It happened when we connected New York, New Mexico, and California. </seg>
<seg id="3114"> It happened when we connected Western Europe, America, and Japan. </seg>
<seg id="3115"> And it will happen when we connect India and China with America, Europe, and Japan. </seg>
<seg id="3116"> The way to succeed is not by stopping the railroad line from connecting you, but by upgrading your skills and making the investment in those practices that will enable you and your society to claim your slice of the bigger but more complex pie. </seg>
<seg id="3117"> SIX The Untouchables </seg>
<seg id="3118"> if the flattening of the world is largely (but not entirely) unstoppable, and holds out the potential to be as beneficial to American society as a whole as past market evolutions have been, how does an individual get the best out of it? </seg>
<seg id="3119"> What do we tell our kids? </seg>
<seg id="3120"> You have to constantly upgrade your skills. </seg>
<seg id="3121"> There will be plenty of good jobs out there in the flat world for people with the knowledge and ideas to seize them. </seg>
<seg id="3122"> I am not suggesting this will be simple. </seg>
<seg id="3123"> It was never good to be mediocre in your job, but in a world of walls, mediocrity could still earn you a decent wage. </seg>
<seg id="3124"> In a flatter world, you really do not want to be mediocre. </seg>
<seg id="3125"> You don't want to find yourself in the shoes of Willy Loman in Death of a Salesman, when his son Biff dispels his idea that the Loman family is special by declaring, ˇ°Pop! I'm a dime a dozen, and so are you!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3126"> An angry Willy retorts, ˇ°I am not a dime a dozen! I am Willy Loman, and you are Biff Loman!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3127"> I don't care to have that conversation with my girls, so my advice to them in this flat world is very brief and very blunt: ˇ°Girls, when I was growing up, my parents used to say to me, ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3128"> Tom, finish your dinnerˇŞpeople in China and India are starving. </seg>
<seg id="3129"> ˇ° My advice to you is: Girls, finish your homework-people in China and India are starving for your jobs.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3130"> The way I like to think about this for our society as a whole is that every person should figure out how to make himself or herself into an untouchable. </seg>
<seg id="3131"> That's right. </seg>
<seg id="3132"> When the world goes flat, the caste system gets turned upside down. </seg>
<seg id="3133"> In India untouchables may be the lowest social class, but in a flat world everyone should want to be an untouchable. </seg>
<seg id="3134"> Untouchables, in my lexicon, are people whose jobs cannot be outsourced. </seg>
<seg id="3135"> Untouchables come in four broad categories: workers who are ˇ°special,ˇ± workers who are ˇ°specialized,ˇ± workers who are ˇ°anchored,ˇ± and workers who are ˇ°really adaptable.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3136"> Workers who are special are people like Michael Jordan, Bill Gates, and Barbra Streisand. </seg>
<seg id="3137"> They have a global market for their goods and services and can command global-sized pay packages. </seg>
<seg id="3138"> Their jobs can never be outsourced. </seg>
<seg id="3139"> If you can't be special-and only a few people can be-you want to be specialized, so that your work cannot be outsourced. </seg>
<seg id="3140"> This applies to all sorts of knowledge workers-from specialized lawyers, accountants, and brain surgeons, to cutting-edge computer architects and software engineers, to advanced machine tool and robot operators. </seg>
<seg id="3141"> My childhood friend Bill Greer is a good example of a person who faced this challenge and came up with a personal strategy to meet it. </seg>
<seg id="3142"> Greer is forty-eight years old and has made his living as a freelance artist and graphic designer for twenty-six years. </seg>
<seg id="3143"> From the late 1970s until right around 2000, the way Bill did his job and served his clients was pretty much the same. </seg>
<seg id="3144"> ˇ°Clients, like The New York Times, would want a finished piece of artwork,ˇ± Bill explained to me. </seg>
<seg id="3145"> So if he was doing an illustration for a newspaper or a magazine, or proposing a new logo for a product, he would actually create a piece of art-sketch it, color it, mount it on an illustration board, cover it with tissue, put it in a package that was opened with two flaps, and have it delivered by messenger or FedEx. </seg>
<seg id="3146"> He called it ˇ°flap art.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3147"> In the industry it was known as ˇ°camera-ready art,ˇ± because it needed to be shot, printed on four different layers of color film, or ˇ°separations,ˇ± and prepared for publication. </seg>
<seg id="3148"> ˇ°It was a finished product, and it had a certain preciousness to it,ˇ± said Bill. ˇ°It was a real piece of art, and sometimes people would hang them on their walls. </seg>
<seg id="3149"> In fact, The New York Times would have shows of works that were created by illustrators for its publications.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3150"> But in the last few years ˇ°that started to change,ˇ± Bill told me, as publications and ad agencies moved to digital preparation, relying on the new software-namely, Quark, Photoshop, and Illustrator, which graphic artists refer to as ˇ°the trinityˇ±-which made digital computer design so much easier. </seg>
<seg id="3151"> Everyone who went through art school got trained on these programs. </seg>
<seg id="3152"> Indeed, Bill explained, graphic design got so much easier that it became a commodity. </seg>
<seg id="3153"> It got turned into vanilla ice cream. </seg>
<seg id="3154"> ˇ°In terms of design,ˇ± he said, ˇ°the technology gave everyone the same tools, so everyone could do straight lines and everyone could do work that was halfway decent. </seg>
<seg id="3155"> You used to need an eye to see if something was in balance and had the right typeface, but all of a sudden anyone could hammer out something that was acceptable.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3156"> So Greer pushed himself up the knowledge ladder. </seg>
<seg id="3157"> As publications demanded that all final products be presented as digital files that could be uploaded, and there was no longer any more demand for that precious flap art, he transformed himself into an ideas consultant. </seg>
<seg id="3158"> ˇ°Ideationˇ± was what his clients, including McDonald's and Unilever, wanted. </seg>
<seg id="3159"> He stopped using pens and ink and would just do pencil sketches, scan them into his computer, color them by using the computer's mouse, and then e-mail them to the client, which would have some less skilled artists finish them. </seg>
<seg id="3160"> ˇ°It was unconscious,ˇ± said Greer. </seg>
<seg id="3161"> ˇ°I had to look for work that not everyone else could do, and that young artists couldn't do with technology for a fraction of what I was being paid. </seg>
<seg id="3162"> So I started getting offers where people would say to me, 'Can you do this and just give us the big idea?' </seg>
<seg id="3163"> They would give me a concept, and they would just want sketches, ideas, and not a finished piece of art. </seg>
<seg id="3164"> I still use the basic skill of drawing, but just to convey an idea-quick sketches, not finished artwork. </seg>
<seg id="3165"> And for these ideas they will still pay pretty good money. </seg>
<seg id="3166"> It has actually taken me to a different level. </seg>
<seg id="3167"> It is more like being a consultant rather than a JAFA (Just Another Fucking Artist). </seg>
<seg id="3168"> There are a lot of JAFAs out there. </seg>
<seg id="3169"> So now I am an idea man, and I have played off that. </seg>
<seg id="3170"> My clients just buy concepts.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3171"> ˇ°They can take my raw sketches and finish them and illustrate them using computer programs, and it is not like I would do it, but it is good enough,ˇ± Greer said. </seg>
<seg id="3172"> But then another thing happened. </seg>
<seg id="3173"> While the evolving technology turned the lower end of Greer's business into a commodity, it opened up a whole new market at the upper end: Greer's magazine clients. </seg>
<seg id="3174"> One day, one of his regular clients approached him and asked if he could do morphs. </seg>
<seg id="3175"> Morphs are cartoon strips in which one character evolves into another. </seg>
<seg id="3176"> So Martha Stewart is in the opening frame and morphs into Courtney Love by the closing frame. </seg>
<seg id="3177"> Drew Barrymore morphs into Drew Carey. </seg>
<seg id="3178"> Mariah Carey morphs into Jim Carrey. </seg>
<seg id="3179"> Cher morphs into Britney Spears. </seg>
<seg id="3180"> When he was first approached to do these, Greer had no idea where to begin. </seg>
<seg id="3181"> So he went onto Amazon.com and located some specialized software, bought it, tried it out for a few days, and produced his first morph. </seg>
<seg id="3182"> Since then he has developed a specialty in the process, and the market for them has expanded to include Maxim magazine, More, and Nickelodeon-one a men's magazine, one a middle-aged women's magazine, and one a kids' magazine. </seg>
<seg id="3183"> In other words, someone invented a whole new kind of sauce to go on the vanilla, and Greer jumped on it. </seg>
<seg id="3184"> This is exactly what happens in the global economy as a whole. </seg>
<seg id="3185"> ˇ°I was experienced enough to pick these [morphs] up pretty quickly,ˇ± said Greer. ˇ°Now I do them on my Mac laptop, anywhere I am, from Santa Barbara to Minneapolis to my apartment in New York. </seg>
<seg id="3186"> Sometimes clients give me a subject, and sometimes I just come up with them. </seg>
<seg id="3187"> Morphing used to be one of those really high-end things you saw on TV, and then they came out with this consumer [software] program and people could do it themselves, and I shaped them so magazines could use them. I just upload them as a series of JPEG files... </seg>
<seg id="3188"> Morphs have been a good business for different magazines. </seg>
<seg id="3189"> I even get fan mail from kids!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3190"> Greer had never done morphs until the technology evolved and created a new, specialized niche, just when a changing market for his work made him eager to learn new skills. </seg>
<seg id="3191"> ˇ°I wish I could say it was all intentional,ˇ± he confessed. </seg>
<seg id="3192"> ˇ°I was just available for work and just lucky they gave me a chance to do these things. </seg>
<seg id="3193"> I know so many artists who got washed out. </seg>
<seg id="3194"> One guy who was an illustrator has become a package designer, some have gotten out of the field altogether; one of the best designers I know became a landscape architect. </seg>
<seg id="3195"> She is still a designer but changed her medium altogether. </seg>
<seg id="3196"> Visual people can adapt, but I am still nervous about the future.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3197"> I told Greer his story fit well into some of the terms I was using in this book. </seg>
<seg id="3198"> He began as a chocolate sauce (a classic illustrator), was turned into a vanilla commodity (a classic illustrator in the computer age), upgraded his skills to become a special chocolate sauce again (a design consultant), then learned how to become a cherry on top (a morphs artist) by fulfilling a new demand created by an increasingly specialized market. </seg>
<seg id="3199"> Greer contemplated my compliment for a moment and then said, ˇ°And here all I was trying to do was survive-and I still am.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3200"> As he got up to leave, though, he told me that he was going out to meet a friend ˇ°to juggle together.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3201"> They have been juggling partners for years, just a little side business they sometimes do on a street corner or for private parties. </seg>
<seg id="3202"> Greer has very good hand-eye coordination. </seg>
<seg id="3203"> ˇ°But even juggling is being commoditized,ˇ± he complained. </seg>
<seg id="3204"> ˇ°It used to be if you could juggle five balls, you were really special. </seg>
<seg id="3205"> Now juggling five balls is like just anteing up. </seg>
<seg id="3206"> My partner and I used to perform together, and he was the seven-ball champ when I met him. </seg>
<seg id="3207"> Now fourteen-year-old kids can juggle seven balls, no problem. </seg>
<seg id="3208"> Now they have these books, like Juggling for Dummies, and kits that will teach you how to juggle. </seg>
<seg id="3209"> So they've just upped the standard.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3210"> There are so many things about the American system that are ideally suited for nurturing individuals who can compete and thrive in a flat world.How so? </seg>
<seg id="3211"> It starts with America's research universities, which spin off a steady stream of competitive experiments, innovations, and scientific breakthroughsˇŞfrom mathematics to biology to physics to chemistry. </seg>
<seg id="3212"> ˇ°Our university system is the best,ˇ± said Bill Gates. </seg>
<seg id="3213"> ˇ°We fund our universities to do a lot of research and that is an amazing thing. </seg>
<seg id="3214"> High-IQ people come here, and we allow them to innovate and turn [their innovations] into products. </seg>
<seg id="3215"> We reward risk taking. </seg>
<seg id="3216"> Our university system is competitive and experimental. </seg>
<seg id="3217"> They can try out different approaches. </seg>
<seg id="3218"> There are one hundred universities making contributions to robotics. And each one is saying that the other is doing it all wrong, or my piece actually fits together with theirs. </seg>
<seg id="3219"> It is a chaotic system, but it is a great engine of innovation in the world, and with federal tax money, with some philanthropy on top of that, [it will continue to flourish]... </seg>
<seg id="3220"> We will really haVe to screw things up for our absolute wealth not to increase. </seg>
<seg id="3221"> If we are smart, we can increase it faster by embracing this stuff.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3222"> The Web browser, magnetic resonance imaging (MRI), superfast computers, global position technology, space exploration devices, and fiber optics are just a few of the many inventions that got started through basic university research projects. </seg>
<seg id="3223"> The BankBoston Economics Department did a study titled ˇ°MIT: The Impact of Innovation.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3224"> Among its conclusions was that MIT graduates have founded 4,000 companies, creating at least 1.1 million jobs worldwide and generating sales of $232 billion. </seg>
<seg id="3225"> What makes America unique is not that it built MIT, or that its grads are generating economic growth and innovation, but that every state in the country has universities trying to do the same. </seg>
<seg id="3226"> ˇ°America has 4,000 colleges and universities,ˇ± said Allan E. Goodman, president of the Institute of International Education. </seg>
<seg id="3227"> ˇ°The rest of the world combined has 7,768 institutions of higher education. </seg>
<seg id="3228"> In the state of California alone, there are about 130 colleges and universities. </seg>
<seg id="3229"> There are only 14 countries in the world that have more than that number.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3230"> Take a state you normally wouldn't think of in this regard: Oklahoma. </seg>
<seg id="3231"> It has its own Oklahoma Center for the Advancement of Science and Technology (OCAST), which, on its Web site, describes its mission as follows: ˇ°In order to compete effectively in the new economy, Oklahoma must continue to develop a well-educated population; a collaborative, focused university research and technology base; and a nurturing environment for cutting-edge businesses, from the smallest start-up to the largest international headquarters... </seg>
<seg id="3232"> [OCAST promotes] University-Business technology centers, which may span several schools and businesses, resulting in new businesses being spawned, new products being manufactured, and new manufacturing technologies employed.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3233"> No wonder that in 2003, American universities reaped $1.3 billion from patents, according to the Association of University Technology Managers. </seg>
<seg id="3234"> Coupled with America's unique innovation-generating machines- universities, public and private research labs, and retailers-we have the best-regulated and most efficient capital markets in the world for taking new ideas and turning them into products and services. </seg>
<seg id="3235"> Dick Foster, director of McKinsey &amp; Co. and the author of two books on innovation, remarked to me, ˇ°We have an 'industrial policy' in the U.S. -it is called the stock exchange, whether it is the NYSE or the Nasdaq.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3236"> That is where risk capital is collected and assigned to emerging ideas or growing companies, Foster said, and no capital market in the world does that better and more efficiently than the American one. </seg>
<seg id="3237"> What makes capital provision work so well here is the security and regulation of our capital markets, where minority shareholders are protected. </seg>
<seg id="3238"> Lord knows, there are scams, excesses, and corruption in our capital markets. </seg>
<seg id="3239"> That always happens when a lot of money is at stake. </seg>
<seg id="3240"> What distinguishes our capital markets is not that Enrons don't happen in America-they sure do. </seg>
<seg id="3241"> It is that when they happen, they usually get exposed, either by the Securities and Exchange Commission or by the business press, and get corrected. </seg>
<seg id="3242"> What makes America unique is not Enron but Eliot Spitzer, the attorney general of New York State, who has doggedly sought to clean up the securities industry and corporate boardrooms. </seg>
<seg id="3243"> This sort of capital market has proved very, very difficult to duplicate outside of New York, London, Frankfurt, and Tokyo. </seg>
<seg id="3244"> Said Foster, ˇ°China and India and other Asian countries will not be successful at innovation until they have successful capital markets, and they will not have successful capital markets until they have rule of law which protects minority interests under conditions of risk... </seg>
<seg id="3245"> We in the U.S. are the lucky beneficiaries of centuries of economic experimentation, and we are the experiment that has worked.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3246"> While these are the core secrets of America's sauce, there are others that need to be preserved and nurtured. </seg>
<seg id="3247"> Sometimes you have to talk to outsiders to appreciate them, such as Indian-born Vivek Paul of Wipro. </seg>
<seg id="3248"> ˇ°I would add three to your list,ˇ± he said to me. ˇ°One is the sheer openness of American society.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3249"> We Americans often forget what an incredibly open, say-anything-do-anything-start-anyming-go-bankrupt-and-start-anything-ag ain society the United States is. </seg>
<seg id="3250"> There is no place like it in the world, and our openness is a huge asset and attraction to foreigners, many of whom come from countries where the sky is not the limit. </seg>
<seg id="3251"> Another, said Paul, is the ˇ°quality of American intellectual property protection,ˇ± which further enhances and encourages people to come up with new ideas. </seg>
<seg id="3252"> In a flat world, there is a great incentive to develop a new product or process, because it can achieve global scale in a flash. </seg>
<seg id="3253"> But if you are the person who comes up with that new idea, you want your intellectual property protected. </seg>
<seg id="3254"> ˇ°No country respects and protects intellectual property better than America,ˇ± said Paul, and as a result, a lot of innovators want to come here to work and lodge their intellectual property. </seg>
<seg id="3255"> The United States also has among the most flexible labor laws in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3256"> The easier it is to fire someone in a dying industry, the easier it is to hire someone in a rising industry that no one knew would exist five years earlier. </seg>
<seg id="3257"> This is a great asset, especially when you compare the situation in the United States to inflexible, rigidly regulated labor markets like Germany's, full of government restrictions on hiring and firing. </seg>
<seg id="3258"> Flexibility to quickly deploy labor and capital where the greatest opportunity exists, and the ability to quickly redeploy it if the earlier deployment is no longer profitable, is essential in a flattening world. </seg>
<seg id="3259"> Still another secret to America's sauce is the fact that it has the world's largest domestic consumer market, with the most first adopters, in the world, which means that if you are introducing a new product, technology, or service, you have to have a presence in America. </seg>
<seg id="3260"> All this means a steady flow of jobs for Americans. </seg>
<seg id="3261"> Finally, the United States has become one of the great meeting points in the world, a place where lots of different people bond and learn to trust one another. </seg>
<seg id="3262"> An Indian student who is educated at the University of Oklahoma and then gets his first job with a software firm in Oklahoma City forges bonds of trust and understanding that are really important for future collaboration, even if he winds up returning to India. </seg>
<seg id="3263"> Nothing illustrates this point better than Yale University's outsourcing of research to China. </seg>
<seg id="3264"> Yale president Richard C. Levin explained to me that Yale has two big research operations running in China today, one at Peking University in Beijing and the other at Fudan University in Shanghai. </seg>
<seg id="3265"> ˇ°Most of these institutional collaborations arise not from top-down directives of university administrators, but rather from long-standing personal relationships among scholars and scientists,ˇ± said Levin. </seg>
<seg id="3266"> How did the Yale-Fudan collaboration arise? To begin with, said Levin, Yale professor Tian Xu, its director, had a deep affiliation with both institutions. </seg>
<seg id="3267"> He did his undergraduate work at Fudan and received his Ph.D. from Yale. </seg>
<seg id="3268"> ˇ°Five of Professor Xu's collaborators, who are now professors at Fudan, were also trained at Yale,ˇ± explained Levin. </seg>
<seg id="3269"> One was Professor Xu's friend when both were Yale graduate students; </seg>
<seg id="3270"> another was a visiting scholar in the laboratory of a Yale colleague; </seg>
<seg id="3271"> one was an exchange student who came to Yale from Fudan and returned to earn his Ph.D. in China; </seg>
<seg id="3272"> and the other two were postdoctoral fellows in Professor Xu's Yale lab. </seg>
<seg id="3273"> A similar story underlies the formation of the Peking-Yale Joint Center for Plant Molecular Genetics and Agrobiotechnology.Professor Xu is a leading expert on genetics and has won grants from the National Institutes of Health and the Howard Hughes Foundation to study the connection between genetics and cancer and certain neuro-degenerative diseases. </seg>
<seg id="3274"> This kind of research requires the study of large numbers of genetic mutations in lab animals. </seg>
<seg id="3275"> ˇ°When you want to test many genes and trace for a given gene that may be responsible for certain diseases, you need to run a lot of tests. </seg>
<seg id="3276"> Having a bigger staff is a huge advantage,ˇ± explained Levin. </seg>
<seg id="3277"> So what Yale did was essentially outsource the lab work to Fudan by creating the Fudan-Yale Biomedical Research Center. </seg>
<seg id="3278"> Each university pays for its own staff and research, so no money changes hands, but the Chinese side does the basic technical work using large numbers of technicians and lab animals, which cost so much less in China, and Yale does the high-end analysis of the data. </seg>
<seg id="3279"> The Fudan staff, students, and technicians get great exposure to high-end research, and Yale gets a large-scale testing facility that would have been prohibitively expensive if Yale had tried to duplicate it in New Haven. </seg>
<seg id="3280"> A support lab in America for a project like this one might have 30 technicians, but the one in Fudan has 150. </seg>
<seg id="3281"> ˇ°The gains are very much two-way,ˇ± said Levin. </seg>
<seg id="3282"> ˇ°Our investigators get substantially enhanced productivity, and the Chinese get their graduate students trained, and their young faculty become collaborators with our professors, who are the leaders in their fields. </seg>
<seg id="3283"> It builds human capital for China and innovation for Yale.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3284"> Graduate students from both universities go back and forth, forging relationships that will no doubt produce more collaborations in the future. </seg>
<seg id="3285"> At the same time, he added, a lot of legal preparation went into this collaboration to make sure that Yale would be able to harvest the intellectual property that is created. </seg>
<seg id="3286"> ˇ°There is one world of science out there,ˇ± said Levin, ˇ°and this kind of international division of labor makes a lot of sense.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3287"> Yale, he said, also insisted that the working conditions at the Chinese labs be world-class, and, as a result, it has also helped to lift the quality of the Chinese facilities. </seg>
<seg id="3288"> ˇ°The living conditions of the lab animals are right up to U.S. standards,ˇ± remarked Levin. </seg>
<seg id="3289"> ˇ°These are not mouse sweatshops.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3290"> So are we still working at it? </seg>
<seg id="3291"> See the next chapter. </seg>
<seg id="3292"> But here's a quick hint: </seg>
<seg id="3293"> The answer is no. </seg>
<seg id="3294"> SEVEN The Quiet Crisis </seg>
<seg id="3295"> Close games for the Americans were rare in previous Olympics, but now it appears to be something the Americans should get used to. </seg>
<seg id="3296"> ˇŞFrom an August 17, 2004, AP article from the Athens Olympics titled ˇ°U.S. Men's Basketball Team Narrowly Beats Greeceˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3297"> You could find no better metaphor for the way the rest of the world can now compete head-to-head more effectively than ever with America than the struggles of the U.S. Olympic basketball team in 2004. </seg>
<seg id="3298"> The American team, made up of NBA stars, limped home to a bronze medal after losing to Puerto Rico, Lithuania, and Argentina. </seg>
<seg id="3299"> Previously, the United States Olympic basketball team had lost only one game in the history of the modern Olympics. </seg>
<seg id="3300"> Remember when America sent only NCAA stars to the Olympic basketball events? </seg>
<seg id="3301"> For a long time these teams totally dominated all comers. </seg>
<seg id="3302"> Then they started getting challenged. </seg>
<seg id="3303"> So we sent our pros. </seg>
<seg id="3304"> And they started getting challenged. </seg>
<seg id="3305"> Because the world keeps learning, the diffusion of knowledge happens faster; </seg>
<seg id="3306"> coaches in other countries now download American coaching methods off the Internet and watch NBA games in their own living rooms on satellite TV. </seg>
<seg id="3307"> Many of them can even get ESPN and watch the highlight reels. </seg>
<seg id="3308"> And thanks to the triple convergence, there is a lot of new raw talent walking onto the NBA courts from all over the world-including many new stars from China, Latin America, and Eastern Europe. </seg>
<seg id="3309"> They go back and play for their national teams in the Olympics, using the skills they honed in America. </seg>
<seg id="3310"> So the automatic American superiority of twenty years ago is now gone in Olympic basketball. </seg>
<seg id="3311"> The NBA standard is increasingly becoming a global commodity-pure vanilla. </seg>
<seg id="3312"> If the United States wants to continue to dominate in Olympic basketball, we must, in that great sports cliche, step it up a notch. </seg>
<seg id="3313"> The old standard won't do anymore. </seg>
<seg id="3314"> As Joel Cawley of IBM remarked to me, ˇ°Star for star, the basketball teams from places like Lithuania or Puerto Rico still don't rank well versus the Americans, but when they play as a team-when they collaborate better than we do-they are extremely competitive.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3315"> Sports writer John Feinstein could have been referring to either American engineering skills or American basketball skills when he wrote in an August 26, 2004, AOL essay on Olympic basketball that the performance of the U.S. basketball team is a result of ˇ°the rise of the international playerˇ± and ˇ°the decline and fall of the U.S. game.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3316"> And the decline and fall of the U.S. game, argued Feinstein, is a result of two long-term trends. </seg>
<seg id="3317"> The first is a steady decline ˇ°in basketball skills,ˇ± with American kids just wanting to shoot either three-point shots or dunk- the sort of stuff that gets you on ESPN's SportsCenter highlight reelˇŞinstead of learning how to make precise passes, or go into the lane and shoot a pull-up jumper, or snake through big men to get to the basket. </seg>
<seg id="3318"> Those skills take a lot of hard work and coaching to learn. </seg>
<seg id="3319"> Today, said Feinstein, you have an American generation that relies almost completely on athleticism and almost not at all on basketball skills. </seg>
<seg id="3320"> And there is also that ugly little problem of ambition. </seg>
<seg id="3321"> While the rest of the world was getting better in basketball, ˇ°more and more NBA players were yawning at the notion of playing in the Olympics,ˇ± noted Feinstein. ˇ°We have come a long way from 1984, when Bob Knight told Charles Barkley to show up to the second Olympic training camp at 265 pounds or else. </seg>
<seg id="3322"> Barkley showed up weighing 280. </seg>
<seg id="3323"> Knight cut him that day. </seg>
<seg id="3324"> In today's world, the Olympic coach wouldn't even have checked Barkley's weight in the first place. He would have sent a limousine to the airport to get him and stopped at Dunkin' Donuts on the way to the hotel if the player requested it... </seg>
<seg id="3325"> The world changes. </seg>
<seg id="3326"> In the case of American basketball, it hasn't changed for the better.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3327"> There is something about post-World War II America that reminds me of the classic wealthy family that by the third generation starts to squander its wealth. </seg>
<seg id="3328"> The members of the first generation are nose-to-the-grindstone innovators; </seg>
<seg id="3329"> the second generation holds it all together; </seg>
<seg id="3330"> then their kids come along and get fat, dumb, and lazy and slowly squander it all. </seg>
<seg id="3331"> I know that is both overly harsh and a gross generalization, but there is, nevertheless, some truth in it. </seg>
<seg id="3332"> American society started to coast in the 1990s, when our third postwar generation came of age. </seg>
<seg id="3333"> The dot-com boom left too many people with the impression that they could get rich without investing in hard work. </seg>
<seg id="3334"> All it took was an MBA and a quick IPO, or one NBA contract, and you were set for life. </seg>
<seg id="3335"> But while we were admiring the flat world we had created, a lot of people in India, China, and Eastern Europe were busy figuring out how to take advantage of it. </seg>
<seg id="3336"> Lucky for us, we were the only economy standing after World War II, and we had no serious competition for forty years. </seg>
<seg id="3337"> That gave us a huge head of steam but also a huge sense of entitlement and complacency-not to mention a certain tendency in recent years to extol consumption over hard work, investment, and long-term thinking. </seg>
<seg id="3338"> When we got hit with 9/11, it was a once-in-a-generation opportunity to summon the nation to sacrifice, to address some of its pressing fiscal, energy, science, and education shortfalls-all the things that we had let slide. </seg>
<seg id="3339"> But our president did not summon us to sacrifice. He summoned us to go shopping. </seg>
<seg id="3340"> In the previous chapters, I showed why both classic economic theory and the inherent strengths of the American economy have convinced me that American individuals have nothing to worry about from a flat world-provided we roll up our sleeves, be ready to compete, get every individual to think about how he or she upgrades his or her educational skills, and keep investing in the secrets of the American sauce. </seg>
<seg id="3341"> Those chapters were all about what we must do and can do.This chapter is about how we Americans, individually and collectively, have not been doing all these things that we should be doing and what will happen down the road if we don't change course. </seg>
<seg id="3342"> The truth is, we are in a crisis now, but it is a crisis that is unfolding very slowly and very quietly. </seg>
<seg id="3343"> It is ˇ°a quiet crisis,ˇ± explained Shirley Ann Jackson, the 2004 president of the American Association for the Advancement of Science and president of Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute since 1999. (Rensselaer is America's oldest technological college, founded in 1824.) </seg>
<seg id="3344"> And this quiet crisis involves the steady erosion of America's scientific and engineering base, which has always been the source of American innovation and our rising standard of living. </seg>
<seg id="3345"> ˇ°The sky is not falling, nothing horrible is going to happen today,ˇ± said Jackson, a physicist by training who chooses her words carefully. </seg>
<seg id="3346"> ˇ°The U.S. is still the leading engine for innovation in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3347"> It has the best graduate programs, the best scientific infrastructure, and the capital markets to exploit it. </seg>
<seg id="3348"> But there is a quiet crisis in U.S. science and technology that we have to wake up to. </seg>
<seg id="3349"> The U.S. today is in a truly global environment, and those competitor countries are not only wide awake, they are running a marathon while we are running sprints. </seg>
<seg id="3350"> Shirley Ann Jackson knows of what she speaks, because her career exemplifies as well as anyone's both why America thrived so much in the past fifty years and why it won't automatically do the same in the next fifty. </seg>
<seg id="3351"> An African-American woman, Jackson was born in Washington, D.C., in 1946. </seg>
<seg id="3352"> She started kindergarten in a segregated public school but was one of the first public school students to benefit from desegregation, as a result of the Supreme Court ruling in Brown v. Board of Education. </seg>
<seg id="3353"> Just when she was getting a chance to go to a better school, the Russians launched Sputnik in 1957, and the U.S. government became obsessed with educating young people to become scientists and engineers, a trend that was intensified by John F. Kennedy's commitment to a manned space program. </seg>
<seg id="3354"> When Kennedy spoke about putting a man on the moon, Shirley Ann Jackson was one of the millions of American young people who were listening. </seg>
<seg id="3355"> His words, she recalled, ˇ°inspired, assisted, and launched many of my generation into science, engineering and mathematics,ˇ± and the breakthroughs and inventions they spawned went well beyond the space program. </seg>
<seg id="3356"> ˇ°The space race was really a science race,ˇ± she said.Thanks in part to desegregation, both Jackson's inspiration and intellect were recognized early, and she ultimately became the first African-American woman to earn a Ph.D. in physics from MIT (her degree was in theoretical elementary particle physics). </seg>
<seg id="3357"> From there, she spent many years working for AT&amp;T Bell Laboratories, and in 1995 was appointed by President Clinton to chair the U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission. </seg>
<seg id="3358"> As the years went by, though, Jackson began to notice that fewer and fewer young Americans were captivated by national challenges like the race to the moon, or felt the allure of math, science, and engineering. </seg>
<seg id="3359"> In universities, she noted, graduate enrollment in science and engineering programs, having grown for decades, peaked in 1993, and despite some recent progress, it remains today below the level of a decade ago. </seg>
<seg id="3360"> So the science and engineering generations that followed Jackson's got smaller and smaller relative to our needs. </seg>
<seg id="3361"> By the time Jackson took the job as Rensselaer Polytechnic's president to put her heart and soul into reinvig-orating American science and engineering, she realized, she said, that a ˇ°perfect stormˇ± was brewing-one that posed a real long-term danger to America's economic health-and she started speaking out about it whenever she could. </seg>
<seg id="3362"> ˇ°The phrase 'the perfect storm' is associated with meteorological events in October 1991,ˇ± said Jackson in a speech in May 2004, when ˇ°a powerful weather system gathered force, ravaging the Atlantic Ocean over the course of several days, [and] caused the deaths of several Massachusetts-based fishermen and billions of dollars of damage. </seg>
<seg id="3363"> The event became a book, and, later, a movie. </seg>
<seg id="3364"> Meteorologists observing the event emphasized... the unlikely confluence of conditions... in which multiple factors converged to bring about an event of devastating magnitude. [A] similar worst-case scenario could arrest the progress of our national scientific and technological capacity. </seg>
<seg id="3365"> The forces at work are multiple and complex. </seg>
<seg id="3366"> They are demographic, political, economic, cultural, even social.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3367"> Individually, each of these forces would be problematic, added Jackson. </seg>
<seg id="3368"> In combination, they could be devastating. </seg>
<seg id="3369"> ˇ°For the first time in more than a century, the United States could well find itself falling behind other countries in the capacity for scientific discovery, innovation and economic development.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3370"> Then this won't be a quiet crisis anymore, said Jackson, ˇ°it will be the real McCoy.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3371"> Dirty Little Secret #1: The Numbers Gap </seg>
<seg id="3372"> Dirty little secret number one is that the generation of scientists and engineers who were motivated to go into science by the threat of Sputnik in 1957 and the inspiration of JFK are reaching their retirement years and are not being replaced in the numbers that they must be if an advanced economy like that of the United States is to remain at the head of the pack. </seg>
<seg id="3373"> According to the National Science Foundation, half of America's scientists and engineers are forty years or older, and the average age is steadily rising.Just take one example-NASA. </seg>
<seg id="3374"> An analysis of NASA records conducted by the newspaper Florida Today (March 7, 2004), which covers the Kennedy Space Center, showed the following: Nearly 40 percent of the 18,146 people at NASA are age fifty or older. </seg>
<seg id="3375"> We don't have any time to waste in addressing the ˇ°dirty little secretsˇ± of our education system. </seg>
<seg id="3376"> Those with twenty years of government service are eligible for early retirement. </seg>
<seg id="3377"> Twenty-two percent of NASA workers are fifty-five or older. </seg>
<seg id="3378"> NASA employees over sixty outnumber those under thirty by a ratio of about three to one. </seg>
<seg id="3379"> Only 4 percent of NASA workers are under thirty. </seg>
<seg id="3380"> Many of these jobs are reserved for American citizens, because of national security concerns. </seg>
<seg id="3381"> A 2003 Government Accounting Office study concluded that NASA was having difficulty hiring people with the sufficient science, engineering, and information-technology skills that are critical to its operations. </seg>
<seg id="3382"> Then-NASA administrator Sean O'Keefe testified before Congress in 2002: ˇ°Our mission of understanding and protecting our home planet and exploring the universe and searching for life will not be carried out if we don't have the people to do it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3383"> The National Commission on Mathematics and Science Teaching for the Twenty-first Century, chaired by the former astronaut and senator John Glenn, found that two-thirds of the nation's mathematics and science teaching force will retire by 2010. </seg>
<seg id="3384"> Traditionally we made up for any shortages of engineers and science faculty by educating more at home and importing more from abroad. </seg>
<seg id="3385"> But both of those remedies have been stalled of late.Every two years the National Science Board supervises the collection of a very broad set of data trends in science and technology in the United States, which it publishes as Science and Engineering Indicators. </seg>
<seg id="3386"> In preparing Indicators 2004, the NSB said, ˇ°We have observed a troubling decline in the number of U.S. citizens who are training to become scientists and engineers, whereas the number of jobs requiring science and engineering (S&amp;E) training continues to grow.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3387"> These trends threaten the economic welfare and security of our country, it said, adding that if the trends identified in Indicators 2004 continue undeterred, three things will happen: ˇ°The number of jobs in the U.S. economy that require science and engineering training will grow; </seg>
<seg id="3388"> the number of U.S. citizens prepared for those jobs will, at best, be level; </seg>
<seg id="3389"> and the availability of people from other countries who have science and engineering training will decline, either because of limits to entry imposed by U.S. national security restrictions or because of intense global competition for people with these skills.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3390"> The NSB report found that the number of American eighteen-to-twenty-four-year-olds who receive science degrees has fallen to seventeenth in the world, whereas we ranked third three decades ago. </seg>
<seg id="3391"> It said that of the 2.8 million first university degrees (what we call bachelor's degrees) in science and engineering granted worldwide in 2003, 1.2 million were earned by Asian students in Asian universities, 830,000 were granted in Europe, and 400,000 in the United States. </seg>
<seg id="3392"> In engineering specifically, universities in Asian countries now produce eight times as many bachelor's degrees as the United States. </seg>
<seg id="3393"> Moreover, ˇ°the proportional emphasis on science and engineering is greater in other nations,ˇ± noted Shirley Ann Jackson. </seg>
<seg id="3394"> Science and engineering degrees now represent 60 percent of all bachelor's degrees earned in China, 33 percent in South Korea, and 41 percent in Taiwan. </seg>
<seg id="3395"> By contrast, the percentage of those taking a bachelor's degree in science and engineering in the United States remains at roughly 31 percent. </seg>
<seg id="3396"> The United States has always depended on the inventiveness of its people in order to compete in the world marketplace, said the NSB. </seg>
<seg id="3397"> ˇ°Preparation of the S&amp;E workforce is a vital arena for national competitiveness. </seg>
<seg id="3398"> [But] even if action is taken today to change these trends, the reversal is 10 to 20 years away.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3399"> The students entering the science and engineering workforce with advanced degrees in 2004 decided to take the necessary math courses to enable this career path when they were in middle school, up to fourteen years ago, the NSB noted. </seg>
<seg id="3400"> The students making that same decision in middle school today won't complete advanced training for science and engineering occupations until 2018 or 2020. </seg>
<seg id="3401"> ˇ°If action is not taken now to change these trends, we could reach 2020 and find that the ability of U.S. research and education institutions to regenerate has been damaged and that their preeminence has been lost to other areas of the world,ˇ± the science board said. </seg>
<seg id="3402"> These shortages could not be happening at a worse time-just when the world is going flat. </seg>
<seg id="3403"> ˇ°The number of jobs requiring science and engineering skills in the U.S. labor force,ˇ± the NSB said, ˇ°is growing almost 5 percent per year. </seg>
<seg id="3404"> In comparison, the rest of the labor force is growing at just over 1 percent. </seg>
<seg id="3405"> Before September 11, 2001, the Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS) projected that science and engineering occupations would increase at three times the rate of all occupations.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3406"> Unfortunately, the NSB reported, the average age of the science and engineering workforce is rising. </seg>
<seg id="3407"> ˇ°Many of those who entered the expanding S&amp;E workforce in the 1960s and 1970s (the baby boom generation) are expected to retire in the next twenty years, and their children are not choosing science and engineering careers in the same numbers as their parents,ˇ± the NSB report said. </seg>
<seg id="3408"> ˇ°The percentage of women, for example, choosing math and computer science careers fell 4 percentage points between 1993 and 1999.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3409"> The 2002 NSB indicators showed that the number of science and engineering Ph.D.'s awarded in the United States dropped from 29,000 in 1998 to 27,000 in 1999. </seg>
<seg id="3410"> The total number of engineering undergraduates in America fell about 12 percent between the mid-1980s and 1998. </seg>
<seg id="3411"> Nevertheless, America's science and engineering labor force grew at a rate well above that of America's production of science and engineering degrees, because a large number of foreign-born S&amp;E graduates migrated to the United States. </seg>
<seg id="3412"> The proportion of foreign-born students in S&amp;E fields and workers in S&amp;E occupations continued to rise steadily in the 1990s. </seg>
<seg id="3413"> The NSB said that persons born outside the United States accounted for 14 percent of all S&amp;E occupations in 1990. </seg>
<seg id="3414"> Between 1990 and 2000, the proportion of foreign-born people with bachelor's degrees in S&amp;E occupations rose from 11 to 17 percent; the proportion of foreign-born with master's degrees rose from 19 to 29 percent; </seg>
<seg id="3415"> and the proportion of foreign-born with Ph.D.'s in the S&amp;E labor force rose from 24 to 38 percent. </seg>
<seg id="3416"> By attracting scientists and engineers born and trained in other countries we have maintained the growth of the S&amp;E labor force without a commensurate increase in support for the long-term costs of training and attracting native U.S citizens to these fields, the NSB said. </seg>
<seg id="3417"> But now, the simultaneous flattening and wiring of the world have made it much easier for foreigners to innovate without having to emigrate. </seg>
<seg id="3418"> They can now do world-class work for world-class companies at very decent wages without ever having to leave home. </seg>
<seg id="3419"> As Allan E. Goodman, president of the Institute of International Education, put it, ˇ°When the world was round, they could not go back home, because there was no lab to go back to and no Internet to connect to. </seg>
<seg id="3420"> But now all those things are there, so they are going back. </seg>
<seg id="3421"> Now they are saying, 'I feel more comfortable back home. </seg>
<seg id="3422"> I can live more comfortably back home than in New York City and I can do good work, so why not go back?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3423"> This trend started even before the visa hassles brought on by 9/11, said Goodman. </seg>
<seg id="3424"> ˇ°The brain gain started to go to brain drain around the year 2000.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3425"> As the NSB study noted, ˇ°Since the 1980s other countries have increased investment in S&amp;E education and the S&amp;E workforce at higher rates than the United States has. </seg>
<seg id="3426"> Between 1993 and 1997, the OECD countries [Organization for Economic Co-operation and Development, a group of 40 nations with highly developed market economies] increased their number of S&amp;E research jobs 23 percent, more than twice the 11 percent increase in S&amp;E research jobs in the United States.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3427"> In addition, it said, visas for students and S&amp;E workers have been issued more slowly since the events of September 11, owing to both increased security restrictions and a drop in applications. </seg>
<seg id="3428"> The U.S. State Department issued 20 percent fewer visas for foreign students in 2001 than in 2000, and the rate fell farther in subsequent years. </seg>
<seg id="3429"> While university presidents told me in 2004 that the situation was getting better, and that the Department of Homeland Security was trying to both speed up and simplify its visa procedures for foreign students and scientists, a lot of damage has been done, and the situation for foreign students or scientists wanting to work in any areas deemed to have national security implications is becoming a real problem. </seg>
<seg id="3430"> No wonder New York Times education writer Sam Dillon reported on December 21, 2004, that ˇ°foreign applications to American graduate schools declined 28 percent this year. </seg>
<seg id="3431"> Actual foreign graduate student enrollments dropped 6 percent. </seg>
<seg id="3432"> Enrollments of all foreign students, in undergraduate, graduate and postdoctoral programs, fell for the first time in three decades in an annual census released this fall. </seg>
<seg id="3433"> Meanwhile, university enrollments have been surging in England, Germany and other countries... </seg>
<seg id="3434"> Chinese applications to American graduate schools fell 45 percent this year, while several European countries announced surges in Chinese enrollment.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3435"> In China, where there are 1.3 billion people and the universities are just starting to crack the top ranks, the competition for top spots is ferocious. </seg>
<seg id="3436"> The math/science salmon that swims upstream in China and gets itself admitted to a top Chinese university or hired by a foreign company is one smart fish. </seg>
<seg id="3437"> The folks at Microsoft have a saying about their research center in Beijing, which, for scientists and engineers, is one of the most sought-after places to work in all of China. </seg>
<seg id="3438"> ˇ°Remember, in China when you are one in a million- there are 1,300 other people just like you.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3439"> The brainpower that rises to the Microsoft research center in Beijing is already one in a million. </seg>
<seg id="3440"> Consider the annual worldwide Intel International Science and Engineering Fair. </seg>
<seg id="3441"> About forty countries participate by nominating talent through local affiliate affairs. </seg>
<seg id="3442"> In 2004, the Intel Fair attracted around sixty-five thousand American kids, according to Intel. </seg>
<seg id="3443"> I asked Wee Theng Tan, the president of Intel China, during a visit to Beijing. In China, he told me, there is a national affiliate science fair, which acts as a feeder system to select kids for the global Intel fair. </seg>
<seg id="3444"> ˇ°Almost every single province has students going to one of these affiliate fairs,ˇ± said Tan. </seg>
<seg id="3445"> ˇ°We have as many as six million kids competing, although not all are competing for the top levels... </seg>
<seg id="3446"> [But] you know how seriously they take it. </seg>
<seg id="3447"> Those selected to go to the international [Intel] fair are immediately exempted from college entrance examsˇ± and basically get their choice of any top university in China. </seg>
<seg id="3448"> In the 2004 Intel Science Fair, China came home with thirty-five awards, more than any other country in Asia, including one of the top three global awards. </seg>
<seg id="3449"> My wife teaches first-grade reading in a local public school, so she gets Education Week, which is read by educators all over America. One day she pointed out an article (July 28, 2004) headlined, ˇ°Immigrants' Children Inhabit the Top Ranks of Math, Science Meets.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3450"> It went on to say, ˇ°Research conducted by the National Foundation for American Policy shows that 60 percent of the nation's top science students and 65 percent of the top mathematics students are children of recent immigrants, according to an analysis of award winners in three scholastic competitions... the Intel Science Talent Search, the U.S. team for the International Mathematical Olympiad, and the U.S. Physics Team.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3451"> The study's author attributed the immigrant students' success ˇ°partly to their parents' insistence that they manage study time wisely,ˇ± Education Week said. </seg>
<seg id="3452"> ˇ°Many immigrant parents also encouraged their children to pursue mathematics and science interests, believing those skills would lead to strong career opportunities and insulate them from bias and lack of connections in the workplace... </seg>
<seg id="3453"> A strong percentage of the students surveyed had parents who arrived in the United States on H-1B visas, reserved for professional workers. </seg>
<seg id="3454"> Dirty Little Secret #2: The Ambition Gap </seg>
<seg id="3455"> The second dirty little secret, which several prominent American CEOs told me only in a whisper, goes like this: When they send jobs abroad, they not only save 75 percent on wages, they get a 100 percent increase in productivity. </seg>
<seg id="3456"> Part of that is understandable. </seg>
<seg id="3457"> When you take a low-wage, low-prestige job in America, like a call center operator, and bring it over to India, where it becomes a high-wage, high-prestige job, you end up with workers who are paid less but motivated more. </seg>
<seg id="3458"> ˇ°The dirty little secret is that not only is [outsourcing] cheaper and efficient,ˇ± the American CEO of a London-headquartered multinational told me, ˇ°but the quality and productivity [boost] is huge.ˇ± In addition to the wage compression, he said, one Bangalore Indian re-trained will do the work of two or three Europeans, and the Bangalore employees don't take six weeks of holidays. </seg>
<seg id="3459"> ˇ°When you think it's only about wages,ˇ± he added, ˇ°you can still hold your dignity, but the fact that they work better is awful.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3460"> A short time after returning from India, I was approached in an airport by a young man who wanted to talk about some columns I had written from there. </seg>
<seg id="3461"> We had a nice chat, I asked him for his card, and we struck up an e-mail friendship. </seg>
<seg id="3462"> His name is Mike Arguello, and he is an IT systems architect living in San Antonio. </seg>
<seg id="3463"> He does high-end IT systems design and does not feel threatened by foreign competition. </seg>
<seg id="3464"> He also teaches computer science. </seg>
<seg id="3465"> When I asked him what we needed to do in America to get our edge back, he sent me this e-mail:I taught at a local university. </seg>
<seg id="3466"> It was disheartening to see the poor work ethic of many of my students. </seg>
<seg id="3467"> Of the students I taught over six semesters, I'd only consider hiring two of them. </seg>
<seg id="3468"> The rest lacked the creativity, problem-solving abilities and passion for learning. </seg>
<seg id="3469"> As you well know, India's biggest advantage over the Chinese and Russians is that they speak English. </seg>
<seg id="3470"> But it would be wrong to assume the top Indian developers are better than their American counterparts. </seg>
<seg id="3471"> The advantage they have is the number of bodies they can throw at a problem. </seg>
<seg id="3472"> The Indians that I work with are the cream of the crop. </seg>
<seg id="3473"> They are educated by the equivalents of MIT back in India and there are plenty of them. </seg>
<seg id="3474"> If you were to follow me in my daily meetings it would become very obvious that a great deal of my time is spent working with Indians. </seg>
<seg id="3475"> Most managers are probably still under the impression that all Indians are doing is lower-end software development-ˇ°software assembly.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3476"> But technologies, such as Linux, are allowing them to start taking higher-paying system design jobs that had previously been the exclusive domain of American workers. </seg>
<seg id="3477"> It has provided them with the means to move up the technology food chain, putting them on par with domestic workers. </seg>
<seg id="3478"> It's brain power against brain power, and in this area they are formidable. </seg>
<seg id="3479"> From a technology perspective, the world is flat and getting flatter (if that is possible). </seg>
<seg id="3480"> The only two areas that I have not seen Indian labor in are networking architects and system architects, but it is only a matter of time. </seg>
<seg id="3481"> Indians are very bright and they are quickly learning from their interaction with system architects just how all of the pieces of the IT puzzle fit together... </seg>
<seg id="3482"> Were Congress to pass legislation to stop the flow of Indian labor, you would have major software systems that would have nobody who knew what was going on. </seg>
<seg id="3483"> It is unfortunate that many management positions in IT are filled with non-technical managers who may not be fully aware of their exposure... </seg>
<seg id="3484"> I'm an expert in information systems, not economics, but I know a high-paying job requires one be able to produce something of high value. </seg>
<seg id="3485"> The economy is producing the jobs both at the high end and low end, but increasingly the high-end jobs are out of reach of many. </seg>
<seg id="3486"> Low education means low-paying jobs, plain and simple, and this is where more and more Americans are finding themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3487"> Many Americans can't believe they aren't qualified for high-paying jobs. I call this the ˇ°American Idol problem.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3488"> If you've ever seen the reaction of contestants when Simon Cowell tells them they have no talent, they look at him in total disbelief. </seg>
<seg id="3489"> I'm just hoping someday I'm not given such a rude awakening. </seg>
<seg id="3490"> In the winter of 2004 I had tea in Tokyo with Richard C. Koo, chief economist for the Nomura Research Institute. </seg>
<seg id="3491"> I tested out on Richard my ˇ°coefficient of flatnessˇ±: the notion that the flatter one's country is-that is, the fewer natural resources it has-the better off it will be in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3492"> The ideal country in a flat world is the one with no natural resources, because countries with no natural resources tend to dig inside themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3493"> They try to tap the energy, entrepreneurship, creativity, and intelligence of their own people-men and women-rather than drill an oil well. </seg>
<seg id="3494"> Taiwan is a barren rock in a typhoon-laden sea, with virtually no natural resources-nothing but the energy, ambition, and talent of its own people-and today it has the third-largest financial reserves in the world. </seg>
<seg id="3495"> The success of Hong Kong, Japan, South Korea, and coastal China can all be traced to a similar flatness. </seg>
<seg id="3496"> ˇ°I am a Taiwanese-American with a father from Taiwan and with a Japanese mother,ˇ± Koo told me. ˇ°I was bom in Japan and went to Japanese elementary school and then moved to the States. </seg>
<seg id="3497"> There is a saying in China that whatever you put in your head and your stomach, no one can take away from you. </seg>
<seg id="3498"> I was told relatively early by my teachers, 'We can never live like Americans and Canadians. </seg>
<seg id="3499"> We have no resources. </seg>
<seg id="3500"> We have to study hard, work hard, and export hard.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3501"> A short time later I read a column by Steven Pearlstein, The Washington Post's business columnist/reporter, under the headline ˇ°Europe's Capitalism Curtain.ˇ± From Wroclaw, Poland (July 23, 2004), Pearlstein wrote: ˇ°A curtain has descended across Europe. </seg>
<seg id="3502"> On one side are hope, optimism, freedom and prospects for a better life. On the other side, fear, pessimism, suffocating government regulations and a sense that the best times are in the past.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3503"> This new curtain, Pearlstein argued, demarks Eastern Europe, which is embracing capitalism, and Western Europe, which is wishing desperately that it would go away. </seg>
<seg id="3504"> ˇ°This time, however, it is the East that is likely to prevail,ˇ± he continued. </seg>
<seg id="3505"> ˇ°The energy and sense of possibility are almost palpable here... </seg>
<seg id="3506"> Money and companies are pouring in-not just the prestige nameplates like Bombardier, Siemens, Whirlpool, Toyota and Volvo, but also the network of suppliers that inevitably follows them. </seg>
<seg id="3507"> At first, most of the new jobs were of the semi-skilled variety. </seg>
<seg id="3508"> Now they have been followed by design and engineering work that aims to tap into the largest concentration of university students in Eastern Europe... </seg>
<seg id="3509"> The secret isn't just lower wages. </seg>
<seg id="3510"> It's also the attitude of workers who take pride and are willing to do what is necessary to succeed, even if it means outsourcing parts production or working on weekends or altering vacation schedules- things that would almost certainly trigger months of acrimony and negotiation in Western Europe. </seg>
<seg id="3511"> 'The people back home, they haven't got any idea how much they need to change if they want to preserve what they have,' said Jose Ugarte [a Basque who heads the appliance manufacturing operations of Mondragon, the giant Spanish industrial cooperative]. ' </seg>
<seg id="3512"> The danger to them is enormous. </seg>
<seg id="3513"> They don't realize how fast this is happening...' </seg>
<seg id="3514"> It's not the dream of riches that animates the people of Wroclaw so much as the determination to work hard, sacrifice what needs to be sacrificed and change what needs to be changed to close the gap with the West. </seg>
<seg id="3515"> It is that pride and determination, says Wroclaw's mayor, Rafal Dutkiewicz, that explain why they are such a threat to the 'leisure-time society' on the other side of the curtain.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3516"> When I asked Bill Gates about the supposed American education advantage-an education that stresses creativity, not rote learning-he was utterly dismissive. </seg>
<seg id="3517"> In his view, those who think that the more rote learning systems of China and Japan can't turn out innovators who can compete with Americans are sadly mistaken. </seg>
<seg id="3518"> Said Gates, ˇ°I have never met the guy who doesn't know how to multiply who created software... </seg>
<seg id="3519"> Who has the most creative video games in the world? </seg>
<seg id="3520"> Japan! </seg>
<seg id="3521"> I never met these 'rote people'... </seg>
<seg id="3522"> Some of my best software developers are Japanese. </seg>
<seg id="3523"> You need to understand things in order to invent beyond them.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3524"> One cannot stress enough: Young Chinese, Indians, and Poles are not racing us to the bottom. </seg>
<seg id="3525"> They are racing us to the top. </seg>
<seg id="3526"> They do not want to work for us; </seg>
<seg id="3527"> they don't even want to be us. </seg>
<seg id="3528"> They want to dominate us-in the sense that they want to be creating the companies of the future that people all over the world will admire and clamor to work for. </seg>
<seg id="3529"> They are in no way content with where they have come so far. </seg>
<seg id="3530"> I was talking to a Chinese-American who works for Microsoft and has accompanied Bill Gates on visits to China. He said Gates is recognized everywhere he goes in China. </seg>
<seg id="3531"> Young people there hang from the rafters and scalp tickets just to hear him speak. </seg>
<seg id="3532"> Same with Jerry Yang, the founder of Yahoo! </seg>
<seg id="3533"> In China today, Bill Gates is Britney Spears. </seg>
<seg id="3534"> In America today, Britney Spears is Britney Spears-and that is our problem. </seg>
<seg id="3535"> Microsoft has three research centers in the world: in Cambridge, England; in Redmond, Washington, its headquarters; and in Beijing. </seg>
<seg id="3536"> Bill Gates told me that within just a couple of years of its opening in 1998, Microsoft Research Asia, as the center in Beijing is known, had become the most productive research arm in the Microsoft system ˇ°in terms of the quality of the ideas that they are turning out. </seg>
<seg id="3537"> Kai-Fu Li is the Microsoft executive who was assigned by Gates to open the Microsoft research center in Beijing. </seg>
<seg id="3538"> My first question to him was, ˇ°How did you go about recruiting the staff?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3539"> Li said his team went to universities all over China and simply administered math, IQ, and programming tests to Ph.D.-level students or scientists. </seg>
<seg id="3540"> ˇ°In the first year, we gave about 2,000 tests all around,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="3541"> From the 2,000, they winnowed the group down to 400 with more tests, then 150, ˇ°and then we hired 20.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3542"> They were given two-year contracts and told that at the end of two years, depending on the quality of their work, they would either be given a longer-term contract or granted a postdoctoral degree by Microsoft Research Asia. </seg>
<seg id="3543"> Yes, you read that right. </seg>
<seg id="3544"> The Chinese government gave Microsoft the right to grant postdocs. </seg>
<seg id="3545"> Of the original twenty who were hired, twelve survived the cut. </seg>
<seg id="3546"> The next year, nearly four thousand people were tested. </seg>
<seg id="3547"> After that, said Li, ˇ°we stopped doing the test. </seg>
<seg id="3548"> By that time we became known as the number one place to work, where all the smart computer and math people wanted to work... </seg>
<seg id="3549"> We got to know all the students and professors. </seg>
<seg id="3550"> The professors would send their best people there, knowing that if the people did not work out, it would be their credibility [on the line]. </seg>
<seg id="3551"> Now we have the top professors at the top schools recommending their top students. </seg>
<seg id="3552"> A lot of students want to go to Stanford or MIT, but they want to spend two years at Microsoft first, as interns, so they can get a nice recommendation letter that says these are MIT quality.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3553"> ˇ°They view this as a once-in-a-lifetime income opportunity/' said Li of the team at Microsoft Research Asia. ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3554"> They saw their parents going through the Cultural Revolution. </seg>
<seg id="3555"> The best they could do was become a professor, do a little project on the side because a professor's pay is horrible, and maybe get one paper published. </seg>
<seg id="3556"> Now they have this place where all they do is research, with great computers and lots of resources. </seg>
<seg id="3557"> They have administrators-we hire people to do the dirty work. </seg>
<seg id="3558"> They just could not believe it. </seg>
<seg id="3559"> They voluntarily work fifteen to eighteen hours a day and come in on weekends. </seg>
<seg id="3560"> They work through holidays, because their dream is to get to Microsoft. </seg>
<seg id="3561"> ˇ° Li, who had worked for other American high-tech firms before coming to Microsoft, said that until starting Microsoft Research Asia, he had never seen a research lab with the enthusiasm of a start-up company. </seg>
<seg id="3562"> Unfortunately, federal funding for research in physical and mathematical sciences and engineering, as a share of GDP, actually declined by 37 percent between 1970 and 2004, the task force found. </seg>
<seg id="3563"> In the fiscal year 2005 budget passed by the Republican-led Congress in November 2004, the budget for the National Science Foundation, which is the federal body most responsible for promoting research and funding more and better science education, was actually cut by 1.9 percent, or $105 million. </seg>
<seg id="3564"> At a time when we need to be doubling our investments in basic research to overcome the ambition and education gaps, we are actually cutting that funding. </seg>
<seg id="3565"> In the wake of the Bush administration and the Republican Congress's decision to cut the National Science Foundation funding for 2005, Republican congressman Vern Ehlers of Missouri, a voice in the wilderness, made the following statement: ˇ°While I understand the need to make hard choices in the face of fiscal constraint, </seg>
<seg id="3566"> I do not see the wisdom in putting science funding behind other priorities. </seg>
<seg id="3567"> We have cut NSF despite the fact that this omnibus bill increases spending for the 2005 fiscal year, so clearly we could find room to grow basic research while maintaining fiscal constraint. </seg>
<seg id="3568"> But not only are we not keeping pace with inflationary growth, we are actually cutting the portion basic research receives in the overall budget. </seg>
<seg id="3569"> This decision shows dangerous disregard for our nation's future, and I am both concerned and astonished that we would make this decision at a time when other nations continue to surpass our students in math and science and consistently increase their funding of basic research. </seg>
<seg id="3570"> We cannot hope to fight jobs lost to international competition without a well-trained and educated workforce.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3571"> No, we cannot, and the effects are starting to show. </seg>
<seg id="3572"> According to the National Science Board, the percentage of scientific papers written by Americans has fallen 10 percent since 1992. </seg>
<seg id="3573"> The percentage of American papers published in the top physics journal, Physical Review, has fallen from 61 percent to 29 percent since 1983. </seg>
<seg id="3574"> And now we are starting to see a surge in patents awarded to Asian countries. </seg>
<seg id="3575"> From 1980 to 2003, Japan's share of world industrial patents rose from 12 percent to 21 percent, and Taiwan's from 0 percent to 3 percent. </seg>
<seg id="3576"> By contrast, the U.S. share of patents has fallen from 60 percent to 52 percent since 1980. </seg>
<seg id="3577"> U.S. policymakers who back overly restrictive immigration policies do so at the risk of cutting off a steady infusion of technological and scientific skill,ˇ± said the study's author, Stuart Anderson, the executive director of the foundation. </seg>
<seg id="3578"> The article quoted Andrei Munteanu, eighteen, a finalist for the 2004 Intel competition, whose parents had moved from Romania to the United States five years earlier. </seg>
<seg id="3579"> Munteanu started American school in the seventh grade, which he found a breeze compared to his Romanian school. </seg>
<seg id="3580"> ˇ°The math and science classes [covered the same subject matter] I was taking in Romania... when I was in fourth grade,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="3581"> For now, the United States still excels at teaching science and engineering at the graduate level, and also in university-based research. </seg>
<seg id="3582"> But as the Chinese get more feeder stock coming up through their improving high schools and universities, ˇ°they will get to the same level as us after a decade,ˇ± said Intel chairman Barrett. ˇ°We are not graduating the volume, we do not have a lock on the infrastructure, we do not have a lock on the new ideas, and we are either flatlining, or in real dollars cutting back, our investments in physical science.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3583"> Every four years the United States takes part in the Trends in International Mathematics and Science Study, which assesses students after fourth grade and eighth grade. </seg>
<seg id="3584"> Altogether, the most recent study involved roughly a half million students from forty-one countries and the use of thirty languages, making it the largest and most comprehensive international study of education that has ever been undertaken. </seg>
<seg id="3585"> The 2004 results (for tests taken in 2003) showed American students making only marginal improvements over the 2000 results, which showed the American labor force to be weaker in science than those of its peer countries. </seg>
<seg id="3586"> The Associated Press reported (December 4,2004) that American eighth-graders had improved their scores in science and math since 1995, when the test first was given, but their math improvement came mainly between 1995 and 1999, and not in recent years. </seg>
<seg id="3587"> The rising scores of American eighth-graders in science was an improvement over 1999, and it lifted the United States to a higher ranking relative to other countries. </seg>
<seg id="3588"> The worrying news, though, was that the scores of American fourth-graders were stagnant, neither improving nor declining in science or math since 1995. </seg>
<seg id="3589"> As a result, they slipped in the international rankings as other countries made gains. </seg>
<seg id="3590"> ˇ°Asian countries are setting the pace in advanced science and math,ˇ± Ina Mullis, codirector of the International Study Center at Boston College, which manages the study, told the AP. ˇ°As one example, 44 percent of eighth-graders in Singapore scored at the most advanced level in math, as did 38 percent in Taiwan. </seg>
<seg id="3591"> Only 7 percent in the United States did.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3592"> Results from another international education test also came out in December 2004, from the Program for International Student Assessment. </seg>
<seg id="3593"> It showed that American fifteen-year-olds are below the international average when it comes to applying math skills to real-life tasks. </seg>
<seg id="3594"> No wonder Johns Hopkins University president Bill Brody remarked to me, ˇ°Over 60 percent of our graduate students in the sciences are foreign students, and mostly from Asia. </seg>
<seg id="3595"> At one point four years ago all of our graduate students in mathematics were from the PRC [Communist China]. </seg>
<seg id="3596"> I only found out about it because we use them as [teaching assistants] and some of them don't speak English all that well.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3597"> A Johns Hopkins parent wrote Brody to complain that his son could not understand his calculus professor because of his heavy Chinese accent and poor English. </seg>
<seg id="3598"> said Tracy Koon, Intel's director of corporate affairs, This is a company that was founded here, but we have two raw materials-sand, which we have a ready supply of, and talent, which we don't.ˇ± (Silicon comes from sand.)ˇ°We looked at two things,ˇ± she continued. </seg>
<seg id="3599"> Because it takes fifteen years to create a scientist or advanced engineer, starting from when that young man or woman first gets hooked on science and math in elementary school, we should be embarking on an all-hands-on-deck, no-holds-barred, no-budget-too-large crash program for science and engineering education immediately. </seg>
<seg id="3600"> Scientists and engineers don't grow on trees. </seg>
<seg id="3601"> They have to be educated through a long process, because, ladies and gentlemen, this really is rocket science. </seg>
<seg id="3602"> EIGHT This Is Not a Test </seg>
<seg id="3603"> We have the power to shape the civilization that we want. But we need your will, your labor, your hearts, if we are to build that kind of society. </seg>
<seg id="3604"> Those who came to this land sought to build more than just a new country. </seg>
<seg id="3605"> They sought a new world. </seg>
<seg id="3606"> So I have come here today to your campus to say that you can make their vision our reality. </seg>
<seg id="3607"> So let us from this moment begin our work so that in the future men will look back and say: It was then, after a long and weary way, that man turned the exploits of his genius to the full enrichment of his life. </seg>
<seg id="3608"> ˇŞˇ°Great Societyˇ± speech, Lyndon B. Johnson, 1964 </seg>
<seg id="3609"> As a person who grew up during the Cold War, I'll always remember driving along down the highway and listening to the radio, when suddenly the music would stop and a grim-voiced announcer would come on the air and say, ˇ°This is a test of the emergency broadcast system,ˇ± and then there would be a thirty-second high-pitched siren sound. </seg>
