<seg id="3610"> Fortunately, we never had to live through a moment in the Cold War where the announcer came on and said, ˇ°This is not a test.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3611"> That, however, is exactly what I want to say here: This is not a test. </seg>
<seg id="3612"> The long-term opportunities and challenges that the flattening of the world puts before the United States are profound. </seg>
<seg id="3613"> Therefore, our ability to get by doing things the way we've been doing them-which is to say, not always tending to our secret sauce and enriching it-will not suffice anymore. </seg>
<seg id="3614"> ˇ°For a country as wealthy as we are, it is amazing how little we are doing to enhance our natural competitiveness,ˇ± said Dinakar Singh, the Indian-American hedge fund manager. </seg>
<seg id="3615"> ˇ°We are in a world that has a system that now allows convergence among many billions of people, and we had better step back and figure out what it means. </seg>
<seg id="3616"> It would be a nice coincidence if all the things that were true before are still true now-but there are quite a few things you actually need to do differently... </seg>
<seg id="3617"> You need to have a much more thoughtful national discussion.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3618"> If this moment has any parallel in American history, it is the height of the Cold War, around 1957, when the Soviet Union leaped ahead of America in the space race by putting up the Sputnik satellite. </seg>
<seg id="3619"> Yes, there are many differences between that age and our own. </seg>
<seg id="3620"> The main challenge then came from those who wanted to put up walls; </seg>
<seg id="3621"> the main challenge to America today comes from the fact that all the walls are being taken down, and other countries can now compete with us much more directly. </seg>
<seg id="3622"> The main challenge in that world was from those practicing extreme communism, namely, Russia, China, and North Korea. </seg>
<seg id="3623"> The main challenge to America today is from those practicing extreme capitalism, namely, China, India, and South Korea. </seg>
<seg id="3624"> The main objective in that era was building a strong state; </seg>
<seg id="3625"> the main objective in this era is building strong individuals. </seg>
<seg id="3626"> What this era has in common with the Cold War era, though, is that to meet the challenges of flatism requires as comprehensive, energetic, and focused a response as did meeting the challenge of communism. </seg>
<seg id="3627"> It requires a president who can summon the nation to get smarter and study harder in science, math, and engineering in order to reach the new frontiers of knowledge that the flat world is rapidly opening up and pushing out. </seg>
<seg id="3628"> And it requires a Great Society that commits our government to building the infrastructure, safety nets, and institutions that will help every American become more employable in an age when no one can be guaranteed lifetime employment. </seg>
<seg id="3629"> I call my own version of this approach compassionate flatism.Getting Americans to rally around compassionate flatism is much more difficult than getting them to rally around anticommunism. </seg>
<seg id="3630"> ˇ°National peril is a lot easier to convey than individual peril,ˇ± noted Johns Hopkins University foreign policy expert Michael Mandelbaum. </seg>
<seg id="3631"> Economics, as noted, is not like war, because economics can always be a win-win game. </seg>
<seg id="3632"> But sometimes I wish economics were more like war. </seg>
<seg id="3633"> In the Cold War, we actually got to see the Soviets parade their missiles in Red Square. </seg>
<seg id="3634"> We all got to be scared together, from one end of the country to the other, and all our politicians had to be focused and serious about marshaling the resources and educational programs to make sure Americans could keep pace with the Soviet Union. </seg>
<seg id="3635"> But today, alas, there is no missile threat coming from India. </seg>
<seg id="3636"> The ˇ°hot line,ˇ± which used to connect the Kremlin with the White House, has been replaced by the ˇ°help line,ˇ± which connects everyone in America to call centers in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="3637"> While the other end of the hotline might have had Leonid Brezhnev threatening nuclear war, the other end of the help line just has a soft voice eager to help you sort out your AOL bill or collaborate with you on a new piece of software. </seg>
<seg id="3638"> No, that voice has none of the menace of Nikita Khrushchev pounding a shoe on the table at the UN, and it has none of the sinister snarl of the bad guys in From Russia with Love. </seg>
<seg id="3639"> There is no Boris or Natasha saying ˇ°We will bury youˇ± in a thick Russian accent. </seg>
<seg id="3640"> No, that voice on the help line just has a friendly Indian lilt that masks any sense of threat or challenge. </seg>
<seg id="3641"> It simply says: ˇ°Hello, my name is Rajiv. </seg>
<seg id="3642"> Can I help you?ˇ±No, Rajiv, actually, you can't. </seg>
<seg id="3643"> When it comes to responding to the challenges of the flat world, there is no help line we can call. </seg>
<seg id="3644"> We have to dig into ourselves. </seg>
<seg id="3645"> We in America have all the tools to do that, as I argued in Chapter 6. </seg>
<seg id="3646"> But, as I argued in Chapter 7, we have not been tending to those tools as we should. </seg>
<seg id="3647"> Hence, our quiet crisis. </seg>
<seg id="3648"> The assumption that because America's economy has dominated the world for more than a century, it will and must always be that way is as dangerous an illusion today as the illusion that America would always dominate in science and technology was back in 1950. </seg>
<seg id="3649"> But this is not going to be easy. </seg>
<seg id="3650"> Getting our society up to speed for a flat world is going to be extremely painstaking. We are going to have to start doing a lot of things differently. </seg>
<seg id="3651"> It is going to take the sort of focus and national will that President John F. Kennedy called for in his famous May 25, 1961, speech to Congress on ˇ°urgent national needs.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3652"> At that time, America was recovering from the twin shocks of Sputnik and the Soviet space launch of a cosmonaut, Yuri Gagarin, less than two months before Kennedy's speech. </seg>
<seg id="3653"> Kennedy knew that while America had enormous human and institutional assets-far more than the Soviet Union-they were not being fully utilized. </seg>
<seg id="3654"> ˇ°I believe we possess all the resources and talents necessary,ˇ± said President Kennedy. ˇ°But the facts of the matter are that we have never made the national decisions or marshaled the national resources required for such leadership. </seg>
<seg id="3655"> We have never specified long-range goals on an urgent time schedule, or managed our resources and our time so as to ensure their fulfillment.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3656"> After then laying out his whole program for putting a man on the moon within ten years, President Kennedy added, ˇ°Let it be clear that I am asking the Congress and the country to accept a firm commitment to a new course of action, a course which will last for many years and carry very heavy costs... </seg>
<seg id="3657"> This decision demands a major national commitment of scientific and technical manpower, materiel and facilities, and the possibility of their diversion from other important activities where they are already thinly spread. </seg>
<seg id="3658"> It means a degree of dedication, organization and discipline which have not always characterized our research and development efforts.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3659"> In that speech, Kennedy made a vow that has amazing resonance today: ˇ°I am therefore transmitting to the Congress a new Manpower Development and Training program, to train or retrain several hundred thousand workers, particularly in those areas where we have seen chronic unemployment as a result of technological factors, in new occupational skills over a four-year periodˇŞin order to replace those skills made obsolete by automation and industrial change with the new skills which the new processes demand.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3660"> We too have to do things differently. </seg>
<seg id="3661"> We are going to have to sort out what to keep, what to discard, what to adapt, what to adopt, where to redouble our efforts, and where to intensify our focus. That is what this chapter is about. </seg>
<seg id="3662"> This is just an intuition, but the flattening of the world is going to be hugely disruptive to both traditional and developed societies. </seg>
<seg id="3663"> The weak will fall farther behind faster. </seg>
<seg id="3664"> The developed will be challenged by the underdeveloped much more profoundly. </seg>
<seg id="3665"> I worry, because so much political stability is built on economic stability, and economic stability is not going to be a feature of the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3666"> Add it all up and you can see that the disruptions are going to come faster and harder. </seg>
<seg id="3667"> Think about Microsoft trying to figure out how to deal with a global army of people writing software for free! We are entering an era of creative destruction on steroids. </seg>
<seg id="3668"> Even if your country has a comprehensive strategy for dealing with flatism, it is going to be a challenge of a whole new dimension. </seg>
<seg id="3669"> But if you don't have a strategy at all... well, you've been warned. </seg>
<seg id="3670"> This is not a test. </seg>
<seg id="3671"> Being an American, I am most focused on my own country. </seg>
<seg id="3672"> How do we go about maximizing the benefits and opportunities of the flat world, and providing protection for those who have difficulty with the transition, without resorting to protectionism or runaway capitalism? </seg>
<seg id="3673"> Some will offer traditional conservative responses; </seg>
<seg id="3674"> some will offer traditional liberal ones. </seg>
<seg id="3675"> I offer compassionate flatism, which is a policy blend built around five broad categories of action for the age of flat: leadership, muscle building, cushioning, social activism, and parenting. </seg>
<seg id="3676"> The job of the politician in America, whether at the local, state, or national level, should be, in good part, to help educate and explain to people what world they are living in and what they need to do if they want to thrive within it. </seg>
<seg id="3677"> One problem we have today, though, is that so many American politicians don't seem to have a clue about the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3678"> As venture capitalist John Doerr once remarked to me, ˇ°You talk to the leadership in China, and they are all the engineers, and they get what is going on immediately. </seg>
<seg id="3679"> The Americans don't, because they're all lawyers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3680"> Added Bill Gates, ˇ°The Chinese have risk taking down, hard work down, education, and when you meet with Chinese politicians, they are all scientists and engineers. </seg>
<seg id="3681"> You can have a numeric discussion with them-you are never discussing 'give me a one-liner to embarrass [my political rivals] with.' </seg>
<seg id="3682"> You are meeting with an intelligent bureaucracy.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3683"> I am not saying we should require all politicians to hold engineering degrees, but it would be helpful if they had a basic understanding of the forces that are flattening the world, were able to educate constituents about them and galvanize a response. </seg>
<seg id="3684"> We have way too many politicians in America today who seem to do the opposite. </seg>
<seg id="3685"> They seem to go out of their way actually to make their constituents stupid-encouraging them to believe that certain jobs are ˇ°American jobsˇ± and can be protected from foreign competition, or that because America has always dominated economically in our lifetimes it always will, or that compassion should be equated with protectionism. </seg>
<seg id="3686"> It is hard to have an American national strategy for dealing with flatism if people won't even acknowledge that there is an education gap emerging and that there is an ambition gap emerging and that we are in a quiet crisis. </seg>
<seg id="3687"> For instance, of all the policy choices that the Republican-led Congress could have made in forging the FY 2005 budget, how in the world could it have decided to cut the funding of the National Science Foundation by more than $100 million? </seg>
<seg id="3688"> We need politicians who are able and willing to both explain and inspire. </seg>
<seg id="3689"> And what they most need to explain to Americans is pretty much what Lou Gerstner explained to the workforce of IBM when he took over as chairman in 1993, when the company was losing billions of dollars. </seg>
<seg id="3690"> At the time, IBM was facing a near-death experience owing to its failure to adapt to and capitalize on the business computing market that it invented. </seg>
<seg id="3691"> IBM got arrogant. It had built its whole franchise around helping customers solve problems. </seg>
<seg id="3692"> But after a while it stopped listening to its customers. </seg>
<seg id="3693"> It thought it didn't have to. </seg>
<seg id="3694"> And when IBM stopped listening to its customers, it stopped creating value that mattered for its customers, and that had been the whole strength of its business. </seg>
<seg id="3695"> A friend of mine who worked at IBM back then told me that when he was in his first year at the company and taking an internal course, his IBM instructor boasted to him that IBM was such a great company, it could do ˇ°extraordinary things with just average people.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3696"> As the world started to flatten, though, IBM found that it could not continue thriving with an overabundance of average people working for a company that had stopped being a good listener. </seg>
<seg id="3697"> But when a company is the pioneer, the vanguard, the top dog, the crown jewel, it is hard to look in the mirror and tell itself it is in a not-so-quiet crisis and better start to make a new history or become history. </seg>
<seg id="3698"> Gerstner decided that he would be that mirror. </seg>
<seg id="3699"> He told IBM it was ugly and that a strategy built largely around designing and selling computers-rather than the services and strategies to get the most out of those computers for each customer-didn't make sense. </seg>
<seg id="3700"> Needless to say, this was a shock for IBMers. </seg>
<seg id="3701"> ˇ°Transformation of an enterprise begins with a sense of crisis or urgency,ˇ± Gerstner told students at Harvard Business School, in a December 9, 2002, talk. </seg>
<seg id="3702"> ˇ°No institution will go through fundamental change unless it believes it is in deep trouble and needs to do something different to survive.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3703"> It is impossible to ignore the parallel with America as a whole in the early twenty-first century. </seg>
<seg id="3704"> When Lou Gerstner came in, one of the first things he did was replace the notion of lifetime employment with the notion of lifetime employability. </seg>
<seg id="3705"> A friend of mine, Alex Attal, a French-born software engineer who was working for IBM at the time, described the shift this way: ˇ°Instead of IBM giving you a guarantee that you will be employed, you had to guarantee that you could stay employable. </seg>
<seg id="3706"> The company would give you the framework, but you had to build it yourself. </seg>
<seg id="3707"> It's all about adapting. </seg>
<seg id="3708"> I was head of sales for IBM France at the time. </seg>
<seg id="3709"> It was the mid-nineties. </seg>
<seg id="3710"> I told my people that in the old days [the concept of] lifetime employment was only a company's responsibility, not a personal responsibility. </seg>
<seg id="3711"> But once we move to a model of employability, that becomes a shared responsibility. </seg>
<seg id="3712"> The company will give you access to knowledge, but you have to take advantage of it... </seg>
<seg id="3713"> You have to build the skills because it will be you against a lot of other people.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3714"> When Gerstner started to change the paradigm at IBM, he kept stressing the issue of individual empowerment. </seg>
<seg id="3715"> Said Attal, ˇ°He understood that an extraordinary company could only be built on a critical mass of extraordinary people.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3716"> As at IBM, so in America. </seg>
<seg id="3717"> Average Joe has to become special, specialized, or adaptable Joe. </seg>
<seg id="3718"> The job of government and business is not to guarantee anyone a lifetime job-those days are over. </seg>
<seg id="3719"> That social contract has been ripped up with the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="3720"> What government can and must guarantee people is the chance to make themselves more employable. </seg>
<seg id="3721"> We don't want America to be to the world what IBM was becoming to the computer industry in the 1980s: the people who opened the field and then became too timid, arrogant, and ordinary to play on it. </seg>
<seg id="3722"> We want America to be the born-again IBM. </seg>
<seg id="3723"> Politicians not only need to explain to people the flat world, they need to inspire them to rise to the challenge of it. </seg>
<seg id="3724"> There is more to political leadership than a competition for who can offer the most lavish safety nets. </seg>
<seg id="3725"> Yes, we must address people's fears, but we must also nurse their imaginations. </seg>
<seg id="3726"> Politicians can make us more fearful and thereby be disablers, or they can inspire us and thereby be enablers. </seg>
<seg id="3727"> To be sure, it is not easy to get people passionate about the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3728"> It takes some imagination. </seg>
<seg id="3729"> President Kennedy understood that the competition with the Soviet Union was not a space race but a science race, which was really an education race. </seg>
<seg id="3730"> Yet the way he chose to get Americans excited about sacrificing and buckling down to do what it took to win the Cold War-which required a large-scale push in science and engineering-was by laying out the vision of putting a man on the moon, not a missile into Moscow. </seg>
<seg id="3731"> If President Bush is looking for a similar legacy project, there is one just crying out-a national science initiative that would be our generation's moon shot: a crash program for alternative energy and conservation to make America energy-independent in ten years. </seg>
<seg id="3732"> If President Bush made energy independence his moon shot, in one fell swoop he would dry up revenue for terrorism, force Iran, Russia, Venezuela, and Saudi Arabia onto the path of reform-which they will never do with $50-a-barrel oil-strengthen the dollar, and improve his own standing in Europe by doing something huge to reduce global warming. </seg>
<seg id="3733"> He would also create a real magnet to inspire young people to contribute to both the war on terrorism and America's future by again becoming scientists, engineers, and mathematicians. </seg>
<seg id="3734"> ˇ°This is not just a win-win,ˇ± said Michael Mandelbaum. </seg>
<seg id="3735"> ˇ°This is a win-win-win-win-win.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3736"> I have consistently been struck that my newspaper columns that have gotten far and away the most positive feedback over the years, especially from young people, have been those that urged the president to call the nation to this task. </seg>
<seg id="3737"> Summoning all our energies and skills to produce a twenty-first-century fuel is George W. Bush's opportunity to be both Nixon to China and JFK to the moon in one move. </seg>
<seg id="3738"> Unfortunately for America, it appears as though I will go to the moon before President Bush will go down this road. </seg>
<seg id="3739"> MusclesSince lifetime employment is a form of fat that a flat world simply cannot sustain any longer, compassionate flatism seeks to focus its energy on how government and business can enhance every worker's lifetime employability. </seg>
<seg id="3740"> Lifetime employment depends on preserving a lot of fat. </seg>
<seg id="3741"> Lifetime employability requires replacing that fat with muscle. </seg>
<seg id="3742"> The social contract that progressives should try to enforce between government and workers, and companies and workers, is one in which government and companies say, ˇ°We cannot guarantee you any lifetime employment. </seg>
<seg id="3743"> But we can guarantee you that government and companies will focus on giving you the tools to make you more lifetime employable.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3744"> The whole mind-set of a flat world is one in which the individual worker is going to become more and more responsible for managing his or her own career, risks, and economic security, and the job of government and business is to help workers build the necessary muscles to do that. </seg>
<seg id="3745"> The ˇ°musclesˇ± workers need most are portable benefits and opportunities for lifelong learning. </seg>
<seg id="3746"> Why those two? </seg>
<seg id="3747"> Because they are the most important assets in making a worker mobile and adaptable. </seg>
<seg id="3748"> As Harvard University economist Robert Lawrence notes, the greatest single asset that the American economy has always had is the flexibility and mobility of its labor force and labor laws. </seg>
<seg id="3749"> That asset will become even more of an advantage in the flat world, as job creation and destruction both get speeded up. </seg>
<seg id="3750"> Given that reality, argues Lawrence, it becomes increasingly important for society, to the extent possible, to make benefits and education- the two key ingredients of employability-as flexible as possible. </seg>
<seg id="3751"> You don't want people to feel that they have to stay with a company forever simply to keep their pension and health benefits. </seg>
<seg id="3752"> The more the workforce feels mobile -in terms of health care, pension benefits, and lifelong learning possibilities-the more it will be willing and able to jump into the new industries and new job niches spawned by the flat world and to move from dying companies to thriving companies. </seg>
<seg id="3753"> Creating legal and institutional frameworks for universal portability of pensions and health care -in addition to Social Security, Medicare, and Medicaid-will help people build up such muscles. </seg>
<seg id="3754"> Today roughly 50 percent of Americans don't have a job-based pension plan, other than Social Security. </seg>
<seg id="3755"> Those who are fortunate enough to have one cannot easily take it with them from job to job. </seg>
<seg id="3756"> What is needed is one simple universal portable pension scheme, along the lines proposed by the Progressive Policy Institute, that would get rid of the confusing welter of sixteen different tax-deferred options now offered by the government and consolidate them all into a single vehicle. </seg>
<seg id="3757"> This universal plan, which you would open with your first job, would encourage workers to establish 401 (k) tax-deferred savings programs. Each worker and his or her employer could make contributions of cash, bonuses, profit sharing, or stock, depending on what sorts of benefits the specific employer offered. </seg>
<seg id="3758"> These assets would be allowed to build up tax-free in whatever savings or investment portfolio options the worker chose. </seg>
<seg id="3759"> But if and when it came time to change jobs, the worker could take the whole portfolio with him or her and not have to either cash it out or leave it under the umbrella of the previous employer. </seg>
<seg id="3760"> Rollover provisions do exist today, but they are complicated and many workers don't take advantage of them because of that. </seg>
<seg id="3761"> The universal pension format would make rollover simple, easy, and expected, so pension lockup per se would never keep someone from moving from one job to another. </seg>
<seg id="3762"> Each employer could still offer his or her own specific 401 (k) benefit plan, as an incentive to attract employees. </seg>
<seg id="3763"> But once a worker moved to another job, the investments in that particular 401 (k) would just automatically dump into his or her universal pension account. </seg>
<seg id="3764"> With each new job, a new 401 (k) could be started, and with each move, the benefits deposited in that same universal pension account. </seg>
<seg id="3765"> In addition to this simple, portable, and universal pension program, Will Marshall, president of the Progressive Policy Institute, proposes legislation that would make it much easier and more likely for workers to obtain stock options in the companies for which they work. </seg>
<seg id="3766"> Such legislation would give tax incentives to companies to give more workers more options earlier and penalize companies that do not. </seg>
<seg id="3767"> Part of making workers more mobile is creating more ways to make more workers owners of financial assets, not just their own labor. </seg>
<seg id="3768"> ˇ°We want a public that sees itself as stakeholders, sharing in the capital-creating side of the flat world, not just competing in global labor markets/' argued Marshall. ˇ±We all have to be owners as well as wage earners. </seg>
<seg id="3769"> That is where public policy has to be focused-to make sure that people have wealth-producing assets as they enter the twenty-first century, the way homeownership accomplished that in the twentieth century. </seg>
<seg id="3770"> ˇ°Why? </seg>
<seg id="3771"> Because there is an increasing body of literature that says people who are stakeholders, people who have a slice of the pie, ˇ°are more deeply invested in our system of democratic capitalism and the policies that keep it dynamic,ˇ± said Marshall. </seg>
<seg id="3772"> It is another way, besides home-ownership, to underpin the legitimacy of democratic capitalism. </seg>
<seg id="3773"> It is also another way to energize it, because workers who are also owners are more productive on the job. </seg>
<seg id="3774"> Moreover, in a flat world where every worker is going to face suffer competition, the more opportunities everyone has to build wealth through the power of markets and compounding interest, the more he or she will be able to be self-reliant. </seg>
<seg id="3775"> We need to give workers every stabilizer we can and make it as easy for them to get stock options as it is for the plutocrats. </seg>
<seg id="3776"> Instead of just being focused on protecting those with existing capital, as conservatives so often seem to be, let's focus instead on widening the circle of capital owners. </seg>
<seg id="3777"> On the health-care side, which I won't delve into in great detail, since that would be a book unto itself, it is essential that we develop a scheme for portable health insurance that reduces some of the burden on employers for providing and managing coverage. </seg>
<seg id="3778"> Virtually every entrepreneur I talked to for this book cited soaring and uncontrolled healthcare costs in America as a reason to move factories abroad to countries where benefits were more limited, or nonexistent, or where there was national health insurance. </seg>
<seg id="3779"> Again, I favor the type of portable health-care program proposed by PPL The idea is to set up state-by-state collective purchasing pools, the way Congress and federal employees now cover themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3780"> These pools would set the rules and create the marketplace in which insurance companies could offer a menu of options. </seg>
<seg id="3781"> Each employer would then be responsible for offering this menu of options to each new employee. </seg>
<seg id="3782"> Workers could choose high, medium, or low coverage. </seg>
<seg id="3783"> Everyone, though, would have to be covered. </seg>
<seg id="3784"> Depending on the employer, he or she would cover part or all of the premiums and the employee the rest. </seg>
<seg id="3785"> But employers would not be responsible for negotiating plans with insurance companies, where they have little individual clout. </seg>
<seg id="3786"> This way employees would be totally mobile and could take their health-care coverage wherever they went. </seg>
<seg id="3787"> This type of plan has worked like a charm for members of Congress, so why not offer it to the wider public? Needy and low-income workers who could not afford to join a plan would get some government subsidy to do so. </seg>
<seg id="3788"> But the main idea is to establish a government-supervised, -regulated, and -subsidized private insurance market in which government sets the broad rules so that there is no cherry-picking of healthy workers or arbitrary denial of treatment. </seg>
<seg id="3789"> The health care itself is administered privately, and the job of employers is to facilitate their workers' entry into one of these state pools and, ideally, help them pay for some or all of the premiums, but not be responsible for the health care themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3790"> In the transition, though, employers could continue to offer health-care plans as an incentive, and workers would have the option of going with either the plan offered by their employers or the menu of options available through the state purchasing pools. </seg>
<seg id="3791"> (For details, go to ppionline.org.) </seg>
<seg id="3792"> One can quibble about the details of any of these proposals, but I think the basic inspiration behind them is exactly right: In a flattening world, where worker security can no longer be guaranteed by Fortune 500 corporations with top-down pension and health plans, we need more collaborative solutions-among government, labor, and business-that will promote self-reliant workers but not just leave them to fend for themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3793"> When it comes to building muscles of employability, government has another critical role to play. </seg>
<seg id="3794"> Somehow we got through this transition from an agriculture-based society one hundred years ago to an industrial-based one-and still ended up with a higher standard of living for the vast majority of Americans. </seg>
<seg id="3795"> How did we do it?ˇ° </seg>
<seg id="3796"> We said everyone is going to have to have a secondary education,ˇ± said Stanford University economist Paul Romer. </seg>
<seg id="3797"> ˇ°That was what the high school movement in the early part of the twentieth century was all about.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3798"> As economic historians have demonstrated in a variety of research (see particularly the work of Harvard economists Claudia Goldin and Larry Katz), both technology and trade are making the pie bigger, but they are also shifting the shares of that pie away from low-skilled labor to high-skilled labor. </seg>
<seg id="3799"> As American society produced more higher-skilled people by making high school mandatory, it empowered more people to get a bigger slice of the bigger, more complex economic pie. </seg>
<seg id="3800"> As that century progressed, we added, on top of the high school movement, the GI Bill and the modern university system. </seg>
<seg id="3801"> ˇ°These were big ideas,ˇ± noted Romer, ˇ°and what is missing at the moment is a political imagination of how do we do something just as big and just as important for the transition into the twenty-first century as we did for the nineteenth and twentieth.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3802"> The obvious challenge, Romer added, is to make tertiary education, if not compulsory, then government-subsidized for at least two years, whether it is at a state university, a community college, or a technical school. </seg>
<seg id="3803"> Tertiary education is more critical the flatter the world gets, because technology will be churning old jobs, and spawning new, more complex ones, much faster than during the transition from the agricultural economy to the industrial one. </seg>
<seg id="3804"> Educating more people at the tertiary level has two effects. </seg>
<seg id="3805"> One is that it produces more people with the skills to claim higher-value-added work in the new niches. </seg>
<seg id="3806"> And two, it shrinks the pool of people able to do lower-skilled work, from road maintenance to home repair to Starbucks. </seg>
<seg id="3807"> By shrinking the pool of lower-skilled workers, we help to stabilize their wages (provided we control immigration), because there are fewer people available to do those jobs. </seg>
<seg id="3808"> It is not an accident that plumbers can charge $75 an hour in major urban areas or that good housekeepers or cooks are hard to find. </seg>
<seg id="3809"> America's ability from the mid-nineteenth century on into the midtwentieth century to train people, limit immigration, and make low-skilled work scarce enough to win decent wages was how we created a middle class without too disparate an income gap. </seg>
<seg id="3810"> ˇ°Indeed,ˇ± noted Romer, ˇ°from the end of the nineteenth century to the middle of the twentieth, we had a narrowing of the income gap. </seg>
<seg id="3811"> Now we have seen an increase of that gap over the last twenty or thirty years. </seg>
<seg id="3812"> That is telling us that you have to run faster in order to stay in the same place.ˇ± With each advance in technology and increase in the complexity of services, you need an even higher level of skills to do the new jobs. </seg>
<seg id="3813"> Moving from being a farmhand to a phone operator who spoke proper English and could be polite was one thing. </seg>
<seg id="3814"> But moving from being a phone operator after the job got outsourced to India, to being able to install or repair phone-mail systems-or write their softwareˇŞrequires a whole new leap upward. </seg>
<seg id="3815"> While expanding research universities on the high end of the spectrum is important, so is expanding the availability of technical schools and community colleges. </seg>
<seg id="3816"> Everyone should have a chance to be educated beyond high school. </seg>
<seg id="3817"> Otherwise upper-income kids will get those skills and their slice, and the lower-income kids will never get a chance. </seg>
<seg id="3818"> We have to increase the government subsidies that make it possible for more and more kids to attend community colleges and more and more low-skilled workers to get retrained. </seg>
<seg id="3819"> JFK wanted to put a man on the moon. </seg>
<seg id="3820"> My vision is to put every American man or woman on a campus. </seg>
<seg id="3821"> Employers have a critical contribution to make to lifetime learning and fostering employability, as opposed to guaranteed employment. </seg>
<seg id="3822"> Take, for instance, CapitalOne, the global credit card company, which began outsourcing elements of its backroom operations to Wipro and Infosys in India over the past few years. </seg>
<seg id="3823"> Competing in the global financial services market, the company felt it had to take advantage of all the cost-saving opportunities that its competitors were. </seg>
<seg id="3824"> CapitalOne began, though, by trying to educate its workers through workshops about the company's competitive predicament. </seg>
<seg id="3825"> It made clear that there is no safe haven where lifetime employment is possible anymore -inside Capital-One or outside. </seg>
<seg id="3826"> Then it developed a whole program for cross-training of computer programmers, those most affected by outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="3827"> The company would take a programmer who specialized in mainframes and teach him or her to be a distributed systems programmer as well. </seg>
<seg id="3828"> CapitalOne did similar cross-training on its business side, in everything from auto loans to risk management. </seg>
<seg id="3829"> As a result, the workers who were eventually let go in an outsourcing move were in a much better position to get new jobs, because they were cross-trained and therefore more employable. </seg>
<seg id="3830"> And those who were cross-trained but retained were more versatile and therefore more valuable to CapitalOne, because they could do multiple tasks. </seg>
<seg id="3831"> What CapitalOne was doing, out of both its own self-interest and a feeling of obligation to workers it was letting go, was trying to make more and more of its workers into versatilists. </seg>
<seg id="3832"> In addition to their own self-interest in making more of their own employees into human Swiss Army knives, companies should be encouraged, with government subsidies or tax incentives, to offer as wide an array as possible of in-house learning opportunities. </seg>
<seg id="3833"> The menu of Internet-based worker-training programs today is enormous-from online degree programs to in-house guided training for different specializations. </seg>
<seg id="3834"> Not only is the menu enormous, but the cost to the company for offering these educational options is very low. </seg>
<seg id="3835"> The more lifetime learning opportunities that companies provide, the more they are both widening the skill base of their own workforce and fulfilling a moral obligation to workers whose jobs are outsourced to see to it that they leave more employable than they came. </seg>
<seg id="3836"> If there is a new social contract implicit between employers and employees today, it should be this: You give me your labor, and I will guarantee that as long as you work here, I will give you every opportunity-through either career advancement or training- to become more employable, more versatile. </seg>
<seg id="3837"> While we need to redouble our efforts to build the muscles of each individual American, we have to continue to import muscles from abroad as well. </seg>
<seg id="3838"> Most of the Indian, Chinese, Russian, Japanese, Korean, Iranian, Arab, and Israeli engineers, physicists, and scientists who come to work or study in the United States make great citizens. </seg>
<seg id="3839"> They are family-oriented, educated, and hardworking, and most would jump at the chance to become an American. </seg>
<seg id="3840"> They are exactly the type of people this country needs, and we cannot let the FBI, CIA, and Homeland Security, in their zeal to keep out the next Mohammed Atta, also keep out the next Sergey Brin, one of the cofounders of Google, who was born in Russia. </seg>
<seg id="3841"> As a computer architect friend of mine says, ˇ°If a foreign-born person is one day going to take my job, I'd prefer they be American citizens helping pay for my retirement benefits.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3842"> I would favor an immigration policy that gives a five-year work visa to any foreign student who completes a Ph.D. at an accredited American university in any subject. </seg>
<seg id="3843"> I don't care if it is Greek mythology or mathematics. </seg>
<seg id="3844"> If we can cream off the first-round intellectual draft choices from around the world, it will always end up a net plus for America. </seg>
<seg id="3845"> If the flat world is about connecting all the knowledge pools together, we want our knowledge pool to be the biggest. </seg>
<seg id="3846"> Said Bill Brody, the president of Johns Hopkins, ˇ°We are in a global talent search, so anything we can do in America to get those top draft choices we should do, because one of them is going to be Babe Ruth, and why should we let him or her go somewhere else?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3847"> Good Fat Cushions Worth Keeping </seg>
<seg id="3848"> While many of the old corporate and government safety nets will vanish under global competition in the flat world, some fat still needs to be maintained, and even added. </seg>
<seg id="3849"> As everyone who worries about his or her health knows, there is ˇ°good fatˇ± and ˇ°bad fatˇ±-but everybody needs some fat. </seg>
<seg id="3850"> That is also true of every country in the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3851"> Social Security is good fat. </seg>
<seg id="3852"> A welfare system that discourages people from working is bad fat. </seg>
<seg id="3853"> The sort of good fat that actually needs to be added for a flat world is wage insurance. </seg>
<seg id="3854"> According to a study by Lori Kletzer, an economist at the University of California, Santa Cruz, in the 1980s and '90s, two-thirds of workers who lost jobs in manufacturing industries hit by overseas competition earned less on their next job. </seg>
<seg id="3855"> A quarter of workers who lost their jobs and were reemployed saw their income fall 30 percent or more. </seg>
<seg id="3856"> Losing a job for any reason is a trauma-for the worker and his or her family-but particularly for older workers who are less able to adapt to new production techniques or lack the education to move up into more skilled service jobs. </seg>
<seg id="3857"> This idea of wage insurance was first proposed in 1986 by Harvard's Robert Lawrence and Robert E. Litan of the Brookings Institution, in a book called Saving Free Trade. </seg>
<seg id="3858"> The idea languished for a while until it started to catch fire again with an updated analysis by Kletzer and Litan in 2001. </seg>
<seg id="3859"> It got further political clout from the bipartisan U.S. Trade Deficit Commission in 2001. </seg>
<seg id="3860"> This commission couldn't agree on anything- including the causes of or what to do about the trade deficit- other than the wisdom of wage insurance. </seg>
<seg id="3861"> ˇ°Trade creates winners and losers, and what we were thinking about were mechanisms by which the winners could compensate the losers, and particularly losers who were enjoying high wages in a particular job and suddenly found their new employment at much lower wages,ˇ± said Lawrence. </seg>
<seg id="3862"> The way to think about this, he explained, is that every worker has ˇ°general skills and specific skillsˇ± for which they are paid, and when you switch jobs you quickly discover which is which. </seg>
<seg id="3863"> So you might have a college and CPA degree, or you might have a high school degree and the ability to operate a lathe. Both skills were reflected in your wages. </seg>
<seg id="3864"> But suppose one day your lathe job gets moved to China or your basic accounting work is outsourced to India and you have to go out and find a new job. </seg>
<seg id="3865"> Your new employer will not likely compensate you much for your specific skills, because your knowledge as a machine tool operator or a general accountant is probably of less use to him or her. </seg>
<seg id="3866"> You will be paid largely for your general skills, your high school education or college degree. </seg>
<seg id="3867"> Wage insurance would compensate you for your old specific skills, for a set period of time, while you take a new job and learn new specific skills. </seg>
<seg id="3868"> The standard state-run unemployment insurance program eases some of this pain for workers, but it does not address their bigger concerns of declining wages in a new job and the inability to pay for health insurance while they are unemployed and searching. </seg>
<seg id="3869"> To qualify for wage insurance, workers seeking compensation for job loss would have to meet three criteria. </seg>
<seg id="3870"> First, they would have to have lost their job through some form of displacement-offshoring, outsourcing, downsizing, or factory closure. </seg>
<seg id="3871"> Second, they would have to have held the job for at least two years. </seg>
<seg id="3872"> And third, the wage insurance would not be paid until the workers found new jobs, which would provide a strong incentive to look for work quickly and increase the chances that they would get on-the-job retraining. </seg>
<seg id="3873"> On-the-job training is always the best way to learn new skills-instead of having to sign up for some general government training program, with no promise of a job at the other end, and go through that while remaining unemployed. </seg>
<seg id="3874"> Workers who met those three conditions would then receive payments for two years, covering half the drop in their income from their previous job (capped at $10,000 a year). </seg>
<seg id="3875"> Kletzer and Litan also proposed that the government pay half the health insurance premiums for all ˇ°displacedˇ± workers for up to six months. </seg>
<seg id="3876"> Wage insurance seems to me a much better idea than relying only on the traditional unemployment insurance offered by states, which usually covers only about 50 percent of most workers' previous wages, is limited to six months, and does not help workers who suffer a loss of earnings after they take a new job. </seg>
<seg id="3877"> Moreover, as Kletzer and Litan noted, although all laid-off workers now have the right to purchase unsubsidized health insurance from their former employer if health coverage was offered when they were employed, many jobless workers do not have the money to take advantage of this guarantee. </seg>
<seg id="3878"> Also, while unemployed workers can earn an additional fifty-two weeks of unemployment insurance if they enroll in an approved retraining program, workers have no guarantee that when they finish such a program they will have a job. </seg>
<seg id="3879"> For all these reasons, the Kletzer-Litan proposal makes a lot of sense to me as the right benefit for cushioning workers in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3880"> Moreover, such a program would be eminently affordable. </seg>
<seg id="3881"> Litan estimated that at an unemployment rate of 5 percent, the wage insurance and health-care subsidy today would cost around $8 billion a year, which is peanuts compared to the positive impact it could have on workers. </seg>
<seg id="3882"> This program would not replace classic state-run unemployment insurance for workers who opt for that, but if it worked as projected, it could actually reduce the cost of such programs by moving people back to work quicker. </seg>
<seg id="3883"> Some might ask, Why be compassionate at all? </seg>
<seg id="3884"> Why keep any fat, friction, or barriers? </seg>
<seg id="3885"> Let me put it as bluntly as I can: If you are not a compassionate flatistˇŞif you are just a let 'er rip free-market flatistˇŞyou are not only cruel, you are a fool. </seg>
<seg id="3886"> You are courting a political backlash by those who can and will get churned up by this flattening process, and that backlash could become ferocious if we hit any kind of prolonged recession. </seg>
<seg id="3887"> The transition to a flat world is going to stress many people. </seg>
<seg id="3888"> As Joshua S. Levine, E*Trade 's chief technology officer, put it to me, 'You know how sometimes you go through a harrowing experience and you need a respite, but the respite never seems to come. </seg>
<seg id="3889"> Look at the airline workers. </seg>
<seg id="3890"> They go through this [terrible] event like 9/11, and management and the airline unions all negotiate for four months and management says, 'If the unions don't cut $2 billion in salary and benefits they will have to shut the airline down.' </seg>
<seg id="3891"> And after these wrenching negotiations the unions agree. </seg>
<seg id="3892"> I just have to laugh, because you know that in a few months management is going to come right back... </seg>
<seg id="3893"> There is no end. </seg>
<seg id="3894"> No one has to ask me to cut my budget each year. </seg>
<seg id="3895"> We all just know that each year we will be expected to do more with less. </seg>
<seg id="3896"> If you are a revenue producer, you are expected to come up with more revenue every year, and if you are an expense saver, you are expected to come up with more savings every year. </seg>
<seg id="3897"> You never get a break from it.ˇ° </seg>
<seg id="3898"> If societies are unable to manage the strains that are produced by this flattening, there will be a backlash, and political forces will attempt to reinsert some of the frictions and protectionist barriers that the flattening forces have eliminated, but they will do it in a crude way that will, in the name of protecting the weak, end up lowering everyone's standard of living. </seg>
<seg id="3899"> Former Mexican president Ernesto Zedillo is very sensitive to this problem, having had to manage Mexico's transition into NAFTA, with all of the strains that put on Mexican society. </seg>
<seg id="3900"> Speaking of the flattening process, he said to me, ˇ°It would be very hard to stop, but it can be stopped for a time. Maybe you can't stop it totally, but you can slow it down. </seg>
<seg id="3901"> And it makes a difference whether you get there in twenty-five years or fifty years. </seg>
<seg id="3902"> In between, two or three generations-who could have benefited a lot from more trade and globalization-will end up with crumbs.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3903"> Always remember, said Zedillo, that behind all this technology is a political infrastructure that enables it to play out. </seg>
<seg id="3904"> ˇ°There have been a series of concrete political decisions, taken over the last fifty years, that put the world where it is right now,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="3905"> ˇ°Therefore, there are political decisions that could screw up the whole process too.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3906"> As the saying goes: If you want to live like a Republican, vote like a Democrat-take good care of the losers and left-behinds. </seg>
<seg id="3907"> The only way to be a flatist is to be a compassionate flatist. </seg>
<seg id="3908"> Social Activism </seg>
<seg id="3909"> One new area that is going to need sorting out is the relationship between global corporations and their own moral consciences. </seg>
<seg id="3910"> Some may laugh at the notion that a global corporation even has a moral conscience, or should ever be expected to develop one. </seg>
<seg id="3911"> But some do and others are going to have to develop one, for one simple reason: In the flat world, with lengthy global supply chains, the balance of power between global companies and the individual communities in which they operate is tilting more and more in favor of the companies, many of them American-based. </seg>
<seg id="3912"> As such, these companies are going to command more power, not only to create value but also to transmit values, than any transnational institutions on the planet. </seg>
<seg id="3913"> Social and environmental activists and progressive companies can now collaborate in ways that can make both the companies more profitable and the flat earth more livable. </seg>
<seg id="3914"> Compassionate flatism very much seeks to promote this type of collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="3915"> Let me illustrate this notion with a couple of examples. </seg>
<seg id="3916"> If you think about the forces that are gobbling up biodiversity around the planet, none are more powerful than farmers. </seg>
<seg id="3917"> It is not that they are intending to be harmful, it is just in the nature of what they do. </seg>
<seg id="3918"> So how and where people farm and fish really matter to whether we preserve natural habitats and species. </seg>
<seg id="3919"> Conservation International, one of the biggest environmental NGOs in the world, has as its main mission preserving biodiversity. </seg>
<seg id="3920"> It is also a big believer in trying, when possible, to collaborate with big business, because when you bring a major global player around, it can have a huge impact on the environment. </seg>
<seg id="3921"> In 2002, McDonald's and Conservation International forged a partnership to use the McDonald's global supply chain-a behemoth that sucks beef, fish, chicken, pork, bread, lettuce, pickles, tomatoes, and potatoes from all four corners of the flat world-to produce not just value but also different values about the environment. </seg>
<seg id="3922"> ˇ°We and McDonald's looked at a set of environmental issues and said, 'Here are the things the food suppliers could do to reduce the environmental impact at little or no cost,'ˇ± explained Glenn Prickett, senior vice president of Conservation International. </seg>
<seg id="3923"> McDonald's then met with its key suppliers and worked out, with them and with CI, a set of guidelines for what McDonald's calls ˇ°socially responsible food supply.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3924"> ˇ°For conservationists the challenge is how do you get your arms around hundreds of millions of decisions and decision makers involved in agriculture and fisheries, who are not coordinated in any way except by the market,ˇ± said Prickett. </seg>
<seg id="3925"> ˇ°So what we look for are partners who can put their purchasing power behind a set of environmentally friendly practices in a way that is good for them, works for the producers, and is good for biodiversity. </seg>
<seg id="3926"> In that way, you can start to capture so many more decision makers... </seg>
<seg id="3927"> There is no global government authority to protect biodiversity. </seg>
<seg id="3928"> You have to collaborate with the players who can make a difference, and one of them is McDonald's.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3929"> Conservation International is already seeing improvements in conservation of water, energy, and waste, as well as steps to encourage better management of fisheries, among McDonald's suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="3930"> But it is still early, and one will have to assess over a period of years, with comprehensive data collection, whether this is really having a positive impact on the environment. </seg>
<seg id="3931"> This form of collaboration cannot and should never be a substitute for government rules and oversight. </seg>
<seg id="3932"> But if it works, it can be a vehicle for actually getting government rules implemented. </seg>
<seg id="3933"> Environmentalists who prefer government regulation to these more collaborative efforts often ignore the fact that strong rules imposed against the will of farmers end up being weakly enforced-or not enforced at all. </seg>
<seg id="3934"> What is in this for McDonald's? </seg>
<seg id="3935"> It is a huge opportunity to improve its global brand by acting as a good global citizen. </seg>
<seg id="3936"> Yes, this is, at root, a business opportunity for McDonald's. </seg>
<seg id="3937"> Sometimes the best way to change the world is by getting the big players to do the right things for the wrong reasons, because waiting for them to do the right things for the right reasons can mean waiting forever. </seg>
<seg id="3938"> Conservation International has struck similar supply-chain collaborations with Starbucks, setting rules for its supply chain of coffee farmers, and Office Depot, with its supply chain of paper-product providers. </seg>
<seg id="3939"> What these collaborations do is start to ˇ°break down the walls between different interest groups,ˇ± said Prickett. </seg>
<seg id="3940"> Normally you would have the environmentalists on one side and the farmers on the other and each side trying to get the government to write the regulations in the way that would serve it. </seg>
<seg id="3941"> Government would end up writing the rules largely to benefit business. </seg>
<seg id="3942"> ˇ°Now, instead, we have a private entity saying, 'We want to use our global supply chain to do some good,' but we understand that to be effective it has to be a collaboration with the farmers and the environmentalists if it is going to have any impact,ˇ± Prickett said. </seg>
<seg id="3943"> In this same vein, as a compassionate flatist, I would like to see a label on every electronics good state whether the supply chain that produced it is in compliance with the standards set down by the new HP-Dell-IBM alliance. </seg>
<seg id="3944"> In October 2004, these three giants joined forces in a collaborative effort with key members of their computer and printer supply chains to promote a unified code of socially responsible manufacturing practices across the world. </seg>
<seg id="3945"> The new Electronics Industry Code of Conduct includes bans on bribes, child labor, embezzlement and extortion, and violations of intellectual property, rules governing usage of wastewater, hazardous materials, pollutants, and regulations on the reporting of occupational injuries. </seg>
<seg id="3946"> Several major electronics manufacturers who serve the IBM, Dell, and HP supply chains collaborated on writing the code, including Celestica, Flextronics, Jabil, Sanmina-SCI, and Solectron. </seg>
<seg id="3947"> All HP suppliers, for instance, will be required to follow the code, though there is flexibility in the timing of how they reach compliance. </seg>
<seg id="3948"> ˇ°We are completely prepared and have terminated relationships with suppliers we find to be repeatedly nonresponsive,ˇ± said HP spokeswoman Monica Sarkar. </seg>
<seg id="3949"> As of October 2004, HP had assessed more than 150 of its 350 suppliers, including factories in China, Mexico, Southeast Asia, and Eastern Europe. </seg>
<seg id="3950"> It has set up a steering committee with IBM and Dell in order to figure out exactly how they collectively can review compliance and punish consistent violators. </seg>
<seg id="3951"> Compliance is everything, and so, again, it remains to be seen just how vigilant the corporations will be with their suppliers. </seg>
<seg id="3952"> Nevertheless, this use of supply chains to create values-not just value-could be a wave of the future. </seg>
<seg id="3953"> ˇ°As we have begun to look to other [offshore] suppliers to do most of our manufacturing, it has become clear to us that we have to assume some responsibility for how they do that work,ˇ± explained Debra Dunn, HP's senior vice president of corporate affairs and global citizenship. </seg>
<seg id="3954"> First and foremost, that is what many of HP's customers want. </seg>
<seg id="3955"> ˇ°Customers care,ˇ± said Dunn, ˇ°and European customers lead the way in caring. </seg>
<seg id="3956"> And human rights groups and NGOs, who are gaining increasing global influence as trust in corporations declines, are basically saying, 'You guys have the power here. </seg>
<seg id="3957"> You are global companies, you can set expectations that will influence environmental practices and human rights practices in emerging markets.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3958"> Those voices are right, and what is more, they can use the Internet to great effect, if they want, to embarrass global corporations into compliance. </seg>
<seg id="3959"> ˇ°When you have the procurement dollars that HP and McDonald's have,ˇ± said Dunn, ˇ°people really want to do business with you, so you have leverage and are in a position to set standards and [therefore] you have a responsibility to set standards.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3960"> The role of global corporations in setting standards in emerging markets is doubly important, because oftentimes local governments actually want to improve their environmental standards. </seg>
<seg id="3961"> They know it is important in the long run, but the pressure to create jobs and live within budget constraints is overwhelming and therefore the pressure to look the other way is overwhelming. </seg>
<seg id="3962"> Countries like China, noted Dunn, often actually want an outside force, like a global business coalition, to exert pressure to drive new values and standards at home that they are too weak to impose on themselves and their own bureaucrats. </seg>
<seg id="3963"> In The Lexus and the Olive Tree I called this form of value creation ˇ°globalution,ˇ± or revolution from beyond. </seg>
<seg id="3964"> Said Dunn: ˇ°We used to say that as long as we complied with the local law, that was all we could be expected to do. </seg>
<seg id="3965"> But now the imbalance of power is so huge it is not practical to say that Wal-Mart or HP can do whatever they want as long as a state government or country does not stop them. </seg>
<seg id="3966"> The leverage HP would leave on the table would be immoral given its superior power... We have the power to transmit global governance to our universe of suppliers and employees and consumers, which is a pretty broad universe.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3967"> Dunn noted that in a country like China there is an intense competition by local companies to become part of the HP or Dell or Wal-Mart supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="3968"> Even though it is high pressure, it means a steady volume of considerable business-the kind that can make or break a company. </seg>
<seg id="3969"> As a result, HP has huge leverage over its Chinese suppliers, and they are actually very open to having their factory standards lifted, because they know that if they get up to the standards of HP they can leverage that to get business from Dell or Sony. </seg>
<seg id="3970"> Advocates of compassionate flatism need to educate consumers to the fact that their buying decisions and buying power are political. </seg>
<seg id="3971"> Every time you as a consumer make a decision, you are supporting a whole set of values. </seg>
<seg id="3972"> You are voting about the barriers and friction you want to preserve or eliminate. </seg>
<seg id="3973"> Progressives need to make this information more easily available to consumers, so more of them can vote the right way and support the right kind of global corporate behavior. </seg>
<seg id="3974"> No discussion of compassionate flatism would be complete without also discussing the need for improved parenting. </seg>
<seg id="3975"> Helping individuals adapt to a flat world is not only the job of governments and companies. It is also the job of parents. </seg>
<seg id="3976"> They too need to know in what world their kids are growing up and what it will take for them to thrive. </seg>
<seg id="3977"> Put simply, we need a new generation of parents ready to administer tough love: There comes a time when you've got to put away the Game Boys, turn off the television set, put away the iPod, and get your kids down to work. </seg>
<seg id="3978"> The sense of entitlement, the sense that because we once dominated global commerce and geopolitics-and Olympic basketball-we always will, the sense that delayed gratification is a punishment worse than a spanking, the sense that our kids have to be swaddled in cotton wool so that nothing bad or disappointing or stressful ever happens to them at school is, quite simply, a growing cancer on American society. </seg>
<seg id="3979"> And if we don't start to reverse it, our kids are going to be in for a huge and socially disruptive shock from the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="3980"> While a different approach by politicians is necessary, it is not sufficient. </seg>
<seg id="3981"> David Baltimore, the Nobel Prize-winning president of Caltech, knows what it takes to get your child ready to compete against the cream of the global crop. </seg>
<seg id="3982"> He told me that he is struck by the fact that almost all the students who make it to Caltech, one of the best scientific universities in the world, come from public schools, not from private schools that sometimes nurture a sense that just because you are there, you are special and entitled. </seg>
<seg id="3983"> ˇ°I look at the kids who come to Caltech, and they grew up in families that encouraged them to work hard and to put off a little bit of gratification for the future and to understand that they need to hone their skills to play an important role in the world,ˇ± Baltimore said. ˇ°I give parents enormous credit for this, because these kids are all coming from public schools that people are calling failures. </seg>
<seg id="3984"> Their parents have nurtured them to make sure that they realize their potential. </seg>
<seg id="3985"> I think we need a revolution in this country when it comes to parenting around education.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3986"> Clearly, foreign-born parents seem to be doing this better. </seg>
<seg id="3987"> ˇ°About one-third of our students have an Asian background or are recent immigrants,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="3988"> A significant majority of the students coming to Caltech in the engineering disciplines are foreign-born, and a large fraction of its current faculty is foreign-born. </seg>
<seg id="3989"> ˇ°In biology, at the postdoc level, the dominance of Chinese students is overwhelming,ˇ± said Baltimore. </seg>
<seg id="3990"> No wonder that at the big scientific conferences today, a majority of the research papers dealing with cutting-edge bioscience have at least one Chinese name on them. </seg>
<seg id="3991"> My friends Judy Estrin and Bill Carrico have started several networking companies in Silicon Valley. </seg>
<seg id="3992"> In July 2004, comedian Bill Cosby used an appearance at Jesse Jackson's Rainbow/PUSH Coalition &amp; Citizenship Education Fund's annual conference to upbraid African-Americans for not teaching their children proper grammar and for black kids not striving to learn more themselves. </seg>
<seg id="3993"> Cosby had already declared, ˇ°Everybody knows it's important to speak English except these knuckleheads. </seg>
<seg id="3994"> You can't be a doctor with that kind of crap coming out of your mouth.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3995"> Referring to African-Americans who squandered their chances for a better life, Cosby told the Rainbow Coalition, ˇ°You've got to stop beating up your women because you can't find a job, because you didn't want to get an education and now you're [earning] minimum wage. </seg>
<seg id="3996"> You should have thought more of yourself when you were in high school, when you had an opportunity.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="3997"> When Cosby's remarks attracted a lot of criticism, Reverend Jackson defended him, arguing, ˇ°Bill is saying, let's fight the right fight. </seg>
<seg id="3998"> Let's level the playing field. </seg>
<seg id="3999"> Drunk people can't do that. </seg>
<seg id="4000"> Illiterate people can't do that.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4001"> That is right. Americans are the ones who increasingly need to level the playing field-not by pulling others down, not by feeling sorry for ourselves, but by lifting ourselves up. </seg>
<seg id="4002"> But when it comes to how to do that, Cosby was saying something that is important for black and white Americans, rich and poor. </seg>
<seg id="4003"> Education, whether it comes from parents or schools, has to be about more than just cognitive skills. It also has to include character building. </seg>
<seg id="4004"> The fact is, parents and schools and cultures can and do shape people. </seg>
<seg id="4005"> The most important influence in my life, outside of my family, was my high school journalism teacher, Hattie M. Steinberg. </seg>
<seg id="4006"> She pounded the fundamentals of journalism into her students-not simply how to write a lead or accurately transcribe a quote but, more important, how to comport yourself in a professional way. </seg>
<seg id="4007"> She was nearing sixty at the time I had her as my teacher and high school newspaper adviser in the late 1960s. </seg>
<seg id="4008"> She was the polar opposite of ˇ°cool,ˇ± but we hung around her classroom like it was the malt shop and she was Wolfman Jack. </seg>
<seg id="4009"> None of us could have articulated it then, but it was because we enjoyed being harangued by her, disciplined by her, and taught by her. </seg>
<seg id="4010"> She was a woman of clarity and principles in an age of uncertainty. </seg>
<seg id="4011"> I sit up straight just thinking about her! </seg>
<seg id="4012"> Our children will increasingly be competing head-to-head with Chinese, Indian, and Asian kids, whose parents have a lot more of Hattie's character-building approach than their own American parents. </seg>
<seg id="4013"> I am not suggesting that we militarize education, but I am suggesting that we do more to push our young people to go beyond their comfort zones, to do things right, and to be ready to suffer some short-run pain for longer gain. </seg>
<seg id="4014"> I repeat: This is not a test. </seg>
<seg id="4015"> DEVELOPING COUNTRIES AND THE FLAT WORLD </seg>
<seg id="4016"> NINE The Virgin of Guadalupe </seg>
<seg id="4017"> It's not that we are becoming more Anglo-Saxon. </seg>
<seg id="4018"> It's that we are having an encounter with reality.ˇŞFrank Schirrmacher, publisher of the German newspaper Frankfurter Allgemeine Zeitung, commenting to The New York Times about the need for German workers to retool and work longer hours </seg>
<seg id="4019"> Seek knowledge even unto China.ˇŞsaying of the Prophet Muhammad </seg>
<seg id="4020"> The more I worked on this book, the more I found myself asking people I met around the world where they were when they first discovered that the world was flat. </seg>
<seg id="4021"> I was in Mexico City in the spring of 2004, and I put the question on the table during lunch with a few Mexican journalist colleagues. </seg>
<seg id="4022"> One of them said he realized that he was living in a new world when he started seeing reports appearing in the Mexican media and on the Internet that some statuettes of Mexico's patron saint, the Virgin of Guadalupe, were being imported into Mexico from China, probably via ports in California. </seg>
<seg id="4023"> When you are Mexico and your claim to fame is that you are a low-wage manufacturing country, and some of your people are importing statuettes of your own patron saint from China, because China can make them and ship them all the way across the Pacific more cheaply than you can produce them, you are living in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4024"> You've also got a problem. </seg>
<seg id="4025"> Over at the Central Bank of Mexico, I asked its governor, Guillermo Ortiz, whether he was aware of this issue. </seg>
<seg id="4026"> He rolled his eyes and told me that for some time now he could feel the competitive playing field being leveled-and that Mexico was losing some of its natural geographic advantages with the U.S. market-by just staring at the numbers on his computer screen. </seg>
<seg id="4027"> ˇ°We started looking at the numbers in 2001 -it was the first year in two decades that [Mexico's] exports to the U.S. declined,ˇ± said Ortiz. </seg>
<seg id="4028"> ˇ°That was a real shock. </seg>
<seg id="4029"> We started reducing our gains in market share and then started losing them. </seg>
<seg id="4030"> We said that there is a real change here... </seg>
<seg id="4031"> And it was about China.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4032"> China is such a powerhouse of low-cost manufacturing that even though the NAFTA accord has given Mexico a leg up with the United States, and even though Mexico is right next door to us, China in 2003 replaced Mexico as the number two exporter to the United States. </seg>
<seg id="4033"> (Canada remains number one.) </seg>
<seg id="4034"> Though Mexico still has a strong position in big-ticket exports that are costly to ship, such as cars, auto parts, and refrigerators, China is coming on strong and has already displaced Mexico in areas such as computer parts, electrical components, toys, textiles, sporting goods, and tennis shoes. </seg>
<seg id="4035"> But what's even worse for Mexico is that China is displacing some Mexican companies in Mexico, where Chinese-made clothing and toys are now showing up on store shelves everywhere. </seg>
<seg id="4036"> No wonder a Mexican journalist told me about the day he interviewed a Chinese central bank official, who told him something about China's relationship with America that really rattled him: ˇ°First we were afraid of the wolf, then we wanted to dance with the wolf, and now we want to be the wolf.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4037"> A few days after returning from Mexico, I had breakfast in Washington with a friend from Egypt, Lamees El-Hadidy, a longtime business reporter in Cairo. </seg>
<seg id="4038"> Naturally I asked her where she was when she discovered the world was flat. She answered that it was a just few weeks earlier, during the Muslim holy month of Ramadan. </seg>
<seg id="4039"> She had done a story for CNBC Arabiya Television about the colorful lanterns called fawanis, each with a burning candle inside, that Egyptian schoolchildren traditionally carried around during Ramadan, a tradition dating back centuries to the Fatimid period in Egypt. </seg>
<seg id="4040"> Kids swing the lanterns and sing songs, and people give them candy or gifts, as in America on Halloween. </seg>
<seg id="4041"> For centuries, small, low-wage workshops in Cairo's older neighborhoods have manufactured these lanterns-until the last few years. </seg>
<seg id="4042"> That was when plastic Chinese-made Ramadan lanterns, each with a battery-powered light instead of a candle, began flooding the market, crippling the traditional Egyptian workshops. </seg>
<seg id="4043"> Said Lamees, ˇ°They are invading our tradition -in an innovative way-and we are doing nothing about it... </seg>
<seg id="4044"> These lanterns come out of our tradition, our soul, but [the Chinese versions] are more creative and advanced than the Egyptian ones.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4045"> Lamees said that when she asked Egyptians, ˇ°Do you know where these are made?,ˇ± they would all answer no. </seg>
<seg id="4046"> Then they would turn the lamps over and see that they came from China. </seg>
<seg id="4047"> Many mothers, like Lamees, though, appreciated the fact that the Chinese versions are safer than the traditional Egyptian ones, which are made with sharp metal edges and glass, and usually still use candles. </seg>
<seg id="4048"> The Chinese versions are made of plastic and feature flashing lights and have an embedded microchip that plays traditional Egyptian Ramadan tunes and even the theme song to the popular Ramadan TV cartoon series Bakkar. </seg>
<seg id="4049"> As Business Monthly, published by the American Chamber of Commerce in Egypt, reported in its December 2001 issue, Chinese importers ˇ°are pitted not only against each other, but also against the several-hundred-year-old Egyptian industry. </seg>
<seg id="4050"> But the Chinese models are destined to prevail, according to [a] famous importer, Taha Zayat. </seg>
<seg id="4051"> Imports have definitely cut down on sales of traditional fawanis,' he said. 'Of all fawanis on the market, I don't think that more than 5 percent are now made in Egypt.' </seg>
<seg id="4052"> People with ties to the Egyptian [fawanis] industry believe China has a clear advantage over Egypt. </seg>
<seg id="4053"> With its superior technology, they said, China can make mass quantities, which helps to keep prices relatively low. </seg>
<seg id="4054"> Egypt's traditional [fawanis] industry, by contrast, is characterized by a series of workshops specialized in different stages of the production process. </seg>
<seg id="4055"> Glassmakers, painters, welders and metal craftsmen all have their role to play. ' </seg>
<seg id="4056"> There will always be fawanis in Ramadan, but in the future I think Egyptian-made ones could become extinct/ Zayat said. 'There is no way they can ever compete with things made in China.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4057"> Think how crazy that statement is: Egypt has masses of low-wage workers, like China. </seg>
<seg id="4058"> It sits right next to Europe, on the Suez Canal. </seg>
<seg id="4059"> It could be and should be the Taiwan of the eastern Mediterranean, but instead it is throwing in the towel to atheistic China on the manufacture of one of Muslim Egypt's most cherished cultural artifacts. </seg>
<seg id="4060"> Ibrahim El Esway, one of the main importers from China of fawanis, gave The Business Monthly a tour of his warehouse in the Egyptian town of Muski: He had imported sixteen different models of Ramadan lanterns from China in 2004. </seg>
<seg id="4061"> ˇ°Amid the crowds at Muski, [El Esway] gestured to one of his employees, who promptly opened a dust-covered box and pulled out a plastic fawanis shaped like the head of Simba, from The Lion King. 'This is the first model we imported back in 1994,' he said. </seg>
<seg id="4062"> He switched it on. </seg>
<seg id="4063"> As the blue-colored lion's head lit up, the song 'It's a Small World' rang out.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4064"> Introspection </seg>
<seg id="4065"> The previous section of this book looked at how individuals, particularly Americans, should think about meeting the challenge posed by the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4066"> This chapter focuses on what sort of policies developing countries need to undertake in order to create the right environment for their companies and entrepreneurs to thrive in a flat world, although many of the things I am about to say apply to many developed countries as well. </seg>
<seg id="4067"> When developing countries start thinking about the challenge of flatism, the first thing they need to do is engage in some brutally honest introspection. </seg>
<seg id="4068"> A country, its people and leaders alike, has to be honest with itself and look clearly at exactly where it stands in relation to other countries and in relation to the ten flatteners. </seg>
<seg id="4069"> It has to ask itself, ˇ°To what extent is my country advancing or being left behind by the flattening of the world, and to what extent is it adapting to and taking advantage of all the new platforms for collaboration and competition?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4070"> As that Chinese banking official boasted to my Mexican colleague, China is the wolf. </seg>
<seg id="4071"> Of all the ten flatteners, the entry of China into the world market is the most important for developing countries, and for many developed countries. </seg>
<seg id="4072"> China can do high-quality low-cost manufacturing better than any other country, and increasingly, it also can do high-quality higher-cost manufacturing. </seg>
<seg id="4073"> With China and the other nine flatteners coming on so strong, no country today can afford to be anything less than brutally honest with itself. </seg>
<seg id="4074"> To that end, I believe that what the world needs today is a club that would be modeled after Alcoholics Anonymous (A.A.). </seg>
<seg id="4075"> It would be called Developing Countries Anonymous (D.C.A.). </seg>
<seg id="4076"> And just as at the first A.A. meeting you attend you have to stand up and say, ˇ°My name is Thomas Friedman and I'm an alcoholic,ˇ± so at Developing Countries Anonymous, countries would have to stand up at their first meeting and say, ˇ°My name is Syria and I'm underdeveloped.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4077"> Or ˇ°My name is Argentina and I'm underachieving. I have not lived up to my potential.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4078"> Every country needs ˇ°the ability to make your own introspection,ˇ± since ˇ°no country develops without going through an X-ray of where you are and where your limits are,ˇ± said Luis de la Calle, one of Mexico's chief NAFTA negotiators. </seg>
<seg id="4079"> Countries that fall off the development wagon are a bit like drunks; to get back on they have to learn to see themselves as they really are. </seg>
<seg id="4080"> Development is a voluntary process. </seg>
<seg id="4081"> You need a positive decision to make the right steps, but it starts with introspection. </seg>
<seg id="4082"> I Can Get It for You Wholesale </seg>
<seg id="4083"> During the late 1990s, but particularly after the fall of the Berlin Wall, a lot of countries started to pursue development in a new way through a process that I call reform wholesale. </seg>
