<seg id="4084"> The era of Globalization 2.0, when the world shrank from a size medium to a size small, was the era of reform wholesale, an era of broad macroeconomic reform. </seg>
<seg id="4085"> These wholesale reforms were initiated by a small handful of leaders in countries like China, Russia, Mexico, Brazil, and India. </seg>
<seg id="4086"> These small groups of reformers often relied on the leverage of authoritarian political systems to unleash the state-smothered market forces in their societies. </seg>
<seg id="4087"> They pushed their countries into more export-oriented, free-market strategies-based on privatization of state companies, deregulation of financial markets, currency adjustments, foreign direct investment, shrinking subsidies, lowering of protectionist tariff barriers, and introduction of more flexible labor laws-from the top down without ever really asking the people. </seg>
<seg id="4088"> Ernesto Zedillo, who served as president of Mexico from 1994 to 2000 and was finance minister before that, once remarked to me that all the decisions to open the Mexican economy were taken by three people. </seg>
<seg id="4089"> How many people do you suppose Deng Xiaoping consulted before he declared, ˇ°To get rich is glorious,ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4090"> Deng tossed over decades of Communist ideology with one sentence: ˇ°Black cat, white cat, all that matters is that it catches mice.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4091"> In 1991, when India's finance minister, Manmohan Singh, took the first tentative steps to open India's economy to more foreign trade, investment, and competition, it was a result not of some considered national debate and dialogue, but of the fact that India's economy at that moment was so sclerotic, so unappealing to foreign investors, that it had almost run out of foreign currency. </seg>
<seg id="4092"> When Mikhail Gorbachev started dabbling with perestroika, it was with his back up against the Kremlin wall and with few allies in the Soviet leadership. </seg>
<seg id="4093"> The same was true of Margaret Thatcher when she took on the striking coal miners' union in 1984 and forced reform wholesale onto the sagging British economy. </seg>
<seg id="4094"> What all these leaders confronted was the irrefutable fact that more open and competitive markets are the only sustainable vehicle for growing a nation out of poverty, because they are the only guarantee that new ideas, technologies, and best practices are easily flowing into your country and that private enterprises, and even government, have the competitive incentive and flexibility to adopt those new ideas and turn them into jobs and products. </seg>
<seg id="4095"> As David Dollar and Art Kray conclude in their book Trade, Growth, and Poverty, economic growth and trade remain the best antipoverty program in the world.The World Bank reported that in 1990 there were roughly 375 million people in China living in extreme poverty, on less than $ 1 per day. </seg>
<seg id="4096"> By 2001, there were 212 million Chinese living in extreme poverty, and by 2015, if current trends hold, there will be only 16 million living on less than $1 a day. </seg>
<seg id="4097"> In South Asia-primarily India, Pakistan, and Bangladesh-the numbers go from 462 million in 1990 living on less than $1 a day down to 431 million by 2001 and down to 216 million in 2015. </seg>
<seg id="4098"> In sub-Saharan Africa, by contrast, where globalization has been slow to take hold, there were 227 million people living on less than $1 a day in 1990, 313 million in 2001, and an expected 340 million by 2015. </seg>
<seg id="4099"> The problem for any globalizing country lies in thinking you can stop with reform wholesale. In the 1990s, some countries thought that if you got your ten commandments of reform wholesale right-thou shall privatize state-owned industries, thou shall deregulate utilities, thou shall lower tariffs and encourage export industries, etc.-you had a successful development strategy. </seg>
<seg id="4100"> But as the world started to get smaller and flatter-enabling China to compete everywhere with everyone on a broad range of manufactured products, enabling India to export its brainpower everywhere, enabling corporations to outsource any task anywhere, and enabling individuals to compete globally as never before -reform wholesale was no longer sufficient to keep countries on a sustainable growth path. </seg>
<seg id="4101"> A deeper process of reform was required-a process I would call reform retail. </seg>
<seg id="4102"> What if regions of the world were like the neighborhoods of a city? </seg>
<seg id="4103"> What would the world look like? </seg>
<seg id="4104"> I'd describe it like this: Western Europe would be an assisted-living facility, with an aging population lavishly attended to by Turkish nurses. </seg>
<seg id="4105"> The United States would be a gated community, with a metal detector at the front gate and a lot of people sitting in their front yards complaining about how lazy everyone else was, even though out back there was a small opening in the fence for Mexican labor and other energetic immigrants who helped to make the gated community function. </seg>
<seg id="4106"> Latin America would be the fun part of town, the club district, where the workday doesn't begin until ten p.m. and everyone sleeps until midmorning. </seg>
<seg id="4107"> It's definitely the place to hang out, but in between the clubs, you don't see a lot of new businesses opening up, except on the street where the Chileans live. </seg>
<seg id="4108"> The landlords in this neighborhood almost never reinvest their profits here, but keep them in a bank across town. </seg>
<seg id="4109"> The Arab street would be a dark alley where outsiders fear to tread, except for a few side streets called Dubai, Jordan, Bahrain, Qatar, and Morocco. </seg>
<seg id="4110"> The only new businesses are gas stations, whose owners, like the elites in the Latin neighborhood, rarely reinvest their funds in the neighborhood. </seg>
<seg id="4111"> Many people on the Arab street have their curtains closed, their shutters drawn, and signs on their front lawn that say, ˇ°No Trespassing. Beware of Dog.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4112"> Their neighborhood is a big teeming market, made up of small shops and one-room factories, interspersed with Stanley Kaplan SAT prep schools and engineering colleges. </seg>
<seg id="4113"> Nobody ever sleeps in this neighborhood, everyone lives in extended families, and everyone is working and saving to get to ˇ°the right side of the tracks.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4114"> On the Chinese streets, there's no rule of law, but the roads are all well paved; there are no potholes, and the streetlights all work. </seg>
<seg id="4115"> On the Indian streets, by contrast, no one ever repairs the streetlights, the roads are full of ruts, but the police are sticklers for the rules. You need a license to open a lemonade stand on the Indian streets. </seg>
<seg id="4116"> Luckily, the local cops can be bribed, and the successful entrepreneurs all have their own generators to run their factories and the latest cell phones to get around the fact that the local telephone poles are all down. </seg>
<seg id="4117"> Africa, sadly, is that part of town where the businesses are boarded up, life expectancy is declining, and the only new buildings are health-care clinics. </seg>
<seg id="4118"> The point here is that every region of the world has its strengths and weaknesses, and all are in need of reform retail to some degree. </seg>
<seg id="4119"> What is reform retail? </seg>
<seg id="4120"> In the simplest terms, it is more than just opening your country to foreign trade and investment and making a few macroeco-nomic policy changes from the top. That is reform wholesale. </seg>
<seg id="4121"> Reform retail presumes you have already done reform wholesale. </seg>
<seg id="4122"> It involves looking at four key aspects of your society-infrastructure, regulatory institutions, education, and culture (the general way your country and leaders relate to the world)-and upgrading each one to remove as many friction points as possible. </seg>
<seg id="4123"> The idea of reform retail is to enable the greatest number of your people to have the best legal and institutional framework within which to innovate, start companies, and become attractive partners for those who want to collaborate with them from elsewhere in the world. </seg>
<seg id="4124"> Many of the key elements of reform retail were best defined by the research done by the World Bank's International Finance Corporation (IFC) and its economic analysis team led by its chief economist, Michael Klein. </seg>
<seg id="4125"> What do we learn from their work? </seg>
<seg id="4126"> To begin with, you don't grow your country out of poverty by guaranteeing everyone a job. </seg>
<seg id="4127"> Egypt guarantees all college graduates a job each year, and it has been mired in poverty with a slow-growing economy for fifty years. </seg>
<seg id="4128"> ˇ°If it were just a matter of the number of jobs, solutions would be easy,ˇ± note Klein and Bita Hadjimichael in their World Bank Study, The Private Sector in Development. </seg>
<seg id="4129"> ˇ°For example, state-owned enterprises could absorb all those in need of employment. </seg>
<seg id="4130"> The real issue is not just employment, but increasingly productive employment that allows living standards to rise.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4131"> State-owned enterprises and state-subsidized private firms usually have not delivered sustainable productivity growth, and neither have a lot of other approaches that people assume are elixirs of growth, they add. </seg>
<seg id="4132"> Just attracting more foreign investment into a country also doesn't automatically do it. </seg>
<seg id="4133"> ˇ°Productivity growth and, hence, the way out of poverty, is not simply a matter of throwing resources at the problem,ˇ± say Klein and Hadjimichael. ˇ°More important, it is a matter of using resources well.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4134"> In other words, countries grow out of poverty not only when they manage their fiscal and monetary policies responsibly from above, i.e., reform wholesale. </seg>
<seg id="4135"> They grow out of poverty when they also create an environment below that makes it very easy for their people to start businesses, raise capital, and become entrepreneurs, and when they subject their people to at least some competition from beyond-because companies and countries with competitors always innovate more and faster. </seg>
<seg id="4136"> The IFC drove home this point with a comprehensive study of more than 130 countries, called Doing Business in 2004. The IFC asked five basic questions about doing business in each of these countries, questions about how easy or difficult it is to 1) start a business in terms of local rules, regulations, and license fees, 2) hire and fire workers, 3) enforce a contract, 4) get credit, and 5) close a business that goes bankrupt or is failing. </seg>
<seg id="4137"> To translate it into my own lexicon, those countries that make all these things relatively simple and friction-free have undertaken reform retail, and those that have not are stalled in reform wholesale and are not likely to thrive in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4138"> The IFC's criteria were inspired by the brilliant and innovative work of Hernando de Soto, who has demonstrated in Peru and other developing nations that if you change the regulatory and business environment for the poor, and give them the tools to collaborate, they will do the rest. </seg>
<seg id="4139"> Doing Business in 2004 tries to explain each of its points with a few colorful examples: ˇ°Teuku, an entreprenuer in Jakarta, wants to open a textile factory. </seg>
<seg id="4140"> He has customers lined up, imported machinery, and a promising business plan. </seg>
<seg id="4141"> Teuku's first encounter with the government is when registering his business. </seg>
<seg id="4142"> He gets the standard forms from the Ministry of Justice, and completes and notarizes them. Teuku proves that he is a local resident and does not have a criminal record. </seg>
<seg id="4143"> He obtains a tax number, applies for a business license, and deposits the minimum capital (three times national income per capita) in the bank. </seg>
<seg id="4144"> He then publishes the articles of association in the official gazette, pays a stamp fee, registers at the Ministry of Justice, and waits 90 days before filing for social security. </seg>
<seg id="4145"> One hundred sixty-eight days after he commences the process, Teuku can legally start operations. </seg>
<seg id="4146"> In the meantime, his customers have contracted with another business. </seg>
<seg id="4147"> ˇ°In Panama, another entrepreneur, Ina, registers her construction company in only 19 days. </seg>
<seg id="4148"> Business is booming and Ina wants to hire someone for a two-year appointment. </seg>
<seg id="4149"> But the employment law only allows fixed-term appointments for specific tasks, and even then requires a maximum term of one year. </seg>
<seg id="4150"> At the same time, one of her current workers often leaves early, with no excuse, and makes costly mistakes. </seg>
<seg id="4151"> To replace him, Ina needs to notify and get approval from the union, and pay five months' severance pay. </seg>
<seg id="4152"> Ina rejects the more qualified applicant she would like to hire and keeps the underperforming worker on staff. </seg>
<seg id="4153"> ˇ°Ali, a trader in the United Arab Emirates, can hire and fire with ease. </seg>
<seg id="4154"> But one of his customers refuses to pay for equipment delivered three months earlier. </seg>
<seg id="4155"> It takes 27 procedures and more than 550 days to resolve the payment dispute in court. </seg>
<seg id="4156"> Almost all procedures must be made in writing, and require extensive legal justification and the use of lawyers. </seg>
<seg id="4157"> After this experience, Ali decides to deal only with customers he knows well. </seg>
<seg id="4158"> ˇ°Timnit, a young entrepreneur in Ethiopia, wants to expand her successful consulting business by taking a loan. </seg>
<seg id="4159"> But she has no proof of good credit history because there are no credit information registries. </seg>
<seg id="4160"> Although her business has substantial assets in accounts receivable, laws restrict her bank from using these as collateral. </seg>
<seg id="4161"> The bank knows it cannot recover the debt if Timnit defaults, because courts are inefficient and laws give creditors few powers. </seg>
<seg id="4162"> Credit is denied. </seg>
<seg id="4163"> The business stays small. </seg>
<seg id="4164"> ˇ°Having registered, hired workers, enforced contracts, and obtained credit, Avik, a businessman in India, cannot make a profit and goes out of business. </seg>
<seg id="4165"> Faced with a 10-year-long process of going through bankruptcy, Avik absconds, leaving his workers, the bank, and the tax agency with nothing.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4166"> If you want to know why two decades of macroeconomic reform wholesale at the top have not slowed the spread of poverty and produced enough new jobs in key countries of Latin America, Africa, the Arab world, and the former Soviet Empire, it is because there has been too little reform retail. </seg>
<seg id="4167"> According to the IFC report, if you want to create productive jobs (the kind that lead to rising standards of living), and if you want to stimulate the growth of new businesses (the kind that innovate, compete, and create wealth), you need a regulatory environment that makes it easy to start a business, easy to adjust a business to changing market circumstances and opportunities, and easy to close a business that goes bankrupt, so that the capital can be freed up for more productive uses. </seg>
<seg id="4168"> ˇ°It takes two days to start a business in Australia, but 203 days in Haiti and 215 days in the Democratic Republic of Congo,ˇ± the IFC study found. ˇ°There are no monetary costs to start a new business in Denmark, but it costs more than five times income per capita in Cambodia and over thirteen times in Sierra Leone. </seg>
<seg id="4169"> Hong Kong, Singapore, Thailand and more than three dozen other economies require no minimum capital from start-ups. </seg>
<seg id="4170"> In contrast, in Syria the capital requirement is equivalent to fifty-six times income per capita... </seg>
<seg id="4171"> Businesses in the Czech Republic and Denmark can hire workers on part-time or fixed-term contracts for any job, without specifying maximum duration of the contract. </seg>
<seg id="4172"> A simple commercial contract is enforced in seven days in Tunisia and thirty-nine days in the Netherlands, but takes almost 1,500 days in Guatemala. </seg>
<seg id="4173"> The cost of enforcement is less than 1 percent of the disputed amount in Austria, Canada and the United Kingdom, but more than 100 percent in Burkina Faso, the Dominican Republic, Indonesia... and the Philippines. </seg>
<seg id="4174"> Credit bureaus contain credit histories on almost every adult in New Zealand, Norway and the United States. </seg>
<seg id="4175"> But the credit registries in Cameroon, Ghana, Pakistan, Nigeria and Serbia and Montenegro have credit histories for less than 1 percent of adults. </seg>
<seg id="4176"> In the United Kingdom, laws on collateral and bankruptcy give creditors strong powers to recover their money if a debtor defaults. </seg>
<seg id="4177"> In Colombia, the Republic of Congo, Mexico, Oman and Tunisia, a creditor has no such rights. It takes less than six months to go through bankruptcy proceedings in Ireland and Japan, but more than ten years in Brazil and India. </seg>
<seg id="4178"> It costs less than 1 percent of the value of the estate to resolve insolvency in Finland, the Netherlands, Norway and Singapore-and nearly half the estate value in Chad, Panama, Macedonia, Venezuela, Serbia and Montenegro, and Sierra Leone.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4179"> As the IFC report notes, excessive regulation also tends to hurt most the very people it is supposed to protect. </seg>
<seg id="4180"> The rich and the well connected just buy or hustle their way around onerous regulations. </seg>
<seg id="4181"> In countries that have very regulated labor markets where it is difficult to hire and fire people, women, especially, have a hard time finding employment. </seg>
<seg id="4182"> ˇ°Good regulation does not mean zero regulation,ˇ± concludes the IFC study. ˇ°The optimal level of regulation is not none, but may be less than what is currently found in most countries, and especially poor ones.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4183"> It offers what I call a five-step checklist for reform retail. One, simplify and deregulate wherever possible in competitive markets, because competition for consumers and workers can be the best source of pressure for best practices, and overregulation just opens the door for corrupt bureaucrats to demand bribes. </seg>
<seg id="4184"> ˇ°There is no reason for Angola to have one of the most rigid employment laws if Portugal, whose laws Angola adapted, has already revised them twice to make the labor market more flexible,ˇ± says the IFC study. </seg>
<seg id="4185"> Two, focus on enhancing property rights. </seg>
<seg id="4186"> Under de Soto's initiative, the Peruvian government in the last decade has issued property titles to 1.2 million urban squatter households. </seg>
<seg id="4187"> ˇ°Secure property rights have enabled parents to leave their homes and find jobs instead of staying in to protect the property,ˇ± says the IFC study. </seg>
<seg id="4188"> ˇ°The main beneficiaries are their children, who can now go to school.ˇ± Three, expand the use of the Internet for regulation fulfillment. </seg>
<seg id="4189"> It makes it faster, more transparent, and far less open to bribery. </seg>
<seg id="4190"> Four, reduce court involvement in business matters. </seg>
<seg id="4191"> And last but certainly not least, advises the IFC study, ˇ°Make reform a continuous process... </seg>
<seg id="4192"> What is striking about the IFC's criteria is that a lot of people think they are relevant only for Peru and Argentina, but in fact some of the countries that score worst are places like Germany and Italy. </seg>
<seg id="4193"> Added John Chambers, the CEO of Cisco Systems, which uses a global supply chain to build the routers that run the Internet and is constantly being wooed to invest in one country or another, ˇ°The jobs are going to go where the best-educated workforce is with the most competitive infrastructure and environment for creativity and supportive government. </seg>
<seg id="4194"> And by definition those people will have the best standard of living. </seg>
<seg id="4195"> This may or may not be the countries who led the Industrial Revolution.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4196"> Culture Matters: Glocalization </seg>
<seg id="4197"> One answer is culture. </seg>
<seg id="4198"> To reduce a country's economic performance to culture alone is ridiculous, but to analyze a country's economic performance without reference to culture is equally ridiculous, although that is what many economists and political scientists want to do. </seg>
<seg id="4199"> This subject is highly controversial and is viewed as politically incorrect to introduce. </seg>
<seg id="4200"> So it is often the elephant in the room that no one wants to speak about. </seg>
<seg id="4201"> But I am going to speak about it here, for a very simple reason: As the world goes flat, and more and more of the tools of collaboration get distributed and com-moditized, the gap between cultures that have the will, the way, and the focus to quickly adopt these new tools and apply them and those that do not will matter more. </seg>
<seg id="4202"> The differences between the two will become amplified. </seg>
<seg id="4203"> One of the most important books on this subject is The Wealth and Poverty of Nations by the economist David Landes. </seg>
<seg id="4204"> He argues that although climate, natural resources, and geography all play roles in explaining why some countries are able to make the leap to industrialization and others are not, the key factor is actually a country's cultural endowments, particularly the degree to which it has internalized the values of hard work, thrift, honesty, patience, and tenacity, as well as the degree to which it is open to change, new technology, and equality for women. </seg>
<seg id="4205"> One can agree or disagree with the balance Landes strikes between these cultural mores and other factors shaping economic performance. </seg>
<seg id="4206"> But I find refreshing his insistence on elevating the culture question, and his refusal to buy into arguments that the continued stagnation of some countries is simply about Western colonialism, geography, or historical legacy. </seg>
<seg id="4207"> In my own travels, two aspects of culture have struck me as particularly relevant in the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4208"> One is how outward your culture is: To what degree is it open to foreign influences and ideas? </seg>
<seg id="4209"> How well does it ˇ°glocalizeˇ±? </seg>
<seg id="4210"> The other, more intangible, is how inward your culture is. </seg>
<seg id="4211"> By that I mean, to what degree is there a sense of national solidarity and a focus on development, to what degree is there trust within the society for strangers to collaborate together, and to what degree are the elites in the country concerned with the masses and ready to invest at home, or are they indifferent to their own poor and more interested in investing abroad?The more you have a culture that naturally glocalizes-that is, the more your culture easily absorbs foreign ideas and best practices and melds those with its own traditions-the greater advantage you will have in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4212"> The natural ability to glocalize has been one of the strengths of Indian culture, American culture, Japanese culture, and, lately, Chinese culture. </seg>
<seg id="4213"> The Indians, for instance, take the view that the Moguls come, the Moguls go, the British come, the British go, we take the best and leave the rest-but we still eat curry, our women still wear saris, and we still live in tightly bound extended family units. </seg>
<seg id="4214"> That's glocalizing at its best. </seg>
<seg id="4215"> ˇ°Cultures that are open and willing to change have a huge advantage in this world,ˇ± said Jerry Rao, the MphasiS CEO who heads the Indian high-tech trade association. </seg>
<seg id="4216"> ˇ°My great-grandmother was illiterate. My grandmother went to grade two. </seg>
<seg id="4217"> My mother did not go to college. </seg>
<seg id="4218"> My sister has a master's degree in economics, and my daughter is at the University of Chicago. </seg>
<seg id="4219"> We have done all this in living memory, but we have been willing to change... </seg>
<seg id="4220"> You have to have a strong culture, but also the openness to adapt and adopt from others. </seg>
<seg id="4221"> The cultural exclusivists have a real disadvantage. </seg>
<seg id="4222"> Think about it, think about the time when the emperor in China threw out the British ambassador. </seg>
<seg id="4223"> Who did it hurt? </seg>
<seg id="4224"> It hurt the Chinese. </seg>
<seg id="4225"> Exclusivity is a dangerous thing.ˇ±Openness is critical, added Rao, ˇ°because you start tending to respect people for their talent and abilities. </seg>
<seg id="4226"> When you are chatting with another developer in another part of the world, you don't know what his or her color is. </seg>
<seg id="4227"> You are dealing with people on the basis of talent-not race or ethnicity-and that changes, subtly, over time your whole view of human beings, if you are in this talent-based and performance-based world rather than the background-based world.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4228"> This helps explain why so many Muslim countries have been struggling as the world goes flat. </seg>
<seg id="4229"> For complicated cultural and historical reasons, many of them do not glocalize well, although there are plenty of exceptions-namely, Turkey, Lebanon, Bahrain, Dubai, Indonesia, and Malaysia. All of these latter countries, though, tend to be the more secular Muslim nations. </seg>
<seg id="4230"> In a world where the single greatest advantage a culture can have is the ability to foster adaptability and adoptability, the Muslim world today is dominated by a religious clergy that literally bans ijtihad, reinterpretation of the principles of Islam in light of current circumstances.Think about the whole mind-set of bin Ladenism. </seg>
<seg id="4231"> It is to ˇ°purgeˇ± Saudi Arabia of all foreigners and foreign influences. </seg>
<seg id="4232"> That is exactly the opposite of glocalizing and collaborating. </seg>
<seg id="4233"> Tribal culture and thinking still dominate in many Arab countries, and the tribal mind-set is also anathema to collaboration. </seg>
<seg id="4234"> If you want to have a modern complex division of labor, you have to be able to put more trust in strangers. </seg>
<seg id="4235"> In the Arab-Muslim world, argues David Landes, certain cultural attitudes have in many ways become a barrier to development, particularly the tendency to still treat women as a source of danger or pollution to be cut off from the public space and denied entry into economic activities. </seg>
<seg id="4236"> A system that privileges the men from birth on, Landes also argues, simply because they are male, and gives them power over their sisters and other female members of society, is bad for the men. It builds in them a sense of entitlement that discourages what it takes to improve, to advance, and to achieve. </seg>
<seg id="4237"> This sort of discrimination, he notes, is not something limited to the Arab Middle East, of course. </seg>
<seg id="4238"> Indeed, strains of it are found in different degrees all around the world, even in so-called advanced industrial societies. </seg>
<seg id="4239"> The Arab-Muslim world's resistance to glocalization is something that some liberal Arab commentators are now focusing on. </seg>
<seg id="4240"> Consider a May 5, 2004, article in the Saudi English-language daily Arab News by liberal Saudi journalist Raid Qusti, titled ˇ°How Long Before the First Step?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4241"> ˇ°Terrorist incidents in Saudi Arabia are more or less becoming everyday news. </seg>
<seg id="4242"> Every time I hope and pray that it ends, it only seems to get worse,ˇ± Qusti wrote. </seg>
<seg id="4243"> ˇ°One explanation to why all of this is happening was brought up by the editor in chief of Al-Riyadh newspaper, Turki Al-Sudairi, on a program about determining the roots of the terrorist acts. </seg>
<seg id="4244"> He said that the people carrying out these attacks shared the ideology of the Juhaiman movement that seized the Grand Mosque in the seventies. </seg>
<seg id="4245"> They had an ideology of accusing others of being infidels and giving themselves a free hand to kill them, be it Westerners-who, according to them, ought to be kicked out of the Arabian Peninsula-or the Muslim believer who does not follow their path. </seg>
<seg id="4246"> The question Al-Sudairi forgot to bring up was: What are we Saudis going to do about it? </seg>
<seg id="4247"> If we as a nation decline to look at the root causes, as we have for the past two decades, it will only be a matter of time before another group of people with the same ideology spring up. </seg>
<seg id="4248"> Have we helped create these monsters? </seg>
<seg id="4249"> Our education system, which does not stress tolerance of other faiths-let alone tolerance of followers of other Islamic schools of thought-is one thing that needs to be re-evaluated from top to bottom. </seg>
<seg id="4250"> Saudi culture itself and the fact that the majority of us do not accept other lifestyles and impose our own on other people is another. </seg>
<seg id="4251"> And the fact that from fourth to 12th grade we do not teach our children that there are other civilizations in the world and that we are part of the global community and only stress the Islamic empires over and over is also worth re-evaluating.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4252"> It is simply too easily forgotten that when it comes to economic activities, one of the greatest virtues a country or community can have is a culture of tolerance. </seg>
<seg id="4253"> When tolerance is the norm, everyone flourishes- because tolerance breeds trust, and trust is the foundation of innovation and entrepreneurship. </seg>
<seg id="4254"> Increase the level of trust in any group, company, or society, and only good things happen. </seg>
<seg id="4255"> It is the nature of the Hindu religion to be tolerant and, in its own curious way, permissive... </seg>
<seg id="4256"> When left to themselves, Indians (like the Chinese) always prosper as a community. </seg>
<seg id="4257"> Take the case of Uganda's Indian population, which was expelled by the horrific dictator Idi Amin and received into the tolerant society of Britain. </seg>
<seg id="4258"> Islam, down through the years, has thrived when it fostered a culture of tolerance, as in Moorish Spain. </seg>
<seg id="4259"> But in its modern form, in too many cases Islam has been captured and interpreted by spiritual leaders who do not embrace a culture of tolerance, change, or innovation, and that, Johnson noted, surely has contributed to lagging economic growth in many Muslim lands. </seg>
<seg id="4260"> Here we come again to the coefficient of flatness. </seg>
<seg id="4261"> Countries without natural resources are much more likely, through human evolution, to develop the habits of openness to new ideas, because it is the only way they can survive and advance. </seg>
<seg id="4262"> The good news, though, is that not only does culture matter, but culture can change. </seg>
<seg id="4263"> Cultures are not wired into our human DNA. </seg>
<seg id="4264"> They are a product of the context-geography, education level, leadership, and historical experience-of any society. </seg>
<seg id="4265"> As those change, so too can culture. </seg>
<seg id="4266"> Japan and Germany went from highly militarized societies to highly pacifist and staunchly democratic societies in the last fifty years. </seg>
<seg id="4267"> Bahrain was one of the first Arab countries to discover oil. It was the first Arab country to run out of oil. </seg>
<seg id="4268"> And it was the first Arab country in the Arab Gulf to hold an election for parliament where women could run and vote. </seg>
<seg id="4269"> Muslim Spain was one of the most tolerant societies in the history of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4270"> Muslim Spain was a trading and merchant culture where people had to live by their wits and therefore learned to live well with others; </seg>
<seg id="4271"> Saudi Arabia today can get by just selling oil. </seg>
<seg id="4272"> Yet right next to Saudi Arabia sits Dubai, an Arab city-state that has used its petrodollars to build the trading, tourist, service, and computing center of the Arab Gulf. </seg>
<seg id="4273"> Dubai is one of the most tolerant, cosmopolitan places in the world, with, it often seems, more sushi bars and golf courses than mosques-and tourists don't even need a visa. </seg>
<seg id="4274"> So yes, culture matters, but culture is nested in contexts, not genes, and as those contexts, and local leaders, change and adapt, so too can culture. </seg>
<seg id="4275"> The Intangible Things </seg>
<seg id="4276"> Like many Indian Americans, Dinakar Singh, the hedge fund manager, regularly goes back to India to visit family. </seg>
<seg id="4277"> In the winter of 2004, he went back to New Delhi for a visit. </seg>
<seg id="4278"> When I saw him a few months later, he told me about the moment when he realized why India's economy, as a whole, still had not taken off as much as it should have-outside of the high-tech sector. </seg>
<seg id="4279"> ˇ°I was on the sixth floor of a hotel in New Delhi,ˇ± he recalled, ˇ°and when I looked out the window I could see for miles. </seg>
<seg id="4280"> How come? </seg>
<seg id="4281"> Because you do not have assured power in Delhi for elevators, so there are not many tall buildings.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4282"> No sensible investor would want to build a tall building in a city where the power could go out at any moment and you might have to walk up twenty flights of stairs. </seg>
<seg id="4283"> The result is more urban sprawl and an inefficient use of space. </seg>
<seg id="4284"> I told Singh that his story reminded me of a trip I had just taken to Dalian, China. </seg>
<seg id="4285"> I had been to Dalian in 1998, and when I went back in 2004,1 did not recognize the city. </seg>
<seg id="4286"> There were so many new buildings, including modern glass-and-steel towers, that I began to question whether I had actually visited there in 1998. </seg>
<seg id="4287"> Then I added another recollection. </seg>
<seg id="4288"> I went to school in Cairo in the summer of 1974. The three most prominent buildings in the city then were the Nile Hilton, the Cairo Tower, and the Egyptian TV building. </seg>
<seg id="4289"> Thirty years later, in 2004, they are still the most prominent buildings there; the Cairo skyline has barely changed. </seg>
<seg id="4290"> Whenever I go back to Cairo, I know exactly where I am. </seg>
<seg id="4291"> I visited Mexico City shortly before Dalian, where I had not visited in five years. </seg>
<seg id="4292"> I found it much cleaner than I had remembered, thanks to a citywide campaign by the mayor. </seg>
<seg id="4293"> There were also a few new buildings up, but not as many as I expected after a decade of NAFTA. </seg>
<seg id="4294"> Inside the buildings, though, I found my Mexican friends a little depressed. </seg>
<seg id="4295"> They told me that Mexico had lost its grooveˇŞit just wasn't growing like it had been, and people's self-confidence was waning.So in Delhi, you can see forever. </seg>
<seg id="4296"> In Cairo, the skyline seems forever the same. </seg>
<seg id="4297"> In China, if you miss visiting a city for a year, it's like you haven't been there in forever. </seg>
<seg id="4298"> And in Mexico City, just when Mexicans thought they had turned the corner forever, they ran smack into China, coming the other way and running much faster. </seg>
<seg id="4299"> What explains these differences? </seg>
<seg id="4300"> We know the basic formula for economic success-reform wholesale, followed by reform retail, plus good governance, education, infrastructure, and the ability to glocalize. </seg>
<seg id="4301"> What we don't know, though, and what I would bottle and sell if I did, is the answer to the question of why one country gets its act together to do all these things in a sustained manner and why another one doesn't. </seg>
<seg id="4302"> Why does one country's skyline change overnight and another's doesn't change over half a century? </seg>
<seg id="4303"> The only answer I have been able to find is something that cannot be defined: I call it the intangible things. </seg>
<seg id="4304"> These are primarily two qualities: a society's ability and willingness to pull together and sacrifice for the sake of economic development and the presence in a society of leaders with the vision to see what needs to be done in terms of development and the willingness to use power to push for change rather than to enrich themselves and preserve the status quo. </seg>
<seg id="4305"> Some countries (such as Korea and Taiwan) seem to be able to focus their energies on the priority of economic development, and others (such as Egypt and Syria) get distracted by ideology or local feuds. </seg>
<seg id="4306"> Some countries have leaders who use their time in office to try to drive modernization rather than personally enrich themselves. </seg>
<seg id="4307"> And some countries simply have venal elites, who use their time in office to line their pockets and then invest those riches in Swiss real estate. </seg>
<seg id="4308"> Why India had leaders who built institutes of technology and Pakistan had leaders who did not is a product of history, geography, and culture that I can only summarize as one of those intangible things. </seg>
<seg id="4309"> But even though these intangibles are not easily measured, they really do matter. </seg>
<seg id="4310"> The best way I know to illustrate this is by comparing Mexico and China. </seg>
<seg id="4311"> Mexico, on paper, seemed perfectly positioned to thrive in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4312"> It was right next door to the biggest, most powerful economy in the world. It signed a free-trade agreement with the United States and Canada in the 1990s and was poised to be a springboard to Latin America for both these huge economies. </seg>
<seg id="4313"> And it had a valuable natural resource in oil, which accounted for more than a third of government income. </seg>
<seg id="4314"> China, by contrast, was thousands of miles away, burdened by overpopulation, with few natural resources, with its best labor crowded onto a coastal plain, and with a burdensome debt legacy from fifty years of Communist rule. </seg>
<seg id="4315"> Ten years ago, if you took the names off these two countries and just gave someone their profiles, he surely would have bet on Mexico. </seg>
<seg id="4316"> And yet China has replaced Mexico as the second-largest exporter of goods into the United States. </seg>
<seg id="4317"> And there is a general sense, even among Mexicans, that even though China is thousands of miles away from America, it is growing closer to America economically, while Mexico, right on America's border, is becoming thousands of miles away.I am by no means writing Mexico off. </seg>
<seg id="4318"> Mexico, in the fullness of time, may turn out to be the slow-but-sure tortoise to China's hare. </seg>
<seg id="4319"> China still has a huge political transition to get through, which could derail it at any moment. </seg>
<seg id="4320"> Moreover, Mexico has many entrepreneurs who are as Chinese as the most entrepreneurial Chinese. Mexico would not have exported $138 billion worth of goods to the United States in 2003 if that were not the case. </seg>
<seg id="4321"> And you have many rural Chinese who are no more advanced or productive than rural Mexicans. </seg>
<seg id="4322"> But on balance, when you add it all up, the fact is that China has become the hare and Mexico has not, even though Mexico seemed to start with so many more natural advantages when the world went flat. </seg>
<seg id="4323"> Why?This is a question Mexicans themselves are asking. </seg>
<seg id="4324"> When you go to Mexico City these days, Mexicans will tell you that they are hearing that ˇ°giant sucking soundˇ± in stereo. </seg>
<seg id="4325"> ˇ°We are caught between India and China,ˇ± Jorge Castaneda, Mexico's former foreign minister, told me in 2004. ˇ°It is very difficult for us to compete with the Chinese, except with high-value-added industries. </seg>
<seg id="4326"> Where we should be competing, the services area, we are hit by the Indians with their back offices and call centers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4327"> No doubt China is benefiting to some degree from the fact that it still has an authoritarian system that can steamroll vested interests and archaic practices. </seg>
<seg id="4328"> Beijing's leadership can order many reforms from the top down, whether it is a new road or accession to the World Trade Organization. </seg>
<seg id="4329"> But China today also has better intangibles-an ability to summon and focus local energies on reform retail. China may be an authoritarian state, but it nevertheless has strong state institutions and a bureaucracy that manages to promote a lot of people on merit to key decision-making positions, and it has a certain public-spiritedness. </seg>
<seg id="4330"> The Mandarin tradition of promoting bureaucrats who see their role as promoting and protecting the interests of the state is still alive and well in China. </seg>
<seg id="4331"> ˇ°China has a tradition of meritocracy-a tradition that is also carried on in Korea and Japan,ˇ± said Francis Fukuyama, author of the classic The End of History and the Last Man. </seg>
<seg id="4332"> ˇ°All of them also have a basic sense of'stateness' where [public servants] are expected to look to the long-term interests of the stateˇ± and are rewarded by the system for doing so. </seg>
<seg id="4333"> Mexico, by contrast, moved during the 1990s from a basically one-party authoritarian state to a multiparty democracy. </seg>
<seg id="4334"> So just when Mexico needs to summon all its will and energy for reform retail on the micro level, it has to go through the much slower, albeit more legitimate, democratic process of constituency building. </seg>
<seg id="4335"> In other words, any Mexican president who wants to make changes has to aggregate so many more interest groups-like herding cats-to implement a reform than his autocratic predecessors, who could have done it by fiat. </seg>
<seg id="4336"> A lot of these interest groups, whether unions or oligarchs, have powerful vested interests in the status quo and the power to strangle reforms. </seg>
<seg id="4337"> And Mexico's state system, like that of so many of its Latin American neighbors, has a long history of simply being an instrument of patronage for the ruling party or local interests, not the national interest. </seg>
<seg id="4338"> Another of these intangible things is how much your culture prizes education. </seg>
<seg id="4339"> India and China both have a long tradition of parents telling their children that the greatest thing they can be in life is an engineer or a doctor. </seg>
<seg id="4340"> But building the schools to make that happen in Mexico simply has not been done. </seg>
<seg id="4341"> India and China each have more than fifty thousand students studying in the United States today. </seg>
<seg id="4342"> Mexico, which is smaller but right next door, has only about ten thousand. </seg>
<seg id="4343"> Mexico is also right next door to the world's biggest economy, which speaks English. </seg>
<seg id="4344"> But Mexico has not launched any crash program in English education or invested in scholarships to send large numbers of Mexican students to the United States to study. </seg>
<seg id="4345"> There is a ˇ°disconnect,ˇ± said President Zedillo, among Mexico's political establishment, the challenges of globalization, and the degree to which anyone is educating and harnessing the Mexican public to this task. </seg>
<seg id="4346"> It would be easy to conclude from just looking at Mexico and China that democracy may be a hindrance to reform retail. </seg>
<seg id="4347"> I think it is premature to conclude that. </seg>
<seg id="4348"> I think the real issue is leadership. </seg>
<seg id="4349"> There are democracies that are blessed with leaders who are able to make the sale and get their people focused on reform retail-Margaret Thatcher in England comes to mind-and there are democracies that drift for a long time without biting the bullet-modern Germany, for example. </seg>
<seg id="4350"> There are autocracies that really get focused-modern China-and there are others that just drift aimlessly, unwilling really to summon their people because the leaders are so illegitimate they are afraid of inflicting any pain-Zimbabwe. </seg>
<seg id="4351"> Mexico and Latin America generally have ˇ°fantastic potential,ˇ± says President Zedillo. ˇ°Latin America was ahead of everyone thirty years ago, but for twenty-five years we have been basically stagnant and the others are moving closer and well ahead. </seg>
<seg id="4352"> Our political systems are not capable of processing and adopting and executing those [reform retail] ideas. </seg>
<seg id="4353"> We are still discussing prehistory. </seg>
<seg id="4354"> China is moving every month, added Zedillo, ˇ°and we are taking years and years to decide on elementary reforms whose needs should be strikingly urgent for any human being. </seg>
<seg id="4355"> We are not competitive because we don't have infrastructure; you need people to pay taxes. </seg>
<seg id="4356"> How many new highways have been built connecting Mexico with the U.S. since NAFTA? [Virtually none.] </seg>
<seg id="4357"> Many people who would benefit from government expenditure don't pay taxes. </seg>
<seg id="4358"> The only way for government to serve is get people to pay higher taxes, </seg>
<seg id="4359"> [but] then the populism comes up and kills it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4360"> A Mexican newspaper recently ran a story about how the Converse shoe company was making tennis shoes in China using Mexican glue. </seg>
<seg id="4361"> ˇ°The whole article was about why are we giving them our glue,ˇ± said Zedillo, ˇ°when the right attitude would be how much more glue can we sell them? </seg>
<seg id="4362"> We still need to break some mental barriers.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4363"> It is not that Mexico has failed to modernize its export industries. </seg>
<seg id="4364"> It is losing ground to China primarily because China has changed even faster and more broadly, particularly in educating knowledge workers. </seg>
<seg id="4365"> As business consultant Daniel H. Rosen pointed out in an essay in The International Economy journal (Spring 2003), Mexico and China both saw their share of global exports grow in many of the same areas during the booming 1990s-from auto parts to electronics to toys and sporting goods-but China's share was growing faster. </seg>
<seg id="4366"> This was not just because of what China was doing right but because of what Mexico was doing wrong, which was not steadily honing its competitiveness with micro-reforms. </seg>
<seg id="4367"> What Mexico succeeded in doing was creating islands of competitiveness, like Monterrey, where it got things right and could take advantage of proximity to the United States, but the Mexican government never had a strategy for melting those islands into the rest of the country. </seg>
<seg id="4368"> This helps explain why from 1996 to 2002, Mexico's ranking in the Global Competitiveness Report actually fell while China's rose. </seg>
<seg id="4369"> And this was not just about cheap wages, said Rosen. </seg>
<seg id="4370"> It was about China's advantages in education, privatization, infrastructure, quality control, mid-level management, and the introduction of new technology. </seg>
<seg id="4371"> ˇ°So China is eating Mexico's lunch,ˇ± concluded Rosen, ˇ°but more due to the Mexican inability to capitalize on successes and induce broader reform than to China's lower wage workers per se.ˇ± In other words, it's reform retail, stupid. </seg>
<seg id="4372"> According to the Doing Business in 200S report, it takes an average of fifty-eight days to start a business in Mexico, compared with eight in Singapore and nine in Turkey. </seg>
<seg id="4373"> It takes seventy-four days to register a property in Mexico, but only twelve in the United States. </seg>
<seg id="4374"> Mexico's corporate income tax rate of 34 percent is twice as high as China's. </seg>
<seg id="4375"> The McKinsey Quarterly report ˇ°Beyond Cheap Laborˇ± noted that since 2000, as China joined the WTO and started to take advantage of the flattening of the world, Mexico lost 270,000 assembly jobs, and hundreds of factories closed. </seg>
<seg id="4376"> But the main advice the report had for Mexico and other middle-income countries feeling squeezed by China was this: ˇ°Rather than fixating on jobs lost to China, these countries should remember a fact of economic life: no place can remain the world's low-cost producer forever-even China will lose that title one day. </seg>
<seg id="4377"> Instead of trying to defend low-wage assembly jobs, Mexico and other middle-income countries should focus on creating jobs that add higher value. </seg>
<seg id="4378"> Only if more productive companies with higher-value-added activities replace less productive ones can middle income economies continue down the development path.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4379"> In short, the only way for Mexico to thrive is with a strategy of reform retail that will enable it to beat China to the top, not the bottom, because China is not focused on beating Mexico as much as it is on beating America. </seg>
<seg id="4380"> But winning that kind of race to the top takes intangible focus and will. </seg>
<seg id="4381"> You cannot maintain rising standards of living in a flattening world when you are up against competitors who are getting not only their fundamentals right but also their intangibles. </seg>
<seg id="4382"> China does not just want to get rich. </seg>
<seg id="4383"> It wants to get powerful. </seg>
<seg id="4384"> China doesn't just want to learn how to make GM cars. </seg>
<seg id="4385"> It wants to be GM and put GM out of business. </seg>
<seg id="4386"> Anyone who doubts that should spend time with young Chinese.Said Luis Rubio, president of Mexico's Center of Research for Development, ˇ°The more self-confidence you have, the more it diminishes your mythologies and complexes. </seg>
<seg id="4387"> One of the great things about Mexico in the early 1990s was that Mexicans saw that they could do it, they could make it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4388"> A lot of that self-confidence, though, has been lost in Mexico in recent years, because the government stopped reforming. </seg>
<seg id="4389"> ˇ°A lack of self-confidence leads a country to keep chewing on the past,ˇ± added Rubio. </seg>
<seg id="4390"> ˇ°A lack of self-confidence [in Mexico] means that everyone in the country thinks the U.S. is going to take Mexico to the cleaners.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4391"> That is why NAFTA was so important for Mexico's self-confidence. </seg>
<seg id="4392"> ˇ°What NAFTA accomplished was to get Mexicans to think forward and outward instead of inward and backward. </seg>
<seg id="4393"> [But] NAFTA was seen [by its architects] as an end more than a beginning. It was seen as the conclusion of a process of political and economic reforms.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4394"> Unfortunately, he added, ˇ°Mexico did not have a strategy for going forward.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4395"> Will Rogers said it a long time ago: ˇ°Even if you're on the right track, you'll get run over if you just sit there.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4396"> The flatter the world gets, the faster that will happen. </seg>
<seg id="4397"> Mexico got itself on the right track with reform wholesale, but then, for a lot of tangible and intangible reasons, it just sat there and reform retail stalled. </seg>
<seg id="4398"> The more Mexico just sits there, the more it is going to get run over. </seg>
<seg id="4399"> And it won't be alone. </seg>
<seg id="4400"> TWELVE The Dell Theory of Conflict Prevention, Old-Time Versus Just-in-Time </seg>
<seg id="4401"> Free Trade is God's diplomacy. There is no other certain way of uniting people in the bonds of peace.ˇŞBritish politician Richard Cobden, 1857 </seg>
<seg id="4402"> Before I share with you the subject of this chapter, I have to tell you a little bit about the computer that I wrote this book on. </seg>
<seg id="4403"> It's related to the theme I am about to discuss. </seg>
<seg id="4404"> This book was largely written on a Dell Inspiron 600m notebook, service tag number 9ZRJP41. </seg>
<seg id="4405"> As part of the research for this book71 visited with the management team at Dell near Austin, Texas. </seg>
<seg id="4406"> I shared with them the ideas in this book and in return I asked for one favor: I asked them to trace for me the entire global supply chain that produced my Dell notebook. </seg>
<seg id="4407"> Here is their report: My computer was conceived when I phoned Dell's 800 number on April 2, 2004, and was connected to sales representative Mujteba Naqvi, who immediately entered my order into Dell's order management system. </seg>
<seg id="4408"> He typed in both the type of notebook I ordered as well as the special features I wanted, along with my personal information, shipping address, billing address, and credit card information. </seg>
<seg id="4409"> My credit card was verified by Dell through its work flow connection with Visa, and my order was then released to Dell's production system. </seg>
<seg id="4410"> Dell has six factories around the world-in Limerick, Ireland; Xiamen, China; Eldorado do Sul, Brazil; Nashville, Tennesee; Austin, Texas; and Penang, Malaysia. </seg>
<seg id="4411"> My order went out by e-mail to the Dell notebook factory in Malaysia, where the parts for the computer were immediately ordered from the supplier logistics centers (SLCs) next to the Penang factory. Surrounding every Dell factory in the world are these supplier logistics centers, owned by the different suppliers of Dell parts. </seg>
<seg id="4412"> These SLCs are like staging areas. </seg>
<seg id="4413"> If you are a Dell supplier anywhere in the world, your job is to keep your SLC full of your specific parts so they can constantly be trucked over to the Dell factory for just-in-time manufacturing. </seg>
<seg id="4414"> ˇ°In an average day, we sell 140,000 to 150,000 computers,ˇ± explained Dick Hunter, one of Dell's three global production managers. </seg>
<seg id="4415"> ˇ°Those orders come in over Dell.com or over the telephone. </seg>
<seg id="4416"> As soon these orders come in, our suppliers know about it. </seg>
<seg id="4417"> They get a signal based on every component in the machine you ordered, so the supplier knows just what he has to deliver. </seg>
<seg id="4418"> If you are supplying power cords for desktops, you can see minute by minute how many power cords you are going to have to deliver.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4419"> Every two hours, the Dell factory in Penang sends an e-mail to the various SLCs nearby, telling each one what parts and what quantities of those parts it wants delivered within the next ninety minutes-and not one minute later. </seg>
<seg id="4420"> Within ninety minutes, trucks from the various SLCs around Penang pull up to the Dell manufacturing plant and unload the parts needed for all those notebooks ordered in the last two hours. </seg>
<seg id="4421"> This goes on all day, every two hours. </seg>
<seg id="4422"> As soon as those parts arrive at the factory, it takes thirty minutes for Dell employees to unload the parts, register their bar codes, and put them into the bins for assembly. </seg>
<seg id="4423"> ˇ°We know where every part in every SLC is in the Dell system at all times,ˇ± said Hunter. </seg>
<seg id="4424"> So where did the parts for my notebook come from? I asked Hunter. </seg>
<seg id="4425"> To begin with, he said, the notebook was codesigned in Austin, Texas, and in Taiwan by a team of Dell engineers and a team of Taiwanese notebook designers. </seg>
<seg id="4426"> ˇ°The customer's needs, required technologies, and Dell's design innovations were all determined by Dell through our direct relationship with customers,ˇ± he explained. ˇ°The basic design of the motherboard and case-the basic functionality of your machine-was designed to those specifications by an ODM [original design manufacturer] in Taiwan. </seg>
<seg id="4427"> We put our engineers in their facilities and they come to Austin and we actually codesign these systems. </seg>
<seg id="4428"> This global teamwork brings an added benefit-a globally distributed virtually twenty-four-hour-per-day development cycle. </seg>
<seg id="4429"> Our partners do the basic electronics and we help them design customer and reliability features that we know our customers want. </seg>
<seg id="4430"> We know the customers better than our suppliers and our competition, because we are dealing directly with them every day.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4431"> Dell notebooks are completely redesigned roughly every twelve months, but new features are constantly added during the year- through the supply chain-as the hardware and software components advance. </seg>
<seg id="4432"> It happened that when my notebook order hit the Dell factory in Penang, one part was not available-the wireless card-due to a quality control issue, so the assembly of the notebook was delayed for a few days. </seg>
<seg id="4433"> Then the truck full of good wireless cards arrived. </seg>
<seg id="4434"> On April 13, at 10:15 a.m., a Dell Malaysia worker pulled the order slip that automatically popped up once all my parts had arrived from the SLCs to the Penang factory. </seg>
<seg id="4435"> Another Dell Malaysia employee then took out a ˇ°travelerˇ±-a special carrying tote designed to hold and protect parts-and started plucking all the parts that went into my notebook. </seg>
<seg id="4436"> Where did those parts come from? </seg>
<seg id="4437"> Dell uses multiple suppliers for most of the thirty key components that go into its notebooks. </seg>
<seg id="4438"> That way if one supplier breaks down or cannot meet a surge in demand, Dell is not left in the lurch. </seg>
<seg id="4439"> So here are the key suppliers for my Inspiron 600m notebook: The Intel microprocessor came from an Intel factory either in the Philippines, Costa Rica, Malaysia, or China. </seg>
<seg id="4440"> The memory came from a Korean-owned factory in Korea (Samsung), a Taiwanese-owned factory in Taiwan (Nanya), a German-owned factory in Germany (Infineon), or a Japanese-owned factory in Japan (Elpida). </seg>
<seg id="4441"> My graphics card was shipped from either a Taiwanese-owned factory in China (MSI) or a Chinese-run factory in China (Foxconn). </seg>
<seg id="4442"> The cooling fan came from a Taiwanese-owned factory in Taiwan (CCI or Auras). </seg>
<seg id="4443"> The motherboard came from either a Korean-owned factory in Shanghai (Samsung), a Taiwanese-owned factory in Shanghai (Quanta), or a Taiwanese-owned factory in Taiwan (Compal or Wistron). </seg>
<seg id="4444"> The keyboard came from either a Japanese-owned company in Tianjin, China (Alps), a Taiwanese-owned factory in Shenzen, China (Sunrex), or a Taiwanese-owned factory in Suzhou, China (Darfon). </seg>
<seg id="4445"> The LCD display was made in either South Korea (Samsung or LG.Philips LCD), Japan (Toshiba or Sharp), or Taiwan (Chi Mei Optoelectronics, Hannstar Display, or AU Optronics). </seg>
<seg id="4446"> The wireless card came from either an American-owned factory in China (Agere) or Malaysia (Arrow), or a Taiwanese-owned factory in Taiwan (Askey or Gemtek) or China (USI). </seg>
<seg id="4447"> The modem was made by either a Taiwanese-owned company in China (Asustek or Liteon) or a Chinese-run company in China (Foxconn). </seg>
<seg id="4448"> The battery came from an American-owned factory in Malaysia (Motorola), a Japanese-owned factory in Mexico or Malaysia or China (Sanyo), or a South Korean or Taiwanese factory in either of those two countries (SDI or Simplo). </seg>
<seg id="4449"> The hard disk drive was made by an American-owned factory in Singapore (Seagate), a Japanese-owned company in Thailand (Hitachi or Fujitsu), or a Japanese-owned factory in the Philippines (Toshiba). </seg>
<seg id="4450"> The CD/DVD drive came from a South Korean-owned company with factories in Indonesia and the Philippines (Samsung); a Japanese-owned factory in China or Malaysia (NEC); a Japanese-owned factory in Indonesia, China, or Malaysia (Teac); or a Japanese-owned factory in China (Sony). </seg>
<seg id="4451"> The notebook carrying bag was made by either an Irish-owned company in China (Tenba) or an American-owned company in China (Targus, Samsonite, or Pacific Design). </seg>
<seg id="4452"> The power adapter was made by either a Thai-owned factory in Thailand (Delta) or a Taiwanese, Korean, or American-owned factory in China (Liteon, Samsung, or Mobility). </seg>
<seg id="4453"> The power cord was made by a British-owned company with factories in China, Malaysia, and India (Volex). </seg>
<seg id="4454"> The removable memory stick was made by either an Israeli-owned company in Israel (M-System) or an American-owned company with a factory in Malaysia (Smart Modular). </seg>
<seg id="4455"> This supply chain symphony-from my order over the phone to production to delivery to my house-is one of the wonders of the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4456"> ˇ°We have to do a lot of collaborating,ˇ± said Hunter. ˇ°Michael [Dell] personally knows the CEOs of these companies, and we are constantly working with them on process improvements and real-time demand/supply balancing.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4457"> Demand shaping goes on constantly, said Hunter. </seg>
<seg id="4458"> What is ˇ°demand shapingˇ±? </seg>
<seg id="4459"> It works like this: At 10 a.m. Austin time, Dell discovers that so many customers have ordered notebooks with 40-gigabyte hard drives since the morning that its supply chain will run short in two hours. </seg>
<seg id="4460"> That signal is automatically relayed to Dell's marketing department and to Dell.com and to all the Dell phone operators taking orders. </seg>
<seg id="4461"> If you happen to call to place your Dell order at 10:30 a.m., the Dell representative will say to you, ˇ°Tom, it's your lucky day! </seg>
<seg id="4462"> For the next hour we are offering 60-gigabyte hard drives with the notebook you want-for only $10 more than the 40-gig drive. </seg>
<seg id="4463"> And if you act now, Dell will throw in a carrying case along with your purchase, because we so value you as a customer.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4464"> In an hour or two, using such promotions, Dell can reshape the demand for any part of any notebook or desktop to correspond with the projected supply in its global supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4465"> Today memory might be on sale, tomorrow it might be CD-ROMs. </seg>
<seg id="4466"> Picking up the story of my notebook, on April 13, at 11:29 a.m., all the parts had been plucked from the just-in-time inventory bins in Penang, and the computer was assembled there by A. Sathini, a team member ˇ°who manually screwed together all of the parts from kitting as well as the labels needed for Tom's system,ˇ± said Dell in their production report to me. </seg>
<seg id="4467"> ˇ°The system was then sent down the conveyor to go to burn, where Tom's specified software was downloaded.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4468"> Dell has huge server banks stocked with the latest in Microsoft, Norton Utilities, and other popular software applications, which are downloaded into each new computer according to the specific tastes of the customer. </seg>
<seg id="4469"> ˇ°By 2:45 p.m., Tom's software had been successfully downloaded, and [was] manually moved to the boxing line. By 4:05 p.m., Tom's system [was] placed in protective foam and a shuttle box, with a label, which contains his order number, tracking code, system type, and shipping code. </seg>
<seg id="4470"> By 6:04 p.m., Tom's system had been loaded on a pallet with a specified manifest, which gives the Merge facility visibility to when the system will arrive, what pallet it will be on (out of 75+ pallets with 152 systems per pallet), and to what address Tom's system will ship. By 6:26 p.m., Tom's system left [the Dell factory] to head to the Penang, Malaysia, airport.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4471"> Six days a week Dell charters a China Airlines 747 out of Taiwan and flies it from Penang to Nashville via Taipei. </seg>
<seg id="4472"> Each 747 leaves with twenty-five thousand Dell notebooks that weigh altogether 110,000 kilograms, or 50,000 pounds. </seg>
<seg id="4473"> It is the only 747 that ever lands in Nashville, except Air Force One, when the president visits. ˇ°By April 15, 2004, at 7:41 a.m., Tom's system arrived at [Nashville] with other Dell systems from Penang and Limerick. </seg>
<seg id="4474"> By 11:58 a.m., Tom's system [was] inserted into a larger box, which went down the boxing line to the specific external parts that Tom had ordered.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4475"> That was thirteen days after I'd ordered it. </seg>
<seg id="4476"> Had there not been a parts delay in Malaysia when my order first arrived, the time between when I phoned in my purchase, when the notebook was assembled in Penang, and its arrival in Nashville would have been only four days. </seg>
<seg id="4477"> Hunter said the total supply chain for my computer, including suppliers of suppliers, involved about four hundred companies in North America, Europe, and primarily Asia, but with thirty key players. </seg>
<seg id="4478"> As Dell reported: On April 15, 2004, at 12:59 p.m., ˇ°Tom's system had been shipped from [Nashville] and was tenured by UPS shipping LTL (3-5-day ground, specified by Tom), with UPS tracking number 1Z13WA374253514697. </seg>
<seg id="4479"> By April 19, 2004, at 6:41 p.m., Tom's system arrived in Bethesda, MD, and was signed for.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4480"> I am telling you the story of my notebook to tell a larger story of geopolitics in the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4481"> To all the forces mentioned in the previous chapter that are still holding back the flattening of the world, or could actually reverse the process, one has to add a more traditional threat, and that is an outbreak of a good, old-fashioned, world-shaking, economy-destroying war. </seg>
<seg id="4482"> It could be China deciding once and for all to eliminate Taiwan as an independent state; </seg>
<seg id="4483"> or North Korea, out of fear or insanity, using one of its nuclear weapons against South Korea or Japan; or Israel and a soon-to-be-nuclear Iran going at each other; </seg>
<seg id="4484"> or India and Pakistan finally nuking it out. </seg>
<seg id="4485"> These and other classic geopolitical conflicts could erupt at any time and either slow the flattening of the world or seriously unflatten it. </seg>
<seg id="4486"> The real subject of this chapter is how these classic geopolitical threats might be moderated or influenced by the new forms of collaboration fostered and demanded by the flat world-particularly supply-chaining. </seg>
<seg id="4487"> The flattening of the world is too young for us to draw any definitive conclusions. </seg>
<seg id="4488"> What is certain, though, is that as the world flattens, one of the most interesting dramas to watch in international relations will be the interplay between the traditional global threats and the newly emergent global supply chains. </seg>
<seg id="4489"> The interaction between old-time threats (like China versus Taiwan) and just-in-time supply chains (like China plus Taiwan) will be a rich source of study for the field of international relations in the early twenty-first century. </seg>
<seg id="4490"> In The Lexus and the Olive Tree I argued that to the extent that countries tied their economies and futures to global integration and trade, it would act as a restraint on going to war with their neighbors. </seg>
<seg id="4491"> I first started thinking about this in the late 1990s, when, during my travels, I noticed that no two countries that both had McDonald's had ever fought a war against each other since each got its McDonald's. </seg>
<seg id="4492"> (Border skirmishes and civil wars don't count, because McDonald's usually served both sides.) </seg>
<seg id="4493"> After confirming this with McDonald's, I offered what I called the Golden Arches Theory of Conflict Prevention. </seg>
<seg id="4494"> The Golden Arches Theory stipulated that when a country reached the level of economic development where it had a middle class big enough to support a network of McDonald's, it became a McDonald's country. </seg>
<seg id="4495"> And people in McDonald's countries didn't like to fight wars anymore. </seg>
<seg id="4496"> They preferred to wait in line for burgers. </seg>
<seg id="4497"> While this was offered slightly tongue in cheek, the serious point I was trying to make was that as countries got woven into the fabric of global trade and rising living standards, which having a network of McDonald's franchises had come to symbolize, the cost of war for victor and vanquished became prohibitively high. </seg>
<seg id="4498"> This McDonald's theory has held up pretty well, but now that almost every country has acquired a McDonald's, except the worst rogues like North Korea, Iran, and Iraq under Saddam Hussein, it seemed to me that this theory needed updating for the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="4499"> In that spirit, and again with tongue slightly in cheek, I offer the Dell Theory of Conflict Prevention, the essence of which is that the advent and spread of just-in-time global supply chains in the flat world are an even greater restraint on geopolitical adventurism than the more general rising standard of living that McDonald's symbolized. </seg>
<seg id="4500"> The Dell Theory stipulates: No two countries that are both part of a major global supply chain, like Dell's, will ever fight a war against each other as long as they are both part of the same global supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4501"> Because people embedded in major global supply chains don't want to fight old-time wars anymore. </seg>
<seg id="4502"> They want to make just-in-time deliveries of goods and services -and enjoy the rising standards of living that come with that. </seg>
<seg id="4503"> One of the people with the best feel for the logic behind this theory is Michael Dell, the founder and chairman of Dell. </seg>
<seg id="4504"> ˇ°These countries understand the risk premium that they have,ˇ± said Dell of the countries in his Asian supply chain. ˇ°They are pretty careful to protect the equity that they have built up or tell us why we should not worry [about their doing anything adventurous]. </seg>
<seg id="4505"> My belief after visiting China is that the change that has occurred there is in the best interest of the world and China. </seg>
<seg id="4506"> Once people get a taste for whatever you want to call it-economic independence, a better lifestyle, and a better life for their child or children-they grab on to that and don't want to give it up.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4507"> Any sort of war or prolonged political upheaval in East Asia or China ˇ°would have a massive chilling effect on the investment there and on all the progress that has been made there,ˇ± said Dell, who added that he believes the governments in that part of the world understand this very clearly. </seg>
<seg id="4508"> ˇ°We certainly make clear to them that stability is important to us. </seg>
<seg id="4509"> [Right now] it is not a day-to-day worry for us... </seg>
<seg id="4510"> I believe that as time and progress go on there, the chance for a really disruptive event goes down exponentially. </seg>
<seg id="4511"> I don't think our industry gets enough credit for the good we are doing in these areas. </seg>
<seg id="4512"> If you are making money and being productive and raising your standard of living, you're not sitting around thinking, Who did this to us? </seg>
<seg id="4513"> or Why is our life so bad?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4514"> Countries whose workers and industries are woven into a major global supply chain know that they cannot take an hour, a week, or a month off for war without disrupting industries and economies around the world and thereby risking the loss of their place in that supply chain for a long time, which could be extremely costly. </seg>
<seg id="4515"> For a country with no natural resources, being part of a global supply chain is like striking oil-oil that never runs out. </seg>
<seg id="4516"> And therefore, getting dropped from such a chain because you start a war is like having your oil wells go dry or having someone pour cement down them. </seg>
<seg id="4517"> They will not come back anytime soon. </seg>
<seg id="4518"> ˇ°You are going to pay for it really dearly,ˇ± said Glenn E. Neland, senior vice president for worldwide procurement at Dell, when I asked him what would happen to a major supply-chain member in Asia that decided to start fighting with its neighbor and disrupt the supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4519"> ˇ°It will not only bring you to your knees [today], but you will pay for a long time-because you just won't have any credibility if you demonstrate you are going to go [off] the political deep end. </seg>
<seg id="4520"> And China is just now starting to develop a level of credibility in the business community that it is creating a business environment you can prosper in-with transparent and consistent rules.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4521"> Neland said that suppliers regularly ask him whether he is worried about China and Taiwan, which have threatened to go to war at several points in the past half century, but his standard response is that he cannot imagine them ˇ°doing anything more than flexing muscles with each other.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4522"> Neland said he can tell in his conversations and dealings with companies and governments in the Dell supply chain, particularly the Chinese, that ˇ°they recognize the opportunity and are really hungry to participate in the same things they have seen other countries in Asia do. </seg>
<seg id="4523"> They know there is a big economic pot at the end of the rainbow and they are really after it. </seg>
<seg id="4524"> We will spend about $35 billion producing parts this year, and 30 percent of that is [in] China.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4525"> If you follow the evolution of supply chains, added Neland, you see the prosperity and stability they promoted first in Japan, and then in Korea and Taiwan, and now in Malaysia, Singapore, the Philippines, Thailand, and Indonesia. </seg>
<seg id="4526"> Once countries get embedded in these global supply chains, ˇ°they feel part of something much bigger than their own businesses,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="4527"> Osamu Watanabe, the CEO of the Japan External Trade Organization (JETRO), was explaining to me one afternoon in Tokyo how Japanese companies were moving vast amounts of low- and middle-range technical work and manufacturing to China, doing the basic fabrication there, and then bringing it back to Japan for final assembly. </seg>
<seg id="4528"> Japan was doing this despite a bitter legacy of mistrust between the two countries, which was intensified by the Japanese invasion of China in the last century. </seg>
<seg id="4529"> Historically, he noted, a strong Japan and a strong China have had a hard time coexisting. </seg>
<seg id="4530"> But not today, at least not for the moment. </seg>
<seg id="4531"> The reason you can have a strong Japan and a strong China at the same time, he said, ˇ°is because of the supply chain.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4532"> It is a win-win for both. </seg>
<seg id="4533"> Obviously, since Iraq, Syria, south Lebanon, North Korea, Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Iran are not part of any major global supply chains, all of them remain hot spots that could explode at any time and slow or reverse the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4534"> As my own notebook story attests, the most important test case of the Dell Theory of Conflict Prevention is the situation between China and Taiwan-since both are deeply embedded in several of the world's most important computer, consumer electronics, and, increasingly, software supply chains. </seg>
<seg id="4535"> The vast majority of computer components for every major company comes from coastal China, Taiwan, and East Asia. </seg>
<seg id="4536"> In addition, Taiwan alone has more than $100 billion in investments in mainland China today, and Taiwanese experts run many of the cutting-edge Chinese high-tech manufacturing companies. </seg>
<seg id="4537"> It is no wonder that Craig Addison, the former editor of Electronic Business Asia magazine, wrote an essay for the International Herald Tribune (September 29, 2000), headlined ˇ°A 'Silicon Shield' Protects Taiwan from China.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4538"> He argued that ˇ°Silicon-based products, such as computers and networking systems, form the basis of the digital economies in the United States, Japan and other developed nations. </seg>
<seg id="4539"> In the past decade, Taiwan has become the third-largest information technology hardware producer after the United States and Japan. </seg>
<seg id="4540"> Military aggression by China against Taiwan would cut off a large portion of the world's supply of these products... </seg>
<seg id="4541"> Such a development would wipe trillions of dollars off the market value of technology companies listed in the United States, Japan and Europe.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4542"> The Dell Theory had its first real test in December 2004, when Taiwan held parliamentary elections. </seg>
<seg id="4543"> President Chen Shui-bian's pro-independence Democratic Progressive Party was expected to win the legislative runoff over the main opposition Nationalist Party, which favored closer ties with Beijing. </seg>
<seg id="4544"> Had Chen won and moved ahead on his agenda to make Taiwan its own motherland, as opposed to maintaining the status quo fiction that it is a province of the mainland, it could have led to a Chinese military assault on Taiwan. </seg>
<seg id="4545"> Everyone in the region was holding his or her breath. </seg>
<seg id="4546"> And what happened? </seg>
<seg id="4547"> Motherboards won over motherland. </seg>
<seg id="4548"> A majority of Taiwanese voted against the pro-independence governing party legislative candidates, ensuring that the DPP would not have a majority in parliament. </seg>
