<seg id="4549"> The voters seemed to understand clearly how interwoven they had become with the mainland, and they wisely opted to maintain their de facto independence rather than force de jure independence, which might have triggered a Chinese invasion and a very uncertain future. </seg>
<seg id="4550"> Warning: What I said when I put forth the McDonald's theory, I would repeat even more strenuously with the Dell Theory: It does not make wars obsolete. </seg>
<seg id="4551"> And it does not guarantee that governments will not engage in wars of choice, even governments that are part of major supply chains. </seg>
<seg id="4552"> To suggest so would be naive. </seg>
<seg id="4553"> It guarantees only that governments whose countries are enmeshed in global supply chains will have to think three times, not just twice, about engaging in anything but a war of self-defense. </seg>
<seg id="4554"> And if they choose to go to war anyway, the price they will pay will be ten times higher than it was a decade ago and probably ten times higher than whatever the leaders of that country think. </seg>
<seg id="4555"> It is one thing to lose your McDonald's. It's quite another to fight a war that costs you your place in a twenty-first-century supply chain that may not come back around for a long time. </seg>
<seg id="4556"> While the biggest test case of the Dell Theory is China versus Taiwan, the fact is that the Dell Theory has already proved itself to some degree in the case of India and Pakistan, the context in which I first started to think about it. </seg>
<seg id="4557"> I happened to be in India in 2002, when its just-in-time services supply chains ran into some very old-time geopolitics-and the supply chain won. </seg>
<seg id="4558"> In the case of India and Pakistan, the Dell Theory was working on only one party-India-but it still had a major impact. </seg>
<seg id="4559"> India is to the world's knowledge and service supply chain what China and Taiwan are to the manufacturing ones. </seg>
<seg id="4560"> By now readers of this book know all the highlights: General Electric's biggest research center outside the United States is in Bangalore, with seventeen hundred Indian engineers, designers, and scientists. </seg>
<seg id="4561"> The brain chips for many brand-name cell phones are designed in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="4562"> Renting a car from Avis online? </seg>
<seg id="4563"> It's managed in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="4564"> Tracing your lost luggage on Delta or British Airways is done from Bangalore, and the backroom accounting and computer maintenance for scores of global firms are done from Bangalore, Mumbai, Chennai, and other major Indian cities. </seg>
<seg id="4565"> Here's what happened: On May 31, 2002, State Department spokesman Richard Boucher issued a travel advisory saying, ˇ°We urge American citizens currently in India to depart the country,ˇ± because the prospect of a nuclear exchange with Pakistan was becoming very real. </seg>
<seg id="4566"> Both nations were massing troops on their borders, intelligence reports were suggesting that they both might be dusting off their nuclear warheads, and CNN was flashing images of people flooding out of India. </seg>
<seg id="4567"> The global American firms that had moved their back rooms and R &amp; D operations to Bangalore were deeply unnerved. </seg>
<seg id="4568"> ˇ°I was actually surfing on the Web, and I saw a travel advisory come up on India on a Friday evening/' said Vivek Paul, president of Wipro, which manages backroom operations from India of many American multinationals. ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4569"> As soon as I saw that, I said, 'Oh my gosh, every customer that we have is going to have a million questions on this.' </seg>
<seg id="4570"> It was the Friday before a long weekend, so over the weekend we at Wipro developed a fail-safe business continuity plan for all of our customers. </seg>
<seg id="4571"> ˇ° While Wipro's customers were pleased to see how on top of things the company was, many of them were nevertheless rattled. </seg>
<seg id="4572"> This was not in the plan when they decided to outsource mission-critical research and operations to India. </seg>
<seg id="4573"> Said Paul, ˇ±I had a CIO from one of our big American clients send me an e-mail saying, I am now spending a lot of time looking for alternative sources to India. </seg>
<seg id="4574"> I don't think you want me doing that, and I don't want to be doing it.' </seg>
<seg id="4575"> I immediately forwarded his message to the Indian ambassador in Washington and told him to get it to the right person. </seg>
<seg id="4576"> ˇ° Paul would not tell me what company it was, but I have confirmed through diplomatic sources that it was United Technologies. </seg>
<seg id="4577"> And plenty of others, like American Express and General Electric, with back rooms in Bangalore, had to have been equally worried. </seg>
<seg id="4578"> For many global companies, ˇ°the main heart of their business is now supported here,ˇ± said N. Krishnakumar, president of MindTree, another leading Indian knowledge outsourcing firm based in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="4579"> ˇ°It can cause chaos if there is a disruption.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4580"> While not trying to meddle in foreign affairs, he added, ˇ°What we explained to our government, through the Confederation of Indian Industry, is that providing a stable, predictable operating environment is now the key to India's development.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4581"> This was a real education for India's elderly leaders in New Delhi, who had not fully absorbed how critical India had become to the world's knowledge supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4582"> When you are managing vital backroom operations for American Express or General Electric or Avis, or are responsible for tracing all the lost luggage on British Airways or Delta, you cannot take a month, a week, or even a day off for war without causing major disruptions for those companies. </seg>
<seg id="4583"> Once those companies have made a commitment to outsource business operations or research to India, they expect it to stay there. </seg>
<seg id="4584"> That is a major commitment. </seg>
<seg id="4585"> And if geopolitics causes a serious disruption, they will leave, and they will not come back very easily. </seg>
<seg id="4586"> When you lose this kind of service trade, you can lose it for good. </seg>
<seg id="4587"> ˇ°What ends up happening in the flat world you described,ˇ± explained Paul, ˇ°is that you have only one opportunity to make it right if something [goes] wrong. </seg>
<seg id="4588"> Because the disadvantage of being in a flat world is that despite all the nice engagements and stuff and the exit barriers that you have, every customer has multiple options, and so the sense of responsibility you have is not just out of a desire to do good by your customers, but also a desire for self-preservation.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4589"> The Indian government got the message. </seg>
<seg id="4590"> Was India's central place in the world's services supply chain the only factor in getting Prime Minister Vajpayee to tone down his rhetoric and step back from the brink? </seg>
<seg id="4591"> Of course not. </seg>
<seg id="4592"> There were other factors, to be sure-most notably the deterrent effect of Pakistan's own nuclear arsenal. </seg>
<seg id="4593"> But clearly, India's role in global services was an important additional source of restraint on its behavior, and it was taken into account by New Delhi. </seg>
<seg id="4594"> ˇ°I think it sobered a lot of people,ˇ± said Jerry Rao, who, as noted earlier, heads the Indian high-tech trade association. </seg>
<seg id="4595"> ˇ°We engaged very seriously, and we tried to make the point that this was very bad for Indian business. </seg>
<seg id="4596"> It was very bad for the Indian economy... </seg>
<seg id="4597"> [Many people] didn't realize till then how suddenly we had become integrated into the rest of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4598"> We are now partners in a twenty-four by seven by three-sixty-five supply chain.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4599"> Vivek Kulkami, then information technology secretary for Bangalore's regional government, told me back in 2002, ˇ°We don't get involved in politics, but we did bring to the government's attention the problems the Indian IT industry might face if there were a war.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4600"> And this was an altogether new factor for New Delhi to take into consideration. </seg>
<seg id="4601"> ˇ°Ten years ago, [a lobby of IT ministers from different Indian states] never existed,ˇ± said Kulkarni. </seg>
<seg id="4602"> Now it is one of the most important business lobbies in India and a coalition that no Indian government can ignore. </seg>
<seg id="4603"> ˇ°With all due respect, the McDonald's [shutting] down doesn't hurt anything,ˇ± said Vivek Paul, ˇ°but if Wipro had to shut down we would affect the day-to-day operations of many, many companies.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4604"> No one would answer the phones in call centers. </seg>
<seg id="4605"> Many e-commerce sites that are supported from Bangalore would shut down. </seg>
<seg id="4606"> Many major companies that rely on India to maintain their key computer applications or handle their human resources departments or billings would seize up. </seg>
<seg id="4607"> And these companies did not want to find alternatives, said Paul. </seg>
<seg id="4608"> Switching is very difficult, because taking over mission-critical day-to-day backroom operations of a global company takes a great deal of training and experience. </seg>
<seg id="4609"> It's not like opening a fast-food restaurant. </seg>
<seg id="4610"> That was why, said Paul, Wipro's clients were telling him, ˇ°'I have made an investment in you. </seg>
<seg id="4611"> I need you to be very responsible with the trust I have reposed in you.' </seg>
<seg id="4612"> And I think that created an enormous amount of back pressure on us that said we have to act in a responsible fashion... </seg>
<seg id="4613"> All of a sudden it became even clearer that there's more to gain by economic gains than by geopolitical gains. </seg>
<seg id="4614"> [We had more to gain from building] a vibrant, richer middle class able to create an export industry than we possibly could by having an ego-satisfying war with Pakistan.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4615"> The Indian government also looked around and realized that the vast majority of India's billion people were saying, ˇ°I want a better future, not more territory.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4616"> Over and over again, when I asked young Indians working at call centers how they felt about Kashmir or a war with Pakistan, they waved me off with the same answer: ˇ°We have better things to do.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4617"> And they do. </seg>
<seg id="4618"> America needs to keep this in mind as it weighs its overall approach to outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="4619"> I would never advocate shipping some American's job overseas just so it will keep Indians and Pakistanis at peace with each other. </seg>
<seg id="4620"> But I would say that to the extent that this process happens, driven by its own internal economic logic, it will have a net positive geopolitical effect. </seg>
<seg id="4621"> It will absolutely make the world safer for American kids. </seg>
<seg id="4622"> Each of the Indian business leaders I interviewed noted that in the event of some outrageous act of terrorism or aggression from Pakistan, India would do whatever it takes to defend itself, and they would be the first to support that-the Dell Theory be damned. </seg>
<seg id="4623"> Sometimes war is unavoidable. </seg>
<seg id="4624"> It is imposed on you by the reckless behavior of others, and you have to just pay the price. </seg>
<seg id="4625"> But the more India and, one hopes, soon Pakistan get enmeshed in global service supply chains, the greater disincentive they have to fight anything but a border skirmish or a war of words. </seg>
<seg id="4626"> The example of the 2002 India-Pakistan nuclear crisis at least gives us some hope. </seg>
<seg id="4627"> That cease-fire was brought to us not by General Powell but by General Electric. </seg>
<seg id="4628"> We bring good things to life. </seg>
<seg id="4629"> Infosys Versus al-Qaeda </seg>
<seg id="4630"> Unfortunately, even GE can do only so much. </seg>
<seg id="4631"> Because, alas, a new source for geopolitical instability has emerged only in recent years, for which even the updated Dell Theory can provide no restraint. </seg>
<seg id="4632"> It is the emergence of mutant global supply chains -that is, nonstate actors, be they criminals or terrorists, who learn to use all the elements of the flat world to advance a highly destabilizing, even nihilistic agenda. </seg>
<seg id="4633"> I first started thinking about this when Nandan Nilekani, the Infosys CEO, was giving me that tour I referred to in Chapter 1 of his company's global videoconferencing center at its Bangalore headquarters. </seg>
<seg id="4634"> As Nandan explained to me how Infosys could get its global supply chain together at once for a virtual conference in that room, a thought popped into my head: Who else uses open-sourcing and supply-chaining so imaginatively? </seg>
<seg id="4635"> The answer, of course, is al-Qaeda. </seg>
<seg id="4636"> Al-Qaeda has learned to use many of the same instruments for global collaboration that Infosys uses, but instead of producing products and profits with them, it has produced mayhem and murder. </seg>
<seg id="4637"> This is a particularly difficult problem. </seg>
<seg id="4638"> In fact, it may be the most vexing geopolitical problem for flat-world countries that want to focus on the future. </seg>
<seg id="4639"> The flat world-unfortunately-is a friend of both Infosys and al-Qaeda. </seg>
<seg id="4640"> The Dell Theory will not work at all against these informal Islamo-Leninist terror networks, because they are not a state with a population that will hold its leaders accountable or with a domestic business lobby that might restrain them. </seg>
<seg id="4641"> These mutant global supply chains are formed for the purpose of destruction, not profit. </seg>
<seg id="4642"> They don't need investors, only recruits, donors, and victims. </seg>
<seg id="4643"> Yet these mobile, self-financing mutant supply chains use all the tools of collaboration offered by the flat world-open-sourcing to raise money, to recruit followers, and to stimulate and disseminate ideas; </seg>
<seg id="4644"> outsourcing to train recruits; </seg>
<seg id="4645"> and supply-chaining to distribute the tools and the suicide bombers to undertake operations. </seg>
<seg id="4646"> The U.S. Central Command has a name for this whole underground network: the Virtual Caliphate. </seg>
<seg id="4647"> And its leaders and innovators understand the flat world almost as well as Wal-Mart, Dell, and Infosys do. </seg>
<seg id="4648"> In the previous chapter, I tried to explain that you cannot understand the rise of al-Qaeda emotionally and politically without reference to the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4649"> What I am arguing here is that you cannot understand the rise of al-Qaeda technically without reference to the flattening of the world, either. </seg>
<seg id="4650"> Globalization in general has been al-Qaeda's friend in that it has helped to solidify a revival of Muslim identity and solidarity, with Muslims in one country much better able to see and sympathize with the struggles of their brethren in another country-thanks to the Internet and satellite television. </seg>
<seg id="4651"> The flattening of the world has also led to more urbanization and large-scale immigration to the West of many of these young, unemployed, frustrated Arab-Muslim males, while simultaneously making it much easier for informal open-source networks of these young men to form, operate, and interconnect. </seg>
<seg id="4652"> This certainly has been a boon for underground extremist Muslim political groups. </seg>
<seg id="4653"> Think about it: A century ago, anarchists were limited in their ability to communicate and collaborate with one another, to find sympathizers, and to band together for an operation. </seg>
<seg id="4654"> Today, with the Internet, that is not a problem. </seg>
<seg id="4655"> Today even the Unabomber could find friends to join a consortium where his ˇ°strengthsˇ± could be magnified and reinforced by others who had just as warped a worldview as he did. </seg>
<seg id="4656"> What we have witnessed in Iraq is an even more perverse mutation of this mutant supply chain-the suicide supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4657"> Since the start of the U.S. invasion in March 2002, more than two hundred suicide bombers have been recruited from within Iraq and from across the Muslim world, brought to the Iraqi front by some underground railroad, connected with the bomb makers there, and then dispatched against U.S. and Iraqi targets according to whatever suits the daily tactical needs of the insurgent Islamist forces in Iraq. </seg>
<seg id="4658"> I can understand, but not accept, the notion that more than thirty-seven years of Israeli occupation of the West Bank might have driven some Palestinians into a suicidal rage. </seg>
<seg id="4659"> But the American occupation of Iraq was only a few months old before it started to get hit by this suicide supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="4660"> How do you recruit so many young men ˇ°off the shelfˇ± who are ready to commit suicide in the cause of jihad, many of them apparently not even Iraqis? </seg>
<seg id="4661"> And they don't even identify themselves by name or want to get credit-at least in this world. </seg>
<seg id="4662"> The fact is that Western intelligence agencies have no clue how this underground suicide supply chain, which seems to have an infinite pool of recruits to draw on, works, and yet it has basically stymied the U.S. armed forces in Iraq. </seg>
<seg id="4663"> From what we do know, though, this Virtual Caliphate works just like the supply chains I described earlier. </seg>
<seg id="4664"> Just as you take an item off the shelf in a discount store in Birmingham and another one is immediately made in Beijing, so the retailers of suicide deploy a human bomber in Baghdad and another one is immediately recruited and indoctrinated in Beirut. </seg>
<seg id="4665"> To the extent that this tactic spreads, it will require a major rethinking of U.S. military doctrine. </seg>
<seg id="4666"> The flat world has also been such a huge boon for al-Qaeda and its ilk because of the way it enables the small to act big, and the way it enables small acts-the killing of just a few people-to have big effects. </seg>
<seg id="4667"> The horrific video of the beheading of Wall Street Journal reporter Danny Pearl by Islamist militants in Pakistan was transmitted by the Internet all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="4668"> There is not a journalist anywhere who saw or even just read about that who was not terrified. </seg>
<seg id="4669"> But those same beheading videos are also used as tools of recruitment. </seg>
<seg id="4670"> The flat world makes it much easier for terrorists to transmit their terror. </seg>
<seg id="4671"> With the Internet they don't even have to go through Western or Arab news organizations but can broadcast right into your computer. </seg>
<seg id="4672"> It takes much less dynamite to transmit so much more anxiety. </seg>
<seg id="4673"> Just as the U.S. Army had embedded journalists, so the suicide supply chain has embedded terrorists, in their own way, to tell us their side of the story. </seg>
<seg id="4674"> How many times have I gotten up in the morning, fired up the Internet, and been confronted by the video image of some masked gunman threatening to behead an American-all brought to me courtesy of AOL's home page? </seg>
<seg id="4675"> The Internet is an enormously useful tool for the dissemination of propaganda, conspiracy theories, and plain old untruths, because it combines a huge reach with a patina of technology that makes anything on the Internet somehow more believable. </seg>
<seg id="4676"> How many times have you heard someone say, ˇ°But I read it on the Internet,ˇ± as if that should end the argument? </seg>
<seg id="4677"> In fact, the Internet can make things worse. </seg>
<seg id="4678"> It often leads to more people being exposed to crazy conspiracy theories. </seg>
<seg id="4679"> ˇ°The new system of diffusion-the Internet-is more likely to transmit irrationality than rationality,ˇ± said political theorist Yaron Ezrahi, who specializes in the interaction between media and politics. </seg>
<seg id="4680"> ˇ°Because irrationality is more emotionally loaded, it requires less knowledge, it explains more to more people, it goes down easier.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4681"> That is why conspiracy theories are so rife in the Arab-Muslim world today-and unfortunately are becoming so in many quarters of the Western world, for that matter. </seg>
<seg id="4682"> Conspiracy theories are like a drug that goes right into your bloodstream, enabling you to see ˇ°the Light.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4683"> And the Internet is the needle. </seg>
<seg id="4684"> Young people used to have to take LSD to escape. </seg>
<seg id="4685"> Now they just go online. </seg>
<seg id="4686"> Now you don't shoot up, you download. </seg>
<seg id="4687"> You download the precise point of view that speaks to all your own biases. </seg>
<seg id="4688"> And the flat world makes it all so much easier. </seg>
<seg id="4689"> Gabriel Weimann, a professor of communication at Haifa University, Israel, did an incisive study of terrorists' use of the Internet and of what I call the flat world, which was published in March 2004 by the United States Institute of Peace and excerpted on YaleGlobal Online on April 26, 2004. </seg>
<seg id="4690"> He made the following points:While the danger that cyber-terrorism poses to the Internet is frequently debated, surprisingly little is known about the threat posed by terrorists' use of the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="4691"> A recent six-year-long study shows that terrorist organizations and their supporters have been using all of the tools that the Internet offers to recruit supporters, raise funds, and launch a worldwide campaign of fear. </seg>
<seg id="4692"> It is also clear that to combat terrorism effectively, mere suppression of their Internet tools is not enough. </seg>
<seg id="4693"> Our scan of the Internet in 2003-04 revealed the existence of hundreds of websites serving terrorists in different, albeit sometimes overlapping, ways... </seg>
<seg id="4694"> There are countless examples of how [terrorists] use this uncensored medium to spread disinformation, to deliver threats intended to instill fear and helplessness, and to disseminate horrific images of recent actions. </seg>
<seg id="4695"> Since September 11, 2001, al-Qaeda has festooned its websites with a string of announcements of an impending ˇ°large attackˇ± on US targets. </seg>
<seg id="4696"> These warnings have received considerable media coverage, which has helped to generate a widespread sense of dread and insecurity among audiences throughout the world and especially within the United States... </seg>
<seg id="4697"> The Internet has significantly expanded the opportunities for terrorists to secure publicity. </seg>
<seg id="4698"> Until the advent of the Internet, terrorists' hopes of winning publicity for their causes and activities depended on attracting the attention of television, radio, or the print media. </seg>
<seg id="4699"> The fact that terrorists themselves have direct control over the content of their websites offers further opportunities to shape how they are perceived by different target audiences and to manipulate their image and the images of their enemies. </seg>
<seg id="4700"> Most terrorist sites do not celebrate their violent activities. </seg>
<seg id="4701"> Instead- regardless of their nature, motives, or location-most terrorist sites emphasize two issues: the restrictions placed on freedom of expression; and the plight of their comrades who are now political prisoners. </seg>
<seg id="4702"> These issues resonate powerfully with their own supporters and are also calculated to elicit sympathy from Western audiences that cherish freedom of expression and frown on measures to silence political opposition... </seg>
<seg id="4703"> Terrorists have proven not only skillful at online marketing but also adept at mining the data offered by the billion-some pages of the World Wide Web. </seg>
<seg id="4704"> They can learn from the Internet about the schedules and locations of targets such as transportation facilities, nuclear power plants, public buildings, airports and ports, and even counterterrorism measures. </seg>
<seg id="4705"> According to Secretary of Defense Donald Rumsfeld, an al-Qaeda training manual recovered in Afghanistan tells its readers, ˇ°Using public sources openly and without resorting to illegal means, it is possible to gather at least 80 percent of all information required about the enemy.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4706"> One captured al-Qaeda computer contained engineering and structural architecture features of a dam, which had been downloaded from the Internet and which would enable al-Qaeda engineers and planners to simulate catastrophic failures. </seg>
<seg id="4707"> In other captured computers, U.S. investigators found evidence that al-Qaeda operators spent time on sites that offer software and programming instructions for the digital switches that run power, water, transportation, and communications grids. </seg>
<seg id="4708"> Like many other political organizations, terrorist groups use the Internet to raise funds. </seg>
<seg id="4709"> Al-Qaeda, for instance, has always depended heavily on donations, and its global fundraising network is built upon a foundation of charities, nongovernmental organizations, and other financial institutions that use websites and Internet-based chat rooms and forums. </seg>
<seg id="4710"> The fighters in the Russian breakaway republic of Chechnya have likewise used the Internet to publicize the numbers of bank accounts to which sympathizers can contribute. </seg>
<seg id="4711"> And in December 2001, the U.S. government seized the assets of a Texas-based charity because of its ties to Hamas. </seg>
<seg id="4712"> In addition to soliciting financial aid online, terrorists recruit converts by using the full panoply of website technologies (audio, digital video, etc.) to enhance the presentation of their message. </seg>
<seg id="4713"> And like commercial sites that track visitors to develop consumer profiles, terrorist organizations capture information about the users who browse their websites. </seg>
<seg id="4714"> Visitors who seem most interested in the organization's cause or well suited to carrying out its work are then contacted. </seg>
<seg id="4715"> Recruiters may also use more interactive Internet technology to roam online chat rooms and cyber cafes, looking for receptive members of the public, particularly young people. </seg>
<seg id="4716"> The SITE Institute, a Washington, D.C.-based terrorism research group that monitors al-Qaeda's Internet communications, has provided chilling details of a high-tech recruitment drive launched in 2003 to recruit fighters to travel to Iraq and attack U.S. and coalition forces there. </seg>
<seg id="4717"> The Internet also grants terrorists a cheap and efficient means of networking. </seg>
<seg id="4718"> Many terrorist groups, among them Hamas and al-Qaeda, have undergone a transformation from strictly hierarchical organizations with designated leaders to affiliations of semi-independent cells that have no single commanding hierarchy. </seg>
<seg id="4719"> Through the Internet, these loosely interconnected groups are able to maintain contact with one another-and with members of other terrorist groups. </seg>
<seg id="4720"> The Internet connects not only members of the same terrorist organizations but also members of different groups. </seg>
<seg id="4721"> For instance, dozens of sites supporting terrorism in the name of jihad permit terrorists in places as far-removed from one another as Chechnya and Malaysia to exchange ideas and practical information about how to build bombs, establish terror cells, and carry out attacks... </seg>
<seg id="4722"> Al-Qaeda operatives relied heavily on the Internet in planning and coordinating the September 11 attacks. </seg>
<seg id="4723"> For all of these reasons we are just at the beginning of understanding the geopolitical impact of the flattening of the world. </seg>
<seg id="4724"> On the one hand, failed states and failed regions are places we have every incentive to avoid today. </seg>
<seg id="4725"> They offer no economic opportunity and there is no Soviet Union out there competing with us for influence over such countries. </seg>
<seg id="4726"> On the other hand, there may be nothing more dangerous today than a failed state with broadband capability. </seg>
<seg id="4727"> That is, even failed states tend to have telecommunications systems and satellite links, and therefore if a terrorist group infiltrates a failed state, as al-Qaeda did with Afghanistan, it can amplify its power enormously. </seg>
<seg id="4728"> As much as big powers want to stay away from such states, they may feel compelled to get even more deeply embroiled in them. </seg>
<seg id="4729"> Think of America in Afghanistan and Iraq, Russia in Chechnya, Australia in East Timor. </seg>
<seg id="4730"> In the flat world it is much more difficult to hide, but much easier to get connected. </seg>
<seg id="4731"> Remarked Michael Mandelbaum, the Johns Hopkins foreign policy specialist. Bin Laden, by contrast, can't show his face, but he can reach every household in the world, thanks to the Internet.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4732"> Bin Laden cannot capture any territory but he can capture the imagination of millions of people. </seg>
<seg id="4733"> And he has, broadcasting right into American living rooms on the eve of the 2004 presidential election. </seg>
<seg id="4734"> Hell hath no fury like a terrorist with a satellite dish and an interactive Web site. </seg>
<seg id="4735"> Too Personally Insecure </seg>
<seg id="4736"> In the fall of 2004,1 was invited to speak at a synagogue in Woodstock, New York, home of the famous Woodstock music festival. </seg>
<seg id="4737"> I asked my hosts how was it that they were able to get a synagogue in Woodstock, of all places, big enough to support a lecture series. </seg>
<seg id="4738"> Very simple, they said. </seg>
<seg id="4739"> Since 9/11, Jews, and others, have been moving from New York City to places like Woodstock, to get away from what they fear will be the next ground zero. </seg>
<seg id="4740"> Right now this trend is a trickle, but it would become a torrent if a nuclear device were detonated in any European or American city. </seg>
<seg id="4741"> Since this threat is the mother of all unflatteners, this book would not be complete without a discussion of it. </seg>
<seg id="4742"> We can live with a lot. </seg>
<seg id="4743"> We lived through 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="4744"> But we cannot live with nuclear terrorism. </seg>
<seg id="4745"> That would un-flatten the world permanently. </seg>
<seg id="4746"> The only reason that Osama bin Laden did not use a nuclear device on 9/11 was not that he did not have the intention but that he did not have the capability. </seg>
<seg id="4747"> And since the Dell Theory offers no hope of restraining the suicide supply chains, the only strategy we have is to limit their worst capabilities. </seg>
<seg id="4748"> That means a much more serious global effort to stanch nuclear proliferation by limiting the supply-to buy up the fissile material that is already out there, particularly in the former Soviet Union, and prevent more states from going nuclear. </seg>
<seg id="4749"> Harvard University international affairs expert Graham Allison, in his book Nuclear Terrorism: The Ultimate Preventable Catastrophe, outlines just such a strategy for denying terrorists access to nuclear weapons and nuclear materials. </seg>
<seg id="4750"> It can be done, he insists. </seg>
<seg id="4751"> It is a challenge to our will and convictions, but not to our capabilities. </seg>
<seg id="4752"> Allison proposes a new American-led international security order to deal with this problem based on what he calls ˇ°a doctrine of the Three No's: No loose nukes, No new nascent nukes, and No new nuclear states.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4753"> No loose nukes, says Allison, means locking down all nuclear weapons and all nuclear material from which bombs could be made-in a much more serious way than we have done up till now. ˇ°We don't lose gold from Fort Knox,ˇ± says Allison. </seg>
<seg id="4754"> ˇ°Russia doesn't lose treasures from the Kremlin armory. </seg>
<seg id="4755"> So we both know how to prevent theft of those things that are super valuable to us if we are determined to do it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4756"> No new nascent nukes means recognizing that there is a group of actors out there who can and do produce highly enriched uranium or plutonium, which is nothing more than nuclear bombs just about to hatch. </seg>
<seg id="4757"> We need a much more credible, multilateral nonprolif-eration regime that soaks up this fissile material. </seg>
<seg id="4758"> Finally, no new nuclear states means ˇ°drawing a line under the current eight nuclear powers and determining that, however unfair and unreasonable it may be, that club will have no more members than those eight,ˇ± says Allison, adding that these three steps might then buy us time to develop a more formal, sustainable, internationally approved regime. </seg>
<seg id="4759"> It would be nice also to be able to deny the Internet to al-Qaeda and its ilk, but that, alas, is impossible-without undermining ourselves. </seg>
<seg id="4760"> That is why limiting their capabilities is necessary but not sufficient. </seg>
<seg id="4761"> We also have to find a way to get at their worst intentions. </seg>
<seg id="4762"> If we are not going to shut down the Internet and all the other creative and collaborative tools that have flattened the world, and if we can't restrict access to them, the only thing we can do is try to influence the imagination and intentions that people bring to them and draw from them. </seg>
<seg id="4763"> When I raised this issue, and the broad themes of this book, with my religious teacher, Rabbi Tzvi Marx from Holland, he surprised me by saying that the flat world I was describing reminded him of the story of the Tower of Babel. </seg>
<seg id="4764"> How so? </seg>
<seg id="4765"> I asked. </seg>
<seg id="4766"> ˇ°The reason God banished all the people from the Tower of Babel and made them all speak different languages was not because he did not want them to collaborate per se,ˇ± answered Rabbi Marx. </seg>
<seg id="4767"> ˇ°It was because he was enraged at what they were collaborating on-an effort to build a tower to the heavens so they could become God.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4768"> This was a distortion of the human capacity, so God broke their union and their ability to communicate with one another. </seg>
<seg id="4769"> Now, all these years later, humankind has again created a new platform for more people from more places to communicate and collaborate with less friction and more ease than ever: the Internet. </seg>
<seg id="4770"> Would God see the Internet as heresy? </seg>
<seg id="4771"> ˇ°Absolutely not,ˇ± said Marx. </seg>
<seg id="4772"> ˇ°The heresy is not that mankind works together-it is to what ends. </seg>
<seg id="4773"> It is essential that we use this new ability to communicate and collaborate for the right ends-for constructive human aims and not megalomaniacal ends. </seg>
<seg id="4774"> Building a tower was megalo-maniacal. </seg>
<seg id="4775"> Bin Laden's insistence that he has the truth and can flatten anyone else's tower who doesn't heed him is megalomaniacal. </seg>
<seg id="4776"> Collaborating so mankind can achieve its full potential is God's hope.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4777"> How we promote more of that kind of collaboration is what the final chapter is all about. </seg>
<seg id="4778"> CONCLUSION: IMAGINATION </seg>
<seg id="4779"> THIRTEEN 11/9 Versus 9/11 </seg>
<seg id="4780"> Imagination is more important than knowledge.ˇŞAlbert Einstein </seg>
<seg id="4781"> On the Internet, nobody knows you're a dog. </seg>
<seg id="4782"> ˇŞTwo dogs talking to each other, in a New Yorker cartoon by Peter Steiner, July 5, 1993 </seg>
<seg id="4783"> Reflecting on this past decade and a half, during which the world went flat, it strikes me that our lives have been powerfully shaped by two dates: 11/9 and 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="4784"> These two dates represent the two competing forms of imagination at work in the world today: the creative imagination of 11/9 and the destructive imagination of 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="4785"> One brought down a wall and opened the windows of the world-both the operating system and the kind we look through. </seg>
<seg id="4786"> It unlocked half the planet and made the citizens there our potential partners and competitors. </seg>
<seg id="4787"> Another brought down the World Trade Center, closing its Windows on the World restaurant forever and putting up new invisible and concrete walls among people at a time when we thought 11 The dismantling of the Berlin Wall on 11/9 was brought about by people who dared to imagine a different, more open world-one where every human being would be free to realize his or her full potentialˇŞand who then summoned the courage to act on that imagination. </seg>
<seg id="4788"> Do you remember how it happened? </seg>
<seg id="4789"> It was so simple, really: In July 1989, hundreds of East Germans sought refuge at the West German embassy in Hungary. </seg>
<seg id="4790"> In September 1989, Hungary decided to remove its border restrictions with Austria. </seg>
<seg id="4791"> That meant that any East German who got into Hungary could pass through to Austria and the free world. </seg>
<seg id="4792"> Sure enough, more than thirteen thousand East Germans escaped through Hungary's back door. </seg>
<seg id="4793"> Pressure built up on the East German government. </seg>
<seg id="4794"> When in November it announced plans to ease travel restrictions, tens of thousands of East Germans converged on the Berlin Wall, where, on 11/9/89, border guards just opened the gates. </seg>
<seg id="4795"> Someone there in Hungary, maybe it was the prime minister, maybe it was just a bureaucrat, must have said to himself or herself, ˇ°ImagineˇŞimagine what might happen if we opened the border with Austria.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4796"> Imagine-imagine if East German citizens, young and old, men and women, were so emboldened by seeing their neighbors flee to the West that one day they just swarmed that Berlin Wall and started to tear it down? </seg>
<seg id="4797"> Some people must have had a conversation just like that, and because they did, millions of Eastern Europeans were able to walk out from behind the Iron Curtain and engage with a flattening world. </seg>
<seg id="4798"> It was a great era in which to be an American. </seg>
<seg id="4799"> We were the only superpower, and the world was our oyster. </seg>
<seg id="4800"> Young Americans could think about traveling, for a semester or a summer, to more countries than any American generation before them. </seg>
<seg id="4801"> Indeed, they could travel as far as their imagination and wallets could take them. </seg>
<seg id="4802"> They could also look around at their classmates and see people from more different countries and cultures than any other class before them. </seg>
<seg id="4803"> Nine-eleven, of course, changed all that. </seg>
<seg id="4804"> It showed us the power of a very different kind of imagination. </seg>
<seg id="4805"> It showed us the power of a group of hateful men who spent several years imagining how to kill as many innocent people as they could. </seg>
<seg id="4806"> At some point bin Laden and his gang literally must have looked at one another and said, ˇ°Imagine if we actually could hit both towers of the World Trade Center at the exact right spot, between the ninety-fourth and ninety-eighth floors. </seg>
<seg id="4807"> And imagine if each tower were to come crashing down like a house of cards.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4808"> Yes, I am sorry to say, some people had that conversation, too. </seg>
<seg id="4809"> And, as a result, the world that was our oyster seemed to close up like a shell. </seg>
<seg id="4810"> There has never been a time in history when the character of human imagination wasn't important, but writing this book tells me that it has never been more important than now, because in a flat world so many of the inputs and tools of collaboration are becoming commodities available to everyone. </seg>
<seg id="4811"> They are all out there for anyone to grasp. </seg>
<seg id="4812"> There is one thing, though, that has not and can never be commoditizedˇŞand that is imagination. </seg>
<seg id="4813"> When we lived in a more centralized, and more vertically organized, worldˇŞwhere states had a near total monopoly of power-individual imagination was a big problem when the leader of a superpower stateˇŞa Stalin, a Mao, or a Hitler-became warped. </seg>
<seg id="4814"> But today, when individuals can easily access all the tools of collaboration and superempower themselves, or their small cells, individuals do not need to control a country to threaten large numbers of other people. </seg>
<seg id="4815"> The small can act very big today and pose a serious danger to world order-without the instruments of a state. </seg>
<seg id="4816"> Therefore, thinking about how we stimulate positive imaginations is of the utmost importance. </seg>
<seg id="4817"> As Irving Wladawsky-Berger, the IBM computer scientist, put it to me: We need to think more seriously than ever about how we encourage people to focus on productive outcomes that advance and unite civilization-peaceful imaginations that seek to ˇ°minimize alienation and celebrate interdependence rather than self-sufficiency, inclusion rather than exclusion,ˇ± openness, opportunity, and hope rather than limits, suspicion, and grievance. </seg>
<seg id="4818"> Let me try to illustrate this by example. </seg>
<seg id="4819"> In early 1999, two men started airlines from scratch, just a few weeks apart. </seg>
<seg id="4820"> Both men had a dream involving airplanes and the savvy to do something about it. </seg>
<seg id="4821"> One was named David Neeleman. </seg>
<seg id="4822"> In February 1999, he started JetBlue. </seg>
<seg id="4823"> He assembled $130 million in venture capital, bought a fleet of Airbus A-320 passenger jets, recruited pilots and signed them to seven-year contracts, and outsourced his reservation system to stay-at-home moms and retirees living around Salt Lake City, Utah, who booked passengers on their home computers. </seg>
<seg id="4824"> The other person who started an airline was, as we now know from the 9/11 Commission Report, Osama bin Laden. </seg>
<seg id="4825"> At a meeting in Kandahar, Afghanistan, in March or April 1999, he accepted a proposal initially drawn up by Khalid Sheikh Mohammed, the Pakistan-born mechanical engineer who was the architect of the 9/11 plot. </seg>
<seg id="4826"> JetBlue's motto was ˇ°Same Altitude. Different Attitude.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4827"> Al-Qaeda's motto was ˇ°Allahu Akbar,ˇ± God is great. </seg>
<seg id="4828"> Both airlines were designed to fly into New York City-Neeleman's into JFK and bin Laden's into lower Manhattan. </seg>
<seg id="4829"> Maybe it was because I read the 9/11 report while on a trip to Silicon Valley that I could not help but notice how much Khalid Sheikh Mohammed spoke and presented himself as just another eager engineer-entrepreneur, with his degree from North Carolina Agricultural and Technical State University, pitching his ideas to Osama bin Laden, who comes off as just another wealthy venture capitalist. </seg>
<seg id="4830"> But Mohammed, alas, was looking for adventure capital. </seg>
<seg id="4831"> As the 9/11 Commission Report put it, ˇ°No one exemplifies the model of the terrorist entrepreneur more clearly than Khalid Sheikh Mohammed (KSM), the principal architect of the 9/11 attacks... </seg>
<seg id="4832"> Highly educated and equally comfortable in a government office or a terrorist safe house, KSM applied his imagination, technical aptitude and managerial skills to hatching and planning an extraordinary array of terrorist schemes. </seg>
<seg id="4833"> These ideas included conventional car bombing, political assassination, aircraft bombing, hijacking, reservoir poisoning, and, ultimately, the use of aircraft as missiles guided by suicide operatives... </seg>
<seg id="4834"> KSM presents himself as an entrepreneur seeking venture capital and people... </seg>
<seg id="4835"> Bin Laden summoned KSM to Kandahar in March or April 1999 to tell him that al-Qaeda would support his proposal. </seg>
<seg id="4836"> The plot was now referred to within al-Qaeda as the 'planes operation.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4837"> From his corporate headquarters in Afghanistan, bin Laden proved to be a very deft supply chain manager. </seg>
<seg id="4838"> He assembled a virtual company just for this project-exactly like any global conglomerate would do in the flat world-finding just the right specialist for each task. </seg>
<seg id="4839"> He outsourced the overall design and blueprint for 9/11 to KSM and overall financial management to KSM's nephew, Ali Abdul Aziz Ali, who coordinated the dispersal of funds to the hijackers through wire transfers, cash, traveler's checks, and credit and debit cards from overseas bank accounts. </seg>
<seg id="4840"> Bin Laden recruited from the al-Qaeda roster just the right muscle guys from Asir Province, in Saudi Arabia, just the right pilots from Europe, just the right team leader from Hamburg, and just the right support staff from Pakistan. </seg>
<seg id="4841"> He outsourced the pilot training to flight schools in America. </seg>
<seg id="4842"> Bin Laden, who knew he needed only to ˇ°leaseˇ± the Boeing 757s, 767s, A32Os, and possibly 747s for his operation, raised the necessary capital for training pilots on all these different aircraft from a syndicate of pro-al-Qaeda Islamic charities and other Muslim adventure capitalists ready to fund anti-American operations. </seg>
<seg id="4843"> In the case of 9/11, the total budget was around $400,000. </seg>
<seg id="4844"> Once the team was assembled, bin Laden focused on his own core competency-overall leadership and ideological inspiration of his suicide supply chain, with assistance from his deputies Mohammed Atef and Ayman Zawahiri. </seg>
<seg id="4845"> You can get the full flavor of the bin Laden supply chain, and what an aggressive adopter of new technology al-Qaeda was, by reading just one entry from the December 2001 U.S. District Court for the Eastern District of Virginia's official indictment of Zacarias Moussaoui, the so-called nineteenth hijacker from 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="4846"> It reported the following: ˇ°In or about June 1999, in an interview with an Arabic-language television station, Osama bin Laden issued a... threat indicating that all American males should be killed.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4847"> It then points out that throughout the year 2000, all of the hijackers, including Moussaoui, began either attending or inquiring about flight school courses in America: ˇ°On or about September 29, 2000, Zacarias Moussaoui contacted Airman Flight School in Norman, Oklahoma, using an e-mail account he set up on September 6 with an Internet service provider in Malaysia. </seg>
<seg id="4848"> In or about October 2000, Zacarias Moussaoui received letters from Infocus Tech, a Malaysian company, stating that Moussaoui was appointed Infocus Tech's marketing consultant in the United States, the United Kingdom and Europe, and that he would receive, among other things, an allowance of $2,500 per month... </seg>
<seg id="4849"> On or about December 11, 2000, Mohammed Atta purchased flight deck videos for the Boeing 767 Model 300ER and the Airbus A320 Model 200 from the Ohio Pilot Store... </seg>
<seg id="4850"> In or about June 2001, in Norman, Oklahoma, Zacarias Moussaoui made inquiries about starting a cropdusting company... </seg>
<seg id="4851"> On or about August 16, 2001, Zacarias Moussaoui, possessed, among other things: two knives; a pair of binoculars; flight manuals for the Boeing 747 Model 400; a flight simulator computer program; fighting gloves and shin guards; a piece of paper referring to a handheld Global Positioning System receiver and a camcorder; software that could be used to review pilot procedures for the Boeing 747 Model 400; letters indicating that Moussaoui is a marketing consultant in the United States for Infocus Tech; a computer disk containing information related to the aerial application of pesticides; and a hand-held aviation radio.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4852"> A devout Mormon, who grew up in Latin America where his father was a UPI correspondent, David Neeleman, by contrast, is one of those classic American entrepreneurs and a man of enormous integrity. </seg>
<seg id="4853"> He never went to college, but he has started two successful airlines, Morris Air and JetBlue, and played an important role in shaping a third, Southwest. </seg>
<seg id="4854"> He is the godfather of ticketless air travel, now known as e-ticketing. </seg>
<seg id="4855"> ˇ°I am a total optimist. I think my father is an optimist,ˇ± he said to me, trying to explain where his innovative genes came from. </seg>
<seg id="4856"> ˇ°I grew up in a very happy home... </seg>
<seg id="4857"> JetBlue was created in my own mind before it was created on paper.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4858"> Using his optimistic imagination and his ability also to quickly adopt all the latest technology because he had no legacy system to worry about, Neeleman started a highly profitable airline, creating jobs, low-cost travel, a unique onboard, satellite-supported entertainment system, and one of the most people-friendly places to work you can imagine. </seg>
<seg id="4859"> He also started a catastrophe relief fund in his company to help employee families who are faced with a sudden death or catastrophic illness of a loved one. </seg>
<seg id="4860"> Neeleman donates $1 of his salary for every $1 any employee puts in the fund. </seg>
<seg id="4861"> ˇ°I think it is important that people give a little,ˇ± said Neeleman. </seg>
<seg id="4862"> ˇ°I believe that there are irrevocable laws of heaven that when you serve others you get this little buzz.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4863"> In 2003, Neeleman, already a wealthy man from his JetBlue stock, donated about $120,000 of his $200,000 salary to the JetBlue employee catastrophe fund. </seg>
<seg id="4864"> In the waiting room outside his New York City office, there is a color photo of a JetBlue Airbus flying over the World Trade Center. </seg>
<seg id="4865"> Neeleman was in his office on 9/11 and watched the Twin Towers burn, while his own JetBlue airliners were circling JFK in a holding pattern. </seg>
<seg id="4866"> When I explained to him the comparison/contrast I was going to make between him and bin Laden, he was both uncomfortable and curious. </seg>
<seg id="4867"> As I closed up my computer and prepared to leave following our interview, he said he had one question for me: ˇ°Do you think Osama actually believes there is a God up there who is happy with what he is doing?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4868"> I told him I just didn't know. </seg>
<seg id="4869"> What I do know is this: There are two ways to flatten the world. </seg>
<seg id="4870"> One is to use your imagination to bring everyone up to the same level, and the other is to use your imagination to bring everyone down to the same level. </seg>
<seg id="4871"> David Neeleman used his optimistic imagination and the easily available technologies of the flat world to lift people up. </seg>
<seg id="4872"> He launched a surprising and successful new airline, some profits of which he turns over to a catastrophe relief fund for his employees. </seg>
<seg id="4873"> Osama bin Laden and his disciples used their twisted imagination, and many of the same tools, to launch a surprise attack, which brought two enormous symbols of American power down to their level. </seg>
<seg id="4874"> Worse, they raised their money and created this massive human catastrophe under the guise of religion. </seg>
<seg id="4875"> ˇ°From the primordial swamps of globalization have emerged two genetic variants,ˇ± observed Infosys CEO Nandan Nilekani-one is al-Qaeda and the other are companies like Infosys or JetBlue. </seg>
<seg id="4876"> ˇ°Our focus therefore has to be how we can encourage more of the good mutations and keep out the bad.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4877"> I could not agree more. </seg>
<seg id="4878"> Indeed, that effort may be the most important thing we learn to do in order to keep this planet in one piece. </seg>
<seg id="4879"> I have no doubt that advances in technology-from iris scans to X-ray machines-will help us to identify, expose, and capture those who are trying to use the easily available tools of the flat world to destroy it. </seg>
<seg id="4880"> But in the end, technology alone cannot keep us safe. </seg>
<seg id="4881"> We really do have to find ways to affect the imagination of those who would use the tools of collaboration to destroy the world that has invented those tools. </seg>
<seg id="4882"> But how does one go about nurturing a more hopeful, life-affirming, and tolerant imagination in others? </seg>
<seg id="4883"> Everyone has to ask himself or herself this question. </seg>
<seg id="4884"> I ask it as an American. </seg>
<seg id="4885"> I stress this last point because I think it starts first and foremost by America setting an example. </seg>
<seg id="4886"> We have to be the best global citizens we can be. </seg>
<seg id="4887"> We cannot retreat from the world. </seg>
<seg id="4888"> We have to make sure that we get the best of our own imaginations-and never let our imaginations get the best of us. </seg>
<seg id="4889"> It is always hard to know when we have crossed the line between justified safety measures and letting our imaginations get the best of us and thereby paralyzing ourselves with precautions. </seg>
<seg id="4890"> I argued right after 9/11 that the reason our intelligence did not pick up the 9/11 plotters was ˇ°a failure of imagination.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4891"> We just did not have enough people within our intelligence community with a sick enough imagination to match that of bin Laden and Khalid Sheikh Mohammed. </seg>
<seg id="4892"> We do need some people like that within our intelligence services. </seg>
<seg id="4893"> But we all don't need to go down that route. </seg>
<seg id="4894"> We all don't need to become so gripped by imagining the worst in everyone around us that we shrink into ourselves. </seg>
<seg id="4895"> In 2003, my older daughter, Orly, was in her high school's symphonic orchestra. </seg>
<seg id="4896"> They spent all year practicing to take part in the national high school orchestra competition in New Orleans that March. </seg>
<seg id="4897"> When March rolled around, it appeared that we were heading for war in Iraq, so the Montgomery County School Board canceled all out-of-town trips by school groups-including the orchestra's attendance at New Orleans- fearing an outbreak of terrorism. </seg>
<seg id="4898"> I thought this was absolutely nuts. </seg>
<seg id="4899"> Even the evil imagination of 9/11 has its limits. </seg>
<seg id="4900"> At some point you do have to ask yourself whether Osama bin Laden and Ayman al-Zawahiri were really sitting around a cave in Afghanistan, with Ayman saying to Osama, ˇ°Say, Osama, d'you remember that annual high school orchestra competition in New Orleans? </seg>
<seg id="4901"> Well, it's coming up again next week. </seg>
<seg id="4902"> Let's really make a splash and go after it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4903"> No, I don't think so. </seg>
<seg id="4904"> Let's leave the cave dwelling to bin Laden. </seg>
<seg id="4905"> We have to be the masters of our imaginations, not the prisoners. </seg>
<seg id="4906"> I had a friend in Beirut who used to joke that every time she flew on an airplane she packed a bomb in her suitcase, because the odds of two people carrying a bomb on the same plane were so much higher. </seg>
<seg id="4907"> Do whatever it takes, but get out the door. </seg>
<seg id="4908"> Apropos of that, let me share the 9/11 story that touched me most from the New York Times series ˇ°Portraits of Grief,ˇ± the little biographies of those who were killed. </seg>
<seg id="4909"> It was the story of Candace Lee Williams, the twenty-year-old business student at Northeastern University, who had worked from January to June of 2001 as a work-study intern at the Merrill Lynch office on the fourteenth floor of 1 World Trade Center. </seg>
<seg id="4910"> Both Candace's mother and colleagues described her to The New York Times as a young woman full of energy and ambition, who loved her internship. </seg>
<seg id="4911"> Indeed, Candace's colleagues at Merrill Lynch liked her so much they took her to dinner on her last day of work, sent her home in a limousine, and later wrote Northeastern to say, ˇ°Send us five more like Candace.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4912"> A few weeks after finishing midterm exams-she was on a June-December academic schedule-Candace Lee Williams decided to meet her roommate at her home in California. </seg>
<seg id="4913"> Candace had recently made the dean's list. </seg>
<seg id="4914"> ˇ°They'd rented a convertible preparing for the occasion, and Candace wanted her picture taken with that Hollywood sign,ˇ± her mother, Sherri, told the Times. </seg>
<seg id="4915"> Unfortunately, Candace took the American Airlines Flight 11 that departed from Boston's Logan Airport on the morning of September 11, 2001, at 8:02 a.m. </seg>
<seg id="4916"> The plane was hijacked at 8:14 a.m. by five men, including Mohammed Atta, who was in seat 8D. </seg>
<seg id="4917"> With Atta at the controls, the Boeing 767-223ER was diverted to Manhattan and slammed Candace Lee Williams right back into the very same World Trade Center tower-between floors 94 and 98-where she had worked as an intern </seg>
<seg id="4918"> Airline records show that she was seated next to an eighty-year-old grandmother-two people at opposites ends of life: one full of memories, one full of dreams. </seg>
<seg id="4919"> When Candace Lee Williams boarded Flight 11 she could not have imagined how it would end. </seg>
<seg id="4920"> But in the wake of 9/11, none of us can now board an airplane without imagining how it could end-that what happened to Candace Lee Williams could also happen to us. </seg>
<seg id="4921"> We all are now so much more conscious that a person's life can be wiped out by the arbitrary will of a madman in a cave in Afghanistan. </seg>
<seg id="4922"> But the fact is, the chances of our plane being hijacked by terrorists today are still infinitesimal. </seg>
<seg id="4923"> We are more likely to be killed hitting a deer with our car or being struck by lightning. </seg>
<seg id="4924"> So even though we can now imagine what could happen when we get on an airplane, we have to get on the plane anyway. </seg>
<seg id="4925"> Because the alternative to not getting on that plane is putting ourselves in our own cave. </seg>
<seg id="4926"> Imagination can't just be about reruns. </seg>
<seg id="4927"> It also has to be about writing our own new script. </seg>
<seg id="4928"> From what I read about Candace Lee Williams, she was an optimist. </seg>
<seg id="4929"> I'd bet anything she'd still be getting on planes today if she had the chance. </seg>
<seg id="4930"> And so must we all. </seg>
<seg id="4931"> America's role in the world, from its inception, has been to be the country that looks forward, not back. </seg>
<seg id="4932"> One of the most dangerous things that has happened to America since 9/11, under the Bush administration, is that we have gone from exporting hope to exporting fear. </seg>
<seg id="4933"> We have gone from trying to coax the best out of the world to snarling at it way too often. </seg>
<seg id="4934"> And when you export fear, you end up importing everyone else's fears. </seg>
<seg id="4935"> Yes, we need people who can imagine the worst, because the worst did happen on 9/11 and it could happen again. </seg>
<seg id="4936"> But, as I said, there is a fine line between precaution and paranoia, and at times we have crossed it. </seg>
<seg id="4937"> Europeans and others often love to make fun of American optimism and naivete </seg>
<seg id="4938"> But I have always believed that deep down the rest of the world envies that American optimism and naivete, it needs it. </seg>
<seg id="4939"> It is one of the things that help keep the world spinning on its axis. </seg>
<seg id="4940"> If we go dark as a society, if we stop being the world's ˇ°dream factory,ˇ± we will make the world not only a darker place but also a poorer place. </seg>
<seg id="4941"> Analysts have always tended to measure a society by classical economic and social statistics: its deficit-to-GDP ratio, or its unemployment rate, or the rate of literacy among its adult women. </seg>
<seg id="4942"> Such statistics are important and revealing. </seg>
<seg id="4943"> But there is another statistic, much harder to measure, that I think is even more important and revealing: Does your society have more memories than dreams or more dreams than memories?By dreams I mean the positive, life-affirming variety. </seg>
<seg id="4944"> The business organization consultant Michael Hammer once remarked, ˇ°One thing that tells me a company is in trouble is when they tell me how good they were in the past. </seg>
<seg id="4945"> Same with countries. </seg>
<seg id="4946"> You don't want to forget your identity. </seg>
<seg id="4947"> I am glad you were great in the fourteenth century, but that was then and this is now. </seg>
<seg id="4948"> When memories exceed dreams, the end is near. </seg>
<seg id="4949"> The hallmark of a truly successful organization is the willingness to abandon what made it successful and start fresh.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4950"> In societies that have more memories than dreams, too many people are spending too many days looking backward. </seg>
<seg id="4951"> They see dignity, affirmation, and self-worth not by mining the present but by chewing on the past. </seg>
<seg id="4952"> And even that is usually not a real past but an imagined and adorned past. </seg>
<seg id="4953"> Indeed, such societies focus all their imagination on making that imagined past even more beautiful than it ever was, and then they cling to it like a rosary or a strand of worry beads, rather than imagining a better future and acting on that. </seg>
<seg id="4954"> It is dangerous enough when other countries go down that route; it would be disastrous for America to lose its bearings and move in that direction. </seg>
<seg id="4955"> I think my friend David Rothkopf, the former Commerce Department official and now a fellow at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, said it best: ˇ°The answer for us lies not in what has changed, but in recognizing what has not changed. </seg>
<seg id="4956"> Because only through this recognition will we begin to focus on the truly critical issues-an effective multilateral response to weapons of mass destruction proliferation, the creation of real stakeholders in globalization among the world's poor, the need for reform in the Arab world and a style of U.S. leadership that seeks to build our base of support worldwide by getting more people to voluntarily sign onto our values. </seg>
<seg id="4957"> We need to remember that those values are the real foundation for our security and the real source of our strength. </seg>
<seg id="4958"> And we need to recognize that our enemies can never defeat us. </seg>
<seg id="4959"> Only we can defeat ourselves, by throwing out the rule book that has worked for us for a long, long time.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4960"> I believe that history will make very clear that President Bush shamelessly exploited the emotions around 9/11 for political purposes. He used those 9/11 emotions to take a far-right Republican domestic agenda on taxes, the environment, and social issues from 9/10-an agenda for which he had no popular mandate-and drive it into a 9/12 world. </seg>
<seg id="4961"> In doing so, Mr. Bush not only drove a wedge between Americans, and between Americans and the world, he drove a wedge between America and its own history and identity. </seg>
<seg id="4962"> His administration transformed the United States into ˇ°the United States of Fighting Terrorism.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4963"> This is the real reason, in my view, that so many people in the world dislike President Bush so intensely. </seg>
<seg id="4964"> They feel that he has taken away something very dear to them-an America that exports hope, not fear. </seg>
<seg id="4965"> We need our president to restore September 11 to its rightful place on the calendar-as the day after September 10 and before September 12. </seg>
<seg id="4966"> We must never let it become a day that defines us. </seg>
<seg id="4967"> Because ultimately September 11 is about them-the bad guys-not about us. </seg>
<seg id="4968"> We're about the Fourth of July. </seg>
<seg id="4969"> We're about 11/9. </seg>
<seg id="4970"> Beyond trying to retain the best of our own imaginations, what else can we do as Americans and as a global society to try to nurture the same in others? </seg>
<seg id="4971"> One has to approach this question with great humility. </seg>
<seg id="4972"> What leads one person to the joy of destruction and what leads another to the joy of creation, what leads one to imagine 11/9 and another to imagine 9/11, is surely one of the great mysteries of contemporary life. </seg>
<seg id="4973"> Moreover, while most of us might have some clue about how to nurture a more positive imagination for our own kids, and maybe-maybe-for our fellow citizens, it is presumptuous to think that we can do it for others, particularly those of a different culture, speaking different languages, and living half a world away. </seg>
<seg id="4974"> Yet 9/11, the flattening of the world, and the continuing threat of world-disrupting terrorism suggest that not thinking about this is its own kind of dangerous naivete. </seg>
<seg id="4975"> So I insist on trying to do so, but I approach this issue with a keen awareness of the limits of what any outsider can know or do. </seg>
<seg id="4976"> Generally speaking, imagination is the product of two shaping forces. </seg>
<seg id="4977"> One is the narratives that people are nurtured on-the stories and myths they and their religious and national leaders tell themselves-and how those narratives feed their imaginations one way or another. </seg>
<seg id="4978"> The other is the context in which people grow up, which has a huge impact on shaping how they see the world and others. </seg>
<seg id="4979"> Outsiders cannot get inside and adjust the Mexican or Arab or Chinese narrative any more than they can get inside the American one. </seg>
<seg id="4980"> Only they can reinterpret their narrative, make it more tolerant or forward looking, and adapt it to modernity. </seg>
<seg id="4981"> No one can do that for them or even with them. </seg>
<seg id="4982"> But one can think about how to collaborate with others to change their context-the context within which people grow up and live their daily lives-to help nurture more people with the imagination of 11/9 than 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="4983"> Let me offer a few examples.eBayMeg Whitman, the CEO of eBay, once told me a wonderful story that went like this: ˇ°We took eBay public in September 1998, in the middle of the dot-com boom. </seg>
<seg id="4984"> And in September and October our stock would go up eighty points and down fifty in a single day. </seg>
<seg id="4985"> I thought, 'This is insane.' </seg>
<seg id="4986"> Anyway, one day I am minding my own business, sitting in my own cubicle, and my secretary runs over and says to me, 'Meg, it's Arthur Levitt [chairman] of the SEC on the phone.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4987"> The Securities and Exchange Commission oversees the stock market and is always concerned about issues of volatility in a stock and whether there is manipulation behind it. </seg>
<seg id="4988"> In those days, for a CEO to hear that ˇ°Arthur Levitt is on the lineˇ± was not a good way to start the day. </seg>
<seg id="4989"> ˇ°So I called my general counsel,ˇ± said Whitman, ˇ°who came over from his cubicle, and he was white like a sheet. </seg>
<seg id="4990"> We called Levitt back together and we put him on the speakerphone, and I said, 'Hi, it's Meg Whitman of eBay.' </seg>
<seg id="4991"> And he said, 'Hi, it's Arthur Levitt of the SEC. </seg>
<seg id="4992"> I don't know you and have never met you but I know that you just went public and I want to know: How did it go? </seg>
<seg id="4993"> Were we [the SEC] customer-friendly?' </seg>
<seg id="4994"> And so we breathed a sigh of relief, and we talked about that a little bit. </seg>
<seg id="4995"> And then [Levitt] said, 'Well, actually, another reason that I am calling is that I just got my tenth positive feedback on eBay and have earned my yellow star. </seg>
<seg id="4996"> And I am so proud.' </seg>
<seg id="4997"> And then he said, 'I am actually a collector of Depression-era glass, post-1929, and so I have bought and sold on eBay and you get feedback as a buyer and seller. </seg>
<seg id="4998"> And I thought you would just like to know.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="4999"> Every eBay user has a feedback profile made up of comments from other eBay users who have done transactions with him or her, relating to whether the goods bought or sold were as expected and the transaction went off smoothly. </seg>
<seg id="5000"> This constitutes your official ˇ°eBay reputation.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5001"> You get +1 point for each positive comment, 0 points for each neutral comment, and -1 for each negative comment. </seg>
<seg id="5002"> A colored star icon is attached to your user ID on eBay for ten or more feedback points. </seg>
<seg id="5003"> My user ID on eBay might be TOMF (50) and a blue star, which means that I have received positive feedback comments from fifty other eBay users. </seg>
<seg id="5004"> Next to that is a box that will tell you whether the seller has had 100 percent positive feedback comments or less, and also give you the chance to click and read all the buyers' comments about that seller. </seg>
<seg id="5005"> The point, said Whitman, is that ˇ°I think every human being, Arthur Levitt or the janitor or the waitress or the doctor or the professor, needs and craves validation and positive feedback.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5006"> And the big misconception is to think that it has to be money. </seg>
<seg id="5007"> ˇ°It can be really small things,ˇ± said Whitman, ˇ°telling someone, ' </seg>
<seg id="5008"> You did a really great job, you were recognized as doing a great history paper.' </seg>
<seg id="5009"> Our users say to us [about eBay's star system], 'Where else can you wake up in the morning and see how much people like you?'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5010"> But what is so striking, said Whitman, is that the overwhelming majority of feedback on eBay is positive. </seg>
<seg id="5011"> That's interesting. </seg>
<seg id="5012"> People don't usually write Wal-Mart managers to compliment them on a fabulous purchase. </seg>
<seg id="5013"> But when you are part of a community that you feel ownership in, it is different. </seg>
<seg id="5014"> You have a stake. </seg>
<seg id="5015"> ˇ°The highest number of feedback we have is well over 250,000 positive comments, and you can see each one,ˇ± said Whitman. ˇ°You can see the entire history of each buyer and seller, and we have introduced the ability to rebut... </seg>
<seg id="5016"> You cannot be anonymous on eBay. </seg>
<seg id="5017"> If you are not willing to say who you are, you should not be saying it. </seg>
<seg id="5018"> And it became the norm of the community really fast... </seg>
<seg id="5019"> We are not running an exchange-we are running a community.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5020"> Indeed, with 105 million registered users from 190 countries trading more than $35 billion in products annually, eBay is actually a self-governing nation-state-the V.R.e., the Virtual Republic of eBay. </seg>
<seg id="5021"> And how is it governed? </seg>
<seg id="5022"> EBay's philosophy, said Whitman, is, ˇ°Let's make a small number of rules, really enforce them, and then create an environment in which people can fulfill their own potential. </seg>
<seg id="5023"> There is something going on here besides buying and selling goods.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5024"> Even allowing for corporate boosterism, Whitman's essential message is really worth contemplating: ˇ°People will say that 'eBay restored my faith in humanity'- contrary to the world where people are cheating and don't give people the benefit of the doubt. </seg>
<seg id="5025"> I hear that twice a week... </seg>
<seg id="5026"> EBay offers the little guy, who's disenfranchised, an opportunity to compete on a totally level playing field. </seg>
<seg id="5027"> We have a disproportionate share of wheelchairs and disabled and minorities, [because] on eBay people don't know who you are. </seg>
<seg id="5028"> You are only as good as your product and feedback.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5029"> Whitman recalled that one day she got an e-mail from a couple in Orlando who were coming to an ˇ°eBay Liveˇ± event at which she was speaking. </seg>
<seg id="5030"> These are big revival meeting-conventions of eBay sellers. </seg>
<seg id="5031"> They asked if they could come backstage to meet Whitman after her speech. </seg>
<seg id="5032"> ˇ°So after the keynote,ˇ± she recalled, ˇ°they come back to my green room, and in comes mom and dad and a seventeen-year-old boy in a wheelchair-very disabled with cerebral palsy. </seg>
<seg id="5033"> They tell me, 'Kyle is very disabled and can't go to school, [but] he built an eBay business and last year my husband and I quit our jobs, and now we help himˇŞwe have made more money on eBay than we ever made on our jobs.' </seg>
<seg id="5034"> And then they added the most incredible thing. </seg>
<seg id="5035"> They said, 'On eBay, Kyle is not disabled.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5036"> Whitman told me that at another eBay Live event a young man came up to her, a big power seller on eBay, and said that thanks to his eBay business he had been able to buy a house and a car, hire people, and be his own boss. </seg>
<seg id="5037"> But the best part, said Whitman, was that the young man added, ˇ°I am so excited about eBay, because I did not graduate from college and was sort of disowned by my family, and I am now the hit of my family. </seg>
<seg id="5038"> I am a successful entrepreneur.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5039"> ˇ°It's this blend of economic opportunity and validationˇ± that makes eBay tick, concluded Whitman. </seg>
<seg id="5040"> Those validated become transparent as good partners, because bad validation is an option for the whole community. </seg>
<seg id="5041"> Bottom line: eBay didn't just create an online market. </seg>
<seg id="5042"> It created a self-governing community-a context-where anyone, from the severely handicapped to the head of the SEC, could come and achieve his or her potential and be validated as a good and trustworthy person by the whole community. </seg>
<seg id="5043"> That kind of self-esteem and validation is the best, most effective way of producing dehumiliation and redignification. </seg>
<seg id="5044"> To the extent that America can collaborate with regions like the Arab-Muslim world to produce contexts where young people can succeed, can achieve their full potential on a level playing field, can get validation and respect from achievements in this world-and not from martyrdom to get into the next world-we can help foster more young people with more dreams than memories. </seg>
<seg id="5045"> India </seg>
<seg id="5046"> If you want to see this same process at work in a less virtual community, study the second largest Muslim country in the world. </seg>
<seg id="5047"> The largest Muslim country in the world is Indonesia and the second largest is not Saudi Arabia, Iran, Egypt, or Pakistan. </seg>
<seg id="5048"> Nevertheless, to have sustained a functioning democracy with all its flaws for more than fifty years in a country of over 1 billion people, who speak scores of different languages, is something of a miracle and a great source of stability for the world. </seg>
<seg id="5049"> Two of India's presidents have been Muslims, and its current president, A.P.J. Abdul Kalam, is both a Muslim and the father of the Indian nuclear missile program. </seg>
<seg id="5050"> While a Muslim woman sits on India's Supreme Court, no Muslim woman is allowed even to drive a car in Saudi Arabia. </seg>
<seg id="5051"> Indian Muslims, including women, have been governors of many Indian states, and the wealthiest man in India today, high on the Forbes list of global billionaires, is an Indian Muslim: Azim Premji, the chairman of Wipro, one of India's most important technology companies. </seg>
<seg id="5052"> I was in India shortly after the United States invaded Afghanistan in late 2001, when Indian television carried a debate between the country's leading female movie star and parliamentarian-Shabana Azmi, a Muslim womanˇŞand the imam of New Delhi's biggest mosque. </seg>
<seg id="5053"> The imam had called on Indian Muslims to go to Afghanistan and join the jihad against America, and Azmi ripped into him, live on Indian TV, basically telling the cleric to go take a hike. She told him to go to Kandahar and join the Taliban and leave the rest of India's Muslims alone. How did she get away with that? Easy. As a Muslim woman she lived in a context that empowered and protected her to speak her mind -even to a leading cleric.Different context, different narrative, different imagination.This is not all that complicated: Give young people a context where they can translate a positive imagination into reality, give them a context in which someone with a grievance can have it adjudicated in a court of law without having to bribe the judge with a goat, give them a context in which they can pursue an entrepreneurial idea and become the richest or the most creative or most respected people in their own country, no matter what their background, give them a context in which any complaint or idea can be published in the newspaper, give them a context in which anyone can run for office-and guess what? They usually don't want to blow up the world. They usually want to be part of it.A South Asian Muslim friend of mine once told me this story: His Indian Muslim family split in 1948, with half going to Pakistan and half staying in Mumbai. When he got older, he asked his father one day why the Indian half of the family seemed to be doing better than the Pakistani half. His father said to him, ˇ°Son, when a Muslim grows up in India and he sees a man living in a big mansion high on a hill, he says, 'Father, one day, I will be that man.'
