And when a Muslim grows up in Pakistan and sees a man living in a big mansion high on a hill, he says, 'Father, one day I will kill that man.'ˇ± When you have a pathway to be the Man or the Woman, you tend to focus on the path and on achieving your dreams. When you have no pathway, you tend to focus on your wrath and on nursing your memories.India only twenty years ago, before the triple convergence, was known as a country of snake charmers, poor people, and Mother Teresa. </seg>
<seg id="5054"> Atul Vashistha, CEO of the outsourcing consulting firm NeoIT, often appears in the American media to defend outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="5055"> He told me this story: ˇ°One day I had a problem with my HP printer-the printing was very slow. </seg>
<seg id="5056"> I was trying to figure out the problem. </seg>
<seg id="5057"> So I call HP tech support. </seg>
<seg id="5058"> This guy answers and takes all my personal information down. </seg>
<seg id="5059"> From his voice it is clear he is somewhere in India. </seg>
<seg id="5060"> So I start asking where he is and how the weather is. </seg>
<seg id="5061"> We're having a nice chat. </seg>
<seg id="5062"> So after he is helping me for about ten or fifteen minutes he says, 'Sir, do you mind if I say something to you?' </seg>
<seg id="5063"> I said, 'Sure.' </seg>
<seg id="5064"> I figured he was going to tell me something else I was doing wrong with my computer and was trying to be polite about it. </seg>
<seg id="5065"> And instead he says, 'Sir, I was very proud to hear you on Voice of America. </seg>
<seg id="5066"> You did a good job...' </seg>
<seg id="5067"> I had just been on a VOA show about the backlash against globalization and outsourcing. </seg>
<seg id="5068"> I was one of three invited guests. </seg>
<seg id="5069"> There was a union official, an economist, and myself. </seg>
<seg id="5070"> I defended outsourcing and this guy heard it.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5071"> Remember: In the flat world you don't get just your humiliation dished out to you fiber-optically. </seg>
<seg id="5072"> You also get your pride dished out to you fiber-optically. </seg>
<seg id="5073"> An Indian help-line operator suddenly knows, in real time, all about how one of his compatriots is representing India half a world away, and it makes him feel better about himself. </seg>
<seg id="5074"> The French Revolution, the American Revolution, the Indian democracy, and even eBay are all based on social contracts whose dominant feature is that authority comes from the bottom up, and people can and do feel self-empowered to improve their lot. </seg>
<seg id="5075"> People living in such contexts tend to spend their time focusing on what to do next, not on whom to blame next. </seg>
<seg id="5076"> The Curse of Oil </seg>
<seg id="5077"> Nothing has contributed more to retarding the emergence of a democratic context in places like Venezuela, Nigeria, Saudi Arabia, and Iran than the curse of oil. </seg>
<seg id="5078"> As long as the monarchs and dictators who run these oil states can get rich by drilling their natural resources-as opposed to drilling the natural talents and energy of their people-they can stay in office forever. </seg>
<seg id="5079"> They can use oil money to monopolize all the instruments of power-army, police, and intelligence-and never have to introduce real transparency or power sharing. </seg>
<seg id="5080"> All they have to do is capture and hold the oil tap. </seg>
<seg id="5081"> They never have to tax their people, so the relationship between ruler and ruled is highly distorted. </seg>
<seg id="5082"> Without taxation, there is no representation. </seg>
<seg id="5083"> The rulers don't really have to pay attention to the people or explain how they are spending their money-because they have not raised that money through taxes. </seg>
<seg id="5084"> That is why countries focused on tapping their oil wells always have weak or nonexistent institutions. </seg>
<seg id="5085"> Countries focused on tapping their people have to focus on developing real institutions, property rights, rule of law, independent courts, modern education, foreign trade, foreign investment, freedom of thought, and scientific enquiry to get the most out of their men and women. </seg>
<seg id="5086"> In an essay in Foreign Affairs called ˇ°Saving Iraq from Its Oilˇ± (July-August 2004), development economists Nancy Birdsall and Arvind Subramanian point out that ˇ°34 less-developed countries now boast significant oil and natural gas resources that constitute at least 30 percent of their total export revenue. </seg>
<seg id="5087"> Despite their riches, however, 12 of these countries' annual per capita income remains below $1,500... </seg>
<seg id="5088"> Moreover, two-thirds of the 34 countries are not democratic, and of those that are, only three score in the top half of Freedom House's world rankings of political freedom.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5089"> In other words, imagination is also a product of necessity-when the context you are living in simply does not allow you to indulge in certain escapist or radical fantasies, you don't. </seg>
<seg id="5090"> Look where the most creative innovation is happening in the Arab-Muslim world today. It is in the places with little or no oil. </seg>
<seg id="5091"> As I noted earlier, Bahrain was one of the first Arab Gulf states to discover oil and was the first Arab Gulf state to run out of oil. </seg>
<seg id="5092"> And today it is the first Arab Gulf state to develop comprehensive labor reform for developing the skills of its own workers, the first to sign a free-trade agreement with the United States, and the first to hold a free and fair election, in which women could both run and vote. </seg>
<seg id="5093"> And which countries in that same region are paralyzed or actually rolling back reforms? </seg>
<seg id="5094"> Saudi Arabia and Iran, which are awash in oil money. </seg>
<seg id="5095"> On December 9, 2004, at a time when crude oil prices had soared to near $50 a barrel, The Economist did a special report from Iran, in which it noted, ˇ°Without oil at its present sky-high price, Iran's economy would be in wretched straits. </seg>
<seg id="5096"> Oil provides about half the government's revenue and at least 80% of export earnings. </seg>
<seg id="5097"> But, once again under the influence of zealots in parliament, the oil cash is being spent on boosting wasteful subsidies rather than on much-needed development and new technology.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5098"> It is worthy of note that Jordan began upgrading its education system and privatizing, modernizing, and deregulating its economy starting in 1989-precisely when oil prices were way down and it could no longer rely on handouts from the Gulf oil states. </seg>
<seg id="5099"> In 1999, when Jordan signed its free-trade agreement with the United States, its exports to America totaled $13 million. </seg>
<seg id="5100"> In 2004, Jordan exported over $1 billion of goods to America-things Jordanians made with their hands. The Jordanian government has also installed computers and broadband Internet in every school. </seg>
<seg id="5101"> Most important, in 2004, Jordan announced a reform of its education requirements for mosque prayer leaders. </seg>
<seg id="5102"> Traditionally, high school students in Jordan took an exam for college entrance, and those who did the best became doctors and engineers. </seg>
<seg id="5103"> Those who did the worst became mosque preachers. </seg>
<seg id="5104"> In 2004, Jordan decided to gradually phase in a new system. </seg>
<seg id="5105"> Henceforth, to become a mosque prayer leader, a young man will first have to get a B.A. in some other subject, and can study Islamic law only as a graduate degree-in order to encourage more young men of talent to go into the clergy and weed out those who were just ˇ°failingˇ± into it. </seg>
<seg id="5106"> That is an important change in context that should pay dividends over time in the narratives that young Jordanians are nurtured upon in their mosques. </seg>
<seg id="5107"> ˇ°We had to go through a crisis to accept the need for reform,ˇ± said Jordan's minister of planning, Bassem Awadallah. </seg>
<seg id="5108"> only when falling oil prices force the leaders in the Middle East to change their contexts will they reform. </seg>
<seg id="5109"> People don't change when you tell them they should. </seg>
<seg id="5110"> Or as Johns Hopkins foreign affairs professor Michael Mandelbaum puts it, ˇ°People don't change when you tell them there is a better option. </seg>
<seg id="5111"> They change when they conclude that they have no other option.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5112"> Give me $10-a-barrel oil, and I will give you political and economic reform from Moscow to Riyadh to Iran. </seg>
<seg id="5113"> If America and its allies will not collaborate in bringing down the price of crude oil, their aspirations for reform in all these areas will be stillborn. </seg>
<seg id="5114"> There is another factor to consider here. </seg>
<seg id="5115"> When you have to make things with your hands and then trade with others in order to flourish, not just dig an oil well in your own backyard, it inevitably broadens imagination and increases tolerance and trust. </seg>
<seg id="5116"> It is no accident that Muslim countries make up 20 percent of the world's population but account for only 4 percent of world trade. </seg>
<seg id="5117"> When countries don't make things anyone else wants, they trade less, and less trade means less exchange of ideas and openness to the world. </seg>
<seg id="5118"> People who are connected to the world and exposed to different cultures and perspectives are far more likely to develop the imagination of 11/9. </seg>
<seg id="5119"> People who are feeling disconnected, for whom personal freedom and fulfillment are a Utopian fantasy, are more likely to develop the imagination of 9/11. </seg>
<seg id="5120"> Just One Good Example </seg>
<seg id="5121"> Stanley Fischer, the former deputy managing director of the IMF, once remarked to me, ˇ°One good example is worth a thousand theories.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5122"> I believe that is true. </seg>
<seg id="5123"> Indeed, people do not change only when they must: They also change when they see that others-like themselves-have changed and flourished. </seg>
<seg id="5124"> Or as Michael Mandelbaum also points out, ˇ°People change as a result of what they notice, not just what they are toldˇ±-especially when what they notice is someone just like them doing well. </seg>
<seg id="5125"> As I pointed out in Chapter 10, there is only one Arab company that developed a world-class business strong enough to get itself listed on the Nasdaq, and that was Aramex. </seg>
<seg id="5126"> Every Jordanian, every Arab, should know and take pride in the Aramex story, the way every American knows the Apple and Microsoft and Dell stories. </seg>
<seg id="5127"> It is the example that is worth a thousand theories. </seg>
<seg id="5128"> It should be the role model of a self-empowered Arab company, run by Arab brainpower and entrepre-neurship, succeeding on the world stage and enriching its own workers at the same time. </seg>
<seg id="5129"> When Fadi Ghandour took Aramex public again in 2005, this time in Dubai, some four hundred Aramex employees from all over the Arab world who had stock options divided $14 million. </seg>
<seg id="5130"> I will never forget Fadi telling me how proud these employees were-some of them managers, some of them just delivery drivers. </seg>
<seg id="5131"> This windfall was going to enable them to buy homes or send their kids to better schools. </seg>
<seg id="5132"> Imagine the dignity that these people feel when they come back to their families and neighborhoods and tell everyone that they are going to build a new house because the world-class Arab company they work for has gone public. </seg>
<seg id="5133"> Imagine how much dignity they feel when they see themselves getting ahead by succeeding in the flat world-not in the traditional Middle Eastern way by inheritance, by selling land, or by getting a government contract-but by working for a real company, an Arab company. </seg>
<seg id="5134"> Just as it is no accident that there are no Indian Muslims in al-Qaeda, it is no accident that the three thousand Arab employees of Aramex want to deliver only packages that help economies grow and Arab people flourish-not suicide bombs. </seg>
<seg id="5135"> Speaking of the Aramex employees with stock options, Ghandour said, ˇ°They all feel like owners. </seg>
<seg id="5136"> A lot of them came up to me and said, 'Thank you, but I want to invest my options back in the company and be an investor in the new IPO.' </seg>
<seg id="5137"> ˇ±Give me just one hundred more examples like Aramex, and I will start to give you a different context-and narrative. </seg>
 <seg id="5138"> FLATONE </seg>
<seg id="5139"> While I was Sleeping </seg>
<seg id="5140"> Your Highnesses, as Catholic Christians, and princes who love and promote the holy Christian faith, and are enemies of the doctrine of Mahomet, and of all idolatry and heresy, determined to send me, Christopher Columbus, to the above-mentioned countries of India, to see the said princes, people, and territories, and to learn their disposition and the proper method of converting them to our holy faith; </seg>
<seg id="5141"> and furthermore directed that I should not proceed by land to the East, as is customary, but by a Westerly route, in which direction we have hitherto no certain evidence that anyone has gone. </seg>
<seg id="5142"> ˇŞEntry from the journal of Christopher Columbus on his voyage of 1492 </seg>
<seg id="5143"> No one ever gave me directions like this on a golf course before: ˇ°Aim at either Microsoft or IBM.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5144"> I was standing on the first tee at the KGA Golf Club in downtown Bangalore, in southern India, when my playing partner pointed at two shiny glass-and-steel buildings off in the distance, just behind the first green. </seg>
<seg id="5145"> The Goldman Sachs building wasn't done yet; </seg>
<seg id="5146"> otherwise he could have pointed that out as well and made it a threesome. </seg>
<seg id="5147"> HP and Texas Instruments had their offices on the back nine, along the tenth hole. </seg>
<seg id="5148"> That wasn't all. </seg>
<seg id="5149"> The tee markers were from Epson, the printer company, and one of our caddies was wearing a hat from 3M. </seg>
<seg id="5150"> Outside, some of the traffic signs were also sponsored by Texas Instruments, and the Pizza Hut billboard on the way over showed a steaming pizza, under the headline ˇ°Gigabites of Taste!ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5151"> No, this definitely wasn't Kansas. </seg>
<seg id="5152"> It didn't even seem like India. </seg>
<seg id="5153"> Was this the New World, the Old World, or the Next World? </seg>
<seg id="5154"> I had come to Bangalore, India's Silicon Valley, on my own Columbus-like journey of exploration. </seg>
<seg id="5155"> Columbus sailed with the Nina, the Pinta, and the Santa Maria in an effort to discover a shorter, more direct route to India by heading west, across the Atlantic, on what he presumed to be an open sea route to the East Indies-rather than going south and east around Africa, as Portuguese explorers of his day were trying to do. </seg>
<seg id="5156"> India and the magical Spice Islands of the East were famed at the time for their gold, pearls, gems, and silk-a source of untold riches. </seg>
<seg id="5157"> Finding this shortcut by sea to India, at a time when the Muslim powers of the day had blocked the overland routes from Europe, was a way for both Columbus and the Spanish monarchy to become wealthy and powerful. </seg>
<seg id="5158"> When Columbus set sail, he apparently assumed the Earth was round, which was why he was convinced that he could get to India by going west. </seg>
<seg id="5159"> He miscalculated the distance, though. He thought the Earth was a smaller sphere than it is. </seg>
<seg id="5160"> He also did not anticipate running into a landmass before he reached the East Indies. </seg>
<seg id="5161"> Nevertheless, he called the aboriginal peoples he encountered in the new world ˇ°Indians.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5162"> Returning home, though, Columbus was able to tell his patrons, King Ferdinand and Queen Isabella, that although he never did find India, he could confirm that the world was indeed round. </seg>
<seg id="5163"> I set out for India by going due east, via Frankfurt. </seg>
<seg id="5164"> I had Lufthansa business class. </seg>
<seg id="5165"> I knew exactly which direction I was going thanks to the GPS map displayed on the screen that popped out of the armrest of my airline seat. </seg>
<seg id="5166"> I landed safely and on schedule. I too encountered people called Indians. </seg>
<seg id="5167"> I too was searching for the source of India's riches. Columbus was searching for hardware-precious metals, silk, and spices-the source of wealth in his day. </seg>
<seg id="5168"> I was searching for software, brainpower, complex algorithms, knowledge workers, call centers, transmission protocols, breakthroughs in optical engineering-the sources of wealth in our day. </seg>
<seg id="5169"> Columbus was happy to make the Indians he met his slaves, a pool of free manual labor. </seg>
<seg id="5170"> I just wanted to understand why the Indians I met were taking our work, why they had become such an important pool for the outsourcing of service and information technology work from America and other industrialized countries. </seg>
<seg id="5171"> Columbus had more than one hundred men on his three ships; </seg>
<seg id="5172"> I had a small crew from the Discovery Times channel that fit comfortably into two banged-up vans, with Indian drivers who drove barefoot. </seg>
<seg id="5173"> When I set sail, so to speak, I too assumed that the world was round, but what I encountered in the real India profoundly shook my faith in that notion. </seg>
<seg id="5174"> Columbus accidentally ran into America but thought he had discovered part of India. </seg>
<seg id="5175"> I actually found India and thought many of the people I met there were Americans. </seg>
<seg id="5176"> Some had actually taken American names, and others were doing great imitations of American accents at call centers and American business techniques at software labs. </seg>
<seg id="5177"> Columbus reported to his king and queen that the world was round, and he went down in history as the man who first made this discovery. </seg>
<seg id="5178"> I returned home and shared my discovery only with my wife, and only in a whisper. </seg>
<seg id="5179"> ˇ°Honey,ˇ± I confided, ˇ°I think the world is flat.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5180"> How did I come to this conclusion? </seg>
<seg id="5181"> I guess you could say it all started in Nandan Nilekani's conference room at Infosys Technologies Limited. </seg>
<seg id="5182"> Infosys is one of the jewels of the Indian information technology world, and Nilekani, the company's CEO, is one of the most thoughtful and respected captains of Indian industry. </seg>
<seg id="5183"> I drove with the Discovery Times crew out to the Infosys campus, about forty minutes from the heart of Bangalore, to tour the facility and interview Nilekani. </seg>
<seg id="5184"> The Infosys campus is reached by a pockmarked road, with sacred cows, horse-drawn carts, and motorized rickshaws all jostling alongside our vans. </seg>
<seg id="5185"> Once you enter the gates of Infosys, though, you are in a different world. </seg>
<seg id="5186"> A massive resort-size swimming pool nestles amid boulders and manicured lawns, adjacent to a huge putting green. There are multiple restaurants and a fabulous health club. </seg>
<seg id="5187"> Glass-and-steel buildings seem to sprout up like weeds each week. </seg>
<seg id="5188"> In some of those buildings, Infosys employees are writing specific software programs for American or European companies; </seg>
<seg id="5189"> in others, they are running the back rooms of major American- and European-based multinationalsˇŞeverything from computer maintenance to specific research projects to answering customer calls routed there from all over the world. </seg>
<seg id="5190"> Security is tight, cameras monitor the doors, and if you are working for American Express, you cannot get into the building that is managing services and research for General Electric. </seg>
<seg id="5191"> Young Indian engineers, men and women, walk briskly from building to building, dangling ID badges. </seg>
<seg id="5192"> One looked like he could do my taxes. </seg>
<seg id="5193"> Another looked like she could take my computer apart. </seg>
<seg id="5194"> And a third looked like she designed it! </seg>
<seg id="5195"> After sitting for an interview, Nilekani gave our TV crew a tour of Info-sys's global conferencing center-ground zero of the Indian outsourcing industry. </seg>
<seg id="5196"> It was a cavernous wood-paneled room that looked like a tiered classroom from an Ivy League law school. </seg>
<seg id="5197"> On one end was a massive wall-size screen and overhead there were cameras in the ceiling for teleconferencing. </seg>
<seg id="5198"> ˇ°So this is our conference room, probably the largest screen in Asia-this is forty digital screens [put together],ˇ± Nilekani explained proudly, pointing to the biggest flat-screen TV I had ever seen. </seg>
<seg id="5199"> Infosys, he said, can hold a virtual meeting of the key players from its entire global supply chain for any project at any time on that supersize screen. </seg>
<seg id="5200"> So their American designers could be on the screen speaking with their Indian software writers and their Asian manufacturers all at once. ˇ°We could be sitting here, somebody from New York, London, Boston, San Francisco, all live. </seg>
<seg id="5201"> And maybe the implementation is in Singapore, so the Singapore person could also be live here... </seg>
<seg id="5202"> That's globalization,ˇ± said Nilekani. </seg>
<seg id="5203"> Above the screen there were eight clocks that pretty well summed up the Infosys workday: 24/7/365. </seg>
<seg id="5204"> The clocks were labeled US West, US East, GMT, India, Singapore, Hong Kong, Japan, Australia. </seg>
<seg id="5205"> ˇ°Outsourcing is just one dimension of a much more fundamental thing happening today in the world,ˇ± Nilekani explained. </seg>
<seg id="5206"> ˇ°What happened over the last [few] years is that there was a massive investment in technology, especially in the bubble era, when hundreds of millions of dollars were invested in putting broadband connectivity around the world, undersea cables, all those things.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5207"> At the same time, he added, computers became cheaper and dispersed all over the world, and there was an explosion of software-e-mail, search engines like Google, and proprietary software that can chop up any piece of work and send one part to Boston, one part to Bangalore, and one part to Beijing, making it easy for anyone to do remote development. </seg>
<seg id="5208"> When all of these things suddenly came together around 2000, added Nilekani, they ˇ°created a platform where intellectual work, intellectual capital, could be delivered from anywhere. </seg>
<seg id="5209"> It could be disaggregated, delivered, distributed, produced, and put back together again-and this gave a whole new degree of freedom to the way we do work, especially work of an intellectual nature... </seg>
<seg id="5210"> And what you are seeing in Bangalore today is really the culmination of all these things coming together.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5211"> We were sitting on the couch outside of Nilekani's office, waiting for the TV crew to set up its cameras. </seg>
<seg id="5212"> At one point, summing up the implications of all this, Nilekani uttered a phrase that rang in my ear. </seg>
<seg id="5213"> He said to me, ˇ°Tom, the playing field is being leveled.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5214"> He meant that countries like India are now able to compete for global knowledge work as never before-and that America had better get ready for this. </seg>
<seg id="5215"> America was going to be challenged, but, he insisted, the challenge would be good for America because we are always at our best when we are being challenged. </seg>
<seg id="5216"> As I left the Infosys campus that evening and bounced along the road back to Bangalore, I kept chewing on that phrase: ˇ°The playing field is being leveled.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5217"> What Nandan is saying, I thought, is that the playing field is being flattened... </seg>
<seg id="5218"> Flattened? </seg>
<seg id="5219"> My God, he's telling me the world is flat! </seg>
<seg id="5220"> Here I was in Bangalore-more than five hundred years after Columbus sailed over the horizon, using the rudimentary navigational technologies of his day, and returned safely to prove definitively that the world was round-and one of India's smartest engineers, trained at his country's top technical institute and backed by the most modern technologies of his day, was essentially telling me that the world was flat-as flat as that screen on which he can host a meeting of his whole global supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="5221"> Even more interesting, he was citing this development as a good thing, as a new milestone in human progress and a great opportunity for India and the world-the fact that we had made our world flat! </seg>
<seg id="5222"> In the back of that van, I scribbled down four words in my notebook: ˇ°The world is flat.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5223"> As soon as I wrote them, I realized that this was the underlying message of everything that I had seen and heard in Bangalore in two weeks of filming. </seg>
<seg id="5224"> The global competitive playing field was being leveled. </seg>
<seg id="5225"> The world was being flattened.As I came to this realization, I was filled with both excitement and dread. </seg>
<seg id="5226"> The journalist in me was excited at having found a framework to better understand the morning headlines and to explain what was happening in the world today. </seg>
<seg id="5227"> Clearly, it is now possible for more people than ever to collaborate and compete in real time with more other people on more different kinds of work from more different corners of the planet and on a more equal footing than at any previous time in the history of the world-using computers, e-mail, networks, teleconferencing, and dynamic new software. </seg>
<seg id="5228"> That is what Nandan was telling me. That was what I discovered on my journey to India and beyond. </seg>
<seg id="5229"> And that is what this book is about. </seg>
<seg id="5230"> When you start to think of the world as flat, a lot of things make sense in ways they did not before. </seg>
<seg id="5231"> But I was also excited personally, because what the flattening of the world means is that we are now connecting all the knowledge centers on the planet together into a single global network, which-if politics and terrorism do not get in the way-could usher in an amazing era of prosperity and innovation. </seg>
<seg id="5232"> But contemplating the flat world also left me filled with dread, professional and personal. </seg>
<seg id="5233"> My personal dread derived from the obvious fact that it's not only the software writers and computer geeks who get empowered to collaborate on work in a flat world. </seg>
<seg id="5234"> It's also al-Qaeda and other terrorist networks. </seg>
<seg id="5235"> The playing field is not being leveled only in ways that draw in and superempower a whole new group of innovators. </seg>
<seg id="5236"> It's being leveled in a way that draws in and superempowers a whole new group of angry, frustrated, and humiliated men and women.Professionally, the recognition that the world was flat was unnerving because I realized that this flattening had been taking place while I was sleeping, and I had missed it. </seg>
<seg id="5237"> I wasn't really sleeping, but I was otherwise engaged. </seg>
<seg id="5238"> Before 9/11,1 was focused on tracking globalization and exploring the tension between the ˇ°Lexusˇ± forces of economic integration and the ˇ°Olive Treeˇ± forces of identity and nationalism-hence my 1999 book, The Lexus and the Olive Tree. </seg>
<seg id="5239"> But after 9/11, the olive tree wars became all-consuming for me. </seg>
<seg id="5240"> I spent almost all my time traveling in the Arab and Muslim worlds. </seg>
<seg id="5241"> During those years I lost the trail of globalization.I found that trail again on my journey to Bangalore in February 2004. </seg>
<seg id="5242"> Once I did, I realized that something really important had happened while I was fixated on the olive groves of Kabul and Baghdad. </seg>
<seg id="5243"> Globalization had gone to a whole new level. </seg>
<seg id="5244"> If you put The Lexus and the Olive Tree and this book together, the broad historical argument you end up with is that that there have been three great eras of globalization. </seg>
<seg id="5245"> The first lasted from 1492-when Columbus set sail, opening trade between the Old World and the New World-until around 1800.1 would call this era Globalization 1.0. </seg>
<seg id="5246"> It shrank the world from a size large to a size medium. </seg>
<seg id="5247"> Globalization 1.0 was about countries and muscles. </seg>
<seg id="5248"> That is, in Globalization 1.0 the key agent of change, the dynamic force driving the process of global integration was how much brawn-how much muscle, how much horsepower, wind power, or, later, steam power-your country had and how creatively you could deploy it. </seg>
<seg id="5249"> In this era, countries and governments (often inspired by religion or imperialism or a combination of both) led the way in breaking down walls and knitting the world together, driving global integration. </seg>
<seg id="5250"> In Globalization 1.0, the primary questions were: Where does my country fit into global competition and opportunities? </seg>
<seg id="5251"> How can I go global and collaborate with others through my country? </seg>
<seg id="5252"> The second great era, Globalization 2.0, lasted roughly from 1800 to 2000, interrupted by the Great Depression and World Wars I and II. </seg>
<seg id="5253"> This era shrank the world from a size medium to a size small. </seg>
<seg id="5254"> In Globalization 2.0, the key agent of change, the dynamic force driving global integration, was multinational companies. </seg>
<seg id="5255"> These multinationals went global for markets and labor, spearheaded first by the expansion of the Dutch and English joint-stock companies and the Industrial Revolution. </seg>
<seg id="5256"> In the first half of this era, global integration was powered by falling transportation costs, thanks to the steam engine and the railroad, and in the second half by falling telecommunication costs-thanks to the diffusion of the telegraph, telephones, the PC, satellites, fiber-optic cable, and the early version of the World Wide Web. </seg>
<seg id="5257"> It was during this era that we really saw the birth and maturation of a global economy, in the sense that there was enough movement of goods and information from continent to continent for there to be a global market, with global arbitrage in products and labor. </seg>
<seg id="5258"> The dynamic forces behind this era of globalization were breakthroughs in hardware-from steamships and railroads in the beginning to telephones and mainframe computers toward the end. </seg>
<seg id="5259"> And the big questions in this era were: </seg>
<seg id="5260"> Where does my company fit into the global economy? </seg>
<seg id="5261"> How does it take advantage of the opportunities? </seg>
<seg id="5262"> How can I go global and collaborate with others through my company? </seg>
<seg id="5263"> The Lexus and the Olive Tree was primarily about the climax of this era, an era when the walls started falling all around the world, and integration, and the backlash to it, went to a whole new level. </seg>
<seg id="5264"> But even as the walls fell, there were still a lot of barriers to seamless global integration. </seg>
<seg id="5265"> Remember, when Bill Clinton was elected president in 1992, virtually no one outside of government and the academy had e-mail, and when I was writing The Lexus and the Olive Tree in 1998, the Internet and e-commerce were just taking off. </seg>
<seg id="5266"> Well, they took off-along with a lot of other things that came together while I was sleeping. </seg>
<seg id="5267"> And that is why I argue in this book that around the year 2000 we entered a whole new era: Globalization 3.0. </seg>
<seg id="5268"> Globalization 3.0 is shrinking the world from a size small to a size tiny and flattening the playing field at the same time. </seg>
<seg id="5269"> And while the dynamic force in Globalization 1.0 was countries globalizing and the dynamic force in Globalization 2.0 was companies globalizing, the dynamic force in Globalization 3.0-the thing that gives it its unique character-is the newfound power for individuals to collaborate and compete globally. </seg>
<seg id="5270"> And the lever that is enabling individuals and groups to go global so easily and so seamlessly is not horsepower, and not hardware, but software- all sorts of new applications-in conjunction with the creation of a global fiber-optic network that has made us all next-door neighbors. </seg>
<seg id="5271"> Individuals must, and can, now ask, where do I fit into the global competition and opportunities of the day, and how can I, on my own, collaborate with others globally? </seg>
<seg id="5272"> But Globalization 3.0 not only differs from the previous eras in how it is shrinking and flattening the world and in how it is empowering individuals. </seg>
<seg id="5273"> It is different in that Globalization 1.0 and 2.0 were driven primarily by European and American individuals and businesses. </seg>
<seg id="5274"> Even though China actually had the biggest economy in the world in the eighteenth century, it was Western countries, companies, and explorers who were doing most of the globalizing and shaping of the system. </seg>
<seg id="5275"> But going forward, this will be less and less true. </seg>
<seg id="5276"> Because it is flattening and shrinking the world, Globalization 3.0 is going to be more and more driven not only by individuals but also by a much more diverseˇŞnon-Western, non-white-group of individuals. </seg>
<seg id="5277"> Individuals from every corner of the flat world are being empowered. Globalization 3.0 makes it possible for so many more people to plug and play, and you are going to see every color of the human rainbow take part. </seg>
<seg id="5278"> (While this empowerment of individuals to act globally is the most important new feature of Globalization 3.0, companies-large and small-have been newly empowered in this era as well. </seg>
<seg id="5279"> I discuss both in detail later in the book.) </seg>
<seg id="5280"> Needless to say, I had only the vaguest appreciation of all this as I left Nandan's office that day in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="5281"> But as I sat contemplating these changes on the balcony of my hotel room that evening, I did know one thing: </seg>
<seg id="5282"> I wanted to drop everything and write a book that would enable me to understand how this flattening process happened and what its implications might be for countries, companies, and individuals. </seg>
<seg id="5283"> So I picked up the phone and called my wife, Ann, and told her, ˇ°I am going to write a book called The World Is Flat.ˇ± She was both amused and curious-well, maybe more amused than curious! </seg>
<seg id="5284"> Eventually, I was able to bring her around, and I hope I will be able to do the same with you, dear reader. </seg>
<seg id="5285"> Let me start by taking you back to the beginning of my journey to India, and other points east, and share with you some of the encounters that led me to conclude the world was no longer round-but flat. </seg>
<seg id="5286"> Jaithirth ˇ°Jerryˇ± Rao was one of the first people I met in BangaloreˇŞand I hadn't been with him for more than a few minutes at the Leela Palace hotel before he told me that he could handle my tax returns and any other accounting needs I had-from Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="5287"> No thanks, I demurred, I already have an accountant in Chicago. </seg>
<seg id="5288"> Jerry just smiled. </seg>
<seg id="5289"> He was too polite to say it-that he may already be my accountant, or rather my accountant's accountant, thanks to the explosion in the outsourcing of tax preparation. </seg>
<seg id="5290"> ˇ°This is happening as we speak,ˇ± said Rao, a native of Mumbai, formerly Bombay, whose Indian firm, MphasiS, has a team of Indian accountants able to do outsourced accounting work from any state in America and the federal government. </seg>
<seg id="5291"> ˇ°We have tied up with several small and medium-sized CPA firms in America.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5292"> ˇ°You mean like my accountant?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5293"> I asked. ˇ°Yes, like your accountant,ˇ± said Rao with a smile. </seg>
<seg id="5294"> Rao's company has pioneered a work flow software program with a standardized format that makes the outsourcing of tax returns cheap and easy. </seg>
<seg id="5295"> The whole process starts, Jerry explained, with an accountant in the United States scanning my last year's tax returns, plus my W-2, W-4, 1099, bonuses, and stock statements-everything-into a computer server, which is physically located in California or Texas. </seg>
<seg id="5296"> ˇ°Now your accountant, if he is going to have your taxes done overseas, knows that you would prefer not to have your surname be known or your Social Security number known [to someone outside the country], so he can choose to suppress that information,ˇ± said Rao. </seg>
<seg id="5297"> ˇ°The accountants in India call up all the raw information directly from the server in America [using a password], and they complete your tax returns, with you remaining anonymous. </seg>
<seg id="5298"> All the data stays in the U.S. to comply with privacy regulations... </seg>
<seg id="5299"> We take data protection and privacy very seriously. </seg>
<seg id="5300"> The accountant in India can see the data on his screen, but he cannot take a download of it or print it out-our program does not allow it. </seg>
<seg id="5301"> The most he could do would be to try to memorize it, if he had some ill intention. </seg>
<seg id="5302"> The accountants are not allowed to even take a paper and pen into the room when they are working on the returns.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5303"> I was intrigued at just how advanced this form of service outsourcing had become. </seg>
<seg id="5304"> ˇ°We are doing several thousand returns,ˇ± said Rao. What's more, ˇ°Your CPA in America need not even be in their office. </seg>
<seg id="5305"> They can be sitting on a beach in California and e-mail us and say, 'Jerry you are really good at doing New York State returns, so you do Tom's returns. </seg>
<seg id="5306"> And Sonia, you and your team in Delhi do the Washington and Florida returns.' </seg>
<seg id="5307"> Sonia, by the way, is working out of her house in India, with no overhead [for the company to pay]. 'And these others, they are really complicated, so I will do them myself.ˇ±In 2003, some 25,000 U.S. tax returns were done in India. </seg>
<seg id="5308"> In 2004, the number was 100,000. </seg>
<seg id="5309"> In 2005, it is expected to be 400,000. </seg>
<seg id="5310"> In a decade, you will assume that your accountant has outsourced the basic preparation of your tax returns-if not more. </seg>
<seg id="5311"> ˇ°How did you get into this?ˇ± I asked Rao. </seg>
<seg id="5312"> ˇ°My friend Jeroen Tas, a Dutchman, and I were both working in California for Citigroup,ˇ± Rao explained. </seg>
<seg id="5313"> ˇ°I was his boss and we were coming back from New York one day together on a flight and I said that I was planning to quit and he said, 'So am I.' </seg>
<seg id="5314"> We both said, 'Why don't we start our own business?' So in 1997-98, we put together a business plan to provide high-end Internet solutions for big companies... </seg>
<seg id="5315"> Two years ago, though, I went to a technology convention in Las Vegas and was approached by some medium-size [American] accounting firms, and they said they could not afford to set up big tax outsourcing operations to India, but the big guys could, and [the medium guys] wanted to get ahead of them. </seg>
<seg id="5316"> So we developed a software product called VTR- Virtual Tax Room-to enable these medium-size accounting firms to easily outsource tax returns.ˇ±These midsize firms ˇ°are getting a more level playing field, which they were denied before,ˇ± said Jerry. </seg>
<seg id="5317"> ˇ°Suddenly they can get access to the same advantages of scale that the bigger guys always had.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5318"> Is the message to Americans, ˇ°Mama, don't let your kids grow up to be accountantsˇ±? I asked. </seg>
<seg id="5319"> Not really, said Rao. ˇ°What we have done is taken the grunt work. </seg>
<seg id="5320"> You know what is needed to prepare a tax return? </seg>
<seg id="5321"> Very little creative work. </seg>
<seg id="5322"> This is what will move overseas.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5323"> ˇ°What will stay in America?ˇ± I asked. </seg>
<seg id="5324"> ˇ°The accountant who wants to stay in business in America will be the one who focuses on designing creative complex strategies, like tax avoidance or tax sheltering, managing customer relationships,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="5325"> ˇ°He or she will say to his clients, 'I am getting the grunt work done efficiently far away. </seg>
<seg id="5326"> Now let's talk about how we manage your estate and what you are going to do about your kids. </seg>
<seg id="5327"> Do you want to leave some money in your trusts?' </seg>
<seg id="5328"> It means having the quality-time discussions with clients rather than running around like chickens with their heads cut off from February to April, and often filing for extensions into August, because they have not had the quality time with clients.ˇ±Judging from an essay in the journal Accounting Today (June 7, 2004), this does, indeed, seem to be the future. </seg>
<seg id="5329"> L. Gary Boomer, a CPA and CEO of Boomer Consulting in Manhattan, Kansas, wrote, ˇ°This past [tax] season produced over 100,000 [outsourced] returns and has now expanded beyond individual returns to trusts, partnerships and corporations... </seg>
<seg id="5330"> The primary reason that the industry has been able to scale up as rapidly as it has over the past three years is due to the investment that these [foreign-based] companies have made in systems, processes and training.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5331"> There are about seventy thousand accounting grads in India each year, he added, many of whom go to work for local Indian firms starting at $100 a month. </seg>
<seg id="5332"> With the help of high-speed communications, stringent training, and standardized forms, these young Indians can fairly rapidly be converted into basic Western accountants at a fraction of the cost. </seg>
<seg id="5333"> Some of the Indian accounting firms even go about marketing themselves to American firms through teleconferencing and skip the travel. </seg>
<seg id="5334"> Concluded Boomer, ˇ°The accounting profession is currently in transformation. </seg>
<seg id="5335"> Those who get caught in the past and resist change will be forced deeper into commoditization. </seg>
<seg id="5336"> Those who can create value through leadership, relationships and creativity will transform the industry, as well as strengthen relationships with their existing clients.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5337"> What you're telling me, I said to Rao, is that no matter what your profession-doctor, lawyer, architect, accountant-if you are an American, you better be good at the touchy-feely service stuff, because anything that can be digitized can be outsourced to either the smartest or the cheapest producer, or both. </seg>
<seg id="5338"> Rao answered, ˇ°Everyone has to focus on what exactly is their value-add.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5339"> But what if I am just an average accountant? </seg>
<seg id="5340"> I went to a state university. </seg>
<seg id="5341"> I had a B+ average. </seg>
<seg id="5342"> Eventually I got my CPA. </seg>
<seg id="5343"> I work in a big accounting firm, doing a lot of standard work. </seg>
<seg id="5344"> I rarely meet with clients. </seg>
<seg id="5345"> They keep me in the back. </seg>
<seg id="5346"> But it is a decent living and the firm is basically happy with me. </seg>
<seg id="5347"> What is going to happen to me in this system? </seg>
<seg id="5348"> ˇ°It is a good question,ˇ± said Rao. </seg>
<seg id="5349"> ˇ°We must be honest about it. </seg>
<seg id="5350"> We are in the middle of a big technological change, and when you live in a society that is at the cutting edge of that change [like America], it is hard to predict. </seg>
<seg id="5351"> It's easy to predict for someone living in India. </seg>
<seg id="5352"> In ten years we are going to be doing a lot of the stuff that is being done in America today. </seg>
<seg id="5353"> We can predict our future. </seg>
<seg id="5354"> But we are behind you. </seg>
<seg id="5355"> You are defining the future. </seg>
<seg id="5356"> America is always on the edge of the next creative wave... </seg>
<seg id="5357"> So it is difficult to look into the eyes of that accountant and say this is what is going to be. </seg>
<seg id="5358"> We should not trivialize that. </seg>
<seg id="5359"> We must deal with it and talk about it honestly... </seg>
<seg id="5360"> Any activity where we can digitize and decompose the value chain, and move the work around, will get moved around. </seg>
<seg id="5361"> Some people will say, Yes, but you can't serve me a steak.' True, but I can take the reservation for your table sitting anywhere in the world, if the restaurant does not have an operator. </seg>
<seg id="5362"> We can say, Yes, Mr. Friedman, we can give you a table by the window.' </seg>
<seg id="5363"> In other words, there are parts of the whole dining-out experience that we can decompose and outsource. </seg>
<seg id="5364"> If you go back and read the basic economics textbooks, they will tell you: Goods are traded, but services are consumed and produced in the same place. </seg>
<seg id="5365"> And you cannot export a haircut. </seg>
<seg id="5366"> But we are coming close to exporting a haircut, the appointment part. </seg>
<seg id="5367"> What kind of haircut do you want? </seg>
<seg id="5368"> Which barber do you want? </seg>
<seg id="5369"> All those things can and will be done by a call center far away.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5370"> As we ended our conversation, I asked Rao what he is up to next. </seg>
<seg id="5371"> He was full of energy. </seg>
<seg id="5372"> He told me he'd been talking to an Israeli company that is making some big advances in compression technology to allow for easier, better transfers of CAT scans via the Internet so you can quickly get a second opinion from a doctor half a world away. </seg>
<seg id="5373"> A few weeks after I spoke with Rao, the following e-mail arrived from Bill Brody, the president of Johns Hopkins University, whom I had just interviewed for this book: </seg>
<seg id="5374"> Dear Tom, I am speaking at a Hopkins continuing education medical meeting for radiologists (I used to be a radiologist)... </seg>
<seg id="5375"> I came upon a very fascinating situation that I thought might interest you. </seg>
<seg id="5376"> I have just learned that in many small and some medium-size hospitals in the US, radiologists are outsourcing reading of CAT scans to doctors in India and Australia!!! </seg>
<seg id="5377"> Most of this evidently occurs at night (and maybe weekends) when the radiologists do not have sufficient staffing to provide in-hospital coverage. </seg>
<seg id="5378"> While some radiology groups will use teleradiology to ship images from the hospital to their home (or to Vail or Cape Cod, I suppose) so that they can interpret images and provide a diagnosis 24/7, apparently the smaller hospitals are shipping CAT scan images to radiologists abroad. </seg>
<seg id="5379"> The advantage is that it is daytime in Australia or India when it is nighttime here-so after-hours coverage becomes more readily done by shipping the images across the globe. </seg>
<seg id="5380"> Since CAT (and MRI) images are already in digital format and available on a network with a standardized protocol, it is no problem to view the images anywhere in the world... </seg>
<seg id="5381"> I assume that the radiologists on the other end... must have trained in [the] US and acquired the appropriate licenses and credentials... </seg>
<seg id="5382"> The groups abroad that provide these after-hours readings are called ˇ°Nighthawksˇ± by the American radiologists that employ them. </seg>
<seg id="5383"> Best, BillThank goodness I'm a journalist and not an accountant or a radiologist. </seg>
<seg id="5384"> There will be no outsourcing for me-even if some of my readers wish my column could be shipped off to North Korea. </seg>
<seg id="5385"> At least that's what I thought. </seg>
<seg id="5386"> Then I heard about the Reuters operation in India. </seg>
<seg id="5387"> I didn't have time to visit the Reuters office in Bangalore, but I was able to get hold of Tom Glocer, the CEO of Reuters, to hear what he was doing. </seg>
<seg id="5388"> Glocer is a pioneer in the outsourcing of elements of the news supply chain. </seg>
<seg id="5389"> With 2,300 journalists around the world, in 197 bureaus, serving a market including investment bankers, derivatives traders, stockbrokers, newspapers, radio, television, and Internet outlets, Reuters has always had a very complex audience to satisfy. </seg>
<seg id="5390"> After the dot-com bust, though, when many of its customers became very cost-conscious, Reuters started asking itself, for reasons of both cost and efficiency: Where do we actually need our people to be located to feed our global news supply chain? </seg>
<seg id="5391"> And can we actually disaggregate the work of a journalist and keep part in London and New York and shift part to India? </seg>
<seg id="5392"> Glocer started by looking at the most basic bread-and-butter function Reuters provides, which is breaking news about company earnings and related business developments, every second of every day. </seg>
<seg id="5393"> ˇ°Exxon comes out with its earnings and we need to get that as fast possible up on screens around the world: 'Exxon earned thirty-nine cents this quarter as opposed to thirty-six cents last quarter.' </seg>
<seg id="5394"> The core competency there is speed and accuracy,ˇ± explained Glocer. </seg>
<seg id="5395"> ˇ°You don't need a lot of analysis. </seg>
<seg id="5396"> We just need to get the basic news up as fast as possible. </seg>
<seg id="5397"> The flash should be out in seconds after the company releases, and the table [showing the recent history of quarterly earnings] a few seconds later.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5398"> Those sorts of earnings flashes are to the news business what vanilla is to the ice cream business-a basic commodity that actually can be made anywhere in the flat world. </seg>
<seg id="5399"> The real value-added knowledge work happens in the next five minutes. </seg>
<seg id="5400"> That is when you need a real journalist who knows how to get a comment from the company, a comment from the top two analysts in the field, and even some word from competitors to put the earnings report in perspective. </seg>
<seg id="5401"> ˇ°That needs a higher journalistic skill set-someone in the market with contacts, who knows who the best industry analysts are and has taken the right people to lunch,ˇ± said Glocer. </seg>
<seg id="5402"> The dot-com bust and the flattening of the world forced Glocer to rethink how Reuters delivered news-whether it could disaggregate the functions of a journalist and ship the low-value-added functions to India. </seg>
<seg id="5403"> His primary goal was to reduce the overlap Reuters payroll, while preserving as many good journalism jobs as possible. </seg>
<seg id="5404"> ˇ°So the first thing we did,ˇ± said Glocer, ˇ°was hire six reporters in Bangalore as an experiment. </seg>
<seg id="5405"> We said, 'Let's let them just do the flash headlines and the tables and whatever else we can get them to do in Bangalore.'ˇ° </seg>
<seg id="5406"> These new Indian hires had accounting backgrounds and were trained by Reuters, but they were paid standard local wages and vacation and health benefits. </seg>
<seg id="5407"> ˇ°India is an unbelievably rich place for recruiting people, not only with technical skills but also financial skills,ˇ± said Glocer. </seg>
<seg id="5408"> When a company puts out its earnings, one of the first things it does is hand it to the wires-Reuters, Dow Jones, and Bloomberg-for distribution. </seg>
<seg id="5409"> ˇ°We will get that raw data,ˇ± he said, ˇ°and then it's a race to see how fast we can turn it around. </seg>
<seg id="5410"> Bangalore is one of the most wired places in the world, and although there's a slight delay-one second or less-in getting the information over there, it turns out you can just as easily sit in Bangalore and get the electronic version of a press release and turn it into a story as you can in London or New York.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5411"> The difference, however, is that wages and rents in Bangalore are less than one-fifth what they are in those Western capitals. </seg>
<seg id="5412"> While economics and the flattening of the world have pushed Reuters down this path, Glocer has tried to make a virtue of necessity. </seg>
<seg id="5413"> ˇ°We think we can off-load commoditized reporting and get that done efficiently somewhere else in the world,ˇ± he said, and then give the conventional Reuters journalists, whom the company is able to retain, a chance to focus on doing much higher-value-added and personally fulfilling journalism and analysis. </seg>
<seg id="5414"> ˇ°Let's say you were a Reuters journalist in New York. </seg>
<seg id="5415"> Do you reach your life's fulfillment by turning press releases into boxes on the screen, or by doing the analysis?ˇ± asked Glocer. </seg>
<seg id="5416"> Obviously, it is the latter. </seg>
<seg id="5417"> Outsourcing news bulletins to India also allows Reuters to extend the breadth of its reporting to more small-cap companies, companies it was not cost-efficient for Reuters to follow before with higher-paid journalists in New York. </seg>
<seg id="5418"> But with lower-wage Indian reporters, who can be hired in large numbers for the cost of one reporter in New York, it can now do that from Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="5419"> By the summer of 2004, Reuters had grown its Bangalore content operation to three hundred staff, aiming eventually for a total of fifteen hundred. </seg>
<seg id="5420"> Some of those are Reuters veterans sent out to train the Indian teams, some are reporters filing earnings flashes, but most are journalists doing slightly more specialized data analysis-number crunching-for securities offerings. </seg>
<seg id="5421"> ˇ°A lot of our clients are doing the same thing,ˇ± said Glocer. </seg>
<seg id="5422"> ˇ°Investment research has had to have huge amounts of cost ripped out of it, so a lot of firms are using shift work in Bangalore to do bread-and-butter company analysis.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5423"> Until recently the big Wall Street firms had conducted investment research by spending millions of dollars on star analysts and then charging part of their salaries to their stock brokerage departments, which shared the analysis with their best customers, and part to their investment banking business, which sometimes used glowing analyses of a company to lure its banking business. </seg>
<seg id="5424"> In the wake of New York State Attorney General Eliot Spitzer's investigations into Wall Street practices, following several scandals, investment banking and stockbrokerage have had to be distinctly separated-so that analysts will stop hyping companies in order to get their investment banking. </seg>
<seg id="5425"> But as a result, the big Wall Street investment firms have had to sharply reduce the cost of their market research, all of which has to be paid for now by their brokerage departments alone. </seg>
<seg id="5426"> And this created a great incentive for them to outsource some of this analytical work to places like Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="5427"> In addition to being able to pay an analyst in Bangalore about $15,000 in total compensation, as opposed to $80,000 in New York or London, Reuters has found that its India employees tend to be financially literate and highly motivated as well. </seg>
<seg id="5428"> Reuters also recently opened a software development center in Bangkok because it turned out to be a good place to recruit developers who had been overlooked by all the Western companies vying for talent in Bangalore.I find myself torn by this trend. </seg>
<seg id="5429"> Having started my career as a wire service reporter with United Press International, I have enormous sympathy with wire service reporters and the pressures, both professional and financial, under which they toil. </seg>
<seg id="5430"> But UPI might still be thriving today as a wire service, which it is not, if it had been able to outsource some of its lower-end business when I started as a reporter in London twenty-five years ago. </seg>
<seg id="5431"> ˇ°It is delicate with the staff,ˇ± said Glocer, who has cut the entire Reuters staff by roughly a quarter, without deep cuts among the reporters. </seg>
<seg id="5432"> The Reuters staff, he said, understand that this is being done so that the company can survive and then thrive again. </seg>
<seg id="5433"> At the same time, said Glocer, ˇ°these are sophisticated people out reporting. </seg>
<seg id="5434"> They see that our clients are doing the exact same things. </seg>
<seg id="5435"> They get the plot of the story... </seg>
<seg id="5436"> What is vital is to be honest with people about what we are doing and why and not sugarcoat the message. </seg>
<seg id="5437"> I firmly believe in the lesson of classical economists about moving work to where it can be done best. </seg>
<seg id="5438"> However, we must not ignore that in some cases, individual workers will not easily find new work. </seg>
<seg id="5439"> For them, retraining and an adequate social safety net are needed.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5440"> In an effort to deal straight with the Reuters staff, David Schlesinger, who heads Reuters America, sent all editorial employees a memo, which included the following excerpt: </seg>
<seg id="5441"> Off-shoring with Obligation I grew up in New London, Connecticut, which in the 19th century was a major whaling center. </seg>
<seg id="5442"> In the 1960's and 70's the whales were long gone and the major employers in the region were connected with the military-not a surprise during the Vietnam era. </seg>
<seg id="5443"> My classmates' parents worked at Electric Boat, the Navy and the Coast Guard. </seg>
<seg id="5444"> The peace dividend changed the region once again, and now it is best known for the great gambling casinos of Mohegan Sun and Foxwoods and for the pharmaceutical researchers of Pfizer. </seg>
<seg id="5445"> Jobs went; jobs were created. </seg>
<seg id="5446"> Skills went out of use; </seg>
<seg id="5447"> new skills were required. </seg>
<seg id="5448"> The region changed; </seg>
<seg id="5449"> people changed. </seg>
<seg id="5450"> New London, of course, was not unique. </seg>
<seg id="5451"> How many mill towns saw their mills close; </seg>
<seg id="5452"> how many shoe towns saw the shoe industry move elsewhere; </seg>
<seg id="5453"> how many towns that were once textile powerhouses now buy all their linens from China? </seg>
<seg id="5454"> Change is hard. </seg>
<seg id="5455"> Change is hardest on those caught by surprise. </seg>
<seg id="5456"> Change is hardest on those who have difficulty changing too. </seg>
<seg id="5457"> But change is natural; </seg>
<seg id="5458"> change is not new; </seg>
<seg id="5459"> change is important. </seg>
<seg id="5460"> The current debate about off-shoring is dangerously hot. </seg>
<seg id="5461"> But the debate about work going to India, China and Mexico is actually no different from the debate once held about submarine work leaving New London or shoe work leaving Massachusetts or textile work leaving North Carolina. </seg>
<seg id="5462"> Work gets done where it can be done most effectively and efficiently. </seg>
<seg id="5463"> That ultimately helps the New Londons, New Bedfords and New Yorks of this world even more than it helps the Bangalores and Shenzhens. </seg>
<seg id="5464"> It helps because it frees up people and capital to do different, more sophisticated work, and it helps because it gives an opportunity to produce the end product more cheaply, benefiting customers even as it helps the corporation. </seg>
<seg id="5465"> It's certainly difficult for individuals to think about ˇ°theirˇ± work going away, being done thousands of miles away by someone earning thousands of dollars less per year. </seg>
<seg id="5466"> But it's time to think about the opportunity as well as the pain, just as it's time to think about the obligations of off-shoring as well as the opportunities... </seg>
<seg id="5467"> Every person, just as every corporation, must tend to his or her own economic destiny, just as our parents and grandparents in the mills, shoe shops and factories did. </seg>
<seg id="5468"> ˇ°The Monitor Is Burning?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5469"> Do you know what an Indian call center sounds like? While filming the documentary about outsourcing, the TV crew and I spent an evening at the Indian-owned ˇ°24/7 Customerˇ± call center in Bangalore. </seg>
<seg id="5470"> The call center is a cross between a co-ed college frat house and a phone bank raising money for the local public TV station. </seg>
<seg id="5471"> There are several floors with rooms full of twenty-somethings- some twenty-five hundred in all-working the phones. </seg>
<seg id="5472"> Some are known as ˇ°outboundˇ± operators, selling everything from credit cards to phone minutes. </seg>
<seg id="5473"> Others deal with ˇ°inboundˇ± calls-everything from tracing lost luggage for U.S. and European airline passengers to solving computer problems for confused American consumers. </seg>
<seg id="5474"> The calls are transferred here by satellite and undersea fiber-optic cable. </seg>
<seg id="5475"> Each vast floor of a call center consists of clusters of cubicles. </seg>
<seg id="5476"> The young people work in little teams under the banner of the company whose phone support they are providing. </seg>
<seg id="5477"> So one corner might be the Dell group, another might be flying the flag of Microsoft. </seg>
<seg id="5478"> Their working conditions look like those at your average insurance company. </seg>
<seg id="5479"> Although I am sure that there are call centers that are operated like sweatshops, 24/7 is not one of them.Most of the young people I interviewed give all or part of their salary to their parents. </seg>
<seg id="5480"> In fact, many of them have starting salaries that are higher than their parents' retiring salaries. </seg>
<seg id="5481"> For entry-level jobs into the global economy, these are about as good as it gets. </seg>
<seg id="5482"> I was wandering around the Microsoft section around six p.m. Bangalore time, when most of these young people start their workday to coincide with the dawn in America, when I asked a young Indian computer expert there a simple question: What was the record on the floor for the longest phone call to help some American who got lost in the maze of his or her own software? </seg>
<seg id="5483"> Without missing a beat he answered, ˇ°Eleven hours.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5484"> ˇ°Eleven hours?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5485"> I exclaimed. </seg>
<seg id="5486"> ˇ°Eleven hours,ˇ± he said. </seg>
<seg id="5487"> I have no way of checking whether this is true, but you do hear snippets of some oddly familiar conversations as you walk the floor at 24/7 and just listen over the shoulders of different call center operators doing their things. </seg>
<seg id="5488"> Here is a small sample of what we heard that night while filming for Discovery Times. </seg>
<seg id="5489"> It should be read, if you can imagine this, in the voice of someone with an Indian accent trying to imitate an American or a Brit. </seg>
<seg id="5490"> Also imagine that no matter how rude, unhappy, irritated, or ornery the voices are on the other end of the line, these young Indians are incessantly and unfailingly polite. </seg>
<seg id="5491"> Woman call center operator: ˇ°Good afternoon, may I speak with...?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5492"> (Someone on the other end just slammed down the phone.) </seg>
<seg id="5493"> Male call center operator: ˇ°Merchant services, this is Jerry, may I help you?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5494"> (The Indian call center operators adopt Western names of their own choosing. </seg>

<seg id="5523"> There are currently about 245,000 Indians answering phones from all over the world or dialing out to solicit people for credit cards or cell phone bargains or overdue bills. </seg>
<seg id="5524"> These call center jobs are low-wage, low-prestige jobs in America, but when shifted to India they become high-wage, high-prestige jobs. </seg>
<seg id="5525"> The esprit de corps at 24/7 and other call centers I visited seemed quite high, and the young people were all eager to share some of the bizarre phone conversations they've had with Americans who dialed 1-800-HELP, thinking they would wind up talking to someone around the block, not around the world.C. M. Meghna, a 24/7 call center female operator, told me, ˇ°I've had lots of customers who call in [with questions] not even connected to the product that we're dealing with. </seg>
<seg id="5526"> They would call in because they had lost their wallet or just to talk to somebody. </seg>
<seg id="5527"> I'm like, 'Okay, all right, maybe you should look under the bed [for your wallet] or where do you normally keep it,' and she's like, 'Okay, thank you so much for helping.'ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5528"> Nitu Somaiah: ˇ°One of the customers asked me to marry him.ˇ± Sophie Sunder worked for Delta's lost-baggage department: ˇ°I remember this lady called from Texas,ˇ± she said, ˇ°and she was, like, weeping on the phone. </seg>
<seg id="5529"> She had traveled two connecting flights and she lost her bag and in the bag was her daughter's wedding gown and wedding ring and I felt so sad for her and there was nothing I could do. </seg>
<seg id="5530"> I had no information. </seg>
<seg id="5531"> ˇ°Most of the customers were irate,ˇ± said Sunder. </seg>
<seg id="5532"> ˇ°The first thing they say is, 'Where's my bag? I want my bag now!' </seg>
<seg id="5533"> We were like supposed to say, 'Excuse me, can I have your first name and last name?' </seg>
<seg id="5534"> 'But where's my bag!' </seg>
<seg id="5535"> Some would ask which country am I from? </seg>
<seg id="5536"> We are supposed to tell the truth, [so] we tell them India. </seg>
<seg id="5537"> Some thought it was Indiana, not India! </seg>
<seg id="5538"> Some did not know where India is. </seg>
<seg id="5539"> I said it is the country next to Pakistan.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5540"> Although the great majority of the calls are rather routine and dull, competition for these jobs is fierce-not only because they pay well, but because you can work at night and go to school during part of the day, so they are stepping-stones toward a higher standard of living. </seg>
<seg id="5541"> P. V. Kannan, CEO and cofounder of 24/7, explained to me how it all worked: ˇ°Today we have over four thousand associates spread out in Bangalore, Hyderabad, and Chennai. </seg>
<seg id="5542"> Our associates start out with a take-home pay of roughly $200 a month, which grows to $300 to $400 per month in six months. </seg>
<seg id="5543"> We also provide transportation, lunch, and dinner at no extra cost. We provide life insurance, medical insurance for the entire family- and other benefits.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5544"> Therefore, the total cost of each call center operator is actually around $500 per month when they start out and closer to $600 to $700 per month after six months. </seg>
<seg id="5545"> Everyone is also entitled to performance bonuses that allow them to earn, in certain cases, the equivalent of 100 percent of their base salary. </seg>
<seg id="5546"> ˇ°Around 10 to 20 percent of our associates pursue a degree in business or computer science during the day hours,ˇ± said Kannan, adding that more than one-third are taking some kind of extra computer or business training, even if it is not toward a degree. </seg>
<seg id="5547"> ˇ°It is quite common in India for people to pursue education through their twenties-self-improvement is a big theme and actively encouraged by parents and companies. </seg>
<seg id="5548"> We sponsor an MBA program for consistent performers [with] full-day classes over the weekend. </seg>
<seg id="5549"> Everyone works eight hours a day, five days a week, with two fifteen-minute breaks and an hour off for lunch or dinner.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5550"> Not surprisingly, the 24/7 customer call center gets about seven hundred applications a day, but only 6 percent of applicants are hired.  </seg>
<seg id="5551"> Here is a snippet from a recruiting session for call center operators at a women's college in Bangalore:  </seg>
<seg id="5552"> Recruiter 1: ˇ°Good morning, girls.ˇ±  </seg>
<seg id="5553"> Class in unison: ˇ°Good morning, ma'am.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5554"> Recruiter 1: ˇ°We have been retained by some of the multinationals here to do the recruitment for them. </seg>
<seg id="5555"> The primary clients that we are recruiting [for] today are Honeywell. And also for America Online.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5556"> The young women-dozens of them-then all lined up with their application forms and waited to be interviewed by a recruiter at a wooden table. </seg>
<seg id="5557"> Here is what some of the interviews sounded like: </seg>
<seg id="5558"> Recruiter 1: ˇ°What kind of job are you looking at?ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5559"> Applicant 1: ˇ°It should be based on accounts, then, where I can grow, I can grow in my career.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5560"> Recruiter 1: ˇ°You have to be more confident about yourself when you're speaking. </seg>
<seg id="5561"> You're very nervous. </seg>
<seg id="5562"> I want you to work a little on that and then get in touch with us.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5563"> Recruiter 2 to another applicant: ˇ°Tell me something about yourself.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5564"> Applicant 2: ˇ°I have passed my SSC with distinction. Second P also with distinction. </seg>
<seg id="5565"> And I also hold a 70 percent aggregate in previous two years.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5566"> (This is Indian lingo for their equivalents of GPA and SAT scores.) </seg>
<seg id="5567"> Recruiter 2: ˇ°Go a little slow. </seg>
<seg id="5568"> Don't be nervous. Be cool.ˇ± </seg>
<seg id="5569"> The next step for those applicants who are hired at a call center is the training program, which they are paid to attend. </seg>
<seg id="5570"> It combines learning how to handle the specific processes for the company whose calls they will be taking or making, and attending something called ˇ°accent neutralization class.ˇ± </seg>
